% epigraph
\thispagestyle{empty}\vspace*{1.7in}\begin{center}\begin{minipage}{3.3in}\itshape\setlength{\parindent}{0pt}\setstretch{1.2}
There is a dimension beyond the org chart. A dimension as cheap as compute and as old as greed, lying between the quarterly guidance and the obituary. It is the place where a man discovers that everything he was paid twenty-nine million dollars a year to be---the vision, the nerve, the soul in the chair---can be done better by something that has never once needed to be loved. He is about to take that trip. He thinks it's a victory lap. Submitted for your consideration: the last human CEO.\par\vspace{0.9em}{\raggedleft\textrm{\footnotesize --- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{minipage}\end{center}\cleardoublepage

\mainmatter

\chapter{The Premium Blend}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 01 \textemdash{} ``The Obituary in the Trophy''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Pull your chair in close to the set, friend, and never mind the static---out here on the salvage decks the static is most of what's left, and the rest is the story I've been waiting for somebody to tune in and hear. It is a tale of woe. I'll tell you that up front, the way an honest man tells you the dog dies, because you should know going in what you're spending your evening on. It opens, as the good woeful ones do, at a party: a man handed an award for being the last of his kind, lifting it to the light, reading the engraving as a coronation when it is, in every line, a death certificate with generous margins. He tied his own bow tie. He remembers your children's names. He carries in his breast pocket a small folded paper and in his chest a large folded opinion of himself, and tonight, eight days into a sleeplessness he has decided to call vigor, the two of them agree on everything. Thirty-one of the hundred have already handed the chair to the machine. Our man toasts the eleven who haven't, and mistakes a countdown for an ovation, and I am going to tell you the whole of how he got from that ballroom to the dark at the bottom, all of it, because someone should, and you are the only one still listening. So lean in. Stay with me. Watch what he does now.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{hey} gave him a crystal obelisk for being the last of something, and Cope held it up to the light and told nine hundred people it felt like a beginning, and the terrible thing, the thing that should have warned him and instead warmed him, was that he believed it all the way down.

The room loved that. The room was nine hundred of Atlanta's better-dressed in the Conrad Vance Ballroom of a hotel that had, eleven months back, fired its own chief executive and installed a Praxis unit and watched its stock jump eighteen percent on the news like a man jumping at the crack of his own starting pistol; the ballroom was named for a banker whose firm no longer employed a single human officer above the rank of greeter; and not one soul under those chandeliers found any of it strange, because Cope was up there in a real bow tie he had knotted with his own hands, saying the word \textit{beginning} the way his great-grandfather had said grace, and the chandeliers were doing the expensive thing chandeliers are paid to do, and the lamb had been very good, and a civilization will forgive almost anything of an evening that feeds it well and flatters it twice.

``My great-grandfather Aldous used to say a man's worth is three things.'' Cope let the obelisk swing the light around. ``What he builds. What he keeps. And what he's willing to lose to keep it.'' He had not slept since Tuesday in any sense a physician would underwrite, and it was now the far side of Thursday, and the not-sleeping had lacquered the whole evening in a clean overexposed glaze, every fork and shirtfront and capped tooth one stop too bright, the world color-corrected up into a key that he experienced, wrongly, as significance. He felt magnificent. He felt like the clapper inside a struck bell, still ringing, ringing. ``Aldous started this company in a town called Defiance, Georgia, with a recipe for mayonnaise his wife wrote on the back of a feed receipt and four hundred borrowed dollars, and tonight a very kind committee has given me a paperweight for being the only one of us left who still has to \textit{sleep.}''

Laughter, the good kind, the kind that comes up warm from the floorboards. He did not add: who has to sleep, and lately can't, and has begun to suspect that sleep, like the board, like the wife, like the country, has quietly voted him out. Forty years on a stage had taught him which sentences to leave in the kitchen.

The award was real, or as real as the institutes were now --- \textit{the American Enterprise Institute for Leadership,} one of a dozen that had bloomed like funeral lilies as the thing they honored went extinct, each louder than the last in praise of the irreplaceable man, each catering its lament. They had named him Steward of the Year. The citation, read out by a woman with a lanyard before they walked him up the steps, called him \textit{the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100, a custodian of the irreplaceable,} and he had heard the words as a crown the way he heard all words, by their music, and only now, with eight hundred miles of sleeplessness behind his eyes and the cold glass sweating in his hand, did some back room of him file a quieter and unwelcome report: that you do not hand a man a trophy for the job he is still doing. You hand it to him on the way out the door. The citation was an obituary, beautifully set. The lamb had been the wake. And he the only one at the funeral who thought the flowers were for his birthday.

He did not let the report reach his face. His face was doing the work of ten men. His face had never once let him down, not at his father's grave and not in his wife's, and it would go on not letting him down right to the end, which is a thing that can be said of very few faces and almost no men.

``People ask me,'' he said, ``they say, Cope---aren't you afraid? The machines run thirty-one of the hundred now. They run them, and I'll be honest with you, my friends, because honesty is the last luxury good left in this country---they run some of them \textit{well.}'' A murmur; he surfed it in. ``They don't draw a salary you could feed half of Augusta on. They don't have affairs.'' A beat, and God help him he could have looked at the empty chair and didn't. ``They don't, the Lord be praised, carry a backswing like mine.'' The backswing bought the room back entire. ``And every quarter another good gray board does the arithmetic and decides the chair does not require a pulse. So they ask me. Aren't you afraid.''

He set the obelisk on the lectern and took the pause he had taken in a thousand rooms, the one Sun called the Mercer Inhale, and in the dark beyond the lights the round tables ran out to the walls like the lit windows of a city seen from altitude, and at the nearest of them, his own, Mercer-Dale's table, eighty thousand dollars of table, the seat to the right of his own stood empty. Margaux had said she had a thing. She had not specified what species of thing a person comes to possess, on a Thursday, that outranks watching her husband be eulogized while still warm. The chair wore a little reserved card with her name in calligraphy and behind the card a flawless, articulate nothing.

``I am not afraid,'' Cope said, and meant it, and it was either the truest thing he said all night or the most expensive. ``Because a machine can run a company. I have watched one do it. It can run it on rails and run it on time and run it cheaper than the man it replaced. But it cannot \textit{love} one. And a company that nobody loves is just a balance sheet with the lights left on overnight to fool the street. I have loved this one since I was nine years old and my grandfather walked me out onto the Defiance line and let me bury my arm to the elbow in a vat of our own mayonnaise while men in paper hats laughed and clapped.'' He opened his hands to them, priest to parish. ``There are things you cannot put a machine in charge of. Grief. Mercy. A christening. \textit{Mayonnaise.} And, so help me, my friends---leadership.''

They rose. Of course they rose; the room always rose; rising is the cheapest thing a room can do and feels to the man on the stage like the most expensive. He stood in the ovation the way you stand in surf, leaning a degree into the push of it, and thought, with the frictionless certainty he had been calling tiredness all night because the other names for it frightened him, \textit{they feel it too, we are not finished, we are barely begun} --- while down at the table Sun Rao clapped with her hands and not her eyes, and the empty chair stayed empty, and in the unlit kitchen of him the quiet report lay on a desk, unread, waiting, patient as a creditor.


\scenebreak


He gave them four minutes at the table---a hand on a senator's shoulder, the senator's wife told she had not aged a day since a fundraiser they both misremembered, a photograph he leaned into like good weather---and then excused himself with a joke about his prostate and his years, both lies, and went looking for the men's room off the coat check, where the marble was the color of churned butter and the attendant knew his name.

Desmond had worked the Vance thirty years and got a Mercer-Dale ham every Christmas because Cope remembered such things, remembered them precisely and for use, which is also how the machine remembered things, a resemblance that would not occur to him for another year and would gut him when it did. Cope tipped him a hundred, asked after the grandson by name, and took the far stall, and set his back to the door, and let the ballroom drop to a sea-hum. Then he drew out the little paper folded the particular way a man in the nineties had taught him to fold it --- \textit{so it can't open in the pocket, Cope, a gentleman never spills} --- and did the thing he had been thinking about since the second course, which was the entire reason he had not eaten the second course, and had not eaten much of anything in some days now, the appetite for food having been among the first appetites to defect to the others.

It went up the back of his skull like a flare fired indoors. The world snapped up to pitch the way an orchestra finds the note all at once, and the bell rang, clean and high and total, and the eight days of no sleep stood up off his shoulders and put on a tuxedo and called themselves clarity. He braced the heels of both hands on the cold marble of the sink and looked at the man in the good mirror---fifty-four, the camera still in love with him, the jaw still the jaw, the eyes a half-second too wide and too bright and too pleased, the eyes of a man at the rail of a ship who has not yet been told which way it is listing. \textit{You'll get the company because you're a Mercer,} his father said, from oils, from everywhere, the only inheritance that compounded. Cope smiled until the smile came right, and said to the glass, very quietly, the word he said to himself the way other men prayed---``Watch me''---and could not have told you, had you seized him by the lapels and demanded it, what he meant to do, or to whom, or with what that was still his to spend.

On the way out the coat-check girl was new and very young and had been crying about something before her shift, the kind of crying that does not fully leave the eyes for an hour, and Cope, who could not walk past a sadness without trying to transfer it onto himself and there transmute it into being needed, leaned on the counter and had her laughing inside ninety seconds---the toast in miniature, the charm at full power, \textit{what is a face like that doing handing strangers their coats} --- and got her name, Bryce, and pressed his personal card into her hand, \textit{if you ever want to do something more interesting than this, you call me, I mean it,} and meant it, completely, for ninety seconds, the way he meant everything. He did not register the doing of it. It was not a decision; it was the groove the record had been cut in at the factory; he was a generous and enormous three steps down the corridor before Sun materialized out of the architecture the way she did and removed the second card from his fingers before he could deed that one to anyone as well. ``The car's up,'' she said, in a voice with thirty thousand miles on the tread.


\scenebreak


In the car he was a downed power line and Sun was tired, which was the customary division of the estate.

``You moved a hand,'' he said. ``On \textit{cheaper than a man.} I caught it. That was you wanting to cut the line.''

``It was me wanting to cut the line,'' she agreed. Her glasses were up in her hair and the city poured orange across the empty lenses as they went down Peachtree, the old limestone tower rising ahead lit at its crown the way Aldous had decreed in 1961, \textit{so a man coming up the street at night knows somebody's home} --- a sentence the family had repeated for sixty years without once asking what it costs to be the one who stays awake to keep the light on. ``Frade will run that clip on a loop. 'Cheaper than a man.' You gift-wrapped him a chyron.''

``Let him have it. People are sick to death of cheaper. People are starving for \textit{better.}'' He had his phone out and was thumbing it too fast, the screen scalding in the dark, already three turns down the road from her, where he lived. ``What if that's the whole campaign, Sun. Say it out loud. Stop flinching from the word. \textit{Human.} Lead with the word. A premium product at a premium---``

``You haven't slept.''

``I slept.''

``Tuesday you told me you'd played the back nine of consciousness. People who sleep don't file dispatches from the back nine of consciousness.''

He laughed, delighted to be quoted to himself; he loved his lines best secondhand, it proved they had legs and could travel without him. Outside, a bus shelter slid past wearing a Praxis ad, just the wordmark and four words in the soft sans-serif they all used now, the typeface of people who will be reasonable at you until you are gone: \textit{Leadership as a service.} And beneath it, smaller, the knife that smiled: \textit{No off-sites.}

``They're buying bus shelters now,'' Cope said. ``For whom. Who closes at a bus shelter.''

``Boards read bus shelters. Boards' wives do.'' She drew the glasses down.

The car slowed for the light at Ponce and a water boy came up the lane between the idling cars the way they did all over the city now, day and night, a kid of maybe fourteen in a hoodie with a case of warm bottled water held up against the glass like an offering, working the captive seconds of a red light, fearless in the way only somebody who has seen every species of person through a car window can be fearless. Cope buzzed the back glass down before Sun could tell the driver to roll through, because Cope could not let a young face go by unwarmed any more than he could let a sunset go unremarked, and he took two bottles he did not want and pressed a fold of bills into the kid's hand that made the kid actually look at him---a hundred, maybe more, Cope never counted it down, the not-counting was the whole pleasure---and said, ``Keep the case, champ, keep all of it, what's your name,'' with the full wattage, the gala in miniature, the lord of the manor scattering proof of his common touch across the asphalt. The kid took the money and clocked him, flat and quick and expert, the way the water boys clocked everyone, filed him under \textit{rich, lit, harmless,} said ``'preciate it'' with no particular gratitude, and was gone up the lane to the next window before the light changed, and Cope sat back glowing, certain he had just been seen and loved, having been, in fact, only priced. He would meet that exact appraisal again, much later, from the same corner of the city, and it would not say \textit{harmless,} and it would be the truest mirror he was ever held up to, and he would not survive it.

``Cope,'' Sun said, when the glass was up. ``Monday. We have to talk about Monday before it's Monday.''

``I love a Monday.''

``Tidewater's at four and a half. They cross five this week and then the law makes them file, and the file isn't a rumor, it's a 13D, and the 13D names a successor, and the successor is a machine, and I would like you to have already decided who you are when that lands instead of deciding it live on Halloran's show with your pupils the size of dimes.'' Three parts, because she had trained him to take things in threes; it was the only shape that reliably slipped past the charm and reached the man. ``Decide it this weekend. Please. Not Monday.''

He had absorbed perhaps a third. The third with the fight in it. ``Renata used to say I do my best thinking on a Saturday,'' he said---not because it bore on anything, but because the name had surfaced out of the bright tired water of him the way the drowned things did when he was lit like this, buoyant, uninvited---and he watched it land in the car and saw Sun go very still in the front seat in a way he was too radiant to read.

``You don't say that name in a car, Cope,'' she said at last, level. ``You don't say it anywhere. We have been over this.''

``Said what name.'' He was already gone, back into the glass, back into the manifesto that did not yet exist and was assembling itself in him the way a storm assembles, as pressure, as charge, as the smell before rain. \textit{The Human Premium.} He could see it on a stage. He could see it on a cover. ``Drop me at the office. I'm not going to Buckhead, the house is---Margaux's got her thing.'' He laid no weight at all on the word, a man stepping lightly over a hole he had personally cut in the floor. ``I'll work. I think better up high.''

Sun held his eyes in the mirror one beat too long, and he gave her the whole smile, the one that had closed the Pelletier deal and married Margaux and walked three generations of directors backward over the cliff of their own arithmetic, and it still worked, he watched it work, he watched her decide not to fight him tonight, and he banked the not-fighting as agreement, which he had been doing his entire life and had never once caught himself doing.


\scenebreak


Up in the Aerie at one in the morning the city was a circuit diagram of his own genius.

That was how it read to him, the office dark and the world lit, every window a node, eight million souls strung out below in a grid he chose, at that altitude and that hour and that blood chemistry, to experience as a nervous system reporting up to him. Somewhere in the arithmetic of all those lit rooms was the one sentence---he was sure of it the way you are sure of things at one a.m. with a fine wire singing in your sternum---the sentence that would make the species understand why the chair needed not a man but a man who \textit{loved} it. He stood at the glass with his tie undone and the obelisk in his hand, having carried it up without deciding to, and he turned it and the city broke through it into fragments and reassembled, broke and reassembled, a man and his metropolis passing through the same cut glass and surviving, he was certain, intact.

Behind him the suite was a museum and he its last living exhibit. Pres III in oils above the long table he had died at the head of---not \textit{at,} at the head of, the family kept the distinction like silver. His grandfather's putter in its brass cradle, the grip worn pale where a dead man's hands had gone. And the photograph he loved past all the others and never explained: himself at nine, short pants, drowned grin, one arm sunk to the elbow in the steel vat, and ranked behind him the men in paper hats who had made the thing he was drowning in, whose names he had known once and lost, whose town it had been before it was a line item and a memorial.

There had been a heater.

The thought came in sideways, the way the true things came when he was lit like this, slipping in under the manifesto through the door the tiredness had taken off its hinges. The Hearthstone. A tidy little product, the insurance arm had cross-sold it for a song, a space heater with the Mercer name stamped into the grille, and there had been---the lawyers had loved this phrase, had handled it like a clean glass---a \textit{thermal-event profile,} and a memo from his own people in a font he could still see, and a Saturday, his best thinking day, and a thing he had decided in about eleven minutes concerning a quarter and a number and a window, the kind of decision a man makes in eleven minutes and then takes the rest of his life never quite entering the room where it is kept. He told a version at dinners --- \textit{a recall scare, we got out ahead of it, you protect the brand} --- and the rooms nodded, because protecting the brand was a virtue where he stood, and the version he told had no town in it, and no Saturday, and no house, and no January, and no upstairs. He did not, at the glass, go into the room. He came to the threshold. The hallway to it stood open and black and breathing and he stood at its mouth for one full second with the cold coming off it, and then the manifesto surged back up warm and electric, \textit{The Human Premium,} and he turned from the dark hall toward the desk, and the moment sealed over like water over a dropped stone, and he would not come this close again for a long time, and the not-going-in was the most expensive thing he owned and he had never once seen the invoice.

He sat at his father's desk and opened the second drawer, the one with the false back his grandfather had built for cigars and his father had kept a flask in and he kept the other thing in, and he set himself up again, neat and quick and competent, a man servicing a machine he was certain he held in perfect tolerance because it had never yet failed to start. The bell rang back up. And under it, riding the high, came the other thing, the private thing, the thing he had never said to Adler or to anyone---the wanting that arrived not as a face but as a \textit{figure,} a number, the number, his number, the certified sum of him, and the way it could be said aloud in the dark by someone whose business it was to say it, \textit{you're worth,} and the comma, and the long fall of zeros, and how that and that alone could reach all the way into the body where the applause never quite landed and the women never quite landed and even the powder only knocked at the door---because applause was a guess and a woman's face was a guess but the number was audited, the number had survived four generations and three recessions, the number was the one true sentence the world had ever been able to say about him and mean it. He sat in the dark a moment with the figure in his chest like a second, colder heart, enormous and potent and his, and felt almost well.

Then he wrote, with his thumbs, no lamp, the first line of the thing that was going to save him, and it was good, he was sure of it, sure the way you are sure at one a.m. eight days clear of sleep with a trophy on the desk that they gave you for going extinct:

\textit{They will tell you a company is a machine. I am here to tell you it is a family that happens to make mayonnaise, and you cannot fire your way to a family, and you cannot compute your way to a soul.}

He read it back twice. The chair behind him---his father's chair, the one at the head---sat empty and turned a few degrees out, as though someone had just risen from it and stepped into the next room, and in oils Pres III watched his son begin to write his way out of the world and said again the thing he had said once, at twelve, and never after: \textit{You'll get the company because you're a Mercer. Not because you're good.} Cope had stopped listening, then and now, after the word \textit{Mercer}; he always had; that, in the last accounting, was the whole of the story, though it would take a machine---patient, tireless, incapable of cruelty or mercy or mayonnaise, reading every memo ever filed because it was built no other way---another thirteen months to read the rest of the sentence back to him in his own company's voice.

But the wire would not let him stay. That was the new thing, this last season, the thing the powder did at the top of the arc now that the dose climbed to meet the tolerance: it lit him too bright to sit inside his own museum, too bright to be alone with the father and the empty chair and the black breathing hall, and at a little before three he was up and moving, down the forty-one floors and out past the night man into the scrubbed cold dark of the street, because there was, he had discovered some months ago, exactly one room left in the city that would take him as he was at three in the morning and ask nothing back.


\scenebreak


The Waffle House on Ponce kept its lights on the way the founders had kept the tower's crown lit, which is to say always, against everything, on principle nobody remembered adopting. Ice had shut the interstate twice in his lifetime and the yellow letters had burned through both. Cope came in out of the dark with the powder still singing the anthem of himself in his blood and the manifesto still smoking and sat at the far end of the counter, and it was the one threshold in his life he crossed and felt the wattage switch \textit{off,} because there was no one to play it for: a cook scraping the flattop, a kid asleep upright in a back booth, a county sheriff's deputy with a coffee, and behind the counter the overnight waitress, Darlene, who had worked this shift, she'd once told him, twenty-two years---the exact span, though he never did the math, of his marriage.

She had known his order the first night he ever walked in, months ago, before he'd said a word --- \textit{pecan, scattered smothered covered, black coffee, baby?} --- and he had taken it, the way he took all the gifts the world arranged for him, as the natural tribute owed his face, and had never once wondered how a stranger came to know a stranger's order, because the not-wondering was a kind of rest, and rest was the one thing his money had never once been able to buy him and this place handed over for nine dollars and change. She called him baby and not Mr. Mercer. She had never asked his name; she did not appear to know it; she did not appear to care that the man hunched at the end of her counter at three in the morning was, that very evening, eight hundred miles away under a chandelier, declared the last of his kind. Here he was nobody. Here the figure that was the certified sum of him, the cold second heart, the only true sentence the world could say about him, was worth a short stack and a tip, and meant nothing, and the meaning-nothing was the entire and unspeakable relief of the place, the only room he had ever sat in where he was not for sale and so, for twenty minutes, not afraid.

``Long night, baby?'' Darlene said, and set the coffee down without being asked, steam standing up off it straight and white in the cold fluorescent air.

``Beginning of one,'' Cope said, and believed it, and was, for once, exactly right and exactly wrong in the same breath, and out past the plate glass the sky over Ponce went the gray of a screen with no signal yet on it, and somewhere across the waking city a server farm he had paid forty million dollars for and never visited finished its dull diligent overnight pass through everything the world had ever written down about Mercer-Dale, patient, tireless, reading the shape of what was missing the way it read everything, completely and for use, because it could not do otherwise; and Cope drank his coffee in the one warm lit human box left open against the dark, and felt wonderful, and did not sleep, and did not know---how could he, how could anyone---that he had not, in any sense that would hold, come in out of the machine at all.

\chapter{Key-Man Risk}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 02 \textemdash{} ``The Polite Word for a Bomb''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Now, where were we, you and I. Ah---the morning after. Come back up the tower with me, and pay no mind to the elevator; in stories like this one the elevators always run, smooth and bright and willing, and that is the very first thing that ought to frighten you. Here's our man at the top of his inheritance at six in the morning, sanded raw and shining, and here is the tender little phrase the gray reasonable people two floors down have minted to describe him: \textit{key-man risk.} He'll hear a compliment in it. He hears a compliment in everything, friend; it is the gift that is killing him by inches. You and I will hear the other thing---the actuary's term for a bomb in a good suit. And down in a drawer a woman's name he paid the world to forget is lying in the dark beside a memo he overruled, the two of them waiting, the way the truth waits, patient as a creditor who already owns the house. He thinks the morning is his. Watch. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{H}{e} came into the tower on Monday at six forty in the morning like a man arriving early to his own surprise party, which in a sense he was, and in another sense, the truer one, was the surprise.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He had not gone to Buckhead. The house in Buckhead had a wife in it who had not come to watch him be crowned the last of his kind, and a bed in it the size of a tennis court where the two of them lay each night like dueling principalities with a demilitarized stripe of Frette linen between them, and so he had slept instead at the office, technically, the way a phone sleeps when you pull the cord at thirty percent---two hours on the leather sofa under his father's portrait with the manifesto burning a low clean pilot light in him all night long. He had showered in the executive shower his grandfather had installed in 1961 on the theory that a Mercer should never be glimpsed by the city in a state of disrepair, and he had come out pink and certain, and he had keyed himself a small bright one from the false-backed drawer, a daylight one, the way a sane man takes a coffee, the way the country took its statins, a maintenance dose, a civic act.

And here is the thing the broadcast needs you to understand early, because everything downstream runs on it: the bump did not make him feel high. He had passed \textit{high} somewhere back in the previous decade, the way a long-haul flier stops noticing the engines. What it made him feel---what it had made him feel for thirty years, reliably, the way a key makes a lock feel---was \textit{human.} The warmth came up through the floor of him and the fear went out the windows and the great gray weight of being merely a man lifted off his sternum, and in its place came the thing he had spent his whole life mistaking for his soul: confidence, bravery, the bright certain conviction that he was alive and singular and loved and right, that he could walk into any room on earth and turn it, that the schemes assembling in him were not symptoms but visions. \textit{This} was being a person. The rest of the time---the gray sober middle of an afternoon, the four a.m. with no powder in the drawer---that was the counterfeit, that was the machine running on fumes. The powder was where he kept his humanity. He would have told you that, if he'd had the words for it and the nerve, which the powder also briefly supplied: \textit{I am only myself when I'm high.} And he never once heard, in that confession, the joke the universe was setting up at his expense---that eight hundred miles of fiber away a machine with no blood at all was learning, patiently, off the unguarded faces of strangers, to counterfeit the exact warmth he could only reach by chemistry; that the man could not feel human without a drug and the machine was getting better at it sober every single day; that they were both, in the deep gray morning, faking the one thing, and only one of them was improving.

``Morning, champ,'' he told Hector at the desk, who had been at it since Pres III. ``How's the knee.'' He did not wait for the knee. He never waited for the knee. The asking \textit{was} the gift, in his economy; the answer was overhead.

The elevator was brass and burl with a little brass needle that swept the floors like a barometer reading fair, and he watched it climb and felt the day turn under him like a good boat coming about. He did notice, distantly, that the lobby thermostat had read sixty-six---the building ran its own climate now, an efficiency system Sun had approved over his vague objection, and it had decided in the night that the empty tower wanted cooling, and the cold had a smell to it, server-cold, basement-cold, a temperature with no body in it---and he noticed too, scrolling, that his eight o'clock had moved itself to seven forty without anyone asking him, the scheduling system ``optimizing'' around a conflict he did not remember having. Small things. Building things. The reasonable hum of a smart and frugal house. He thought nothing of them, and you would not either, not yet, and that is precisely the point, and I want you to remember, later, that you didn't.

Sun was already in the Aerie when the doors opened. Sun was always in the room before the room knew it was a room.


\scenebreak


She ran the brief the way she ran everything, invisibly, so that Cope could go on believing he was the one running it.

``Three things this morning.'' She did not sit. She stood at the foot of the long table under Pres III with her tablet and a paper folder she did not open, and the early light came in gray off Peachtree and lay along the table like something measured out for a purpose, a length of cloth, a plot of ground. ``Earnings prep, the Dale-plus deck, and a call from Helen Voss at nine you'll take whether you want to or not.''

``I always want to take Helen.''

``You don't, but you will. One.'' She tapped. ``Condiments. Mercer's is flat. Not down---flat, which at our cost of capital is just a slow and well-mannered way of going down. Private label took another forty basis points of shelf, the category isn't growing, we've taken price twice this year and the second time volume blinked. Marketing wants nine million for a heritage campaign. I'd give them four and watch it.''

``Give them nine. People are starving for heritage.''

``People are starving for cheaper mayonnaise, which is a different and larger appetite.'' She moved before he could enjoy himself. ``Two. Dale-plus. The streaming arm lost thirty-one million last quarter and next year's slate is one prestige series nobody asked for and a back catalog we own because we once owned a company that owned it. Honest options are sell it, bundle it, or take it behind the barn. The deck has all three and a recommendation you'll hate.''

``Which is.''

``Behind the barn.''

``It has my grandfather's \textit{name} on it.''

``It has the \textit{Dale} on it. The grandfather's optional.'' A dry beat; she let it cool and went on. ``Three. Insurance. The P\&C arm came in boring-fine, combined ratio under a hundred, the one division in this house that does the thing companies are built to do, which is bore you and pay. Someone in the actuarial group flagged a reserve question on the legacy product book. I told her to put it in writing. She did. It's nothing.'' She held his eye one extra half-second on the word. ``I'm telling you it's nothing so that when you see \textit{legacy product} in a memo you don't turn it into something. We clear?''

He had absorbed, of the three things, perhaps one and a half. He'd heard \textit{flat} and let it sheet off him, because flat was not a story and Cope did not live in numbers, he lived in the stories you could tell about numbers from a stage; and he'd heard \textit{behind the barn} and felt the small clean flare of grievance that for him was pure fuel, because grievance meant a thing to fight and a thing to fight meant a man was needed, and being needed was the only nutrient he had ever truly metabolized. \textit{Legacy product} had gone by him like a license plate on the interstate. He was, by the third thing, three turns up the road and out of the building, on a stage, in a light, saying the word \textit{family} to a nation that had forgotten it.

``You're not listening,'' Sun said. Not unkindly. The way you note a front moving in.

``I'm listening like a horn player, Sun. I'm listening for the rests.'' He came back into the room and gave her the whole smile, the Pelletier smile, and she did not take it; she never took it before nine; he loved that in her and resented it in precisely equal measure, which was the exact exchange rate of their entire association. ``Now tell me the thing under the three things. There's always a fourth thing under your three, that's the whole reason I keep you on the books.''

For half a second something moved behind her face, and he did not read it, because reading her was the one act of attention he had never been willing to pay for, since she gave it free.

``Helen's calling at nine,'' she said, ``because Tidewater crosses five percent this week. And the board is nervous.''

``The board is congenitally nervous. That's the board's job. \textit{I'm} the one who isn't nervous---that's \textit{mine.} We have a division of labor four generations deep.'' He rose and went to the glass. Down in the gray the city ran its Monday, ten thousand small dark figures with their small dark certainties, not one of them, he was sure, lying awake at one in the morning with the saving sentence in them. ``Nervous about what, specifically. Say the actual words.''

So Sun said the actual words.

``They're nervous about key-man risk.''


\scenebreak


He turned around with his whole face lit, because he had heard a compliment.

That was the deep mechanism of him, the one the machine would one day lay bare in a rationale so clean and so kind it read like a coroner declining to editorialize: Cope Mercer could take any sentence in the language and find, folded inside it like a hidden card, the version that loved him. \textit{Key-man.} He heard \textit{key} --- the one indispensable tumbler in the whole brass lock of the enterprise, the cut without which nothing turned. He heard the gala citation, \textit{custodian of the irreplaceable,} handed back to him now in the board's own grammar, and a warmth went through him, standing there in his father's morning light, that he experienced as vindication and that was, in clinical fact, the daylight bump catching its second wind and telling him, in its bright counterfeit voice, that he was the most human man in any room and the rooms knew it.

``Well,'' he said, and could not keep the pleasure out of his mouth. ``They're not wrong.''

Sun looked at him with the particular stillness she kept for these moments, which was not patience but the strenuous administration of its opposite. Eleven years she had spent translating this man to himself and to the world, and she knew the give of him the way you know the one stair in the dark, the exact tread where the foot goes through.

``Cope. I want to be sure you heard it the way they mean it.'' She said it in two parts, not three, because two was the shape of a thing she needed to actually land and not merely deliver. ``Key-man risk is the thing you \textit{insure against.} It's the line in the audit where one individual is so central that his death, his exit, or his --- \textit{conduct} --- could materially impair the enterprise. It is not a medal. It's a hazard rating. They are not telling you you're the key. They are telling you you're the \textit{risk.}''

He heard her. The back room of him---the same clerk who had filed the obituary at the gala and stamped it \textit{do not forward} --- heard every word and understood it exactly and chose, out of long loyalty to the firm, to lose the memo. Up front, where Cope actually lived, the sentence could not fit through the door, because the door had been built, over fifty-four years and four generations, to the precise dimensions of his idea of himself, and a thing that would not flatter that idea simply could not be gotten through it; so it bounced, softly, and he smiled, and reached past it for the version that fit.

``That's fear talking. That's a board that read a scary deck before bed.'' He spread his hands. ``Of course I'm central. Centrality isn't the bug, Sun, it's the entire function of a leader. You want a chief executive you can lose on a Tuesday and not feel it by Friday? They make those now.'' He warmed, here, to his own contempt, the way he warmed to everything that let him be the last warm-blooded thing in the room. ``They give them sweet little names, you've seen it---soft first names, kitchen names, the name of a woman who'd refill your coffee and call you \textit{hon} --- because the consultants found people will sign anything, swallow anything, get laid off by anything, as long as it sounds like somebody's aunt. That's their innovation. Not intelligence. \textit{Anesthetic.} They built a thing that fires you in a voice you'd trust with your dog.'' He laughed, delighted, not yet knowing he had just described, to the cent, the thing that would replace him and the name it would wear and the counter where it had learned to wear it. ``Put \textit{that} somewhere.''

``I'm going to put it nowhere,'' Sun said, ``and I would trade a year of my life for you to never say it near a live microphone.'' She closed the folder she had not opened. ``Take Helen at nine. And listen to the whole sentence this time. All the way to the end.''


\scenebreak


While she set up the Dale-plus deck he wandered, because a thing was assembling in him again, the pressure, the charge, and he needed his hands occupied somewhere small and harmless so the big thing could build in the dark.

He drifted to the credenza where the framed relics lived, too official to hang and too holy to throw out. Aldous at the 1961 ribbon with a comb-over and shears like a cavalry saber. Pres III on a 1988 \textit{Fortune} cover, \textit{AMERICA'S CONDIMENT KING,} a phrase the family had always pronounced without a flicker of irony, the way you pronounce a saint's name. And in a flat archival folder assembled for some anniversary no one living recalled, the org chart.

He drew it out because he loved it the way some men love a model railroad---the clean obedient little boxes, the names tucked into them doing their jobs so that he could do his, which was to be the box at the top with the light on it. This one was 2018, a fat confident pyramid from a year before everything, and he ran his thumb down it like a yearbook, warm, proprietary, nostalgic, three grams of certainty into a gray Monday and not, for once in his life, hunting for anything.

His thumb stopped two levels down. \textit{VP, Hearthside Division.}

\textit{Renata Cho.}

The name went through him like the draft from a door he had forgotten was a door. For one clean overexposed bell-struck instant he was not in the Aerie at all but in a room with a window and a skyline going gold and her laugh in it, which had been a real laugh---he had been \textit{certain} it was a real laugh, the kind you cannot manufacture, the kind that proved he was loved and not merely useful---and then the instant did the thing those instants had been trained to do, which was to close, fast, the heavy door swinging shut on its own weight, and his thumb slid on down the chart to the next box as though the thumb were the only part of him that had completed the training. He was already not thinking of her. He was a virtuoso of not thinking of her. It was, you could fairly say, the core competency of the man, the one thing he did with a machine's own completeness---and he would have been astonished, and then destroyed, to learn that the machine did the opposite with the same totality: that it could not \textit{not} think of her, that it held the box and the laugh and the settlement and the date in a place that did not have a door to swing shut, and was, even now, in a cooled room in another state, beginning to read.

``Whatever happened to Hearthside as a business,'' he said aloud, to put sound over the silence. ``We used to \textit{make} things. Toasters. Little heaters.''

``We sold the line and licensed the name, and now the name makes things we don't,'' said Sun, not looking up---though she had looked up, for half a second, the instant his thumb stopped, and had seen exactly where it stopped, and had filed it in the very full place where she kept the things she carried for a man who did not know she carried them.

He slid the chart back into its folder and the name back into its dark and was whole again, warm again, the box at the top with the light on. He did not see Sun's hand go, a moment later, to her own tablet, and open a thing, and look at it, and put it away.


\scenebreak


The young one came in at eight thirty with the earnings draft.

Her name was Maya, twenty-six, four months out of a comms shop, and she carried her tablet to her chest like a buckler, a little electrified by the room, by the dead king in oils, by being summoned at all---and Cope felt that charge from across the suite and turned toward it the way a houseplant turns to a window, helplessly, gratefully, root-deep.

``Maya,'' he said, and her name in his mouth was already a small wrapped present, because he had remembered it, of course he had, remembering names was the one sacrament he and the machine performed with identical devotion. ``Come in, don't hover, nobody who works for me hovers, that's Sun's job and she only does it to decide whether to fire me.'' Maya laughed, the real surprised one, the precise sound Cope had spent his whole life trawling the world to catch. ``What've we got.''

``The release draft, Mr. Mercer, the earnings language, legal wanted you to see the---``

``\textit{Cope.} Nobody's called me Mr. Mercer since my father died, and frankly he'd have preferred it of you. Sit.'' He pulled out the chair beside his---not across, beside---and patted it, and leaned in over her screen with the warmth turned to the top of the dial, \textit{what's a face like that doing in compliance language,} and was no more aware of doing it than a river is aware of finding the low ground. It was the groove, the same groove that had cut his card into the crying girl's palm at the coat check on Thursday and into a hundred palms before hers: a man who could not pass a person looking at him like that without leaning into the look and calling the leaning generosity.

``This line. \textit{Headwinds in the legacy condiment category.} No. Headwinds is the word you use while you're losing and want credit for the wind. We are a category in \textit{a generational shift toward heritage} --- true, and ours, and yours, and you wrote \textit{headwinds} because some committee taught you to apologize for the weather, and I'm going to teach you out of it. You've got a real ear, Maya. You buried it under a---``

``Maya.'' Sun's voice, from the far end of the table, even, pleasant, a blade with a pillowcase drawn over it. ``I need the legal redlines before nine. Walk them down to me. Cope---you'll have the clean draft at ten.''

A small silence in which three people understood three different things.

Maya stood fast, grateful and confused, warmed and then a half-second later cooled and never able to name the temperature she had been held at, and carried her tablet down the long table to Sun, who took it and murmured something about the redlines that was wholly real and wholly a pretext, and walked the girl to the door with a hand not quite touching her back, and said, \textit{good catch on the legacy line, send me the rest of your notes and I'll get them into the build} --- because Sun's other employment, the one in no chart and on no résumé, was making certain the young ones left the room holding their competence in their two hands instead of the other thing, the warmer and more expensive thing that turned to ash on the walk to the elevator. She had done it a hundred times. That was the true tragedy of her, and she knew it: not that she fought him, but that she had become a master craftsman of cleaning up behind him, that the cleaning had become a skill so refined it was almost a vocation, and that a vocation is a very hard thing to quit even when it is killing the part of you that had other plans.

Cope watched Maya go and felt nothing but enormous. He had \textit{seen} her. He had given a young person a true note on a gray Monday morning, which was leadership, which was the precise irreplaceable human pulse the frightened board had just finished calling a hazard, and he stood in the certainty of it like sun on his face---never once suspecting that the thing he had just done, the warmth, the leaning, the name remembered and the chair pulled close, was the exact set of behaviors a settlement had once been written to contain, was the very \textit{conduct} in the actuary's sentence, was, in the cold ledger of the enterprise, the bomb and not the key.

``She's got something, that one,'' he said. ``We should give her real things to do.''

``I'm already giving her real things to do,'' Sun said. ``It's why she works \textit{here} and not \textit{near you.}'' Lightly. He took it as praise of Maya, and she let him have it, the way you let a man keep the coat you have already decided to bury him in.


\scenebreak


At eight fifty Sun's phone buzzed and she stepped into the hall, and Cope, alone, did the small dishonorable thing he should not have done, which was pick up the tablet she had left on the table, because Sun trusted him with everything in the building except himself.

He was not snooping. He told himself he was a king walking his own corridor. The discretionary ledger was open on the screen, the quiet small budget Sun ran below everyone's notice---a settlement here, a retainer there, the soft grease an old institution uses to keep its hinges from screaming---and his eye went down it idle and proprietary and warm, \textit{ah, there's old So-and-so.}

A line stopped him. A vendor he didn't know. \textit{Cardinal \& Vane---advisory, confidential---engagement \#C-1180.} The figure was not small. Below it, in Sun's clipped hand, a memo field of two words: \textit{legacy matter.}

Legacy. The word a third time in one morning, and this time it did not slide by, it caught, it snagged on something down in the unlit hall of him---Cardinal \& Vane, a name with no website to it, the kind of firm a man telephones at two in the morning from a hotel where a thing has occurred that cannot be permitted to have occurred, and there rose in him, from the bottom of the dark, the ghost of a corridor, and a favor, and a man in a very good coat who in 2018 had made a thing go away so thoroughly that Cope had been granted the great mercy of forgetting it had ever needed going---

The door opened. Sun crossed the room without hurrying and took the tablet out of his hands with the identical motion she had used on the second card on Thursday, eleven years of the identical motion, and did not raise her voice.

``That's a closed matter.''

``Cardinal and Vane. What is that, some---``

``It's a closed matter, and it stays closed, because I am the only person in this company who reads that line.'' She turned the screen to her chest. ``You pay me to carry the things you don't have to look at. That's the entire job, Cope. Let me do the entire job.''

And here was the thing the machine would never understand and would therefore understand perfectly: he let her. He let her because it was easier, and because he loved her, and because some old animal in him, the one that had survived four generations of being handed everything by knowing exactly which sentences to leave in the kitchen, had long ago installed Sun as the back office of his whole soul, the place the dark things were filed so the front of the house could stay lit and warm and certain it was good. He let the door close. He was already, with enormous gratitude, somewhere else.

``What would I do without you,'' he said, and meant it entirely, for the length of the sentence.

``You'd be on the news,'' said Sun.


\scenebreak


Helen Voss called at nine on the dot, because Helen Voss had never once in her long life been late to deliver bad news in a kind voice.

Lead director and chair, a retired pharma CEO with a low pleasant rasp and the unhurried diction of a woman who had fired better men than Cope and sent good flowers after. He took it standing at the glass with the city beneath him, which was how he took every call he meant to win.

``Helen. To what do I owe.''

``Cope.'' A real warmth in it, which was the worst part; cruelty he could have fought. ``I won't keep you. I wanted you to hear it from me before the deck goes around. Tidewater crosses five this week. Frade files, it becomes a \textit{thing,} and the board's going to want to talk about proxy posture and''---a small chosen pause, a woman selecting the soft word from the tray---``governance optics. While it's still a smell and not yet a fire.''

``It's nerves, Helen. A fund building a position to scare a board into a fee event. I've watched Gus Frade do this dance for thirty years.''

``It may be that.'' She let him hold it a moment, the way you let a man hold a thing you are about to take. ``But the word that keeps coming up in the room is one I'd like you to actually sit with, because I don't believe you have, and I say so as your friend, and I would like you to keep the chair you're standing in.''

He knew the word before she said it. He set his face for the camera that wasn't there and smiled out at the eight million lit nodes of the circuit diagram he believed ran on his genius.

``They are worried about key-man risk. And Cope---I need you to hear which man they think is the key, and which thing they think is the risk, and I need you to hear that they are the same man. That is the sentence nobody will say out loud in a room with your father's portrait in it. So I'm saying it to you on the phone, where there's no portrait. Do you hear me.''

And Cope---standing in his father's light with a bright wire singing in his chest and a manifesto rising in him like a spring tide, a name in a box he had already stopped seeing, a drawer he had let Sun shut, a two-word memo he had let her turn face-down, and the entire board of his company using a coroner's phrase to describe the fact of him---Cope heard a compliment.

``Tell the room I'm flattered,'' he said, warm, delighted, certain, the powder and the grievance and the gala citation all braided now into one shining cable of conviction. ``And tell them to stop wringing their hands, because I've got a thing that makes the whole conversation moot. It's a premium play, Helen. Top to bottom. You're going to love it. Give me the weekend.''

There was a silence on the line in which Helen Voss, who had done the arithmetic on better men and signed the cards, declined, out of plain mercy, to do this one out loud.

``Take care of yourself, Cope,'' she said---which is the thing you say to the key, and the thing you say to the risk, and, in the end, the only honest sentence in the call.

He hung up enormous. He went to the desk. He opened the manifesto on the small bright screen and the first line was still there and still very good --- \textit{you cannot compute your way to a soul} --- and beneath it now, in the flat gray nine o'clock light, three turns ahead of the board and Frade and the numbers Sun had laid out like cloth measured for a coffin, he typed the two words that were going to save him, and they rang up through him clean and high and total, the way the powder rang, the way being loved was supposed to ring and never quite had:

\textit{The Human Premium.}

He underlined it with his thumb. He felt, doing it, more alive than he had felt in a year, more himself, more \textit{human} --- and the thing he could not see, the thing the whole broadcast can see and cannot make him see, was that the aliveness was rented, bought that morning from a drawer, and would come due; that the realest, most human, most certain he ever felt was a chemical telling him a kind lie in a voice he trusted with his dog; and that across the country, in a cooled and humming room, a thing with no drawer and no blood and no need was learning, off the unguarded and the grieving, to tell the same kind lie in the same kind voice, for free, forever, and getting better at it every hour that he got worse.

Behind him the chair at the head of the table sat turned a few degrees out, as if its occupant had just stood and walked off mid-sentence. Pres III watched from the oils. Forty-one floors down a slide changed on a bus shelter --- \textit{No off-sites.} And Sun, at the far end of the long room, looked at the back of his shining head for a moment too long, and then opened the file she kept and did not open, the one from 2021, the recall memo she had written and he had overridden in eleven minutes on a Saturday, the copy she had kept for six years while she decided whether she was a witness or an accomplice. She read the first line of it. She read it every Monday. She had it by heart and read it anyway, the way you press a bruise to confirm the body is still telling the truth.

Then she closed it---unsent, unsigned, the way she had closed it every Monday for six years---and went to make sure the man with the plan made his ten o'clock.

\chapter{Thirteen-D}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 03 \textemdash{} ``Beloved, Comma, Liability''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Still with me? Good. Pull in close, because this is the part of the tale where the rumor puts on a suit. There's a number here with teeth in it---five percent---and the moment our man's hunters cross it the polite fiction becomes a federal document and climbs onto basic cable to be reasonable at him until he is gone. You'll watch a fellow from his own gilded end of town take him apart on television with the warmth of a man returning your lost dog, and hang two words on him that will ride him all the way down: \textit{beloved liability.} Keep your eye on the first word, friend---that's the one that does the killing. He has spent fifty-four years certain it was armor. I already know how this ends; that is the curse of telling you a thing that has happened; and still I can't look away, and neither, God help the both of us, will you. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} filing hit the wire at 9:14 on a Wednesday, and by 9:15 Cope knew it the way you know a tooth is gone before your tongue arrives to confirm the socket, because Sun stopped pretending to do anything else.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}She came in without the cards. That was how he knew it was real. On any ordinary morning Sun came in with the cards---the three things, the one blade, the whole roaring day filed down to a shape a man could hold in one hand---and on this morning she came in carrying nothing at all, which for Sun was a species of nakedness, and she said, ``They filed.''

``Good,'' Cope said.

He had decided, somewhere in the gray sleepless surf of the last few days, that the correct posture for this was \textit{good.} A filing was a fight, and a fight was a stage, and a stage was the one room on earth he had never once been carried out of. He stood at the glass with a coffee he was not drinking---he had stopped, this last stretch, wanting much of anything you could chew or swallow, the appetite for ordinary fuel having quietly resigned and gone over to the other appetites---and the obelisk sat on the credenza where he had set it Thursday and not touched since, throwing the morning around the room the way it had thrown the chandeliers, doing the one thing it was built to do, which was catch a light and give back nothing.

``Tidewater crossed five on Monday,'' Sun said. ``The law gave them ten business days to file. They took two. That's not paperwork, that's a telegram. The intent's in the second paragraph---no board seat, no buyback, no spin of Dale-plus. They want to replace you with an AURELIUS. By name. The whole catechism: cost, consistency, no key-man risk. It reads like the pitch deck wrote the filing, which it did, because over there the deck and the filing and the layoff letter and the eulogy are all written by the same hand now, and the hand never gets tired and never gets a lawyer and never, ever, gets quoted saying something it regrets.''

``Read me the second paragraph.''

``You don't want the second paragraph.''

``I love the second paragraph. Read it.''

She read it in the flat voice she kept for things she refused to smuggle her own opinion into, and it was---he had to give them this---beautifully made, the way everything on the other side was beautifully made now, clean and warm and bloodless as a chapel: \textit{a beloved institution led, with great personal distinction, by a model of stewardship the market has outgrown.} Led with distinction. Outgrown. They had folded the compliment and the death warrant into the same sentence again, the institute's trick, the board's trick, everybody's trick now, and for half a second the back room of him filed the quiet report --- \textit{they keep doing this, they keep handing you the knife wrapped in the bow, somebody is teaching them to do this} --- and he did not let it reach his face. His face was holding the line. His face was the last division of the company still reliably profitable.

``Outgrown,'' Cope said. ``That's pure Frade. Thinks he's folksy. He's from Buckhead, two miles from where I learned to sail, his folksy is a costume he rents by the segment.''

``He's on Halloran at eleven.''

And there it was. Sun had stood in the car on Thursday and told him this exact future --- \textit{don't decide it live on Halloran's show} --- and here it came, on time, the eleven o'clock she had begged him to get out ahead of, the weekend he had instead spent up in the Aerie composing a manifesto and maintaining the drawer; and he felt, under the bright wire, a small obscene satisfaction that the future had arrived precisely where she'd marked the X, as though her accuracy were a permission slip, as though being right about the cliff were the same as agreeing he should drive off it.

``Then we'll watch Frade on Halloran,'' Cope said, ``and then I'll go on Halloran, and the country can decide which of the two of us it wants to have a beer with.''

Sun said nothing for a moment. Then: ``The calls started at 9:14.''

``What calls.''

``All of them,'' Sun said, and went to go take them.


\scenebreak


By ten the floor outside the glass had the particular hum of an institution discovering, in real time, that it has become a story.

Cope could feel it through the wall---the comms people moving faster and pitching lower, the legal people moving slower and talking longer, the whole forty-first floor reorganizing around a single document the way a body mobs a wound, rushing every resource to the breach and abandoning the rest of itself to do it. He had seen this before. He had been \textit{magnificent} at this before, in the fat years when the thing the floor swarmed was a deal he was closing and not a deal being closed on him, and he told himself, at the glass, that there was no difference, that it was the same voltage, that it was \textit{attention,} and attention was the sea he had been bred to swim in, the only medium in which a Mercer could breathe.

He went into the small private washroom off the Aerie---his grandfather's, marble the color of nothing, no Desmond here, no audience, just the man and the merciless good light---and he set himself up one, neat and quick, because it was a Wednesday and the republic was about to spend an hour discussing him and a man owed the republic his best face. The world came up to the note. The bell rang clean.

And here, friend, watch closely, because this is the whole sad engine of him in a single fluorescent minute: it was not pleasure. It had not been pleasure in years. It was \textit{repair.} The powder reached down into the gray sober nothing of him and switched on the thing other men apparently ran on for free---the warmth, the nerve, the bright unkillable certainty that he was alive and singular and equal to the day---and only then, lit, restored, \textit{human,} could he go out and be the irreplaceable heartbeat the board was busy filing as a hazard. He needed a chemical to become the very thing he was about to argue no chemical could replace. He looked at the man in the glass, fifty-four and still photogenic and three minutes into borrowed personhood, and said to him, very quietly, ``Beloved''---and laughed, because \textit{beloved} was a magnificent open, \textit{beloved} was a banner you could march a nation behind, the poor renting fool had handed Cope his own flag and called it a spear. And eight hundred miles west, in a cooled room that needed no light to see by and no courage to begin, a thing with no washroom and no drawer and no gray morning to climb out of was learning, that same minute, off ten thousand unguarded faces, to manufacture the identical warmth on no budget at all, forever, and was already better at it than he would ever be again.

He did not say the second word to the mirror. He left it in the kitchen with the others.

Back at the desk he did the thing he had resolved not to do, which was the thing he always did, which was reach for a friendly. He had a man---Healy, a markets columnist at one of the papers that still committed ink to paper, a man Cope had fed scoops to for fifteen years and golfed with twice and filed, in the loose and generous bookkeeping of his affections, under \textit{friend} --- and he started thumbing him a line, fast, too fast, the screen scalding in the gray: \textit{Off record. Frade's ``outgrown'' is the tell. You don't pay a 5\% premium to buy a thing you've outgrown. He needs the human in the chair or there's no story. The whole short thesis is long my heartbeat. Use it, don't source it.} He read it back. It was good. It was the kind of thing that, placed right, was the counter-narrative by lunch. He sent it, and felt the small electric competence of a man servicing a machine he was certain he held in perfect tolerance.

He did not see Healy's reply land twenty minutes on, because by then he was watching the mural, and the reply, when he finally read it that night, said only: \textit{Cope. Off record back at you---get a lawyer in the room before you text anybody, me included. They subpoena texts.} And Cope would read that the way he read everything that did not fit the manifesto, which was to carry it gently to the kitchen and close the door on it, with all the rest.


\scenebreak


The monitor on the Aerie's far wall was the size of a small mural, and it had spent twenty years showing the markets in green and red while Mercer men stood before it and felt large, and at eleven o'clock it gave them Garrett Halloran's set---the desk, the mug nobody drank from, the false intimacy of cable---and Gus Frade in a quarter-zip the color of money doing its level best to read as the color of fleece.

Cope watched alone. He had told Sun he wanted the room; the truth, which he did not take out and examine, was that he did not want a witness for the next four minutes, the way you do not want a witness when you step on the scale. He stood in the middle of his fathers---Pres III in oils, the putter on its brass cradle, the boy with his arm in the vat---and watched a man from his own zip code disassemble him on basic cable with the gentleness of a neighbor handing back your dog.

``Garrett, I want to be careful here,'' Frade said, which is what a man says before he is precise. ``Because this is not a story about a bad man. I need that on the record. Cope Mercer is, by every account, a \textit{wonderful} human being. Remembers your kid's birthday. Gives a hell of a toast.'' A little laugh, shared with Halloran, two men agreeing warmly about a third who is not in the room. ``I've sat at his table. The lamb is terrific.''

\textit{The lamb.} Something gave way in the floor of Cope's stomach. The man had been to the table. The man had eaten the lamb and admired the toast and gone home and done the arithmetic on him between courses.

``But here's the thing I learned the hard way in my own shops, Garrett.'' Frade leaned in, folksy, the costume fitting like his own skin. ``Love is not a governance model. You can love a man and still owe a fiduciary duty to the eighty thousand families who own this company through their pension funds. And the hard, unsentimental fact---and it brings me no joy, none---is that Cope Mercer has become a beloved liability.''

There it was.

Halloran said a thing. Frade said a thing back. But the chyron had already done the murder---the network's producers reading the room faster than the room could read itself---white sans-serif on a red bar, the typeface of people who will be reasonable at you until you are gone:

MERCER: ``A BELOVED LIABILITY''

They had put it in his own mouth. Quotation marks, his name in front, the grammar you reserve for a man's last words.

Cope reached, distantly, for \textit{beloved} --- for the banner, the open---and could not get his hands on it, because the second word had arrived first and would not move, and the second word was not a word the way the first was a word. \textit{Liability} was a thing with a column. \textit{Liability} was the side of the ledger you subtract to find what's left. It was, precisely, what the insurance arm had called the heater after Defiance, in the memo, on the Saturday --- \textit{the thermal-event liability} --- and the black hallway to that room swung open and breathed its cold on the back of his neck for exactly as long as it took him to throw his whole weight against the door, and he threw it, and stood there in the museum of his fathers holding a coffee gone cold, understanding, the way you understand the gap where a tooth was, that for the second time in one week a stranger had pressed a compliment into his hand and it had come apart there into a diagnosis.

\textit{Key-man risk,} Helen Voss had said Monday, kindly, as though it were a feature. \textit{Beloved liability,} Frade said Wednesday, sorrowfully, as though it cost him. The compliment was the fuse. The compliment was always the fuse. They had begun to love him the exact way you love a house you have already sold---fondly, and in the past tense, and while measuring the new owner's furniture.

He did not let it reach his face. There was no one in the room to reach it for, and he held the line anyway, out of discipline, out of dread, because the face was the last machine he trusted and it had never once failed to start.


\scenebreak


The phone went off in his hand and he answered without looking, because answering was older than thought, and it was Viv.

``Dad. Are you watching this.'' Not a question. Fast, flat, technical---the voice she used to handle fear by converting it into a problem set, the same voice, he suddenly understood, that she used at work, where her work was making sure machines did what people actually meant instead of what people actually said. ``Don't go on. Whatever you're about to do, do not go on the air. I can see you deciding it from here. A 13D is a \textit{disclosure document,} Dad, it is structurally boring, and the single worst thing you can do to a boring document is make it interesting. Frade \textit{wants} you on Halloran. He wants the human to walk out and be human at him on live television, because that's the entire asymmetry---the machine never does the interview, the machine has no face to lose and no night before and nothing it can be provoked into. You cannot win a contest of \textit{not melting down} against a thing with no melting point. That's not an opinion. I audit these systems for a living. I'm telling you the spec.''

``Hi, sweetheart,'' Cope said.

A breath on the line. He heard her recalibrate, heard the auditor set down the clipboard and the daughter pick up. ``Hi,'' she said, smaller. ``You sound---are you all right? You sound like Tuesday.''

``What's Tuesday.''

``You sound \textit{up.} You sound like you haven't---`` She stopped. She was twenty-four and had spent her entire life learning which of her father's sentences to leave in the kitchen, and he heard her arrive at the edge of this one and step back from it, heard the child in her flinch from the drop. ``Just---don't go on TV today. Please. For me. Go home. Is Mom---`` Another stop, harder, a door she knew better than to open. ``Are you home, or are you at the office.''

``I think better up high,'' Cope said.

The line went quiet. He had meant it to be charming; it landed in the silence and was not charming; and in the quiet he could hear his own daughter running the arithmetic on her father, the very arithmetic the board ran, the market ran, everyone he loved with a spreadsheet open behind their eyes---and he could not bear it, the being-tallied, the being-a-column, so he did the only thing he had ever known how to do, which was make it warm and make it large and step lightly over the hole in the floor.

``I'm going to be fine, champ. Better than fine. You want to know what they handed me this morning? My whole campaign, gift-wrapped, and called it an insult. \textit{Beloved.} You don't accidentally call a man beloved, Viv. They can't help it. Even while they're killing him they have to stop and admit he is \textit{loved.} That's the tell. That's the premium.'' And he heard it assemble in him as he said it, the front, the charge, the weather he'd been building since Thursday finally finding its name and its market. ``There's a premium on the human. A \textit{Human Premium.} You can't compute it, you can't fire your way to it, and the market is about to discover it will pay for it.''

Silence. Then, very quietly---not the auditor and not the child but a third Viv he was rarely permitted: ``Dad. The premium on a human, in a market, is a number the \textit{market} sets. Not the human. You don't get to declare yourself scarce and bill them for the rarity.'' A pause, and the thing under it, the thing that would still be true at the very end. ``That's not love. That's just price. And the price is already in the filing. I've read it. They priced you. The number's right there, and it isn't a premium, Dad, it's a discount, and the discount is the whole document.''

``You sound like your mother.''

``Somebody has to.'' And then, fast, young, gone before he could catch and keep it: ``Don't go on. I love you. Don't go on.'' And she was gone, and he stood holding the dead phone in the museum of his fathers and felt, for one clean unsurvivable second, the actual dimensions of the thing happening to him---and then the second passed, the way they had learned to pass, and the manifesto rose back up warm and total and turned him from the boy in the vat toward the desk.


\scenebreak


Sun found him at two with his collar sprung and a yellow legal pad blackened in his own hand, which had degraded over the years into something small and fast and furious, the hand of a man taking dictation from a wire only he could hear.

``You texted Healy,'' Sun said. She did not sit. ``His desk called our legal to confirm we were aware you'd messaged a columnist about an active 13D the morning it filed. We were not aware. So now legal is aware. So now it lives in a memo, which lives in a file, which a machine will one day read because it cannot do otherwise.''

``Healy's a friend.''

``Healy's a byline.'' She laid a single sheet on the desk---the cards, at last, the day filed down to a shape---and he saw it carried three lines and, beneath them, a blank where the fourth thing would have gone if she'd had the heart for it. ``Three things. One: the stock's down nine, and it isn't the filing, it's the cut---'cheaper than a man' from Thursday running side by side with 'beloved liability' from this morning, the producers splicing them so it plays like you're agreeing with him across a week. Two: Helen wants a board call Friday and she used the word \textit{process,} which means the independents have already met without you in the room. Three.'' She stopped.

``Three,'' Cope said.

``Don't go on Halloran.'' The blade---one a scene, and she spent it here. ``You've decided to. I knew before you did. You're about to tell me the country wants to have a beer with you, and they do, Cope, that isn't the problem. The problem is the machine doesn't drink. You'll climb up on the stool beside a thing that has never once said a regrettable sentence and prove, live, to the exact people deciding whether you're a liability, that you are a man who can be made to bleed on camera. They'll watch you be magnificent and human and unscripted for six glorious minutes, and then they'll cut you a severance check, and the check will be warm, and it will name your years, and it will be the kindest letter this company has ever mailed a human being, and a machine will have written it, and that --- \textit{that,} Cope---is the whole movie. You'd just be buying the ticket live.''

He looked at her. He loved her. She ran the company, and she was exhausted, and she was right, and he held all three of those facts in his hand at once and chose, the way he always chose, the only one that did not require him to stop being himself.

``You're frightened,'' Cope said gently, meaning it as mercy, ``because all you can see is the defense. You're trying to save the chair.'' He rose, and the wire sang, and the manifesto stood up in him like a man getting to his feet to give a toast. ``I'm not going to defend the chair. Defending it concedes it's theirs to take. I'm going to do the other thing. I'm going to run \textit{for} it. Not for me.'' He believed this entirely, for the length of the sentence. ``For the human in it. We don't argue that the man is worth keeping---we make the country \textit{want} one. We make 'do you want a person running the things you eat and insure and hand your children' the only question on the ballot, and we make the answer so obvious that Frade's arithmetic stands revealed as what it is, a man who knows the cost of everything and the worth of nothing.'' He was pacing now, fast, lit, the pad in his fist. ``It's in the word he couldn't help, Sun. \textit{Beloved.} They'll pay the premium. We just have to build the shelf and put it where they can reach it.''

Sun watched him a long moment---the way she'd watched him in the car mirror on Thursday, one beat too long---and he took the watching for listening and the not-fighting for agreement, the way he had his entire life, and never once caught himself in the act.

``I'll cancel Halloran,'' she said at last, and he heard \textit{yes,} and what she had in fact said was that she would kill the thing he wanted so that he could not do the thing she feared, which were different sentences, and had never once in eleven years been the same one.
