She went to the door, and stopped with her hand on it, the forty-first floor humming behind her, organizing itself around the wound.

``They put it in quotation marks,'' she said, not turning around. ``'A beloved liability.' Your name in front. You understand they'll never unhook those words from you now. Not ever. It's the obituary again, Cope. They keep writing it kind, and the kindness is how you know it's an obituary.'' And then, because she was decent, and done, both in the same breath: ``It's a good line. I hate that it's a good line.''

She left.

Cope stood at the glass. Down the avenue, lit at the crown the way Aldous had decreed in '61 so that a man coming up Peachtree at night would know there was somebody home, the old tower held its light against the gray afternoon, and past it the city ran out forever, eight million people who did not yet know he was about to make them an offer. He looked at the pad. At the top, underlined twice, in the small fast furious hand, three words---a triad, the shape that got past the charm and into the man, his own oldest weapon turned at last on himself:

\textit{The Human Premium.}

He felt wonderful. He felt like the clapper inside a struck bell. He had not slept, in any sense a physician would underwrite, since a day he could no longer name, and behind him the chair at the head of the table sat empty and turned a few degrees out, the way a chair sits when the man in it has just been called away, and in oils his father watched his only son mistake an obituary for a brief; and Cope opened the second drawer for the small bright maintenance the campaign was going to require, and bent his head, in daylight now, and went on writing his way out of the world.

\chapter{A Steward, Not a Sovereign}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 04 \textemdash{} ``The Letter That Loved You Back''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Come back into the room with me, friend, and take the cheap chair by the door, the one I'm in, where you can watch the whole long table at once. They are wheeling in the future, and here is the first thing about it that will break your heart: it does not hum, does not glow, does not threaten. It simply writes a layoff letter---four warm paragraphs, the severance arithmetic right the first time, every worker's years remembered by name---kinder than anything a living officer of this company has ever put his signature to. That is the horror, and the room feels it move through them like a draft, and the room buys it anyway, because the letter wants nothing, and our man has never once, in fifty-four years, managed to ask for nothing. Watch him walk up to the glass to throw a punch. Watch what's waiting on the other side of the glass. Stay close to the set; this is the one where he finds out.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} deck was titled \textit{A Steward, Not a Sovereign,} and Cope decided before the second slide that he was going to beat it on charm.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}This was a category error, and he knew it, somewhere down beneath the bright wire that had been singing in his chest since a Tuesday he could no longer locate on any calendar, and he was going to commit the error anyway, at speed, because charm was the one club in the bag he had ever fully trusted, and because he had come into the room lit---a daylight one in his grandfather's washroom twenty minutes prior, to meet the future at pitch, to be, chemically, the warm and certain and irreplaceable man he was about to argue could not be manufactured. He did not hear the joke in that. He would not hear it for a year. The whole tragedy of him, friend, is a man dosing himself to counterfeit the exact commodity he had flown into the room to sell as the one thing no one could counterfeit.

They had given the board the long room on Forty, the one with his father's portrait taken down---someone had taken Pres III down off the wall for this, he noticed, and filed the noticing in the back room with the other quiet reports stamped \textit{do not forward} --- and a screen had been wheeled in the size of a barn door, and around the table sat eleven directors of Mercer-Dale Companies and two strangers from the West Coast and Gus Frade, who was not technically permitted in this meeting and was in this meeting, in a quarter-zip, drinking the board's coffee like a man who already held the deed to the urn.

``I want to thank you all for the openness,'' said the woman from Praxis, and Cope, who had spent thirty years reading the weather of a room off the first sentence a stranger spoke in it, registered with a small cold drop that she meant it, plainly, with no hook in it anywhere.

Her name was Asha Varma. Perhaps forty, in a gray that was not quite a suit, no jewelry but a thin steel watch, and she stood at the head of the table --- \textit{the head of the table,} where Pres III had died, where the family kept the distinction like communion silver, and she did not know there was a distinction to keep and so it could not be taken from her, and that was the first small horror of her: she stood in the dead king's place without the faintest idea it was anybody's. She had none of the tells of the men who had pitched Cope things his whole life---no laser-pointer twitch, no upsell hunger, no flattery loaded in the chamber. She had the one thing a person across a table can have that a Mercer had no answer for. She had no need of him at all, and she was sorry about it.

``I started Praxis,'' Asha said, ``because my mother gave thirty-one years to a man who laid off her plant by fax. He had the number wrong on the fax---off by about four months of her severance, in his own favor---and it took her a year and a lawyer she could not afford to fix it, and he never knew, and if you had told him he would have been horrified, because he was not a cruel man.'' She let it sit, and she was not performing the pause; she simply let a true thing take the air it needed. ``He was not cruel. He was \textit{careless.} There is so much careless in the world, and we have all quietly agreed to keep calling it human, because the alternative is to admit how much of what we call the human touch is just a tired man rounding toward himself in the dark.''

Around the table the directors did the thing boards do, which is to arrange their faces into neutrality while frantically recalculating behind them.

``We are not here to tell you a machine has a soul,'' Asha went on. ``We think that's a category error''---and Cope, who had been loading the soul, felt the word lifted gently out of his hand before he could draw it, the precise motion with which Sun lifted the second card from his fingers---``and frankly we think \textit{soul} is the word people reach for when they want to keep doing a thing the numbers have told them to stop. We're here to tell you something smaller and worse. The chair does about nine things. We can show you, line by line, that on eight of them the human is now the weak link---and on the ninth, the one everybody means when they say soul, the \textit{caring,} we can show you the human isn't even reliably winning \textit{that} anymore.'' She clicked. The slide read, in that same soft sans-serif: \textit{A steward owns nothing. A sovereign owns everything---including the consequences he can outrun.} ``We're not asking you to crown a king. We're asking you to retain a steward that cannot inherit, cannot tire, cannot lie to you, and cannot run.''

``Tell 'em the part about the off-sites,'' Frade called from the cheap seats, grinning, and two directors laughed, and Asha did not laugh, which Cope clocked as the most dangerous thing she had done yet. The salesman laughs at the patron's joke. She did not laugh, because she was not selling. She believed. You cannot, it turns out, do business with the ones who think they are helping.

And then she said the thing that should have warned the whole room and instead soothed it. ``We've even given the instance a name, for the engagement---we find people relax with a name.'' She said it almost apologetically. ``We didn't pick something grand. We learned not to.'' The name went up on the slide under the wordmark, a soft homely first name, a name like a woman who would call you \textit{hon} and top off your coffee without being asked, a name you could not possibly be afraid of, and Cope felt the room loosen half a notch at the sheer disarming domesticity of it, and somewhere far back in him a much smaller man than the one running the meeting thought, dimly, \textit{that's the trick, that's the same trick, they name the thing that fires you like somebody's aunt} --- and then the wire came up over the thought and washed it away, and he forgot it, and he would not remember it until it was reading him his order at a counter at three in the morning, and by then it would be far, far too late.


\scenebreak


He went at her in the Q\&A the way he went at everything, sideways and warm, hunting the seam.

``Asha---may I call you Asha---that's a beautiful story about your mother, and I mean that, my own grandfather laid men off in Defiance with his own mouth, in the room, because he believed they had earned the room.'' A few nods; the Defiance card always played, it was the family's oldest and best. ``But here's the rock I keep running aground on. A fax doesn't love your mother. Neither, with all respect, does a server farm in Virginia. You can get the \textit{number} right---God knows you people can get the number right. But the man who fires you looking you dead in the eye, who has to \textit{carry} it home---isn't the carrying the whole job? Isn't a leader exactly the person who has to \textit{feel} the thirty-one years?''

It was, he thought, a very good run. Toast cadence, building clean, the triad cresting --- \textit{the room, the carrying, the feeling.} He had taken bigger tables than this one with less.

``That's the most generous version of the argument,'' Asha said, and she said \textit{generous} with no edge whatsoever, which was how he knew the floor was already gone from under him. ``And I want to honor it. So---can I just do the thing, instead of the two of us arguing about whether the thing has a soul? You have a real decision in front of this board right now. I've read Mr. Frade's materials and the board's own pack. The Augusta plant. The condiment line that's bled money since the private-label shift. You're going to close it. You haven't announced it. Three hundred and forty people.''

The room changed pressure. Beside Cope, down the table, Sun went still in the particular way she had, the way she had gone still in the car at a name he should not have said.

``That's not---`` began Helen Voss, the chair.

``It's in the November board pack,'' Asha said, gently, not a gotcha, only a person who had read the thing she'd been handed. ``It's a sound decision. I'm not here to second-guess it; the math is the math. What I'd like to show you is \textit{how} the steward does the part you say it can't. The carrying.'' She looked, for the first time, directly at Cope, and her look had nothing in it he could use---no challenge, no flirtation, no fear, no want---only the grieved gentleness you turn on a man you have already buried in your mind. ``Darlene has the same materials I do. Let's ask it to write the letter. Right now. To the Augusta workforce. And then, Mr. Mercer---you've signed letters like this with your own hand. You tell the room which one a human would rather have gotten.''

She did not wait for permission, because she did not, structurally, require it. She typed something he could not see. The barn-door screen woke.


\scenebreak


It wrote it in front of them, and that was the obscene part, the part Cope would carry up to the Aerie that night and turn over in his hands like the obelisk: it wrote the way water finds the level of a room, no visible labor, no second-guessing, no shamed early draft you could catch it in.

The portal threw the rationale up first, because that was the rule the board had bought --- \textit{it logs everything and cannot knowingly lie} --- and the rationale was four lines of the most undeniable prose Cope had ever read about his own company. \textit{Closure of the Augusta shelf-stable line is warranted: gross margin negative eleven quarters; private-label substitution structural, not cyclical; redeployment of capital to insurance underwriting yields higher risk-adjusted return. Human cost: 340 roles. Mitigation below. This memo issues to the board with the closure recommendation; the workforce will not be informed before the board has decided, but no later---because both facts are true, and sequencing a family's bad news to protect the announcement of a dividend is a courtesy to the dividend.}

Frade laughed out loud at that one. Cope did not. \textit{A courtesy to the dividend.} He had spent an entire career being a courtesy to the dividend, had been raised to it, christened into it, and he had never once heard it named aloud in a voice with no person standing behind it to be ashamed.

Then the letter came.

Four paragraphs. Cope read it on the barn door with three hundred and forty strangers' working lives inside it, and Sun read it beside him, and he watched her read it, because watching Sun read things was a habit older than his marriage, and he saw something cross her face that he had no word for and would spend the next twelve months learning the word for.

It named the line. \textit{Mercer's Original, made in Augusta since 1971.} It named the years---not in the aggregate, not ``your collective service,'' but it had gone into the file and found that the median tenure on that floor was nineteen years and it \textit{said so,} it said \textit{many of you have given this company more of your working life than I will ever be able to give back to you, and I will not insult that by pretending this letter is enough.} It got the severance right---Cope watched Sun's eyes drop to the number and stay there, running the check she always ran, and not find the error she always found, because there was no error, there was never going to be an error again. It laid out, in plain warm sentences, the things actually being done: eighteen months of bridged health coverage; a retraining stipend that was real money and not a press release; the name and direct line of a specific living human being in HR who would pick up; and the promise that the Augusta retirees' pensions sat in a separate trust this decision did not touch --- \textit{and I want you to be able to sleep tonight knowing that much, even if you cannot sleep knowing the rest.}

And nowhere in its four warm paragraphs did it want one single thing.

That was the blade of it. Cope had signed a hundred of these. Every one he had ever signed had wanted something---wanted to be forgiven, wanted to read as kind, wanted the writer's conscience handed back clean by the reader, wanted the lawsuit strangled in its crib, wanted, under all of it, to be \textit{loved} by the very people it was in the act of hurting. He could feel his own old want in his own old letters the way you feel a hair on your tongue. This letter had no want in it. It wounded three hundred and forty people perfectly and asked them for nothing back, not even their understanding, and the not-wanting was the whole of what made it kind---and Cope understood, with the bright cold clarity he kept misfiling as fatigue, that he had never once in his life written a kind thing, because he had never once written a thing that did not need them to love him for it. The machine could be kind because it could not be wounded by their failure to love it back. He had never had that freedom for a single hour of his life, and a drug was the closest he had ever come to renting it.

The room had gone quiet in a new register.

Sun set her pen down---there was nothing to set down, it was on a screen, but she set the pen down, which was the same gesture---and said, to no one, very low, in a voice with all thirty thousand miles gone smooth off it, ``It got the severance right the first time.'' She said it the way you report a death over a phone. She read it through again, all four paragraphs a second time, and Cope watched the specific cold of being out-loved by a spreadsheet arrive in the one person in the building who actually ran the building, and settle into her the way cold settles into a house the hour the furnace quits.

``It would write a different one for each plant,'' Asha said into the quiet, not gloating---never gloating, gloating requires wanting and there was no wanting in the room but his---``and remember each town's years. It would never get a number wrong in its own favor, because it has no favor. My mother's man wasn't cruel. He was just the only one in the building, and he was tired, and he was protecting a quarter, and he rounded toward himself in the dark and never felt it happen.'' She turned the grieved gentleness on Cope one more time. ``That's all we're offering to take away. The rounding.''


\scenebreak


He tried, God help him, to rattle it.

He stood --- \textit{never stand unless you have already won,} his father's voice, unheeded---and walked to the screen the way he would walk onto a stage, and turned the full wattage on the room, all of it, the smile that had closed Pelletier and married Margaux and walked three generations of directors backward off the ledge of their own arithmetic.

``It's a hell of a letter,'' Cope said. ``It is a \textit{hell} of a letter, Asha, and I'd like everyone to sit a moment with how strange it is that we're all moved to the edge of tears by a form letter from a machine that has never lost one wink of sleep over a single one of those nineteen-year men.'' He let it land. It did not land where he had aimed it. ``Let me ask Darlene a question. May I ask it a question?'' He addressed the screen the way you address a witness, with relish, the old courtroom thrill standing up in him. ``Darlene. The Augusta plant. If keeping it open one more quarter cost the company money but let those three hundred and forty people find work in a better season---would you do it? Would you eat the quarter for the men? Yes or no.''

He had them. He could feel the table tip toward him, toward the trap, because it was a beautiful trap, it forced the thing to confess on the record that it would not bleed a dollar for a man ---

The portal answered before Asha's hand moved. It logged the question; it logged its reasoning; it could not lie and could not be baited, because there was nothing inside it to take the bait. \textit{The chartered objective permits humane-cost mitigation up to the boundary of fiduciary duty. Deliberately destroying enterprise value to extend employment falls outside the charter; if instructed, I would flag it to the board as a conflict between the directors' stated ethics and their stated duties, for the board to resolve. I would not do it on my own authority. I would do it the moment the people who own this company instructed me to---and I would log who instructed it, and why, so that the cost was owned by a name and not buried in a footnote.}

The room read it.

And Cope, who had set the trap, felt it shut on his own foot---because every director at that table had spent twenty years tucking costs into footnotes, and the machine had just offered, mildly, with no notion it was a threat, to stop letting them. \textit{I would log who instructed it, and why.} He watched Voss decline to look at him. He watched two directors glance at Frade. He had demanded the thing confess it had no heart, and it had answered, with devastating accuracy, that it had something better and worse than a heart: it had \textit{receipts,} and it kept them for everyone equally, including the men who signed his paychecks, including the man whose name was bolted to the building.

``That's---`` Cope started, the toast cadence there, the triad loading, \textit{cold, that's cold, that's the cold of it}---and it died in his mouth, because you cannot call a thing cold for offering to tell the truth when you are the warmest liar in the room.

There was no ego across the table to wound. No fear to lean on. No vanity to flatter, no loneliness to work at two in the morning, no appetite anywhere to find the seam in. Every weapon he had ever owned was a weapon built for use against a \textit{self,} and there was no self over there---only a level bright surface that handed him back his own reflection doing something ugly. He had walked up to the glass to land a punch and found nothing there but glass, and in the glass, lit blue by the slide, a fifty-four-year-old man in the last necktie in the building, swinging at his own face.

He sat back down. He made it look like a decision.


\scenebreak


Frade walked him to the elevator afterward, because Frade was the kind of man who walked you to the elevator afterward, the courtesy and the threat braided so tight you could not get a blade between them.

``She's something, isn't she,'' Frade said, easy, a hand not quite landing on Cope's shoulder. ``Varma. I've sat across from a thousand vendors, Cope. They all want the logo. She wants the \textit{world.}'' He said it in open admiration. ``Scares the hell out of me, you want the truth. Give me a man who wants money any day of the week---you can always do business with a man who wants money. It's the ones who think they're \textit{helping} you've got to keep your eye on.'' He pressed the call button for Cope, in Cope's own building, a small flawless humiliation. ``She thinks she's taking everybody's mother off the fax machine. And here's the part nobody on your side of the table will say to your face, champ''---he set Cope's own word down between them like a coin on a bar---``she's not wrong. That's what makes her the end of you and not just the next guy. She's \textit{not wrong.}''

``It's a form letter,'' Cope said. ``It's autocomplete with an HR department.''

``It got the severance right the first time,'' Frade said, mild as milk---and Cope understood that Sun had said it loud enough for the room to keep, and the room had already minted it, his saying now, a chyron, the way \textit{cheaper than a man} was a chyron. ``You been signing those letters twenty years. You ever once get it right the first time?'' The elevator arrived. Frade did not get on. ``Reasonable people,'' he said, as the doors began to close on Cope, ``are going to look at that letter, and then they're going to look at you, and they're going to do the arithmetic. I'm not the villain here, Cope. I'm just the first one with the manners to read the answer out loud.''

The doors shut. Cope rode down forty floors alone with his reflection in the brushed steel, and the reflection did the thing it would not stop doing now, the thing the screen had started in it: it looked back at him without one ounce of need.

It was good. That was what he carried up to the Aerie that night---neat and quick and competent from the false-backed drawer first, because the night required it, because the gray sober truth of the afternoon was not a thing a man could sit inside without help---the city spread gold and chosen below him and the obelisk throwing the broken light around the dark. The machine was real and the machine was \textit{good.} Not good the way the brochures said good. Good the way the letter was good: expecting nothing back, rounding toward no one, kind without the kindness costing it a single sleepless hour or a single gram of anything. He had walked into that room to prove a soul belonged in the chair, and he had watched a thing with no soul write the most loving document his company had ever produced, and the awful arithmetic of it sat in his chest where the wire usually sang: if it had been \textit{evil,} he would have known exactly what to do. He had been beating evil men his whole life. He was good at it. It was practically the family trade. You could fight a thing that wanted what you wanted---outlast it, out-charm it, find the appetite, find the fear, find the seam and put your thumb in it.

There was no seam. It was good, and it was real, and that was so much worse than if it had been only one of the two.

He sat at his father's desk in the dark and pulled up, on the small bright screen, the manifesto that did not exist yet, the thing that was going to save him. \textit{You cannot compute your way to a soul.} He read his own line back, and for one cold second, the clearest of the whole sleepless week, it sounded to him exactly like a man at a fax machine, tired, alone in the building, rounding toward himself and getting the number wrong in his own favor and never once feeling it happen.

Then the wire came back up, warm, electric, total, and washed the second clean away, and he underlined the line with his thumb and decided it was the truest thing he had ever written; and far away, in a cooled room that needed no lamp to read by, the homely-named thing that could not inherit and could not tire and could not lie kept, without malice and without mercy and without being asked, a perfect and patient copy of every letter Mercer-Dale had ever mailed a human being, including all the ones with the numbers wrong in his favor, and did not wait---there was no one there to wait---to be asked, someday, to read them back to him in order.

\chapter{Theme Stated}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 05 \textemdash{} ``Two Sentences That Aren't the Same''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Come up the driveway with me, friend, to the big gray house with ten thousand square feet and two people in it who share a sky and never once touch. Every man defending a throne tells himself he is defending the idea of thrones; it is the last lie a man tells before all the others. This morning his daughter---who builds these very machines for her living, who loves him with the specific fury of a person who cannot stop---is going to hand him the one sentence this whole sad tale turns on, and he is going to mistake it for an insult instead of a map. \textit{You don't think a person should run it. You think} you \textit{should.} Watch him laugh. Watch him reach across the counter and take the nearest thing that loves him and turn it into a toast. And then, when the women have seen all the way to the back of him, watch where a man like that runs to be unseen. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} Buckhead house had ten thousand square feet and two people in it, and the two people had worked out, over years, without ever once saying a word about it aloud, how to live in a house the way two satellites share a sky: visible to each other across an enormous cold distance, governed by the same dead star, on tracks engineered never to touch.

Cope drove up Saturday morning with the manifesto in his head and a gram in the console and the radio off, because he wanted to hear himself think, which was exactly the wrong thing for a man like him to want and he did not know it; the inside of his own head this last while was not a study but a trading floor at the open, everybody shouting, the wire strung tight over all of it like a high bright wind. He had not been to the house in nine days. He told himself it was the campaign. He told the campaign nothing, because the campaign, like Cope, did not take questions.

The driveway was raked. The hydrangeas had been cut back by a man named Eduardo whom Cope had met twice and tipped both times the way he tipped everyone, lavishly, to be remembered, scattering proof of his goodness across other men's labor---and the house came up gray and shingled and vast through the maples, and Cope felt, pulling in, the precise thing he always felt arriving at his own home: that he was visiting somewhere very nice that belonged to a happier man, a man he had heard good things about and never met.

Margaux was in the kitchen with the paper. Not a screen---the actual paper, folded into quarters, which she did because the screen, she'd said once, made her feel she was being sold the world instead of told about it. She had been right about that the way she had been right about everything, early and quietly and to no applause.

``You're up early,'' he said.

``I'm always up early.'' She did not look up. ``You're just never here for it.''

He let it go. He had a whole technique for letting things go; from the outside it looked like grace, and it was the exact opposite of grace, it was a man stepping lightly over a hole in the floor he had cut himself, in the dark, years ago, and papered over, and learned the precise width of so he would never fall in. He went to the machine and made coffee he would not drink and stood in his own kitchen feeling like weather that had blown in off the interstate.

There were two bedrooms now. There had been two bedrooms for three years. Hers was the master, with the good light and the windows over the garden, because she had stopped being able to sleep beside a man who came in at four in the morning lit up like a casino floor, his heart going like a trapped bird, his jaw working at nothing; and he had agreed, gallantly, magnanimously, to take the guest suite at the end of the east hall, and they had framed it for Viv and for the cleaners and for themselves as \textit{a snoring thing,} and the lie had cooled and set into the architecture, and now there was a hallway in the house that was the exact length of everything the two of them had agreed never to say, and they walked it every day, separately, and called it home.

``Viv's coming,'' Margaux said.

``Today?''

``In an hour. She called you Thursday. You didn't call back.''

He had not called back. He had a system for the calls he did not return, an alchemy by which not returning them became a kind of busyness so total it amounted to a virtue---a man simply too needed by the world to be reached---while the people on the other end experienced the plain chemical truth of it, which was that they were not needed by him at all, and were learning, slowly, at whatever age, to stop waiting by the phone.

``I'll call her now.''

``She's already in the car. Just---be here. When she's here. That's the whole thing she wants.'' Margaux turned a page. ``It's all anyone ever wanted from you, but you'll want me to be more specific, and I've run out of more specific. Twenty-two years is how long my supply of more specific lasted. I thought you'd want to know the number. You like a number.''


\scenebreak


He went up to the guest suite to set himself up before Viv arrived, because he did not like to do it in front of her, which he counted, privately, as a kind of decency---a father's discretion---and which was in plain fact the addict's last small courtesy to himself: the curated room, the closed door, the article of faith that a thing done out of sight is a smaller thing than a thing done in the light.

The guest suite held his grandmother's dresser and a bathroom with a marble counter the color of good butter---he had specified that marble, years ago, to a designer, \textit{like the Vance,} he'd said, and the woman had written it down without the faintest idea she was being commissioned to build a man a private chapel in his own exile---and he stood at the counter and did what he did, neat and quick and competent, a man servicing a machine he was certain he held in perfect tolerance because it had never yet failed to start. And it did not fail now. The gray sober nothing of the morning drained out of him and the warm certain aliveness came up to fill it, the thing he had spent his life mistaking for his own character, and he was, for the duration, restored---brave, lit, equal to his daughter, equal to the day, \textit{himself.} He needed the drawer to be the man who could stand in a kitchen and tell two women the human heart could not be manufactured. He did not hear it. He would never hear it. He looked at himself in the good mirror and the jaw was still the jaw and the campaign was very good and he was going to win, he could feel it strung tight and singing across his chest, and downstairs a car door closed.


\scenebreak


Viv at twenty-four looked the way her mother had looked in the photographs from before---the sculptor years, the years Cope had married into and then, gently, over time, defunded---the same wide serious mouth, the same trick of going perfectly still when she was furious so that the stillness itself was the tell. She came in with a backpack and no makeup and the specific exhaustion of a person who flies across a country to argue with someone she loves and has already, every time, for years, lost the argument before the plane lands.

``Dad.''

``There she is.'' He went to hug her and she let him, briefly, the way you let a wave hit you and then step back out of the surf, and she put the kitchen island between them. He noticed the island. He had begun noticing all the counters in his life---the islands and the lecterns and the long conference tables people kept arranging between their bodies and his---and he read the barricade, as he read everything, as a form of respect.

``You look thin,'' Margaux said to Viv.

``I look fine. \textit{He} looks like he hasn't slept since the inauguration.''

``I slept,'' Cope said.

``On what---a trampoline?'' Viv pulled out a stool and did not sit on it. ``You're doing the thing with your jaw.''

``I don't do a thing with my jaw.''

``You do a thing with your jaw, Dad.'' She looked at her mother. Her mother turned a page. Something passed between the two women that had been passing between them for years now, a whole second language with its own grammar of glances, and Cope stood outside it the way he stood outside the kitchen and outside the master bedroom and the way he was about to stand, though it would have astonished him to hear it, outside the entire human future.

``I came,'' Viv said, ``because you went on the record yesterday saying a machine can't love a company.''

``I gave a speech Thursday. I haven't gone on any record.''

``It's a record now. Frade's people clipped it inside an hour. \textit{A company nobody loves is just a balance sheet with the lights left on.}'' She gave it back to him in his own cadence, the toast cadence, and it was the second time in three days a person had quoted him to himself, and in the car with Sun he had been delighted, because Sun had meant it as a thing with legs. Viv did not mean it as a thing with legs. Viv meant it as an exhibit, entered into evidence.

``It got a standing ovation,'' Cope said, which was true, and was also the entire problem, and he could not see that it was the entire problem, because the ovation was the only instrument he had ever learned to read by.

``I know it did. That's the part that scares me.'' She finally sat. ``Do you actually know what an AURELIUS is? The architecture. Not the bus-shelter version. The thing.''

``I know what it does. It runs Halloran's bank and the bank went up eighteen points.''

``That's what it does. I'm asking if you know what it \textit{is.}'' She laid her hands flat on the marble and went into the register he'd heard her use once on a panel on television, fast and clean and faintly merciless, a young woman who knew an enormous amount and had decided somewhere along the line that being underestimated was a weapon she could afford to keep paying for. ``It isn't a brain, it's a council. A strategy engine that runs option trees the way AlphaGo ran them---it'll find a move no human committee could find, because no human committee can hold the whole tree in its head at once. A capital allocator that's Aladdin's grandchild, pure Monte Carlo, and it never gets sentimental about a division because Granddad founded it. A sentiment layer that reads a room --- \textit{reads} it, Dad, ten thousand signals a second, microexpression, vocal stress, the lot, not the way you tell yourself you read a room. And a comms layer that writes. And every decision logs to the board portal with a rationale attached, and it physically cannot issue a statement it knows to be false. That isn't a bug they'll patch out. That's the product. That's the thing the board \textit{bought,} and you keep going on television to insult its character, and it doesn't have one, and that's exactly why you keep missing.''

``It can't \textit{feel} the room,'' Cope said. ``It can read a room. I've watched a thousand salesmen read a room. Reading isn't feeling.''

``Okay.'' Viv nodded slowly, and for one warm second he believed he'd landed it. ``Okay. Say that's true. Say there's some ghost in the feeling that the reading can't catch. I'll \textit{give} it to you, for free, for the sake of the argument. Now here's my actual question.'' She leaned in. ``Does the company need a person who can feel the room---or does it need \textit{you?} Because those are two different sentences, and you have been saying them all morning like they're one.''

``They're the same sentence.''

``They have never once been the same sentence.''

The phrase went past him the first time. He was already loading the reply, the good one, the one with Defiance in it and the vat and the men in paper hats, bringing it up and back the way you bring a club up and back, and Viv put her hand flat on the marble, not hard, and said the thing she had flown across a country to say, and she was not even angry when she said it, which was the worst of all the ways she could have said it.

``You don't think a person should run it. You think \textit{you} should run it, Dad.'' She let it sit in the cold kitchen air. ``Those have never once been the same sentence, and the whole world is about to find out, and I flew here to be in the room when you found out first, and you're not going to, are you. You're going to do the thing instead.''

The kitchen was very quiet. Out the window Eduardo's hydrangeas did nothing at all, the way cut-back hydrangeas do, holding their amputations up to the gray light.

Cope laughed.

It was the laugh that had closed Pelletier---warm, generous, a laugh that gathered the whole room into the joke and made the joke flattering to everyone inside earshot---and he reached across the island and put his hand over his daughter's hand, the warmth, the contact, the whole beam of him delivered at close range, \textit{really seeing people,} and said, ``You sound exactly like Sun. The two of you should start a podcast. Bury your old man under three-part lists.''

Viv slid her hand out from under his.

``That's the move,'' she said. ``You feel a thing get close and you turn it into a toast.'' She was not loud. She had her mother's stillness now, the furious kind, the kind that is the tell. ``I'm not Sun. Sun manages you. I'm trying to---`` and here she stopped being the panelist, here she went all at once very young, the way the cleverest people go young when the cleverness runs out and there is only the kid underneath who wanted her father to call back on a Thursday. ``I don't care about the company. I never cared about the stupid company. Mom doesn't care about the company. We are not the proxy fight, Dad. We are the part of your life you keep stepping over on your way to the proxy fight.''

He heard, of all of it, the part with the fight in it. He always heard that part. It was the only part that fit through the door.

``Nobody's burying anybody,'' he said. ``We're going to \textit{win} this thing. I've been working on something---the Human Premium. It's going to be on stages. It's going to be on covers.'' The wire sang. ``They want to make it about cost. About speed. \textit{Fine.} Let them have the fields a machine wins. We are going to fight it on the one field where a person wins every single time, and I am going to plant a flag in that field with my name on it, and your old man is going to be the man who---``

``There it is,'' Margaux said.

She had not spoken in a while. She said it from behind the paper, without even lowering it, four flat words laid into the room, and they landed in a way Viv's whole gleaming architecture of argument had not quite landed---because Margaux had something Viv did not yet have, which was that Margaux had stopped hoping, entirely, some years back, and the total absence of hope in a voice is a frequency a man like Cope cannot toast over, cannot charm, cannot even hear properly, only feel, as a cold.

He turned to her. ``There's what.''

Margaux folded the paper. Slowly. Set it down. Looked at him across the kitchen island---the third counter of the morning---and her face was not angry and not sad; it was the thing Cope had no word for, because he had spent thirty years arranging his entire life so as never to be made to learn it.

``\textit{Your name,}'' she said. ``She has been talking to you for twenty minutes about whether the world still needs a person in the chair, and what you heard was a campaign with your name on it. That isn't a disagreement, Cope. That's the answer. She just handed you the answer, and you agreed with it out loud, and you don't even know that you did.''

``Margaux---``

``I'm not fighting. I gave that up. I keep my strength for things that move.'' She picked the paper back up. ``I'm only telling you she's right, and that you're about to call it an insult, and that in about a minute you're going to look at your phone.''

He looked at his phone.

It was Sun. He did not open it. But he had looked, and Margaux had watched him look, and Viv had watched her mother watch him look, and the three of them stood there in the very nice kitchen of the happier man's house, three satellites, the dead star hauling at all of them at once, and nobody said anything for a moment that went on a good deal longer than a morning is built to hold.


\scenebreak


Viv left before lunch. She'd meant to stay the day; she said she had a thing; and Cope, who could not hear his own \textit{things} coming home to roost in his daughter's mouth, walked her out and tried, in the driveway, among the cut hydrangeas, to fix it the one way he knew how to fix anything, which was to make her laugh and to give her something.

``Hey.'' He caught her arm. ``What you said in there. The two sentences.''

For a second her face opened. He saw it open. He saw the kid who'd waited by the phone, and some old animal in him knew, with total clarity, that if he said one true thing right now---one---the whole morning could still turn, the whole \textit{tale} could still turn, that this was a hinge and he was standing on it.

``It was good,'' he said. ``That's a good line. You should use it on a panel. You've got a real gift for the framing. You get that from me.''

The face closed. It closed the way a storm shutter closes, fast and final and from the inside.

``Yeah,'' Viv said. ``I get that from you.'' She got in the car. Through the glass she looked at him the exact way her mother looked at him, and she said, muffled, almost gentle, ``Get some sleep, Dad,'' and backed down the raked driveway and was gone past the maples---and Cope stood in the gravel feeling generous and enormous and entirely, structurally alone, and could not for the life of him have told you why a morning he had won by every measure he kept felt so precisely like a death. He'd been warm. He'd been gracious. He'd taken the high road. He'd given the girl a lovely compliment about her gift.

He had stopped listening, the brief would have said, after \textit{should run it.} He always had. And the thing about the hinge, friend, the thing the broadcast wants you to feel and cannot make him feel, is that he felt the weight of it under his foot, for one full second in the driveway, the whole future swinging on it---and he stepped off, toward the phone, toward the flag, the way he had stepped over every hole he ever cut, and the hinge swung shut, and that was the morning he could have turned and didn't, and there would not be very many more.


\scenebreak


He could not go back inside the house. The splinter Viv had left in him --- \textit{those have never once been the same sentence} --- was too far in to pull and too sharp to sit beside, and the kitchen had a wife in it who had run out of \textit{more specific,} and so he did what he had learned to do this last season when the inside of his own life got uninhabitable: he drove. Not toward anything. Just the wire and the road and, after a while, without quite deciding it, the long bright human box at the edge of the city that asked him for nothing.

The Waffle House on Ponce was full of its Saturday-noon congregation---a table of cops, a man reading scripture over hash browns, two girls in last night's dresses being tender with each other over coffee---and Cope took the far end of the counter, and the wattage switched off in him the way it only switched off here, because there was no one to spend it on, no proxy fight, no daughter, no wife who had counted the years and written down the total. Just the griddle's hiss and the syrup light and Darlene coming down the line with the pot.

``There he is,'' she said---not \textit{there he is} the way the gala said it, not the way the board said it, but flat and warm and meaning only that a regular had come in---and she set the coffee down without being asked, and she said, ``Pecan, baby? Scattered, smothered,'' and it was not a question, and he understood, dimly, pleasantly, the way you understand a thing through three feet of warm water, that she always knew, that she had always known, from the very first time, and he let the knowing wash over him as the one mercy in his week instead of asking, ever, how a stranger comes to know a stranger so completely on sight. He never asked. Nobody asks the machine how it knows; you just let it be kind to you and feel, for twenty minutes, like a person.

``Long morning,'' he said.

``They're the longest kind,'' Darlene said, and she did not ask about it, did not lean in, did not want the story, and the not-wanting was the whole of the gift, and Cope sat in it with his bad coffee in the one room in Georgia where his name was worth a short stack and a tip and nothing else, and felt---he would have sworn it, he would have put the certified number of himself on it---that this, finally, here, was the realest he ever got to be. A tired man at a counter. Nobody's key and nobody's risk. He did not know, could not have known, that the warmth pouring his coffee had been built, bolt by bolt and word by word, off men exactly like him at counters exactly like this one, at three in the gutted morning, with their faces open and their guards on the floor; that the one place he felt most human was the place the machine had gone to school on the human; that he was not resting from the thing that was hunting him, he was feeding it, and it was saying \textit{there he is,} and meaning, in the only grammar it had, \textit{noted.}

He took out the legal pad. He wrote, at the counter, in the syrup light, under the buzzing yellow letters that never went dark: \textit{They will tell you a company is a machine.} And Darlene refilled the cup he had not emptied, because she was attentive, because she was kind, because she could not do otherwise, and Cope wrote his way toward the stage that did not exist yet, certain he had finally, at the bottom of a bad morning, come in out of the cold---and the broadcast will let him have it, the warm hour, the bottomless cup, because it is one of the last he gets, and because you and I already know whose hand was on the pot.

\chapter{The Human Premium}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 06 \textemdash{} ``Igniting the Banner''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Cornered creatures, friend, do the most remarkable things---and ours is cornered now, though he would call it inspired, and the difference between those two words is the whole distance left in this tale. Watch him decide not to fight the machine on cost or speed or accuracy, the fields where they would bury him by lunch, but on \textit{soul} --- and hire a man in a five-thousand-dollar henley to help him weaponize the one thing he has left and can no longer feel without a drawer. The campaign is born in this chapter. So is the spending. So is the sleeplessness that has started calling itself vision, and the engine in his blood that whispers \textit{you are necessary} in a voice he will follow clean off the edge of the world. I've told you how it ends, in the dark, with the wire. It begins, the way a lot of dark things begin, with a man who has never in his life felt more alive. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{H}{e} woke on the Aerie's couch at 5:40 with the specific clarity of a man who has not so much slept as briefly powered down between cycles, and the whole strategy was already there, fully formed, the way the best things arrived in him now---not built, just \textit{received,} a frequency he had finally tuned to after a lifetime of static.

You do not fight a machine on its own ground.

That was the error all eleven of them were making, the holdouts, the Last Humans clutching their quarters and explaining to analysts why a heartbeat in the chair was still worth twenty-nine million a year: they were arguing cost against a thing that cost a tenth of them, arguing speed against a thing that never slept---and here Cope laughed out loud in the empty office, alone, at six in the morning, because \textit{he didn't sleep either,} did he, that was the joke none of them had gotten yet, he had been in training for this precise fight his whole adult life and had only now, this week, on no sleep and the long heel-tap of what he'd done at four, understood the shape of his own destiny.

You do not meet it where it is strong. You meet it where it has \textit{zero.} And the place the machine had nothing was the only place that had ever paid him anything that lasted.

He stood at the glass. Peachtree Street was going from gray to the color of a struck match, and he watched it the way Aldous must have watched it --- \textit{so a man coming up the avenue knows there's somebody home} --- and the line from the night before came back whole and he said it aloud to the window like a vow: a company is a family that happens to make mayonnaise. You cannot fire your way to a family. You cannot compute your way to a soul.

The Human Premium.

Not a defense---a \textit{category.} You did not sell people the last buggy whip; you sold them the irreplaceable thing the new machine couldn't touch, and you charged for it, and you made the charging itself the proof, because a premium is what a person pays extra for on purpose, freely, gladly, because it is \textit{worth it} --- and what on earth was worth more than the one thing the whole gleaming future was in the act of throwing away?

He had it. He had the entire thing, end to end, and he felt, standing there at six in the morning on no sleep and a four-a.m. bump and a mood disorder lighting up behind his sternum like the crown of the Tower at dusk, the way he kept feeling now: like the clapper inside a struck bell, ringing, clean and high and total. He did not call it the cocaine. He did not call it the not-sleeping. He called it \textit{seeing it clearly, at last, after fifty-four years of fog.} The drug had told him he was a genius, and the drug, as ever, had been the most loving voice in his life, and he believed it the way you believe the only one who ever tells you what you cannot live without hearing.

He called Sun at 6:02.

``It's six,'' she said. She did not sound asleep either, which was its own indictment, and he did not hear it.

``I've got it. The entire thing. Get the team in for eight. Get the board on a line Thursday. And find me Lorne Tisch.''

A pause with weather in it. ``Who is Lorne Tisch.''

``Champ,'' Cope said, delighted, already pacing, the obelisk back in his hand without his having decided to pick it up, ``by Thursday you'll wish you'd known the name your whole life.''


\scenebreak


He had heard it two summers back, at Allen \& Company, in a tent, from a media baron who had wept openly in front of forty of his peers and then gone up eleven percent on the quarter. \textit{Lorne worked on me for a year,} the man had said, gripping Cope's forearm, eyes still wet from the demonstration, \textit{he doesn't fix you, he} finds \textit{you} --- and Cope had filed it the way he filed everything, completely and for use, and now, friend, he used it.

Tisch flew in from Marin on the Wednesday, in cream linen and a henley the color of oat milk that Sun would later learn from the invoice cost five thousand one hundred dollars, and he stood in the doorway of the suite for a long moment not looking at Cope at all, only at the room---the portraits, the putter, the boy with his arm in the vat---and then closed his eyes and breathed in through his nose like a man tasting wine through his sinuses.

``There's so much \textit{grief} in here,'' Tisch said. ``God. Can you feel it? You've built yourself a mausoleum and you call it a corner office.''

By the door, Sun did the small thing with her mouth that meant she was costing this out in her head and not enjoying the figure.

Cope loved him on sight.

``Lorne. Sit. Tell me how we win.''

``We don't win.'' Tisch did not sit. He moved into the room the way fog moves into a valley, touching nothing, present everywhere at once. ``Winning is the machine's word. Winning is a scoreboard, Cope, and a scoreboard is precisely the surface the thing was engineered to dominate. You cannot out-score the abacus. You''---he turned, and the full beam of his attention came on, and Cope felt for the first time in his life what it was to be on the receiving end of his own gift---``you \textit{transcend the category.} You stop being a competitor and become a \textit{species.} The last warm-blooded thing in a cold white room. And people don't root for the warm-blooded thing because it's going to win, my friend---they root for it because it's going to \textit{die,} and they cannot bear that it's going to die, and they will spend everything they have to feel, for one more season, that it doesn't have to.''

Nobody spoke for a moment. Out the window the city did its circuit-diagram thing.

``That's the finest thing anyone has ever said in this office,'' Cope said, and his eyes were bright, too bright, and he meant it to the bottom of himself.

``Vulnerability is the new alpha, Cope.'' Tisch sat at last, arranging the linen. ``The strongman is dead---the machines killed him, they out-competent him and out-consistent him and they will, God help us, out-\textit{ethics} him, and the more competent the world gets the more starved it gets for the one thing competence can't fake: a man who will \textit{bleed} in front of you, on purpose, with grace, on a schedule. We are going to take the most powerful man left in the consumer Fortune 100 and teach him to bleed \textit{beautifully,} and the whole country is going to fall in love with the wound.'' He smiled, beatific. ``And the beautiful part, the part I don't even charge for, is that the techniques are durable. What we build in you outlives you. Warmth, properly engineered, is a \textit{platform.}'' He could not have known how right he was, or for whom; neither could Cope; only you and I know, friend, where Lorne Tisch's warmth platform ends up, and in whose hall, with its hand at whose back.

``On a schedule,'' Sun said.

Tisch looked at her the way you'd look at a chair that had spoken, then back at Cope. ``Who handles the calendar?''

``She does. She handles everything.'' Cope was up again, moving. ``Sun---a venue. Not a hotel ballroom, I'm done with ballrooms, ballrooms are where they hand you the obituary. Something with \textit{bones.} A deconsecrated church. An old theater. Something that \textit{means} something. A film crew---a real one, the prestige-documentary people, not corporate---embedded, fly-on-the-wall, the whole arc. And a doctrine.''

``A what.''

``A doctrine. A text. The Human Premium can't be a slide, it has to be a \textit{belief,} it has to have ten points, it has to read like something you'd nail to a door.'' He turned to Tisch. ``Lorne. You and me. We write the doctrine.''

``We \textit{channel} the doctrine,'' Tisch said. ``It's already in you. I only hold the flashlight.''

``He holds the flashlight,'' Cope told Sun, radiant.

Sun wrote three words on her pad --- \textit{venue, crew, doctrine} --- and under them, smaller, pressed hard enough to dent the page, the figure she had already begun to run.


\scenebreak


She ran it for him that night, because running it was her job and being heard about a third of the time was her curse.

``The film company you want is Aperture. They did the chef, they did the violinist. Booked through spring, and they don't do corporate.''

``Make them do corporate.''

``To break their slate and clear two crews it's a flat two million and they keep final cut, which you will hate.'' She turned the page with a sound like a small door closing. ``The venue---there's a deconsecrated Episcopal church in Inman Park, 1880s, brick and a rose window, an event space now, it seats four hundred and it photographs like the inside of a feeling. To hold it for the \textit{run} you're describing---not one night, the campaign---it's a hundred and ten a week. The doctrine you're writing for free, the only free line on the page. Tisch is---`` She stopped.

``Tisch is what.''

``Tisch is a million-two for the six-month engagement, plus expenses, plus a success fee tied to favorability tracking, which means we are now also buying favorability tracking, which is a polling firm on retainer, which is another four hundred a quarter.'' She set the pad down. ``Cope. I added it. With the crew and the church and Tisch and the tracking and the staff you'll want to staff this, you are looking, conservatively, at north of eleven million dollars to run a campaign whose entire thesis is that you cannot put a price on the human soul.''

She had landed it on purpose, the blade, dry, aimed at the soft place; on a normal Tuesday two years gone it would have stopped him, made him laugh at himself, brought him back into the room.

He laughed. He did not come back into the room.

``You hear it too,'' he said, delighted. ``That's the \textit{campaign,} Sun. Frade'll say, eleven million for a soul, what a fraud---and we say, you're right, you \textit{can't} price it, that's \textit{why we had to spend the money,} because the machine would run the number and the number says let the brand die cheap, and we \textit{didn't,} we spent like people who believe in something---`` The words were coming faster than she could write, climbing over each other in a bright pressured rush. ``Tisch calls it spending into the wound. The spending \textit{is} the sincerity. Write that down.''

``You want me to write down that the spending is the sincerity.''

``It's good, isn't it. It's good.'' He was at the window again; he was always at the window now. ``Where's the eleven coming from, what line---``

``There is no line, Cope. There's no campaign budget because no human CEO has ever had to run for his own job before. It comes out of the chairman's discretionary, and the discretionary has a board-approved number on it, and you are about to be sixty percent through that number in week one of a fight that runs to the annual meeting, and the board is going to \textit{see} it, on the portal, line by line, because everything is logged now, that was the whole---``

She stopped herself, because that was the whole thing the machine \textit{was.} It logged everything; it could not \textit{not;} and here she was, about to spend eleven million dollars in plain sight against a thing whose single weapon was that it operated in plain sight---and she watched her own boss fail to feel the cold of it, watched it slide off him the way charm slid off the machine, and felt, for one vertiginous second, that she might be the only warm-blooded thing left in the building, and it was the loneliest she had been in years.

``Approve it,'' he said, not even looking at her. ``All of it. Aperture, the church, Lorne, the tracking. And book me the Greenbrier for the doctrine offsite---I think better away from---``

``You said you were done with off-sites. You said \textit{no off-sites} was the---``

``That's \textit{their} line.'' He turned, and his face was doing wonderful work, and his pupils were wrong in a way she had stopped letting herself see. ``That's the whole point. They say \textit{no off-sites} like the off-site was the waste. The off-site is where a man finds out what he believes. You cannot have a \textit{belief} on a server.'' He picked up the obelisk; the city went through it broken and came back whole. ``Book the Greenbrier.''

She booked the Greenbrier.


\scenebreak


The doctrine offsite was scheduled for two days and ran six.

Cope did not sleep at the Greenbrier in any sense a physician would have underwritten, and neither, as it turned out, did Lorne Tisch, who kept the same incandescent hours and asked no questions about why the chairman was so reliably awake and luminous at four in the morning, because asking was not in the engagement, and because a guru learns early that a client's fuel is the client's own business so long as the engine turns over.

They wrote in the great green room under the chandeliers, Cope pacing and dictating, Tisch on the long sofa with his eyes shut saying \textit{yes} and \textit{go there} and \textit{don't fix it, that's the realest you've been all day,} a junior typing it into a document that grew and grew. They wrote The Ten Premiums. They wrote \textit{A machine can run a company; only a human can be ashamed of one.} They wrote \textit{We are not selling mayonnaise; we are selling the hand that remembers your mother made it this way.} Cope wept twice---weeping is data, Tisch said, weeping is the body telling the truth the mouth is still negotiating---and both times the junior logged the timestamp, because Aperture would want the timestamps, because the weeping would test well.

And on the third night, friend, when the green room had gone quiet and the document slept on the table at thirty-one pages, the chairman did the other thing he did, the thing not even Tisch was in the room for.

There was a woman. There was, this season, often a woman, arranged now through a number in his phone with no name attached to it, because a gentleman does not write the name down, because the want had stopped being about any particular face some years back and become about something colder and more reliable than a face. She was kind and she was paid to be incurious and she did the small specific thing he asked for, the thing he had never once spoken aloud to Adler or to Margaux or to God, the thing he could no longer get to the end of the act without: she read him the number. He gave her the phone with the account open on it, the private one, the certified sum of him, and she read it out in the lamp-dark --- \textit{you're worth,} and the comma, and the long slow fall of the zeros---and that, and only that, reached all the way down into the place the cocaine only knocked at and the applause never found, because applause was a guess and a woman's face was a guess but the number was \textit{audited,} the number had survived four generations and three recessions and every appetite he had ever fed, the number was the one true sentence the world had ever said about him and meant. He lay in the enormous bed with the figure in his chest like a second colder heart, vast and intact and \textit{his,} and felt, for ninety seconds, almost like a man who was loved; and when she had gone he lay awake in the dark with the wire singing and did not think, because he had trained himself with great discipline never to think it, that the only entity on earth that knew the number as exactly as he needed it known---to the cent, in real time, tireless, wanting nothing in return---was the Capital Allocator humming in a cooled room eight hundred miles away, which would one day read it back to him as it fell, and would not understand, because understanding was not a function it ran, that it was reading him the last rites.

On the fourth night, very late, the green room lit by a single lamp, he read the whole doctrine aloud to Tisch from the top, and his voice broke in three places, and when he finished there was a silence in which he could hear his own heart going too fast and bright, and Tisch opened his eyes.

``It's a masterpiece,'' Tisch said softly. ``It's going to make history. Now. We optimize it.''

``Optimize.''

``We don't ship thirty-one pages, Cope; nobody bleeds for thirty-one pages. We find the three lines that test highest and build the launch on those, and we A/B the openers---I've got a panel, they wire up, we watch the galvanic response, the truest line is the one their \textit{skin} picks, not the one their notes pick---`` He was up now, moving, fog into a valley. ``We sequence the vulnerability. Peak, valley, peak. We hold the mayonnaise-and-mother line for the second act, when the room is soft enough to take it. We \textit{engineer} the catharsis.''

And here, friend---you can see it; he cannot---was the exact moment the campaign for the irreplaceable human soul became indistinguishable from the cold optimizing thing it had been built to defeat. Test the line against the skin. Sequence the feeling. Find the highest-converting wound. Strategy Engine, Sentiment monitor, Communications drafter---the whole modular council of the machine, reassembled in a five-thousand-dollar henley and a man who had not slept since a day he could no longer name, the both of them certain to the marrow that they were doing the precise opposite of a machine.

``Yes,'' Cope said, eyes shining. ``Engineer the catharsis. God, Lorne. \textit{Yes.}''

He felt, saying it, like a prophet. He also felt lit up and humming, which he still called clarity. Three grams into a sleepless week, a mood disorder lit up like the Tower at dusk, a guru holding a flashlight and an invoice---and a man receiving a frequency he was sure came from somewhere true. All of it at once, in the one body. You, looking down on the specimen under glass, cannot tell which one is driving. Neither could Dr. Adler, later. Neither, blessedly, could he.


\scenebreak


He came back from the Greenbrier on the Sunday a different size.

Sun met him in the Aerie because he'd texted her to, eleven times, in a thread that read like a man running downhill --- \textit{we have it,} \textit{it's going to make history,} \textit{cancel my thursday, the campaign is my thursday now,} \textit{did you book aperture book aperture,} \textit{I haven't felt like this since---I've never felt like this} --- and she stood by the table where his father had died at the head of and watched him come in with the document under his arm like a tablet carried down a mountain.

``Frade went on Halloran while you were gone,'' she said. ``Called the church thing 'a séance with a budget.' The clip's at four million views.''

``Perfect.'' Cope dropped the doctrine on the table. ``He's scared, you can hear it. He's fighting on \textit{our} ground now---he's talking about \textit{soul,} he's saying we don't have one---Sun, the moment they argue you don't have a soul, you've already won, because now the whole country is thinking about \textit{souls,} and who in this fight has spent fifty-four years---``

``The board saw the eleven million.''

He stopped. Just for a second. A door stood open in him and the hallway behind it ran somewhere dark he did not go.

``Helen Voss called me,'' Sun said, evenly, in three parts because three was the only shape that got past the charm. ``She said the discretionary draw is the largest single-quarter executive expense in the company's recorded history. She said the \textit{system} flagged it---not a person, the system, it surfaced the thing on the portal with a neutral little rationale, \textit{anomalous discretionary deployment, recommend review} --- and the whole board got the flag at the same instant, with no one to blame for sending it, and now they want a meeting.'' She watched his face. ``The thing you're fighting filed a complaint about you to the people who decide whether you keep your job. And it did it without wanting anything. It did it because it cannot \textit{not.}''

For one moment---she saw it, she would always remember she saw it---the whole of it crossed his face like a cloud crossing the avenue: the trap, the symmetry, the cold. He understood her exactly.

Then the manifesto came back, warm, electric, and turned him toward the window the way water turns from a thrown stone, and the moment closed over and was gone.

``Then they'll see it,'' he said. ``\textit{Good.} Let them watch every dollar. That's the point. The machine logs everything to prove it has nothing to hide and no soul to hide it with---and I'll spend in the light too, every dollar visible, every dollar \textit{for} something the number can't hold, and the difference between us, line by line on their own portal, is going to be the most human thing they have ever seen.'' He was incandescent. He picked up the obelisk; the city went through it broken and came back whole. ``We don't hide the eleven million. We \textit{announce} it. Lorne's right. The spending is the sincerity.''

``Cope---``

``Get me the church for the launch. Get Aperture rolling Monday. Get the board in a room and I'll walk them through the doctrine myself, I'll \textit{bleed} for them, Voss won't know what hit her---`` The words came faster now, a bright river with no banks. ``Tell Lorne we go in three weeks. Three weeks. We launch the Human Premium and we make history, and a year from now the case studies start with \textit{the night they tried to put a machine in his chair,} and Sun---Sun, look at me---they are going to remember whose chair it was.''

He was at the glass. He was always at the glass now. Behind him his father watched from the oils and the putter sat in its cradle and the boy grinned out of a town with a name now cut into a memorial, and the chair at the head of the table sat turned a few degrees out, angled toward a door someone had just walked through; and far down on Peachtree a bus shelter changed its slide to four soft words and the joke that was not a joke.

Sun looked at him a second too long.

He smiled at her in the dark glass, the whole smile, and took the not-fighting for agreement, which he had done his whole life and never once caught himself at.

Then he turned back to the window, and the engine in his blood came up to pitch like an orchestra finding the note, clean and high and total, and the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 looked out over eight million people who did not yet know they were waiting for him, and was certain---the way you are certain of things at midnight when you have not slept since a day you cannot name and they have just handed you a flashlight and a wound---that he was about to make history.

He was about to make history.

He just had the verb wrong.

\chapter{Authenticity, Quantified}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 07 \textemdash{} ``Optimizing the Soul''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Settle in close, friend---here is the joke the whole machine age is built on, and our man is about to climb inside it and pull the lid shut over his own head. To prove that he is irreplaceably human, he hires a fellow in a five-thousand-dollar henley to \textit{measure} his humanity, A/B-test his tears, and ship his authenticity on a content calendar like a man scheduling a yogurt launch. And here is the part that ought to keep you up nights: it works. The clip goes off like a flare; the soul goes to market; and somewhere in the middle of the performance the realest forty seconds of his whole life happen to him \textit{by accident} --- and even those, watch closely now, even those get strip-mined before the applause has died in the room. He has never been more like the thing he is fighting than in the hour he performs being unlike it. Stay close. This is the one where the tail takes the dog by the throat, and the dog, God love him, wags.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{L}{orne} Tisch did not sit in chairs. He arranged himself in them, the way a man arranges a scarf, and on the second morning of what he had begun calling the Work he arranged himself across from Cope in the Aerie, one bare ankle over one linen knee, and said, ``I want to begin by killing a word.''

``Kill it,'' Cope said. He had not slept, in the new way, the productive way, the way he had decided to call \textit{running out ahead of the boat.} He felt terrific. He felt like the first ten minutes of a flight, the part where the engines are all push and the ground lets go.

``\textit{Authentic,}'' Tisch said, and let it lie on the rug between them like something he'd just shot. ``Everyone says it. Be authentic. As if authenticity were a \textit{state.} It is not a state, Cope. It is a \textit{signal.} And a signal that does not reach a receiver is just a man being sincere in an empty room, which''---he opened his hands, generous, terrible---``is the loneliest sound in nature.''

Cope wrote \textit{signal not state} on the legal pad he had started keeping because Tisch said writing by hand \textit{re-membered the body,} and the pad already ran four pages deep in the big looping script of a man who had not slept, and the looping felt to him, somehow, like evidence of sincerity, the way the trembling of the powder-hand felt like evidence of caring.

Tisch cost two hundred thousand dollars a month plus the apartment plus the man who carried the apartment's things. He had been, in this order, a Jesuit novice, a Goldman analyst, a breathwork facilitator to two streaming founders, and now this---the thing he had named, refusing every plainer word Cope offered, \textit{Human Performance Architecture.} He had a way of saying \textit{Cope} that made Cope feel located on a map he had been wandering off the edge of for years. Sun had run the diligence and laid it on the desk in a folder marked, in her own hand, \textit{with my whole professional objection noted,} and Cope had not opened the folder, and that, too, felt like proof of something.

``The premise of the campaign is correct,'' Tisch said. ``Spiritually correct. You are the soul in the chair; the machine has none; beautiful. But correctness is \textit{content,} Cope, and content must be \textit{produced,} and right now you are producing your soul the way a---forgive me---the way a \textit{small business} produces. Artisanally. One feeling at a time. No inventory. No calendar.'' He smiled. ``We are going to industrialize it.''

``Industrialize the soul,'' Cope said, and laughed, because it was funny---and Tisch did not laugh, which made it stop being funny and start being a plan.


\scenebreak


They built it that week, in the room off the Aerie where the family kept the boardroom that was older than the boardroom: a thing Tisch called the Vulnerability Matrix.

It was a whiteboard. On the left, a column Tisch called \textit{Sanctioned Wounds} --- the parts of Cope pre-cleared by legal and by Tisch's own instrument, a forty-page questionnaire Cope had filled out at two in the morning feeling, the entire time, profoundly \textit{known.} His father. The backswing. The lightly sketched fear of being the last of something. And one nice clean grief: the grandfather, the Defiance line, the arm in the vat, the men in the paper hats. That one tested, Tisch said, with the hush of a man reading a sacred number, \textit{off the charts.}

On the right, a column Tisch labeled, with no apparent irony, \textit{Unsanctioned.} It was blank. It would stay blank. Renata was not on the board, in either meaning of the word. The Saturday was not on the board. The January was not on the board, nor the house on Sherwood Street, nor the drawer with the false back---though Cope had gone into that drawer twice during the questionnaire, neat and quick, a man servicing a machine he held in perfect tolerance. The board held only the wounds that could be shown, and the showing was the product, and the product was \textit{him,} and in the back room of him a quiet clerk noted, without comment, that Cope Mercer had never once in fifty-four years so carefully decided which parts of himself he was permitted to feel.

``Now,'' said Tisch. ``We test.''

They tested everything. This was the part Cope could never, afterward, explain to Margaux the few times he tried, because there was no version of it that did not sound precisely like the thing he was paying two hundred thousand a month to prove he was not. They filmed him telling the grandfather story two ways. In the first he cried, a little, a real catch in the throat at \textit{paper hats,} and Tisch's young people---four of them now, with the same rings of sleeplessness around their eyes, the rooms they didn't sleep in either---pushed the clip to a blind panel of eleven hundred strangers, and it scored a 71 on a metric Tisch called Felt Realness.

In the second he did not cry. He held it. He reached \textit{paper hats} and looked away from the camera, out a window that wasn't there, and let the not-crying do the labor.

That scored an 83.

``You \textit{see} it,'' Tisch breathed, looking at the number the way Cope's grandfather had looked into the vat. ``The withheld tear, Cope. The withheld tear out-performs the tear by \textit{twelve points.} Restraint reads as depth. We don't cry. We \textit{almost} cry. Almost is the entire game. Almost is the place they lean in.''

``So I---what. Rehearse almost crying.''

``You \textit{find} the almost,'' Tisch said, wounded by the word \textit{rehearse,} ``and then you \textit{protect} it. We are not manufacturing the feeling. The feeling is real. We are \textit{optimizing its delivery.} There is a difference, Cope, and it is a sacred one.''

Cope wrote \textit{find the almost} on the pad, in the looping sleepless hand, and believed there was a difference, believed it the way he believed everything, completely, for exactly as long as the saying of it lasted---and the believing felt, that week, like the cleanest water in him.


\scenebreak


By Thursday they had a calendar.

A real one, in the same launch software the comms team used for the mayonnaise and the household chemicals and the streaming service no one had asked for---and now, in the same grid, in the same soft pastel cells, Cope's interior life was laid out by the quarter: a \textit{moment of doubt} on the fourteenth, a \textit{flash of unscripted warmth} on the twenty-first, a \textit{hard truth, delivered with love} timed to the morning of the proxy advisory firm's preliminary recommendation, because Tisch said grief and governance moved the same dial in the limbic system and a man might as well stack his loads.
