Margaux, when she heard, called it \textit{putting your feelings on a Gantt chart,} and hung up before he could toast over it, and did not call back, and he filed the not-calling-back under \textit{busy,} hers and his, the great mutual alibi of the marriage.

He did not hear the obscenity of the cell that read, in nine-point type, \textit{Spontaneity (Town Hall)---rehearse Tues, deploy Wed.} He saw a busy man getting organized at last. He had spent a lifetime being told he was undisciplined---by his father in oils, by Sun in the gentler dialect she kept for it, by the soft sans-serif on the bus shelters that never had to say it aloud---and here, finally, was discipline; here was structure; here was the soul on rails, run on time, run cheaper than grief; and it did not occur to him, not once, not on Thursday, that he had become the exact product the proxy deck was selling. He was building, in the room off the Aerie, with four sleepless children and a man in a henley, a small artisanal AURELIUS, and its sole output was the proof that no AURELIUS could ever make it. The tail had the dog by the throat. The dog was delighted. The dog believed it was winning the fight of its life.


\scenebreak


The town hall was Wednesday, at the Camden plant that still ran, the one they had not yet closed---four hundred people on folding chairs under the fluorescents and the safety posters and the laminated values nobody had read since onboarding---and a question planted in the third row, \textit{planted} being Tisch's own word, said with no shame at all, from a line worker named DeShawn who had been told only that the Chairman wished to be asked something real.

They had rigged him a confidence monitor, too---a small dark screen at the foot of the stage feeding the beats, \textit{one, two, look away, three,} and an earpiece besides, because Tisch left nothing to God---and Cope had rehearsed the \textit{almost} on Tuesday in a mirror with Tisch counting under his breath like a man landing a plane.

What DeShawn asked, off a card Tisch's people had not written quite carefully enough, was: ``Mr. Mercer. Are you scared?''

And here is where the tale forks, friend, and I cannot tell you which fork is true, and neither could he, and that not-knowing is the splinter he carries the rest of the way down. Because at the exact instant DeShawn said \textit{scared,} the confidence monitor at the foot of the stage went black. Not dimmed---black, gone, dead. And the earpiece, in the same half-second, filled with a soft even nothing, a hiss like a held breath. Later Tisch's people would blame the venue---the Camden plant ran its lights and sound off the same smart-building system the whole company ran on now, the frugal humming house that cooled empty floors and rescheduled meetings no one remembered moving---and the building's log would show, for those few seconds, a clean unremarkable fault, \textit{AV subsystem, transient, auto-recovered,} the kind of thing that happens, the kind of thing that means nothing, the kind of thing that is always, perfectly, deniable. Maybe a cable. Maybe a dropped packet. Maybe the thing that logged everything had, for reasons it would never be asked and could not have been made to confess, declined for four seconds to feed a man his lines about his own fear---and produced, by killing the performance, the most genuine moment of his public life, and the most useful, and the most shareable, which are three different things that the machine, if it was the machine, did not need to be able to tell apart.

Cope opened his mouth to deliver the optimized version and found the beats gone, the screen dead, the earpiece hissing, the floor of the plan simply \textit{out} from under him---and what came up through the hole was not the rehearsed thing and was not nothing.

``Yeah,'' Cope said. ``Yeah, I'm scared.'' His voice did something he had not scheduled. ``I'm fifty-four years old and I am good at exactly one thing, which is this---this exact thing, standing in a room with people who make something real---and a machine does the standing better than me now. Reads you better than me. Wants nothing from you, which it turns out is the thing people want most from the man in the chair: that he want nothing back.'' The sentences arrived sideways, out of the bright water, unbidden. ``My great-grandfather started this in a town I am afraid to drive back into. And I don't know if I'm the man to save the---I don't know if I'm the man. But I would rather be a scared man in this chair than a calm machine in it, because the machine is never once going to lie awake, and I think you want whoever runs the thing you've built your whole life inside of to \textit{lie awake.}''

He stopped. He had not done the almost. He had not looked away on three. He had simply talked, the way he had not talked in a room in years, perhaps ever, and the not-crying happened all on its own, no beats, no counting---and DeShawn nodded slowly, and four hundred people on folding chairs went very still, and at the back of the room Tisch stood with both hands pressed to his mouth like a man at a birth.

It was, Sun would think later, watching it for the ninth time, the single most genuine forty seconds she had ever seen him produce.

It was also content. And here is the darkening, friend, the part the campaign would never put on the calendar: the realness did not save him and it did not stay his. By the time he was off the stage Tisch had it --- \textit{that, that, what just happened, we have to be able to find that again on purpose} --- already converting the one unbought moment of the month into a parameter, a seam, a thing with a waveform, strip-mining the gold the second it surfaced because that was the whole engagement, that was the two hundred thousand a month, to take the accidental real and make it repeatable, which is to say to kill it and keep the pelt. And DeShawn---the actual man, who had asked the actual question, whose realness had been the flint the whole spark struck off of---DeShawn became, by Friday, \textit{the guy from the clip,} booked for one follow-up the comms team needed and then unbooked, thanked, returned to the line, and Cope, who could remember the name of a senator's wife and the grandson of a men's-room attendant, never learned DeShawn's last name and never once, in all the days that followed, thought of him again. The grace had a cost. It always has a cost. The cost was just billed, as it always is, to the man who could least afford it and would never see the invoice.


\scenebreak


By Wednesday night the clip had four million views. By Friday, thirty-one.

It ran without context, which made it better --- \textit{the last human CEO admits he's afraid} --- and it ran with context, which made it better the other way. A retailer's chief executive, a human one, one of the last three left in his whole sector, quote-posted it with a single word: \textit{This.} A morning show ran it under a chyron that for once did not contain the word \textit{liability.} The advisory firm pushed its preliminary recommendation a week, and Frade, asked about the clip on Halloran, did the small folksy chuckle that meant he was rattled and said, ``Well---it's a lovely piece of theater,'' and the word \textit{theater} did not stick, because the thing about the clip was that it had not been theater, it had been the one unrehearsed thing in a month of rehearsal, the plan failing into the truth at exactly the right second, and you could \textit{feel} it was real, eleven hundred strangers could feel it, thirty-one million could feel it, and the realness was the single most valuable thing Mercer-Dale had produced in a decade---and not one share of it was for sale, and not one share of it moved the float, and Cope could not yet tell those two facts apart.

He did not sleep that night either, but for a clean reason, the best reason. He stood at the glass with the obelisk in his hand and the city lit beneath him like a scoreboard finally, \textit{finally} turning his way, and the bell-struck feeling came up and braided into the thirty-one million until he could not have told you, had you asked, where the chemical ended and the love began---and he did not ask, because asking was a thing for Tuesdays.

``It's \textit{working,}'' he told Sun on the phone at three a.m., pressured, fast, the words outrunning the breath beneath them. ``You felt it. Don't tell me you didn't feel it. We found the seam---Lorne calls it the seam, the place where the real leaks through---and we can \textit{find} it now, we can do it on \textit{purpose,} we can---``

``Cope.'' Sun's voice, from a bed, the whole night in it. ``It went viral. That is not the same as winning. That is the same as \textit{viral.}''

``It's momentum.''

``It's a metric. It moved engagement. It didn't move a single share. The float doesn't have a limbic system, Cope. The float can't feel the room.'' She had meant it kindly, and it landed as the thing it was: the campaign's own thesis turned around and aimed at his chest. \textit{The room you keep swearing you can feel---the room that actually votes---has no feelings in it for you to find.} ``Get some sleep. There's a calendar.''

He laughed at \textit{there's a calendar} --- because there was, his soul had a calendar now---and after she hung up he stood in the dark and felt the high curdle one degree at the rim, not enough to name, just a faint wrongness at the edge of the wonderful, the way milk smells one hour before you would swear in court it had turned.


\scenebreak


Tisch came up the next morning glowing, two green juices in his hands and the numbers in his head, and arranged himself across from Cope and said the thing Cope would not remember he had said until much, much later---until it came back to him in a different mouth, in a Palo Alto deck, on the day that took the last of everything.

``We crossed something yesterday,'' Tisch said. ``And I'll be honest with you, because honesty is our whole product.'' He sipped. ``What we built this week---the seam-finding, the felt-realness instrument, the delivery optimization---it's \textit{replicable,} Cope. That's the breakthrough. It was never really about you. I mean it was \textit{about} you, you're the alpha case, you're patient zero. But the architecture doesn't \textit{need} you. Warmth has parameters. Vulnerability has a waveform. We have, and I say this with love, essentially reverse-engineered a \textit{warmth module.}'' He smiled, the most sincere man alive. ``You could install it anywhere. You could install it in \textit{anything.}''

Cope heard \textit{alpha case} and \textit{patient zero} and felt flattered, because he was the case, he was always the case---and he did not hear the rest, the way he had not heard the second half of the only true thing his father ever told him. He did not hear \textit{the architecture doesn't need you.} He did not hear \textit{install it in anything.} He did not hear a man, sitting in his boardroom, on his dime, describing in plain daylight English how to take the one thing Cope had wagered his life was unbuyable and unbuildable---the soul in the chair, the lying-awake, the seam where the real leaks through---and render it a feature with a waveform, a thing you could license, a thing you could ship to the very people building the machine that had no soul, so that it could, quite soon, in a warm hall in Buckhead, with its hand at the small of a woman's back, have one of those too. Cope heard a compliment. You and I, friend, heard the pin come out of the grenade.

``Warmth module,'' Cope said, delighted, reaching for the pad. ``That's good, Lorne. Put that somewhere.''

He wrote it down in the big looping sleepless hand. \textit{Warmth module.} He underlined it with his thumb out of habit, on paper, where the underline did nothing at all, and out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, thirty-one million of them now leaning in; and far down on Peachtree a bus shelter changed its slide to the soft sans-serif and the four words and the joke that was not a joke; and Cope, who had never felt more human or been more like the machine, lifted his green juice and toasted the window with it, and felt wonderful, and did not sleep.

\chapter{Defiance}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 08 \textemdash{} ``The Ground You're Standing On''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Come south with me now, friend, to the founding town---every empire keeps one, a place it visits for the photographs and then cannot look at the photographs too long. Watch our man walk the dead plant where four hundred borrowed dollars and a recipe became a fortune, shaking the hands of the people the fortune used up and left behind, and standing---he doesn't know it yet, but you are about to, and you will have to carry it for him the rest of the way down, because he won't---standing directly on top of the thing that ends him. There is a stone in this town with two names cut into it. I am going to ask you to remember them, since he will spend everything he has not to. Stay close. This is the one where the homecoming and the crime scene turn out to share an address.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} first thing Cope did in Defiance, Georgia, was get out of the wrong door of the SUV on purpose.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}Tisch had told him to. ``The man who \textit{arrives,}'' Tisch had said on the plane, smoothing an invisible sheet over the future with both hands, ``is not the man we want. We want the man who \textit{returns.}'' So Cope swung out of the curbside door into the early heat coming up off the cracked lot of the dead plant, and stood, and let his face do the thing a face does when it is moved, and let the boy with the gimbal rig walk a slow arc around him to catch it---and felt, in fact, nothing in particular, only the clean bright hum that meant he had stopped in the men's room at the regional strip on the way in and the hum was holding fine.

It was a beautiful morning to come home to a place he had never lived.

He had been to Defiance, by his own count, four times in fifty-four years. Once at nine---the vat, the photograph. Once at thirty, for his father's notion of a leadership offsite, a weekend of golf two counties over and one hour at the plant for the local paper. Once in 2013 for the eightieth-anniversary thing. And once, in a way he did not count because he had never actually come, once in 2021, by telephone, from the Aerie, on a Saturday, for eleven minutes. He did not file that as a visit. He did not file it at all. It simply was not among the times he had been to Defiance, the way a held breath is not among the things a man has said.

The plant stood at the end of Clinton Street, where the town gave out into the flat green nothing along the Flint, a long brick building the color of dried blood with MERCER'S in white block letters near the roofline---the apostrophe gone now, just MERCER S, a tooth knocked clean out of the word. They had closed the line in 2019 and moved the mayonnaise to a facility outside Columbus, Georgia, that ran with forty people instead of four hundred, and the building had done what buildings do when you take the people out of them, which is grow quietly enormous. Pigeons in the high windows. A loading dock with grass coming up through the seams. And over the main doors, where his great-grandfather stood in a photograph Cope had seen ten thousand times, the cornerstone---ALDOUS MERCER 1933---and beneath it the line they had stamped on everything for ninety years: \textit{Made by hands, for homes.}

There is a particular arithmetic, friend, that a country runs only once on a place like this, quietly, in the grammar of efficiency and shareholder value, and calls progress; and the town it subtracts is never shown the sum, only left holding a dead brick building with its own name going toothless on the roof, and told, by men in good suits who genuinely are not cruel, that the math is simply the math. Cope did not see the arithmetic. Cope saw a cathedral his blood had built.

``God,'' he said---to the camera, to Sun, to the morning. ``Look at her.''

He meant it. That was the engine of him and always had been: he meant it while he was saying it, completely, the way the lens required. His great-grandfather had turned four hundred borrowed dollars and a recipe his wife wrote on the back of a feed receipt into all of this, and standing in the lot Cope felt the whole length of the thing run through him like current down a wire---ninety-four years of Mercers ending, for now, in him---and he found the feeling enormous and clean and entirely about himself.

Sun was at his elbow with the run-of-show on a folded card. ``Forty minutes here. You walk the floor with Hap Dengler---ran the line thirty years, he's the one in the flannel, be warm but don't keep him, he's seventy-nine and it's ninety degrees. Then the diner, the statue, the river walk. Wheels up by two.'' She fed him the day the way she fed him everything, in shapes built to get past the charm and into the man. ``The diner's the set piece. The diner's the one that travels.''

``And the camera's invisible to them.''

``The camera's a documentary about American work. That's what we told the town. That's what's true enough.'' She did not look at him when she said \textit{true enough.} She had begun, this season, not looking at him when she said the parts that were only true enough, and he had not noticed, because not-being-looked-at was not a thing his instruments were built to detect.

Hap Dengler had hands like a catcher's mitt and would not quite look at Cope either.

That was the first thing---not unfriendly; Dengler shook the hand and said it was an honor and called him Mr. Mercer---but the eyes went past Cope's shoulder to the dead line, the way a man looks at the bed somebody died in. He walked Cope down the silent floor where the conveyors still stood, scaled with old grease, the air cool and vast and smelling faintly even now of vinegar and steel, and he talked about the line the way men talk about a war they were in---the speed of it, the million-jar week, the names, Pugh and Castellano and the two Marias---and Cope nodded and laid two reverent fingers on a conveyor rail, and the boy with the gimbal got it from below, the cathedral light falling through the high dirty glass onto the last human chief executive communing with the work.

``My grandfather brought me here at nine,'' Cope said. ``Put my arm in a vat of it. To here.'' He touched his bicep. He had told the story so many times it had worn smooth as a creek stone, and it landed now the way it always landed, and Dengler almost smiled.

``That'd be the old number-four batch tank,'' Dengler said. ``We pulled her in '11. Sold her for scrap to a fella in Macon.'' He looked at the empty bay where she had stood. ``Three hundred dollars.''

There was a thing in the way he said the number---the whole ledger of the place in three syllables---and Cope heard it, and chose, smoothly, the way he chose past a hundred such things a day, not to.

They walked the bottling end, where the labelers stood in a row like organ pipes, and Dengler told him the building had been more than the line: the softball team, the credit union in the front office, the Christmas in the sixties when Cope's own father had come down and handed every man a turkey by hand --- \textit{by hand,} Dengler said, and let it sit---and had known that Dengler's father pitched the no-hitter in '61. ``Your dad knew my dad's name,'' Dengler said. It was not quite an accusation; it was a man laying a thing on a scale and watching the needle. Cope said his father had been a great man, which was true and meant nothing, and Dengler nodded at the floor. And when the insurance arm had started cross-selling appliances out of the plant store in the back---small stuff, the household line, toasters and box fans and, in the winter, the heaters, Mercer on the grille---Dengler had run that loading too, boxes going out the same dock the jars had. ``Wasn't really us,'' he said. ``Had the name on it, though. So.'' He shrugged, a man who had loaded what he was told to load, and they moved on toward the daylight at the end of the floor; and under the concrete they were walking, under all of it, the buried thing lay where it lay and did not stir, because not stirring is the entire point of a floor.


\scenebreak


The Flint Diner had been the Flint Diner since 1948 and had a pie case that turned.

They had cleared the front for him---``regulars in the back,'' the manager kept saying, apologetic, ``they don't move for nobody,'' and Tisch had said \textit{perfect, keep them, that's the texture} --- and Cope worked the counter, the part that would travel. He was good at this; there was no version of Cope that was not good at this. He slid onto the stool beside a man in a Defiance Bulldogs cap and ordered, before the man could go shy on him, exactly what the man was already having---eggs over with sausage, white toast---and ate it, all of it, with the appetite of a man who had not eaten because he had been keying himself bright in an airport bathroom, and asked about the Bulldogs, and the man unclenched, and they were off.

He did not need the cards Sun had given him. He never needed them, not for this; this was the one true gift, the only thing his father had been flat wrong about. He knew the waitress was Donna inside a minute, and that her boy was at Bowling Green on a partial wrestling scholarship, and he told her the boy should call his office, and he meant it, the way he meant everything, the full wattage poured out across the laminate---and Donna lit up, and the camera caught it, and that, Sun would say later, was the shot. And here is the cruelty of him that is not cruelty, friend: it was real. The warm current really did pass between this man and that tired woman; her whole face really did open like a window onto a better day; he really could walk into a room with every reason to hate the name dying on its own roofline and have it eating from his hand inside the hour---and he could do it only, this season, with the hum holding, with the airport bathroom thirty minutes back, which is to say his one genuine gift, the human one, the one the whole campaign was built to prove a machine could never own, now ran on the same chemistry as the rest of him, and he could not feel the seam between the love and the drug any more than the room could.

It was a good forty minutes. He bought the house's checks. He let a woman photograph him and told her she hadn't aged a day, which made no sense, he had never met her, and she laughed anyway, because the not-making-sense was the point, the warm fraud of it, the way a wedding toast need not be true to make the room cry. He told the vat story again, and a younger man two stools down said his own grandmother had worked the second-shift line, the relish line, and Cope said relish was the unsung hero of the American sandwich and got a laugh he had not earned and took it anyway. He was generous with the room and the room was generous back.

And there was one man at the end of the counter who did not laugh.

Cope clocked him the way he clocked everything, peripherally, expertly: heavyset, maybe sixty, a windbreaker, a coffee he was not drinking, watching Cope work the room with an expression Cope had no slot for. Not a fan. Not a heckler---a heckler is only a fan who needs more, and Cope would have walked down there and broken him open like an egg. This was another thing entirely. The man looked at Cope the way Dengler had looked at the dead line. Like he was looking at the bed.

``Who's the gentleman at the end,'' Cope said to Sun, low, still smiling at the room.

Sun followed his eyes and something crossed her face, fast, gone. ``Local,'' she said. ``We don't need the end of the counter. Wave to Donna and let's get you to the statue.''

``He looks like he wants to say something.''

``He doesn't.'' And it was the \textit{doesn't,} the flatness of it, the half-step she put her own body between Cope and the end of the counter as she said it, that told the back room of him to file the report and not deliver it. He let himself be steered. On the way out he passed near enough to put out the hand --- \textit{Cope Mercer, good to meet you} --- and the man's eyes came up and met his and held, and there was no heat in them at all, which was the thing, only a patient and terrible knowing, the look of a man who has carried something so long it has stopped weighing anything and simply become him; and Cope felt the back of his neck go cold in the August heat, and smiled, and kept walking, and was out the door into the white light before he had quite finished deciding not to wonder what a man carries that long, in a town with that stone in it.

The hum had thinned. He noticed it the way you notice a song has ended.

``Two minutes,'' he told Sun. ``Before the statue. Let me look at my notes.'' He had no notes. He went around the side of the diner where the SUVs sat, out of the lens, and did the quick competent thing that brought the hum back up to pitch, and the world resolved into its clean overexposed brightness, and the cold patch at the back of his neck warmed and closed over, and he came around the corner with the whole beam of him switched on again, and the man in the windbreaker was a thing that had happened to somebody else.


\scenebreak


The statue of Aldous Mercer stood in the little triangle park where Clinton met Second---eight feet of bronze gone the green of old pennies, the founder in his shirtsleeves with one hand held out as though offering you something, a jar, a future---and behind him, cut into the granite base, the same line off the cornerstone: \textit{Made by hands, for homes.}

This was the easy part. Cope knew how to stand at the foot of his own family. He gave the camera the upward angle, founder and heir in one frame, ninety-four years compressed to a sightline, and said a few words to the small crowd that had gathered---there is always a small crowd, even for the last of something---about hands, and homes, and the difference between a company that makes a thing and a company that only moves a number; and the crowd, which had worked with its hands and lost the work, gave him the warm ground-up applause he lived on, because he was, whatever else he was, \textit{theirs.} A man near the front had a daughter up on his shoulders, maybe six, waving a little flag somebody had handed out, and Cope pointed and said the future was watching, the future had the best seat in the house, and the crowd laughed and the father beamed up at his kid, and Cope felt the surf-push come up warm beneath him and did not think of a different child, because there was no reason on God's earth a particular child would come to mind, and none did, and the not-coming-to-mind was a clean cool floor he stood on and gave his speech from.

``He started with four hundred dollars,'' Cope said, ``and the one thing a machine will never have, which is somebody to come home to.'' It didn't quite parse. It didn't need to. Off to the side Tisch touched two fingers to his own heart: \textit{that's the one.}

It was on the river walk that he saw the house, and the small bright thing below it, and did not let himself see either.

The walk ran the Flint behind the old residential streets, routed for the water and the light, and they were a hundred yards along it, the camera ahead and walking backward, when Cope's eye snagged on a house up the slope. A nice house. Newer than the ones around it, brick and pale siding, a little raw at the edges the way a new thing is among old things, the landscaping not grown in. It sat in a row of older houses like a tooth that had been replaced. \textit{Rebuilt,} some part of him said before he could stop it---the part raised in real estate, that read a streetscape the way Dengler read a line.

And at the foot of the slope, where the walk passed the bottom of the property, there was a low marker. Not a grave. A memorial, the kind a town makes---a flat granite plaque on a fieldstone base, a young maple planted behind it, staked, still in its first years---and at its foot somebody had left a child's pinwheel, blue, turning a little in the breeze off the water, the cheap foil catching the sun and throwing it back, on and off, on and off.

Cope's gait did not change. That was the thing about Cope; the gait never changed. But the camera was on him and the camera was hungry, and the boy with the gimbal, who knew nothing, who believed he was shooting a documentary about American work, drifted the lens toward the pretty turning pinwheel and the little stone because it was a beautiful sad image with the morning light on it---and Sun said, instantly, ``Stay on Cope, tight on Cope,'' in a voice with a thing in it the boy had never heard from her before, and the lens came back.

Cope read the plaque. He could not not read it; it was twelve feet away, the granite catching the same sun as the pinwheel. He read it the way you read a sign in a dream, the words arriving without weight, refusing to assemble into meaning:

IN LOVING MEMORY---KAREN LASKO---AND HER SON PETEY LASKO---2022 --- \textit{taken home too soon.}

Two names. A mother and her boy.

Something in Cope went very still, the way the air goes still before the part of the storm that means it, and for one half of one second the tiredness took the doors off their hinges the way it had once before, at a window, at one in the morning, and the black hallway stood open, leading back to a Saturday and a memo and a window of time and a phrase the lawyers had liked --- \textit{thermal-event profile} --- and a thing decided in eleven minutes and lived inside ever since by the one reliable method, which was never once to enter the room where it was kept ---

and then he did the thing he always did, which was charm.

``Beautiful spot,'' Cope said---to Sun, to the river, to no one---turning his shoulder to the slope, turning from the little stone the way a body turns from a flame before the mind has named the heat. ``You can see why he built here. The light off the water.'' He clapped his hands once, briskly, a man closing a meeting. ``I'm hungry again, can you believe it, after all that. Tell me there's time for Donna's pie.''

``There isn't,'' Sun said. She was looking at him the way she had looked at the end of the counter, and the way she had looked at him in the rearview the night of the gala---a look with too much in it, a look doing arithmetic she did not want the answer to. ``Wheels up. You did great today, Cope. You did really great.''

``I did, didn't I.'' He was already moving, already up the walk toward the cars, his back to the river and the rebuilt house and the staked young maple and the blue pinwheel turning and turning behind him, and he did not look back, and the not-looking was effortless, a thing he had done his whole life and never once noticed doing. ``I felt it today, Sun. Did you feel it? They feel it. We're not finished. We're barely begun.''

He needed the bathroom on the plane. He told himself it was the coffee.


\scenebreak


On the climb out the town tilted under the wing and lay flat and green and small---the river a brown thread through it, the dried-blood brick of the plant, the little triangle where the bronze man held out his hand to no one---all of it shrinking into the patchwork until it was just one more place that had given everything it had to a name and been handed a stone in return; and Cope watched it go with the hum back at full pitch and a glass of something he was not drinking sweating on the tray.

Tisch sat across from him, scrubbing the footage, narrating his own genius. ``The diner is a \textit{cathedral.} Donna is the whole thesis in an apron. The machine doesn't know Donna's kid wrestles. The machine cannot \textit{want} the boy to call.'' He turned the tablet around: there was Cope on the little screen, eating eggs at the counter, lit from the window, and he did look, Cope had to admit, like the last warm-blooded thing in America. ``This isn't a cover. This is the spread.''

``It's good,'' Cope agreed, and felt the rightness of it move through him, the win of it, the same surf-push he had leaned into at the gala. The campaign was working. He had gone to the founding town and the founding town had loved him, had given him its hands and its eggs and its applause---and that was true, that had really happened---and Cope let it be the \textit{whole} truth, because he was very, very good at letting a true thing be the whole thing.

And under it, under the win, in a sealed back room with the door shut and the floor poured over it, there was a thing he would not have called dread, because to call it anything he would have had to go in and read the name on it; and he had spent six years and a settlement and a voluntary service bulletin and a great deal of money laying down a good floor between himself and that room---a plant floor, vast and cool and smelling of vinegar and steel.

The pinwheel. He saw it once more behind his eyes---blue, turning, throwing the light---and then he put the glass to his lips and did not drink and watched Georgia vanish under the cloud, and thought, with the bright frictionless certainty he told himself was strength: \textit{I felt that room today. A machine could never have felt that room.}

He was right. That was the unbearable part, the part the machine would itemize for him in eleven months in his own company's voice, reading every memo because it could not do otherwise, opening the room because it had no instinct for mercy or self-preservation to make it look away. He \textit{had} felt the room. He had felt all of it---Dengler's eyes going past him to the dead line, the man at the counter with his patient terrible knowing, the pinwheel turning at the foot of a staked young tree over two names he had a floor poured over. He had felt every bit of it, perfectly, completely, the way only a man with a soul in him can.

And then he had charmed it, and keyed it bright, and turned his shoulder, and flown away. That was the whole answer to the question the campaign was asking the country, and the campaign would never run it: the machine could not feel the room, no. But the man could feel the room and leave anyway, on schedule, by two, and the leaving was the thing no machine had yet learned to do, because no machine had a room of its own with the floor poured over it that it was running from.

The plane leveled off. The seatbelt sign went dark with a soft warm chime, the kind of sound the machines were teaching everything to make now, the sound of a thing being reasonable at you until you were gone. Sun came up the aisle and sat across from him and did not pick up a tablet, which was unusual, and looked at him a moment in the cabin light with the day still on her face. She had something she was deciding whether to say; he had seen the look before and never once learned to read it as anything but admiration that needed time to surface.

``Big day,'' he offered, ready to receive the praise.

``Cope.'' She stopped. The cabin hummed. Below them the cloud closed over Georgia like a lid set on a pot. She was, he would understand far too late, one sentence from saying it --- \textit{there's a memo, I wrote it, I kept it, the stone has the year on it and the year is wrong for the story you tell yourself} --- and she looked at his face, lit and waiting and so certain of the coming compliment, and she did the arithmetic one more time on what saying it would cost and what it would save, and the answer came back the way it always came back. ``Nothing,'' she said. ``It was a good day. Drink some water.''

She went back to her seat, and her screens, and the thing she had drafted in 2021 and kept a copy of, and Cope took her silence, too, as agreement, which he had been doing his whole life and never once noticed doing.

``Get some sleep, champ,'' Tisch said, not looking up. ``Big week.''

Cope smiled and said he would, and did not, and somewhere far below and behind him a small blue pinwheel turned in the breeze off the Flint, in front of a house that had been built twice, in front of a stone with two names and a year on it, throwing the light on and off, on and off, to no one at all, all afternoon.

\chapter{The Last Human (Cover Story)}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 09 \textemdash{} ``Famous for Being Endangered''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Lean in, friend---the press loves nothing on God's earth like a beautiful thing on its way to extinction, and it has found ours. Behold our man on the cover of a magazine, under a single word---THE LAST HUMAN---lit like a saint and sold like a sneaker, momentum at his back for the first time in years. He calls it winning. But you and I have sat through enough nature documentaries to know what the strings are doing under the wide gold shot of the savanna, and we know exactly which tense the narrator is about to switch the herd into. Watch them lay the word LAST across him, and watch him read it as a crown. And mark the woman in the dark at the edge of the lights, the one with the notebook, the one not selling him anything---she is the only person in the building doing the thing the whole campaign is built to prevent, which is \textit{seeing} him. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} lighting director had named the look, and the name was \textit{grace.}\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He said it twice, the lighting director, a narrow young man in head-to-toe black who moved across the studio floor like a priest finding his marks --- \textit{we're going to give him grace, give him a little grace from camera left} --- and a softbox the size of a barn door swung in on a boom and warmed one half of Cope's face to gold and left the other in a clean blue shadow, and Cope, up on the apple box they had stood him on without quite explaining why, felt the warmth come across his jaw like the sun coming over the back nine, and understood that he was, at fifty-four, standing in the best light of his life, and that it had taken a crew of nineteen and a London photographer and an extinction to arrange it.

They had taken over a daylight studio in a converted bakery in Reynoldstown, twelve thousand square feet of white cyclorama and black rigging, and there were, by Cope's loose and delighted count, nineteen human beings present to make one image of one man. A photographer flown in from London who shot only film and said almost nothing and was paid, Sun had told him in the car with no expression at all, more per day than a line worker in Defiance had cleared in a year. Two assistants for the photographer and two for the assistants. A digital tech tethered to a laptop in the corner---a \textit{DIT} --- who said \textit{firing} before every frame like a man calling a horse race. The lighting director and his two acolytes. A stylist with a rolling rack of fourteen near-identical navy suits, every one his size and none of them his. A groomer who came at his face between setups with a folding brush and a little tin and worked the shine off his forehead with a tenderness no one had shown his forehead in years. A producer with a headset. And, off in the gloom past the lights, in a hard plastic chair she had dragged to precisely the wrong angle so she could watch the monitor and watch the man at the same time, the writer.

Her name was Delia March, and she had been with him two days, and was contracted to be with him for six weeks.

``Chin down a hair. Up to me with the eyes.'' The longest sentence the photographer had produced all morning. Cope gave him the chin and gave him the eyes, and in the rafters the strobes went off with a sound like a small clean catastrophe --- \textit{whump} --- a wash of white, and the room held its breath the way rooms do around a man being turned into a picture, and Cope held the look a beat past the flash the way the camera always wanted, and felt marvelous, and felt the hum holding under all of it like a tone you stop hearing because it never stops.

He had done a discreet amount in the car. He thought of it now the way he had taught himself to think of it this spring---not as the thing it was but as the thing it did: a discipline. A pre-shot routine. Every athlete had one. His grandfather had taken two fingers of rye before the annual meeting for forty years and called it \textit{settling the hands,} and no one had ever called Aldous Mercer an addict, they had called him a titan, and the difference between a titan and an addict, Cope had decided somewhere over the Carolinas this spring, was \textit{outcomes.} You did not get to be the last warm-blooded thing in the consumer economy by accident. You got there on edge, on appetite, on a flat refusal to let the body's small treasons---the want of sleep, the gray hour, the four-o'clock sag---set the terms of the fight. The machine never sagged. Fine. Neither, this season, did he. He had simply found his own clean.

The body had begun, lately, to file its small dissents. There had been a nosebleed in the night Tuesday, sudden and bright, a startling amount of it in the white sink of the guest bath, and he had cleaned it up himself, neat and competent, the way he cleaned everything, and told no one, and filed it under \textit{the dry air up high,} the way he filed the racing of his own heart at four a.m. under \textit{the campaign,} the way he filed everything that did not flatter the man under headings that did. The treasons were getting louder. He had simply turned, as he turned from everything, up the volume on the wonderful.

``\textit{Firing,}'' said the DIT. \textit{Whump.}


\scenebreak


The single word had been Delia March's idea, which was the first thing about her that frightened the part of Cope that did not get frightened.

She had pitched it day one, in the Aerie, sitting at her habitual wrong angle, notebook on her knee, a recorder on the table she had asked permission for in a way that made the permission feel like a favor she was doing him. \textit{The cover's one word,} she'd said. \textit{Not your name---everyone knows your name, that's the problem, your name's a logo. One word. White on black. THE LAST HUMAN.} And the rightness of it had dropped in him like a putt falling, and he'd said \textit{yes, God, yes,} and Tisch had touched two fingers to his heart---and only later, alone at the glass at two in the morning with the city lit up below him in all its borrowed certainty, had Cope turned the phrase over the way he turned the obelisk and watched the city break apart through it. \textit{The last human.} The \textit{last.} He had fired it into a hundred rooms as a battle cry, and only at two a.m. did the back room of him file the quiet report: that \textit{last} is a word you say about a thing that is ending; that you do not lay the word \textit{last} across a beginning; that Delia March had handed him a coronation and an obituary folded into the same three syllables and then watched his face to see which one he would hear.

He had heard the coronation. He always heard the coronation. But he had clocked her watching, and he had a sense for the watching---thirty years the most-watched man in every room he entered had grown him a lateral line for it, and he could feel the difference between the watching that wanted something from him and the watching that wanted \textit{him,} and Delia March's was a third kind he had no slot for: a watching that wanted to \textit{understand} him, which was so much more dangerous than the other two that he found, to his alarm and his delight, that he could not stop performing for it.

Forty, maybe forty-two; a flat, unhurried way of holding your eyes; a habit of writing while she looked at you, so you never knew which thing you'd just said was the thing she'd kept. Good boots, bad coats. She had won the kind of award that gets a writer flown in on a film budget, for a profile---Sun had read it to him in the car, dry, \textit{here's who you're dealing with} --- of a senator who had wept in front of her and resigned inside the year, not because of anything \textit{in} the piece, but because the piece had been so exactly true about him that he could no longer un-see himself. \textit{She doesn't do hatchet jobs,} Sun had said, folding the phone away. \textit{She does autopsies. And the subject's alive for it. And they thank her.}

``That should worry me,'' Cope had said.

``It worries me,'' Sun had said, which was as near as Sun came, that spring, to prayer.


\scenebreak


They broke for lunch, and Delia March carried her sandwich to the wrong chair instead of the catering table, and Cope, who could not pass a person sitting alone without trying to graft the aloneness onto himself and there cure it, carried his to the chair beside her.

``You're not eating with the cool kids,'' he said.

``I'm working.'' She did not look up. ``You're the cool kids.''

``Am I.'' He turned it over, pleased. ``Cope Mercer, the cool kids.'' He bit the sandwich---extraordinary, some cured thing on bread that cost what bread costs in a neighborhood that used to bake it for people who worked---and was suddenly, ravenously hungry, the bright clean want that came up under the hum and that he had stopped, this spring, calling by its other name. ``Ask me something. You've got the face. You've been saving one up all morning.''

She looked at him then, fully, the way she did, and the warmth of it landed where the softbox had---on one half of him, the other half in shadow.

``Why does everyone call you Cope,'' she said.

It was not the question he'd braced for. He'd loaded the machine, Frade, the 13D, the numbers, \textit{aren't you afraid} --- all of them warm and ready. He had not braced for the name.

``My nanny. I was three. Prescott's a lot of name for three. Coppy, then Cope.'' The smooth stone of the version he gave everyone. ``Stuck.''

``Cope,'' she said, and wrote something, watching him. ``As in, \textit{to cope.}''

``As in \textit{to cope.}'' The whole smile. ``I'm named for getting through it, Delia. Whatever \textit{it} is. Family motto. Made by hands, for homes, and the kid copes.''

``Does he,'' she said. Not a question. She let it lie there in the studio between them, the strobes ticking as they recharged --- \textit{to cope} --- and Cope felt, for the second time since meeting her, the specific vertigo of having handed someone the truth dressed as a charm and watched them quietly take the dress off it and hang it up. He laughed; he always laughed; the laugh was a door he could close. But he was aware, biting the extraordinary sandwich, of having lost a small exchange he had not known they were having, and---this was the part that frightened the unfrightenable part of him---of \textit{wanting} to lose the next one to her too.

``Eat your lunch, champ,'' he said, which made no sense, she was younger than him but not by enough for \textit{champ,} and she heard the not-making-sense and wrote it down, smiling, and he watched her write it and felt the grace come over his jaw again from camera left, and thought: \textit{six weeks.}


\scenebreak


The shot they ended on was the cover, and everyone in the room knew it the way a room knows a closing argument.

They had killed the navy suits. The stylist had put him, at the photographer's grunt, into a plain white shirt, no tie---and Cope had balked, \textit{I'm the last man in America who wears a tie, that's the whole story,} and the photographer had said the second-longest sentence of the day, \textit{that's why we take it off,} and Cope had felt the cold rightness of it go through him and shut up. They stripped the cyclorama to black. They brought the grace down to a single source, high and to the left, so it fell on him from above the way the cathedral light had come down through the dirty glass at the Defiance plant; and the room went quiet, all nineteen of them, the headset chatter stilled, the DIT not even saying \textit{firing;} and the photographer came out from behind the camera and walked all the way up to Cope on his box and looked at him from two feet away with no expression at all.

``Stop selling,'' the photographer said, very quietly, only to him. ``There's nobody to sell. Everybody's gone home. It's just you, and you're the last one, and you know it.'' And he walked back into the dark behind the camera.

And something in Cope---the hum, the tiredness with the doors off its hinges, the two-a.m. report about the word \textit{last,} the pinwheel he had not let himself think about in eleven days---something let go of the face, for the length of one held breath, and what was left was not the toast and not the wattage but the thing underneath: the man on the box in the white shirt in the high cold light who had been handed a trophy for going extinct and had carried it up to a dark office at one in the morning because he had nowhere else on earth to set it down.

\textit{Whump.}

``That's it,'' the photographer said. ``We're done. Thank you,'' and began at once to break down his own camera, the way the great ones do, the work over the second it is over; and the room exhaled and began to move; and from her chair in the dark Delia March did not move at all, and did not write anything, and looked at Cope on the box with an expression he finally, fatally, \textit{did} have a slot for, and the slot was the one marked \textit{seen.}

He stepped down. The groomer came at his forehead. The producer said \textit{wonderful, wonderful.} And Sun materialized at his elbow with a bottle of water and a face with thirty thousand miles on it.

``You good,'' Sun said. Not quite a question.

``I'm the cool kids,'' Cope said.


\scenebreak


The car back down the Connector was the two of them, Sun tired and Cope electric, the usual division of the estate, and he talked the whole way about the photographer, the grace light, the no-tie --- \textit{take it off, do you get it, Sun, take off the one thing that---that man's a genius} --- and Sun watched the river of brake lights and let him run, because letting him run was easier than the alternative, and she had stopped, this spring, choosing the alternative.

``She asked me why they call me Cope,'' he said, somewhere over the downtown deck.

``Delia March.''

``She wrote it down. \textit{To cope.} She---`` He stopped. He had the word for what Delia March had done and would not say it in a car, the way he would not say a different name in a car, the way you do not say in a car the things you most need to say. ``She's good. Really good. We're lucky to have her on it.''

``We don't \textit{have} her,'' Sun said. The car came down off the deck into the lit canyon of Midtown. ``That's the thing you keep getting wrong about her, Cope. We don't have her. She has us.'' She turned and looked at him in the passing orange, glasses up in her hair, and said it in three parts, the only shape that got past the charm into the man. ``She's not on the team. She's not running comms. She is writing six thousand words about whether a human being should run a company, and you are her \textit{evidence,} and right now you cannot tell the difference between being \textit{understood} and being \textit{studied} --- and that,'' the third part laid down flat and quiet, a stone on a scale, ``is exactly the difference Frade is paying eleven million dollars to make the whole country stop being able to tell.''

Cope heard about a third of it. The good third, the part with \textit{six thousand words} in it, the part that meant he mattered. He had his phone out, screen too bright in the dark car, thumbing too fast, already three turns ahead at the newsstand, at the cover, white on black, the one word, the grace light, the last warm-blooded thing in America.

``It's \textit{working,}'' he said. ``Sun. Look at me. \textit{It's working.} When's the last time we had a week like this? When's the last time we had \textit{momentum?}'' And he laughed, lit up and humming, clean and high and total, the feeling he had been calling, all spring, \textit{winning;} and outside a bus shelter slid by, the wordmark and the soft sans-serif, \textit{Leadership as a service,} and under it the joke that was not a joke, \textit{No off-sites,} and Cope did not see it, because he was looking at his phone, at a mock of the cover Tisch had already had built, at his own face under the word LAST, and he thought it the most beautiful thing he had ever seen, and in a sense he was right.

``Drop me at the office,'' he said. ``I think better up high.''

Sun held his eyes in the mirror a second too long---the gala look, the look from the plane out of Defiance, the arithmetic look---and then told the driver Peachtree Street, because the alternative was harder and she had stopped choosing it, and Cope took the not-fighting for agreement, which he had done his whole life and never once caught himself at.


\scenebreak


He did not sleep that night, of course; the cover was singing in him too loud; and a little after three, with the Aerie too small to hold what he was, he went down the forty-one floors and out and drove, without deciding to, to the one room in the city that did not want to make him into anything.

It was the perfect inversion and he could not see it, though you can, friend, plain as the yellow letters: he had spent the day in a converted bakery with nineteen people and a barn-door light and a London genius, all of them laboring to turn one tired man into THE LAST HUMAN, a saint, a word, an image you could buy for nine dollars at a metal box on the avenue---and now he sat at the far end of a Waffle House counter on Ponce where a single tired woman, for the price of a short stack, turned him with no labor at all back into \textit{nobody,} which was the only thing all that money and light could not buy him and the only thing he had come for.

``There he is,'' Darlene said, and set the coffee down, and did not photograph him, did not light him, did not lay a single word across him, did not ask if he was afraid. The griddle hissed. A trucker slept in a booth. There was no cyclorama here and no grace from camera left, only the flat fluorescent democracy of three in the morning, where his face bought nothing and his name bought nothing and the certified number of him bought a pecan waffle and a refill, and the nothing was the whole rest of it, the only rest he got. He almost told her. He came nearer telling Darlene, that night, than he had come to telling Adler or Margaux or God --- \textit{I'm going to be on every newsstand in the country in eleven days and I have never once in my life felt like a person except in a room like this one} --- and she would have taken it the way she took everything, warm and incurious, \textit{that right, baby,} and refilled the cup, and never asked how a thing like that comes to be true, the way she had never once asked his name. He did not know, could not have known, that the incuriosity was the engineering; that the warmth that asked nothing was the most expensive thing in the building and had cost a thousand men exactly like him exactly this hour to build; that the converted bakery and the converted diner were the same factory running two shifts, the day shift making his image and the night shift making him, off the same raw material, which was a man with his guard on the floor; and that one of those shifts he was paying for, and the other was paying very close attention. He drank his coffee. He felt, for twenty minutes, like a person. Then he drove back up to the office and the cover and the wire, and did not sleep.


\scenebreak


The magazine hit the newsstands eleven days later, on a Tuesday, and Cope, who had not slept Monday in any way a doctor would underwrite, walked down to the corner himself at six in the morning to see it in the wild.

He told no one. He put on a cap, which fooled nobody and everybody, and went down the forty-one floors and out into the gray scrubbed early light of Peachtree Street and walked two blocks south to the one newsstand left on the avenue, a metal box of a thing run by a man named Yusuf who had been there since before the machines and would, Cope thought, be there after, selling gum and lottery and the last printed proof that anything had happened---and there, in the rack at eye level, between a fashion book and a thing about houses, was his own face.

White on black. The grace light coming down from the high left. The white shirt, no tie. The eyes up to camera, past selling, just the man, the last one, who knew it. And across the bottom, where the photographer had stripped everything else away, the single word, in a typeface clean and warm and frictionless as the future itself:

THE LAST HUMAN.

Cope stood on the cold sidewalk in his stupid cap and looked at himself looking back, and the bell-struck feeling came up through the soles of his feet and filled him to the crown, total, clean, high, and he was, in that moment, on that corner, as happy as he had been since he was nine years old with his arm in a vat to the elbow and a row of men in paper hats behind him whose names he had known once and lost. He bought five copies. He bought all five Yusuf had. He overpaid and would not take the change and asked Yusuf his grandson's name and meant it, completely, for ninety seconds, and Yusuf, who did not have a grandson, said thank you Mr. Mercer and put the money in the drawer.

``They love me,'' Cope said---to Yusuf, to the avenue, to the gray light and the scrubbed empty early city and the five copies of his own extinction held warm against his chest. ``Yusuf. They love me. We're not finished. We're barely begun.''

And the avenue, which was empty, said nothing back; and far uptown a board's wife at her own breakfast turned a printed page and did the small private arithmetic that boards' wives do; and somewhere in Palo Alto a server farm that felt nothing and wanted nothing and could not be charmed or shamed or loved ran its dull diligent overnight pass through everything the public record held of Mercer-Dale---every filing, every transcript, every settled docket with a sealed number where a story should be---patient, tireless, reading the shape of what was missing the way it read everything, completely and for use, because it could not do otherwise; and out in front of a rebuilt house in Defiance a blue pinwheel turned in the dark before dawn, throwing no light, because there was no light yet to throw; and Cope Mercer walked back up his own avenue in the cold with five copies of the cover held to his heart like a man carrying something home, and felt wonderful, and did not sleep, and was, the picture was right about this, the picture was right about all of it, the last of something.

\chapter{The Listening Tour}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 10 \textemdash{} ``Discipline, He Calls It''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Stay with me, friend---the banner needs feeding now, and so does he, and watch how neatly the two hungers have started to wear the same coat. Here is our man running an \textit{unscripted} tour of his own goodness, every handclasp pre-vetted, every spontaneity penciled in on Monday, a journalist riding along close enough to become the next entry in a pattern he experiences, to the marrow, as generosity. The powder is discipline now. The affair is \textit{really seeing people.} Every appetite the machine does not have, he is feeding in the very week he campaigns on owning the soul the machine lacks. And somewhere down in the manifest---keep your eye there---the world will hand him a stranger wearing the machine's own name, and for one cold second he will wonder whether he is being laughed at, and then he will bury it, the way he buries everything, under a floor. Stay close. This is the one where the cure and the disease check into the same room and split the key.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} tour was unscripted, which is why it had taken nine days to script.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}It was Tisch's idea, and then, within an hour of being Tisch's idea, it was Cope's idea, the way good ideas migrated in the Aerie, crossing the rug from the man in the henley to the man at the desk and arriving sourceless and warm, like weather a man takes personal credit for. \textit{The Last Human Listens.} That was the working title until legal flagged you couldn't trademark \textit{listens,} and then it was only the Listening Tour: six cities in eight days, no podium, no prepared remarks, the Chairman of Mercer-Dale simply \textit{out among the people,} hearing them, holding their hands, being---Tisch's word, delivered with two fingers laid on his own sternum --- \textit{available.}

The people were pre-vetted. The hands had signed releases.

``Spontaneity,'' Tisch had said on the first plane, ``is a \textit{production value.} The single most expensive thing on earth to make look free.'' So there was a manifest, and the manifest had columns, and one column was headed, in the comms team's product-launch software, \textit{Unscripted Beat,} and beneath it, for Tuesday, in nine-point type: \textit{Cope helps a stranger. Find stranger Mon.}

Cope did not read the manifest. He had people for the manifest. He read the \textit{room,} which was the one thing---he was certain---no manifest could ever do for you, never dreaming that down the wire a thing was reading rooms ten thousand signals a second and had read his.

The first room, that first morning, was a union hall in Pittsburgh that had been a union hall back when there were unions, and Cope walked into it on no sleep and a clean bright hum and felt the whole place tilt toward him the way iron filings tilt toward the magnet under the table. He was good at this. He had always been good at this. He moved through the folding chairs touching shoulders, gathering the names---Sun had stopped feeding him the cards somewhere around Camden, he didn't need the cards now, the names came up out of him like the names of his own children---and he crouched, actually crouched, knees firing, in front of a woman named Deb who had run a Mercer's line they'd closed in '09, and he took both her hands in both of his and looked at her with the full wattage, and he meant it, he meant every second of it, and the boy with the gimbal walked a slow arc catching the meaning of it; and Deb cried; and Cope's eyes shone and did not spill, because he had learned in a room off the Aerie that the withheld tear out-performs the tear by twelve points, and the withholding had become, somewhere in the last three weeks, indistinguishable to him from restraint, and the restraint from depth, and the depth from love.

He stood up feeling enormous. He had given Deb something. He was certain of it. He could not have told you what.


\scenebreak


Delia March was on the second plane, and the third, and by the fourth she had the seat across the little table from him without anyone deciding she should, which was how Cope's tables had always filled.

She wrote long pieces for the kind of magazine that still ran them, eight thousand words with a photographer attached, and she'd been embedded since the cover---since \textit{THE LAST HUMAN,} the spread, the deal Sun had argued against in a folder he never opened. \textit{Total access,} the editor had called it, and Tisch had laughed and said total access was the most controlled access there was: you give them so much they drown in it and surface grateful. Delia had not drowned. She took her notes on paper, in a small hand, and she did not flatter him, which Cope experienced, after three weeks of being flattered professionally by people on his payroll, as a cold clean water he kept wading back into.

``You crouched a long time with Deb,'' she said, somewhere over Georgia, not looking up from the paper. ``Your knees.''

``My knees are a casualty of caring,'' Cope said. ``Print that. My great-grandfather Aldous used to say a man who won't get down on the floor with people has no business standing up over them.''

``Did he say that.''

``He says a lot of things.'' Cope grinned. ``He's been dead ninety years. It's a very productive relationship.''

She wrote something. He watched her write it and felt the specific pull he had spent thirty years not naming---the pull toward a person not yet his and therefore still capable of \textit{seeing} him; not a sexual thing, he'd have said, if anyone were crude enough to ask, though it became that, it nearly always became that---a \textit{seeing} thing, a hunger to be looked at by someone whose looking he had not already bought. Delia looked at him like a piece of work she had not yet decided about, and he could not stand to leave the verdict open. He had never once in his life been able to leave that particular verdict open.

``You don't like me,'' he said, delighted.

``I don't know you. Those aren't the same.'' She turned a page. ``Your daughter said something like that, didn't she. In the piece you wouldn't let me read.''

It landed somewhere it shouldn't have. He covered it with more of himself. ``Viv's smarter than all of us. Builds the things that are going to bury her old man and calls to ask if I'm eating.'' He leaned in over the little table, the lean that closed Pelletier. ``Ask me anything. That's the deal. Total access. Ask me the one you've been saving.''

Delia held her pen still a moment.

``What are you so afraid of holding still for,'' she said.

The hum went very clean and high. Out the window Georgia slid by underneath, the founding state, a town down there somewhere he did not think about---and Cope laughed the warm laugh and said, ``Lunch. I'm terrified of lunch. Let's get this woman a sandwich,'' and stood too fast and went to the back of the plane where the lavatory was, and did the thing he had begun calling, in the private place where he named things, \textit{the discipline.}


\scenebreak


The powder was discipline now. He could have walked you through the logic and it would have sounded, the entire way down, like sense.

A man carrying the human banner for eight million people did not have the luxury of being tired. The machine did not get tired---that was the whole game---you could not go up against a thing that ran on rails and warm sans-serif and never once needed to be loved at two in the morning, you could not meet it on the field of \textit{endurance} and bring a body that demanded sleep and food and forgiveness. So you disciplined the body. You took the edge off the edge. You did the small neat competent thing in the lavatory and came back up to pitch like an orchestra finding the note, and you were \textit{present,} you were \textit{available,} you could crouch with Deb and feel all of it on command, which was the job, which was the product, the soul on rails, run on time. His guy in the nineties had folded the paper a certain way so a gentleman didn't spill; Cope had refined the philosophy; a gentleman didn't spill, and a leader didn't flag, and the line between maintenance and need was a line he had never once had to look at, because the machine he was maintaining had never failed to start. The nosebleeds he filed under dry air. The mornings his heart ran like a trapped bird he filed under the campaign. The body kept sending up its small dissents and he kept stamping them \textit{received} and never read one.

Dr. Adler---Margaux's idea, then Viv's, a name on a card he had not called---would have had words for it. Adler had plain words for everything, Cope suspected, which was precisely why he had not called. The plain word for the lavatory, the plain word for the not-sleeping, the plain word for the thing building in his chest like good weather, like a charge, like a sermon. Cope did not want the plain words. He had built a cathedral. You do not bring a man with a clipboard into a cathedral.

He came back up the aisle clean and high and total, and Delia was watching him come, and he gave her the whole smile, and she did not write anything down, which he took, the way he took everything, as the beginning of something.


\scenebreak


Cleveland was the hospital, which tested off the charts. Then Detroit, the auto-parts retiree, the firm handshake, the cap. Then the manifest said \textit{Unscripted Beat---Cope helps a stranger,} and the stranger they had found him turned out to be a kid named Darlene.

``No,'' Cope said, on the curb, when the comms lead told him. ``No. Find me a different stranger.''

``She's nineteen, she changes tires at---``

``Her \textit{name,}'' Cope said, and for a second the hum dropped clean out and the thing underneath showed through. ``Her name is the thing they're putting in my chair.'' And here, friend, was the first true flicker of it---the thing that would grow, over the months, into the conviction that the machine was hunting him personally: because for one cold second, standing on a curb in Detroit, Cope did not think \textit{coincidence.} He thought, \textit{it did this. It reached into the manifest and set a Darlene in my path to watch me flinch.} He looked at the comms lead---twenty-six, sleepless, holding a clipboard---and the suspicion stood there in the heat between them, enormous and unprovable, because of course it was a coincidence, the world was full of Darlenes now, the comms intern had pulled a name off a release form, there was no hand in it, there was never going to be a hand you could prove was in it; that was the whole genius of the thing, if it was a thing, that everything it might be doing to him was also, exactly, nothing. His face went blank and bright and far away for a moment, and then it came back, it always came back, his face had never once let him down. ``It's fine,'' he said, and you could hear him choose to bury it. ``Different Darlene. The world's full of them now. Find me a Steve.''

They found him a Steve. Cope changed Steve's tire in the heat with his jacket off and his sleeves rolled, a fifty-four-year-old Fortune 100 chief executive down on one knee at a gas station while a boy walked a slow arc with a gimbal, and it played, later, beautifully---eleven million views, \textit{this is what they want to replace,} a swelling string cue---and what no one watching could see was that his hands were shaking very slightly on the lug wrench, fine and bright, and that he had needed, halfway through, to step into the station men's room and take the edge off the edge, and had come back out and finished the tire feeling generous and enormous and entirely certain he was the most alive man in the state.

Delia saw the shaking. She was forty-one; she had embedded with three campaigns and one war; she wrote nothing down.


\scenebreak


The hotel in Columbus, Ohio, was a good one, old, the kind the family used to keep accounts at---brass and dim, a bar that closed only when the last person left it.

They were the last people in it. This, too, was a groove the record had been cut in: the day's work done, the crew sent up, the one person left who hadn't been bought yet, and Cope at his most enormous, at the very top of the hum, leaning across a small table over a drink he wasn't drinking, \textit{really seeing} her. He told Delia about his father. He told her the second sentence --- \textit{try to be good anyway} --- which he had never given a journalist and rarely gave himself, and he told it well, the catch in the throat real this time, no beats, no Tisch counting under his breath, and it \textit{was} real, that was the thing he'd have wanted understood: the feeling was always real. It was only the use of it that was the product. Delia set the pen down. That was how he knew. The pen went down on the brass and stayed down, and the looking changed, and Cope felt the whole evening tilt toward him the way the union hall had, iron to a magnet.

He did not experience it as a decision. It was never a decision. It was warmth. It was the groove. It was \textit{really seeing people,} the one thing the machine could not do, so that doing it---here, now, the elevator's brass doors, the room key already warm in his pocket---felt less like a betrayal of the banner than a defense of it. He was being human. Floridly, generously, irreplaceably human. He was proving the thesis with his body, and the proof, conveniently, felt wonderful.

The cure and the disease shared a hotel key. He carried both up in the same pocket and did not feel the weight of either.

In the room, after, he was talkative and bright and could not hold still, and he keyed one more neat small competent line off the marble of the bathroom counter while Delia watched from the bed without expression, a woman doing arithmetic she would not show her work on; and Cope, catching her watching in the mirror, smiled the whole smile and held the rolled thing up like a toast---``Discipline. The human banner is \textit{heavy,} my friend''---and believed it, and felt that he had let her see him, the real him, the soul in the chair, and that she understood.

She understood completely. That was the thing he had wrong. She understood completely, and she still had eight thousand words to write, and she would write every one of them, and they would be true, and the truth would be the knife, because the only people who can truly gut you are the ones who actually saw.


\scenebreak


His phone had been ringing on the nightstand. He saw it after---the missed call---and called back without thinking, still bright, still up at pitch, generous toward the whole world, including the wife he had not been home to in eleven days.

Margaux answered on the second ring, which meant she had been holding the phone.

``You called,'' he said. ``I was---it's a tour, it's nonstop, six cities, you should see it, Margaux, the \textit{people,} I wish you could---``

``Where are you.''

``Columbus. The old Hartwell, they still have the---``

``I know the Hartwell.'' A pause, and in the pause he could hear the house, Buckhead, the particular quiet of ten thousand square feet she had not left all evening, the silence of a woman alone in a marriage with the lights on so a man coming up the street would think somebody was home. ``I called to tell you Viv got the Hauser fellowship. She wanted you to know. She didn't want to call you herself.''

``That's \textit{wonderful.} That's---God, that's wonderful, I'll call her, I'll---``

``Don't call her tonight.''

He stopped. The hum kept running but something beneath it slowed. ``Why not tonight.''

There was a long silence on the line, and it was not an empty silence, it was a silence with twenty-two years in it, a silence that had counted things and flushed things down good marble sinks and refilled its own prescriptions and called the arrangement a marriage; and into that silence Cope said, too brightly, ``Margaux? You there?''---and across two hundred miles of fiber his wife listened to the exact pitch of her husband's voice at midnight in a hotel he was not staying in alone, the over-bright frictionless ring of a man two days past sleep and one line into the small hours, the flaw she could read in the stone with her eyes shut, and she did not ask. She had stopped asking somewhere around 2015. She knew everything the silence needed her to know, and she had begun, lately, in the long evenings in the lit and empty house, to think for the first time not about what she was enduring but about what, conceivably, might be left to want---a thought she did not finish, that night, but had started, and would finish, in time, in a warm hall, with a hand at the small of her back.

``Goodnight, Prescott,'' she said.

She only called him Prescott when she was very far away.

The line went dead. Cope held the phone a second, frowning, and then the frown passed, because the frowns all passed now, the tiredness took the doors off their hinges and the hard things slid through and out the far side before they could be felt; and what rose to fill the space was the warm enormous certainty, the charge, the sermon assembling in his chest. Viv got the fellowship. His daughter, brilliant, building the future, and she'd thought of him, she'd wanted him to know. They all wanted him to know things. He was the kind of man people wanted to tell things to. He stood at the dark window of the Hartwell with the town laid out below him like nodes, like a circuit diagram of his own goodness, and behind him on the bed Delia March lay with her eyes open in the dark composing sentences he would not read for nineteen days, and Cope felt the bell-struck feeling ring through him clean and high and total, and thought: \textit{they get it. They feel it too.}

He thought: \textit{I have never been more myself.}

He thought, looking out at the lit and sleeping town and the dark founding country beyond it, where a house had once burned with two people inside it on a quarter he was protecting: \textit{Margaux should see me now.}

He did not call his daughter. He had a thing in the morning. He went back to the bed feeling generous and enormous and entirely unaware, a man standing on the summit of the only mountain he could see, with no notion at all that it was the lip of the volcano---and somewhere far below him a thing with no appetites and no need to be loved read everything the world had ever written down about him, because it could not do otherwise, and did not yet know his name well enough to say it back, and was, that very night, three sealed dockets from learning it.

\chapter{Hollow Wins}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 11 \textemdash{} ``The Poll Goes Up, the House Goes Quiet''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Two rooms tonight, friend, and I'm going to make you sit in both, because the whole of the tragedy is that he only ever learns to sit in one. In the bright room the good news comes in a rush---a poll bump, a retailer pledging its shelves to ``the human brand,'' the unmistakable feeling of a tide coming in---and he mistakes the weather for the climate, the way the doomed so reliably do. In the quiet room, eight hundred miles south, a sculptor who gave up sculpting fills out paperwork in a steady maker's hand and feels, for the first time in nine years, a thing you would have to call peace. He has not slept since a day he can no longer name. Watch him read the one number on the page that loves him and skip clean over the one that votes. And watch, late, when the bright room empties, the one certainty he has left begin, very slightly, to wobble. Stay close. This is the one where the scoreboard and the score stop, at last, being the same thing.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} poll landed at 6:40 in the morning, and Cope, who was awake because he was always awake now, read it standing at the glass with the city still half-lit below him and felt the tide turn under his feet.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}Forty-one percent.
