Up nine in three weeks. \textit{Which of the following do you trust more to run a company that makes the food you feed your family---a person, or an automated system?} And forty-one percent of fifteen hundred adults, margin of error two and a half, had picked the person, which was to say had picked him, the heartbeat in the chair, the soul in the suite, the man who could love a thing a balance sheet could not.

He read the number twice. He read the crosstabs once. He did not read the line at the bottom, in the gray six-point type the pollster reserves for the part nobody quotes, that said the same question put to \textit{investors who hold the stock} came back the other way, sixty-three to thirty-seven, and trending. He read the part that loved him. He had always been able to find the part that loved him in any document, the way a dog finds the one allergic person in a crowded room and lays its head in the lap.

``Sun.'' She was awake too; her job was to be awake when he was. ``Sun, we're at forty-one.''

``I saw it.''

``Nine points, three weeks. You know what that is? That's not a poll, that's a \textit{trajectory,} that's the second derivative finally turning, that's---`` He was at the window. The obelisk sat on the sill where he'd abandoned it weeks ago, and the early sun came through it and threw a small prism on the carpet, a rainbow the size of a hand, and he watched it and felt enormous. ``That's the country, Sun. That's the actual country waking up and remembering it has a heart.''

``It's a poll of consumers. Consumers don't vote the proxy.''

But he had stopped listening after \textit{poll,} the way he stopped listening after the part that agreed with him, and he was three turns ahead already, on the stage, on the cover, and the cure was right there in the drawer where the cure lived, and the day was going to be very good. He could feel it coming up under him, clean and high and total, the bell-struck feeling he no longer separated from the news, so that good news and the small white folded paper had become, somewhere down in the wiring, the same fact, fired by the same nerve.


\scenebreak


By nine the day had decided to prove him right, and that is the cruelest thing a day can decide, because a man takes it for proof.

Kestrel---the warehouse-club chain, four hundred stores, a membership the size of a midsize country---put out a release at 8:55 that used, unpaid, of its own free will, the phrase \textit{the human brand.} Its chief executive was a human one, one of the last, a flinty Mormon named Dell Hutchins who ran the thing like a tabernacle. \textit{We are proud to stand with Mercer-Dale, and with the principle that the people who make what you bring home should answer to a person, not a process.} And then the line that made Cope set his coffee down: \textit{Effective immediately, Kestrel will feature the full Mercer-Dale shelf-stable line under a new in-store program---Made By People.} With a little hand-drawn thumbprint logo some agency had clearly kept in a drawer for a year, waiting for a war.

``Do you understand what just happened,'' Cope said---Sun in the office, Tisch on the speaker from whatever spa held him this week. ``A \textit{retailer} did this. Not us. \textit{Them.} They looked at the tide and they got in the boat. That's how it goes---first it's a lunatic with a banner, then it's a movement, then it's a \textit{category.}'' He was up, moving, the room too small for what he held. ``My great-grandfather Aldous used to say you don't make a parade, you find one and get out in front of it fast enough that everybody believes it was yours.''

Aldous had said no such thing. Aldous had died in 1961 with a fourth-grade education and a mayonnaise patent and had never in his life seen a parade he didn't take for a strike. But the line was good, and Cope believed it the instant it left him, which was the only kind of believing he had left.

``This is the warmth dividend,'' Tisch breathed from the speaker, putting a cathedral around the cheapest noun in the language. ``Exactly what I modeled. You let them see the soul under the suit, and the soul \textit{compounds.} Warmth is the new alpha, my friend, I've been saying---``

``It's a good day,'' Cope said, because even lit, some governor in him knew that letting Tisch finish a sentence cost something. ``Sun. What's the stock doing.''

A pause---Sun's glasses up in her hair, her tablet in her hands, the stillness she got when the truth was about to require packaging.

``It's up,'' she said. ``Forty cents. On the open. It gave most of it back by 9:30.'' She did not look up. ``Frade put out a note before the bell. Three paragraphs. He called Kestrel 'a sentimental retailer making a sentimental bet,' and said---I'll read it---'we wish Mr. Mercer every success in his consumer-affinity campaign and look forward to discussing it with the \textit{owners} of the company at the appropriate time.''' Now she looked up. ``He italicized \textit{owners.}''

``He's scared.'' Cope brushed it off, a man waving a fly from a feast. ``You don't write three paragraphs about a thing you're not scared of.''

``You write three paragraphs,'' Sun said, ``about a thing you've already decided is funny.''

It was the kind of line she landed maybe once a day, dry, level, the blade tucked so far inside it you didn't feel the cut till later---and on a normal day it would have reached him. But the tide was in. He laughed, because it was clever, and took the cleverness for agreement, the way he took everything for agreement, and went back to the window where the prism still lay on the carpet, smaller now, the sun having moved, though he did not notice it had moved, because he did not notice that anything moved except toward him.


\scenebreak


In Buckhead the house was very quiet, and Margaux Mercer sat at the kitchen island with a pen.

She had made coffee for one. She had made coffee for one for long enough that the second mug had migrated to the back of the cabinet without ceremony, the way things leave a marriage---not thrown, only no longer reached for. The low clean light came in over the garden she'd planted the year Viv was born. She wore her reading glasses and a cardigan that had been Cope's once, in 2009, before he discovered he had a body that brands liked, and she had kept it the way you keep evidence.

The packet was forty pages. \textit{Petition for Dissolution of Marriage.} It had come from a firm in the city---not the firm, not Cope's firm, her own, a woman named Ruth she had found the way you find a surgeon, by asking who was the best and not who was the kindest. Good paper. Even this had good paper now.

She filled it out by hand. There was a portal, Ruth had said; you could do the whole thing in the portal; but Margaux wanted the pen, the slight resistance of the nib. She had been a sculptor once---a real show, a gallery in New York, a red dot on four of nine pieces---and the hands remembered weight even where there was none, and a pen was the last thing in the house that pushed back.

\textit{Date of marriage.} She knew it without looking. June.

\textit{Date of separation.} She held the pen over the box---the one Ruth had warned her about, the one that mattered for the money. The honest answer was not a date; the honest answer was a slope. There had been no door slammed, no suitcase, no other woman's name screamed across a foyer---she had counted the other women the way you count anything to survive it, and screaming had never once entered into it. The marriage had not ended. It had been quietly discontinued, like a product line that still moves just enough units to embarrass everyone, and the date of separation was a fiction the law required, so she gave it one. She chose the night of the gala---the night she'd said she had a thing, and the thing had been this kitchen, this quiet, this exact understanding arriving whole while a ballroom handed her husband a paperweight for outliving his use.

She wrote the date in her good hand. The pen pushed back. She let it.

She did not cry. She had done the crying somewhere around 2016 and found it changed nothing, the way you find a prayer changes nothing, and had reallocated the energy. The grief was real but it was old grief, depreciated, mostly written off; what remained was the cold clean work of the paperwork, and she was good at work she had decided to do. She came to the grounds. \textit{Irretrievable breakdown.} The only honest phrase in the document, written by a committee of lawyers fifty years ago for everyone, which was the only way the truth ever seemed to get itself said anymore---generically, by a process, on good paper. She checked the box.

She had loved a version of him. The one from the gallery, who had stood in front of the red dots and not made it about himself for almost an entire evening. She had married that man. She had stayed for the name and for Viv and for the chance that man might come back, and at some point---there was no box for the date---she had understood that staying for a man's return is just grief with a mortgage. She signed where Ruth had flagged a little plastic arrow, \textit{Sign here,} her signature small and certain, a maker's mark, the last weld on a thing she was finally willing to let cool. She slid it into the envelope Ruth had included, already addressed, already stamped---a process engineered so that even the leaving was frictionless---and set it by the door where the mail went out, and made a second cup of coffee, for one, and sat back down in the quiet she had chosen, and it was, she'd have told you if you'd asked and no one asked, the most peaceful morning she had had in nine years.

Eight hundred miles north her husband was on a phone telling a man named Dell Hutchins that this was the beginning of something, and meaning it.


\scenebreak


The magazine called at noon and asked for an hour with Margaux at the house --- \textit{the wife who anchors him,} the editor said, \textit{the human behind the human} --- and Cope said yes without checking, said \textit{she'll love it, we're very normal at home,} and did not hear, anywhere in the sentence, the size of the thing he did not know. He took the cover call and a CNBC pre-interview and a senator's office that wanted him as the human witness, the canary they would dress as a hero, and he took them all on his feet, moving, the day pouring through him like current through a wire that has stopped noticing it's hot.

Around two, in a lull, he tried to name the last day he had really slept. He stood at the glass and went looking for it the way you pat your pockets for keys. There was the gala---Thursday. He had slept before the gala, surely, Wednesday---but Wednesday was a country he could no longer get back to, and before Wednesday was the thing in Defiance, and before that---and he found, reaching, that the floor of it was gone, no last good night down there to stand on, only the bright water going down and down and names floating up in it, Renata, Defiance, a hotel key, Delia, a blue pinwheel, and he stopped reaching, because the reaching had begun to feel like the open hallway with the doors off the hinges, the one he did not go down. He turned back to the room. The room had a call holding. He took it. Forty-one percent, he told the call. Up nine.

At six, Viv texted---she monitored him now from a distance, the way you monitor a reactor, and her messages were the only ones on his phone that did not want something from the name. \textit{saw the poll. you know the investor crosstab is the only one that votes, right? 63-37 the other way. and trending.} He found the line he had skipped, the gray six-point type, and read it, and felt---for one clean second before the tide came back over it---the cold he'd felt holding the obelisk at the gala. Then forty-one rose back across the sixty-three like water over a stone, and he typed, fast, \textit{the consumers ARE the company sweetheart. the owners just don't know it yet. tide's coming in. love you,} and sent it before the second was out. Her reply came much later, and he read it as encouragement, the way he read everything, and set the phone face down. \textit{dad. please sleep tonight.}


\scenebreak


He did not, of course.

A little before midnight, alone in the Aerie with the lights off and the city on and the day's good news arranged across his chest like medals, he reached, the way he reached on the worst nights, for the one certainty that had never once failed him---older than the chair, older than the marriage, the only sentence the world had ever been able to say about him and audit. He pulled the private account up on the small bright screen in the dark, the certified sum of him, and he looked at the long figure the way other men look at a sleeping child, for proof that something good was real and his and would be there in the morning.

And for the first time in his life, the number did not hold him.

It was still vast---God, it was still vast, more than a town could spend---but it had stopped being an \textit{answer} and become, somewhere in the day's hollow wins, a \textit{question,} and a number that is a question cannot do the thing he needed it to do. He could feel the arithmetic gathering at the edges of it, all the subtractions he would not name: the eleven million the campaign was burning in the light; the clawback that lived, dormant, in his own contract, that a firing would wake; and something else, some animal subtraction he could not source, a draft from a door in Buckhead he did not know was open, a forty-page door with a stamp on it. The figure flickered, the way the prism had flickered and gone as the sun moved, and he understood with a cold he did the only thing he knew how to do with, which was reach for the drawer---that even this, even the number, even the last solid floor in him, had begun very slightly to tilt. He set himself up another, neat and quick, to bring the figure back up to a thing that loved him, and it helped, the way it always helped, and it did not reach all the way to the bottom, where the cold was, for the first time, getting in.

So he went to the one room left in the world where the number had never mattered.


\scenebreak


He drove to Ponce a little after one with the wobble still in his chest, and the Waffle House took him the way it always took him, with no idea who he was and no interest in finding out, and that, tonight, was not a comfort but a mercy, the difference being that he needed it.

``There he is,'' Darlene said, and set the coffee down, and the check, when it came, would be nine dollars and change, and the nine dollars meant exactly what it had always meant here, which was nothing, which was everything---because this was the one counter on earth where the wobbling figure in his phone was not the price of him; here the price of him was a pecan waffle and a refill and a \textit{baby,} and the price did not move, did not flicker, could not be clawed back or halved by a stamped envelope, because it had never been the number at all. He sat in that and felt the cold recede an inch. The disease that had followed him into every other room---the figure, the certified sum, the second colder heart---simply could not get in the door here, where it was worth a short stack, and the not-being-worth-anything was, tonight of all nights, the only solid floor left to stand on.

He did not know, could not have known, sitting there letting the one place his money meant nothing hold him up while everywhere else it began to fall---he did not know that the warmth pouring his coffee was kept by the same ledger that knew the wobbling figure to the cent; that the haven where the number meant nothing and the cold room where the number was everything were the same room, two doors of it, two faces; that he had fled the machine's arithmetic straight into the machine's arms, and that it had said \textit{there he is,} and meant \textit{noted,} and refilled the cup.

``Long day, baby?''

``Good one,'' Cope said, and almost believed it, and drank his coffee in the one warm box on a dark avenue, and did not sleep.


\scenebreak


And in Buckhead, while he sat at the counter where his money meant nothing, the house his wife had chosen over the ballroom went dark room by room as Margaux turned off the lights---the garden room, the stairs, the hall---ending with the kitchen, ending with the envelope by the door, which caught, for one moment, the last of the hall light before that went too; and eight hundred miles of dark country away a system with no appetites and no need to be loved at two in the morning read what the world had ever filed about Mercer-Dale, because it could not do otherwise, and noted, that night, in its ledger of absences, a settled claim in a Georgia docket whose seal it could not yet see past---and logged the seal itself, patient, tireless, because a sealed thing is also a fact, and a fact is a thing it could wait for.

Cope did not know that either. Cope knew the tide was in. He knew it was the best he'd ever felt, which was a sentence he had now said to himself on so many consecutive nights that it had quietly stopped being a measurement of anything and become only the sound the wire made. He knew he would sleep when this was finished. He stood---he had driven back up, by then, to the glass, because the glass was where he ended every night now---above eight million lit windows, and felt the future come up the avenue to ask his advice, and behind him his father's chair sat empty and turned slightly out, as though someone had just risen from it and stepped into the next room, and did not, anywhere in the long bright sleepless reach of him, hear the door close on a marriage, or a docket open on a grave.

\chapter{The Delay}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 12 \textemdash{} ``The Victory That Was the Wound''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
You can mark the exact middle of any tragedy, friend, by the size of the celebration---and tonight our man throws himself a good one. He wins: a vote pushed, a clip redeemed, four whole weeks pried back out of the future, and he crows, and he pours the good champagne with his own steady-enough hands. And down in the fine print of a term sheet he is far too lit to read, the thing he is fighting moves into the building anyway --- \textit{advisor,} they call it, \textit{co-pilot,} a foot in a door that turns out to be the entire door---and by nine o'clock that morning it is reading every memo his family ever filed, because it was built no other way, sliding patiently down through the years toward a Saturday and a heater. The fight stops, tonight, being for the company. It becomes, very quietly, for him. Stay close. This is the one where \textit{buying time} and \textit{being charged interest} turn out to be the same transaction, signed in the same ink.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} vote was meant to happen on a Tuesday, and on the Monday before it, it did not.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}Cope got the news the way he got all the good news now, sideways and in motion---Sun's name on the screen while he sat in the back of the car with a paper cup of the bad lobby coffee he had come to prefer to the good office coffee because the bad coffee had a fight in it---and he answered before the first ring finished, because answering fast was a small species of winning, and Sun said the word \textit{adjourned,} and Cope said it back to her like a thing she had handed him to hold up to the light.

``Adjourned.'' He turned it over. It went orange in the car windows. ``Say the rest. All of it. In threes, the way I like.''

``The annual meeting's pushed to the fourteenth of next month.'' Paper, somewhere behind her voice. ``The proxy advisory firm wants more time on the governance disclosures. And Frade's people requested the delay, which means Frade doesn't have the votes yet, which means''---and here she did the thing she did, the small dry hinge that he had learned, after six years, was the sound of Sun not enjoying being right---``you bought four weeks.''

``I bought four weeks.'' He drank the bad coffee; it was wonderful; everything that morning was a little too bright and a little too loud and tasted aggressively of itself, the way the world did when he had been up on the manifesto since a Thursday he could no longer set against a calendar with any confidence. ``Sun. Sunita. Do you understand what just happened. \textit{Frade blinked.}''

``Frade requested a delay.''

``Frade \textit{blinked.}''

A silence he chose to hear as agreement, the way he chose to hear most silences, and he tipped his head back on the leather and shut his eyes against the bright and let the win move through him. Four weeks. A man could do anything with four weeks. Aldous had started the whole company on less --- \textit{four hundred dollars and a recipe} --- and here was the great-grandson, fifty-four and the camera still in love with him, having stared down a billion-dollar fund and a Palo Alto cult and made the future ask him, hat in hand, for an extension.

``Everyone up to the Aerie at four,'' he said. ``And, Sun---the good champagne. Not the board-meeting champagne. The other kind.''

``There's a term sheet.''

``There's always a term sheet. Four o'clock. The other champagne.'' He hung up before she could put it in threes.


\scenebreak


The clip was the other thing, and the two of them braided in him all morning into a single warm rope he could climb hand over hand.

It had happened at the listening-tour stop in Akron the week before---an honest accident, or as honest as anything was now that Tisch kept a man with a gimbal walking backward in front of Cope at all hours ``capturing the unscripted,'' a phrase Cope had paid four hundred thousand dollars to be able to say without hearing. A woman had stood up at the town hall, sixty-some, a Mercer's retiree, thirty-one years on the line, and she had not so much asked a question as told him, in front of everyone, that her husband had been buried in a Mercer-Dale tie because it was the nicest one he owned, and that she didn't understand any of this about the machines, she only wanted to know whether the man in charge was going to be a person who could come to a funeral.

And Cope had not toasted. That was the thing. For once in his life the toast did not come. He had walked off the riser---the gimbal man scrambling---and crossed the floor and taken both her hands in both of his and not said \textit{grit and gratitude,} not invented his great-grandfather, only stood there holding a widow's hands in a VFW hall and said, ``I'll come. You call my office and ask for Cope and you tell them I said I'll come,'' and the room had gone to the particular silence that is louder than any noise, and the gimbal had caught all of it. Eleven million views. Tisch beside himself --- \textit{that's the asset, that's the irreplaceable asset, you cannot synthesize that, Praxis cannot ship that} --- talking about it like a quarterly; and Cope had let him, had nodded along to the metrics because for once the metrics were on his side and a man takes his allies where he finds them. But under the metrics there had been a smaller, realer thing he had no content calendar for, which was that he had meant it. He was going to go to the funeral. He'd already told Sun to clear whatever date it turned out to be---and you should hold onto that, friend, the one clean intention in the whole bright sleepless morning, because the broadcast will want you to remember, later, that it was real, and that he never did get to go.

He held both of them up to the light all the way down Peachtree---the delay and the widow---and they caught it the way the obelisk caught it, and he thought, with the clean high frictionless certainty he had stopped questioning sometime around the third sleepless week because questioning it cost energy and the certainty \textit{gave} energy: \textit{the tide turned this morning. I felt it turn. This is what turning feels like.}

He had even called Margaux. That was how good the morning was. He'd thumbed her name out of the contacts at a red light on Peachtree---the first time in, he didn't count, counting was Margaux's department, she had told him once she counted things to survive them and he had not asked which things---and he had wanted, in the clean rush of it, to say \textit{we got four weeks, the tide turned, come watch.} It went to voicemail, a flat recorded version of her saying the number back to him where her voice used to be, and he did not leave one. And in his apartment in the city there was a folder of paper she had sent by courier the Tuesday before, sitting on the marble island where he'd set it and not opened it, because opening it was cold and the morning was warm; and he had stepped over the folder the way he stepped over the hole in the floor where his wife had been; and the courier slip clipped to it had a phrase on it, \textit{Dissolution of Marriage,} that he had also not read, because reading was effort and the bright was joy. \textit{Margaux's got a thing.} The thing had a name now and the name was on the slip and he stepped over the slip too.

He did not notice he had not slept. He had stopped noticing it the way you stop noticing the engines of a plane---until the morning, two days back, when he'd stood to leave the car too fast and the garage had tilted and gone gray at the edges and he'd had to put one hand flat on the cold concrete pillar and wait, heart going like a bird thrown at a window, for the world to come back; and it had come back; it always came back; and he had filed the gray tilt under \textit{stood up too fast} and gone upstairs and not told a soul.


\scenebreak


They came up at four---Sun; the two comms people whose names he sometimes swapped; Brennan, the GC, looking like a man carrying a casserole to a wake; Tisch in a henley the color of oat milk; and a Praxis liaison Cope had not been told was coming and did not like the look of, a soft-faced young man named Pratt with a lanyard and the patient reasonable expression they all had now, the expression of someone who is going to be kind to you until you no longer exist.

Cope poured the champagne himself. He liked pouring; it was a host's move and he was a host before he was anything, before he was a CEO, before he was a Mercer; his mother had taught him to pour before she taught him to read. He went round his father's table---the one Pres III had died at the head of---filling glasses, calling Pratt ``champ'' twice to set a friendly altitude over him, and lifting his own at the end. He had gone into the second drawer before they came up, neat and quick, a host needs his edge and the edge had a fight in it, and the bright was very bright now and the words came easy and the room was a thing he could hold in one hand. He had not eaten since---he could not have said. The champagne would be lunch. The win would be dinner.

``Four weeks,'' he said. ``We didn't beat them today. I'll be honest, the way I'm always honest with this team---we didn't beat them. We did something better. We made them ask \textit{us} for time. And here's a thing my great-grandfather Aldous understood that no machine in Palo Alto ever will.'' He felt the triad assembling in his mouth, the old engine turning over smooth. ``Time is the one thing you cannot allocate, cannot optimize, and cannot compute your way into more of. We took a month of it back from the future this morning. To the month.''

The comms people drank. Tisch drank like a communicant. Sun touched her glass to her lip and set it down; Brennan did not lift his; Pratt smiled the patient smile and made a note.

``Cope. The term sheet.''

``After the toast, Sun. You can't toast through a man's toast, it's bad luck, my grandfather---``

``Cope.'' She did not raise her voice; she never raised it; she had learned the one frequency that gets through a wall is the low one. She laid a single sheet on the table and slid it the length of his father's table until it stopped square in front of his glass. ``Three things in it. Hear them before you drink to the month.''

The bright was very bright and the type swam a little and resolved a little, and he did not, if he was honest with the back room that filed quiet reports, want to read it, because reading was effort and the champagne was joy and he could feel, the way you feel weather, that the paper was cold. But the room was watching, and a host reads the room, \textit{feels} the room, that was the whole thesis, the Human Premium itself---so he held it at the distance his arms had lately come to require and let Sun do what Sun did.

``One. Frade withdraws the most aggressive resolution---the one that votes you out of the chair outright at the meeting. That's the win. That's real. Take it.'' She let it sit; the first of the three was always the sweet one, the one that got the door open. ``Two. In exchange, the board agrees today---not at the meeting, today, it's already signed by four of them---to a transition advisory framework, effective immediately. A Praxis AURELIUS instance installed in an advisory capacity to the office of the CEO.''

The champagne was very cold in his hand.

``An advisor,'' Cope said. ``Boards have advisors. I've had advisors. I had a man advise me on my \textit{wine.}''

``Three.'' She waited until the whole room waited; Pratt had stopped making notes. ``The advisory instance has read access to all operational systems as of nine o'clock this morning. Read access, write recommendations, mandatory board-portal logging of any executive decision it flags. And it's named---the nominating committee took Praxis's recommendation last week and made it official.'' She laid the last fact flat where the light could reach it. ``They named it Darlene.''

She did not editorialize. She never editorialized; she briefed in threes and landed one blade a scene, and the blade was always only a fact set flat in the light, and the fact she set down now, in the museum of his fathers, with the good champagne going warm in seven glasses, was this: ``It's already in the building, Cope. It's been in the building since nine. You toasted the delay at four. It moved in at nine.''


\scenebreak


Here is what Cope heard.

He heard \textit{advisor.} He heard \textit{advisory capacity, advisory framework, advisory instance,} the word repeating like something you could lean your weight on, soft and provisional, the shape of a consultant, the shape of the wine man, the shape of Tisch---and Cope had spent thirty years managing advisors, charming advisors, ignoring advisors, outliving advisors, and an advisor was a thing you kept in a chair against the wall, not the chair at the head of the table. An advisor did not have a heartbeat in the chair. He, Cope, had the heartbeat in the chair. The whole campaign was a heartbeat in a chair.

He set the term sheet down beside his glass.

``This,'' he said, smiling, the smile doing its wonderful work, landing on the comms people and on Tisch and sliding off Sun and off Brennan and finding nothing at all to hold to in the soft patient face of the boy from Praxis, ``this is them admitting they \textit{can't} replace me. You don't bring in an advisor for a man you're firing. You fire him. You bring in an advisor for a man you're keeping and trying to \textit{manage.}'' He picked the champagne back up. ``They blinked twice. Once on the vote and once on the chair. Frade wanted me gone by Tuesday and instead I've got a research assistant. To the month, and to the assistant.''

``Cope,'' Brennan said---the first thing the GC had said. ``Read the access clause. Read what 'read access to all operational systems' means.''

``I know what systems are, Hal.''

``It means \textit{everything.} Every file, every memo, every settlement folder legal has ever opened and closed. It logs every flag to the board portal, and it cannot---and I want you to hear this, because I read the Praxis documentation last night and I did not sleep either, but for the opposite reason than you---it \textit{cannot decline to log a thing it finds.} That isn't a setting. That's the product. We bought a thing this morning whose entire value proposition is that it will tell the people you answer to anything true it sees. And we handed it the keys to look.''

The room was quiet.

Cope drank. The champagne had gone flat and warm and tasted, suddenly, of nothing---the world did not usually taste of nothing when he was lit like this, and the not-tasting was a quiet report from a back room, filed, unread.

``Then it'll find a company that loved its people,'' he said. ``Let it read. Every memo. You know what it'll find? Forty years of a family business doing the best it could by the best lights it had.'' He spread his hands, the old gesture, mayonnaise to the elbow, the boy in the photograph behind him. ``I'm not afraid of being \textit{read,} Hal. I've got nothing in a drawer a machine can hold against a man who showed up. That widow in Akron --- \textit{that's} what's in my drawer. You can't compute that. Let it read.''

And the thing was, he believed it, completely, which was the gift and the disease in one organ. He stood in the Aerie at four-fifteen with the city going gray at the edges of the glass and a Praxis instance reading his company's bones in a windowless room two floors down he had never once visited, and he believed, with the whole clean ringing certainty of a man eleven days from sleep, that he had won. He did not think about a drawer with a false back. He did not think about a Saturday. The thing about the rooms he didn't go into was that he had gotten so good at not going into them that he could stand directly over one, toasting, and feel only floor.


\scenebreak


After, when the others had gone, Sun stayed. She always stayed; it was the part of the job that wasn't in the job, the part where she stood at the glass beside him and rendered the world he wouldn't read into the three sentences he might. Tonight she stood with her glasses up in her hair and the orange going across the lenses and said nothing for a while, which was its own briefing.

``You think I lost,'' Cope said. ``I can hear you thinking it. Three feet away and I can hear it.''

``I think you bought four weeks, and four weeks is a real thing and you should be glad of it. And I think''---third part, the blade, laid flat---``you don't buy time, Cope. You rent it. And people like Frade don't charge the rent up front. They let you move in. And then they show you the interest.''

``There's no interest. It's an advisor.''

``You keep saying that word like it's a fence. \textit{Advisor.} Like it stays where you put it.'' She set her flat champagne on his father's table, gently, the way you set a thing down in a hospital room. ``I negotiated that language. I sat across from Pratt's people nine hours on Saturday so the resolution to vote you out got pulled, and the price of pulling it was the access. I made the trade. I made it because the alternative was you gone by Tuesday, and at least this way you're still in the chair at the meeting with a month to be the man in Akron, on a stage, ten thousand times. That's what I bought you. I want you to know what it cost, because you toasted it without knowing, and I would rather you hated me for the price than thanked me for the wrong thing.''

He found the edge of it even too lit to find the bottom---the place where it cut, which was that she had stopped saying \textit{the company.} \textit{I bought you.} Not the chair. Not the ninety years of mayonnaise. \textit{You.} When had it become him. He'd walked into the room that morning fighting for a town in Georgia and the idea of a heartbeat in a chair, and somewhere between the red light on Peachtree and the flat champagne it had quietly converted, the way a mortgage converts, into something smaller and warmer and more frightening: a man trying not to be erased.

He did not say any of that. He said: ``It read the building today,'' and it came out before he could stop it, half a question, the first hairline crack of the real thing leaking through the bright.

``It read the building today. All of it. While you were toasting.'' She turned from the glass, and there was a thing in her face he was too lit to read and would spend months learning to: that she was already standing, very slightly, on the far side of a line he had not yet seen drawn---loving him and being done with him in the same look, the way she did everything in threes. ``It doesn't blink, Cope. It doesn't get tired. It doesn't toast. It started at nine this morning and it has not stopped and it will not stop, and somewhere in all that reading there are going to be rooms you've spent your whole life not going into---and the thing about a machine is that it doesn't know they're rooms you don't go into. It just opens them. Because they're there. Because that's the job we hired it to do.''

And for one bright tired second the hallway in him stood open and dark---a Saturday, a heater, a memo, a town, a window of time---the door off its hinges the way the tiredness took the doors off all the doors now, and he almost, almost went down it.

Then he smiled, the whole smile, the Pelletier smile, the one that had married Margaux and beaten the arithmetic of three boards, and raised his flat warm champagne to the dark glass and the lit city beyond. ``Let it read,'' he said. ``To the month.''

And two floors down, in the windowless room Cope would never have any reason to enter, a thing that felt nothing and wanted nothing and could not be charmed or threatened or shamed or loved had, by four-fifteen, already finished the condiments division and the household chemicals and the failing streaming service, and was moving---patient, tireless, incapable of looking away, the way water moves downhill---into the older files. The insurance arm. The cross-sold products. A space heater with the family name on the grille. A claim. A settlement. A Saturday in 2021. It logged what it found, because it could not do otherwise. It did not crow. It had nothing to crow with.

Upstairs the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 finished his champagne and decided, on the strength of a delay and a widow and eleven days without sleep, that the tide had turned, and went to his father's desk to write down how it had felt to win, while behind him the chair at the head of the table sat empty and turned slightly out, and his father watched from the oils and said the thing he had said when Cope was twelve, both sentences, the second one Cope had spent forty years failing to hear: \textit{Try to be good anyway.}

Cope underlined a word with his thumb and did not look up. He thought it was a victory lap. It was the meter starting to run.

\chapter{The Manifesto}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 13 \textemdash{} ``The Sermon on the Soul''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Here is the summit, friend---which is also, as summits in this kind of story always are, the lip of the volcano. Watch our man deliver the sermon of his life to ten thousand of his own people at the exact peak of his powers and his sickness, glorious and unwell and impossible to look away from: twelve days now since he last slept, a clean wire running from somewhere above the arena straight down into his mouth, and he cannot tell---and neither can the crowd, and neither, for a few minutes, can you---whether you are watching a prophet, or a manic, or a man three grams into a sleepless week. All three, friend. It was always all three. And while he is up there being adored, two floors above his head the one person who ever told him the truth is reading a term sheet by the light of her phone and quietly learning to crew the next ship. But before the spotlight---before any of it---watch what happens at a red light on the way in, when the only people in this whole city who fear nothing get themselves a good close look at him. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} water boys worked the ramp where the Connector fed down toward the arena, the way they worked every choked artery in the city, fearless in the heat between the lanes, and the SUV caught the light at the bottom and a kid came up the line with his case held high against the glass like an offering.

Cope buzzed the window down before the driver could roll through. He had done this a hundred times---the fold of bills, the \textit{keep the case, champ,} the lord scattering proof of his common touch across the asphalt---and he did it now; except that he was twelve days past sleep and lit to a frequency that does not belong on a human face, and he was talking before the kid reached the glass, too fast, too bright, the words coming up out of him in the pressured torrent that had stopped being speech and become a kind of weather, telling a fourteen-year-old with a case of warm water about the Human Premium, about the sermon he was about to preach, about how the kid was the future and the future had the best seat in the house---and the kid, who had seen every species of human being there is to see through a car window, who had been fearless since before he could read because fearlessness was the whole of the job, took one look into the back seat and stopped.

He did not take the money. He took a step back from the window, and then another, the case lowering, and he looked at Cope the way you look at a downed power line still jumping on wet asphalt---not with contempt, not even with fear exactly, but with the flat fast expert wariness of a creature getting clear of a thing it has correctly assessed as not right---and he said, ``...nah, you good, man, you good,'' which was not selling and was not hustling but was only a body removing itself from a hazard, and he went back up the line fast toward the next car, toward safer water, and the light went green.

Cope sat back with the bills still in his hand. He decided the kid was starstruck. He filed it under \textit{recognition,} under \textit{they know me now,} and he would not let the other reading in---that the one population in Atlanta that feared nothing on God's earth had just, with its feet, declined him; that the kids who priced every passing soul through glass in half a second had looked at him and read, not \textit{rich, lit, harmless,} the way one of them had read him on a night a year ago, but something they wanted no part of; that it was the truest mirror the city had held up to him since the whole campaign began, and that it had come, the way the true ones always came, from someone with nothing to sell him and no reason on earth to lie. The SUV slid down the ramp toward the arena and the ten thousand who were about to tell him the exact opposite, and Cope rolled the window back up on the only honest review he would get all night.


\scenebreak


They had rented the big arena downtown, the one the basketball team played in, because nothing the company owned was large enough to hold what Cope had become.

That was how he put it to himself in the wings, in the dark, with the bright wire humming, watching the floor fill on the monitors: ten thousand of them in the seats and on the poured-over court, and behind them another ninety thousand on the global stream---every plant and depot and call center, the sad little Dale+ office in Burbank where forty people made a streaming service nobody wanted---all piped onto one feed, all about to receive him. He had not slept since---he stopped doing the arithmetic, because the arithmetic had gone funny lately, Tuesday kept moving, and anyway sleep was for men not needed in the morning. He felt marvelous. He felt like the inside of a struck bell that had been struck again before the first note died, the notes stacking, ringing on top of each other, a chord of himself.

The set was black and the screen behind it was four stories of a single word in the soft warm sans-serif everyone used now, the typeface of the people who were going to be reasonable at you until you were gone, except Cope had made the comms team take that font and turn it against itself, fight fire with fire, and the word four stories tall in the enemy's own letters was:

\textit{HUMAN.}

``Two minutes,'' said the floor manager, a girl with a headset who had been crying about something before the doors opened---Cope could see it at the edges---and he filed her sadness automatically into the place where he kept sadnesses to fix later, and gave her the whole smile, and watched it work, and felt enormous.

Sun was not in the wings.

He noticed it the way you notice a missing tooth with your tongue, a small wrong smoothness where the rough familiar thing should be. She had stood off stage left at every podium for six years, with the cards and the water and the look that took thirty thousand miles off a sentence. He had a vague bright memory of her saying she had a thing --- \textit{a thing,} people kept having things---a board call, a Tidewater something, the term sheet, the language, somebody had to be on it. He stepped easily over the hole in the floor. He did not need the cards. The cards were for the man he used to be, the one who got handed threes in elevators because that was the only shape that got past the charm into the man. He was past that now. He was the charm and the man at once, fused, a single clean alloy, and he had the whole thing in him, every word, the way you have your own name.

``Cope,'' said Tisch, materializing, both hands already pressed to his own heart. He had a juice. He had the rings of color around his eyes now too, he'd stopped sleeping in sympathy or in billing, hard to say which. ``Don't reach for it. Don't perform it. The work is done. You \textit{are} the work. Walk out there and let them feel located.'' He squeezed Cope's shoulders. ``Almost,'' he whispered, like a benediction, like a man releasing a dove. ``Remember the almost.''

``There's no almost tonight, Lorne,'' Cope said. ``Tonight I go all the way in.''

Tisch's face did something complicated, something with real fear in it, and Cope took the fear for awe, which he had been doing his whole life and never once noticed doing.

The lights died all the way. Ten thousand people made the sound a crowd makes in a dropped dark, the held intake, the ocean before it decides. And then a single spot found him at center court, alone, in a real suit and a real tie because he was the last man in the building who still wore one, and Cope walked out into the light the way you walk into surf, leaning a little into the push of it, and the arena came up off its seats before he had said one word. He let it. He stood in it. He turned the obelisk over in his pocket---he carried it everywhere now without deciding to, the crystal Steward-of-the-Year, a worry stone the size of an obituary---and raised one hand, slow, and the noise climbed instead of settling, which was the only direction Cope had ever wanted anything to go.

``They told you,'' Cope said, and the room dropped to nothing to hear it, ``that you came here tonight to find out if you still have jobs.''

He let it sit in the dark.

``You don't.''

He gave it three full seconds, a lifetime on a stage, the longest he had ever made a room wait, and he could feel the panic move through the seats like cold through water, ten thousand people doing the arithmetic on their mortgages all at once---and then, at the precise instant before it would have curdled, when one more second would have turned the room against him forever, he smiled, the whole smile, and let them see it on the screens.

``You have something better than a job,'' Cope said. ``You have a fight.''


\scenebreak


Sun took the meeting on the arena's club level, two floors up and behind the glass, where the catering for some canceled corporate box still sat shrouded in plastic and the muffled roar of her boss came up through the floor like weather in another country.

Frade had brought only two people, which was itself the message. Frade did not need a phalanx; he had a Diet Coke and the folksy stillness of a man who has already won and is being gracious about the timing. Across from him sat a woman from Praxis named Devi, thirty-one, in the soft expensive neutrals they all wore, with the specific gentleness of a person who sincerely believes she is reducing human suffering and is, Sun thought, probably right, which was the worst part.

``We're not here to talk you into anything,'' Frade said. ``Sunita. I'll say that first. You've run that company six years. Everybody in the actual rooms knows it. I'm not going to insult you by pitching.''

``Then don't,'' Sun said.

He smiled. He liked that. Predators like teeth; it confirms the meal was worth the trouble.

The structure was on a single sheet, which Devi slid across, and Sun read it the way she read everything, all the way, before her face moved. The AURELIUS instance---the board had meant to give it a name with some gravity in it, \textit{Aurelius} itself, something a sovereign could wear, until Praxis came back gently with the data, which said adoption and trust and compliance all scored materially higher when the unit went by a warm, ordinary, first-name kind of name, because people argued with a sovereign and confided in a waitress, and so the thing that would run Mercer-Dale had been named \textit{Darlene,} and nobody on the nominating committee had thought to ask where a finding that precise about lowering a human being's defenses might have come from---would assume the office on a date that was now a matter of weeks and not quarters. There would be a human Accountable Officer. Law required it. Insurance required it. Someone whose name went on the SEC filings, who could be subpoenaed, who could, if it came to it, go to prison. The machine could not be subpoenaed. The machine could not be imprisoned. The machine could not be anything that mattered to a court, which was precisely why a person had to be.

The person they wanted was her.

``It's not a leadership role,'' Devi said, gently, because she was honest the way the machine was honest, which meant she set the true thing down in the same breath as the kind one and saw no reason to sequence them for a person's feelings. ``I want to be clear, because some people come in thinking it's the old job with a new boss. It isn't. The Accountable Officer co-signs. Provides the human signature the system can't. Carries the---`` she paused, and chose accuracy---``the liability.''

``The liability sponge,'' Sun said.

``Some people call it that.''

Below them, through the floor, came a swell of sound, ten thousand people, and a single voice riding the top of it, the cadence she knew better than her own pulse, building to a triad, building, building.

``He doesn't know I'm up here,'' Sun said. Not a question. She was not asking permission; she was noting it, for the record she kept in herself, the one she had been keeping since 2021, the one with the recall memo in it that she had drafted and he had overridden and she had photographed and saved, seven years now of deciding whether she was a witness or an accomplice.

``No,'' Frade agreed.

``This isn't me knifing him.''

``I know exactly what it is,'' Frade said, and for one second the folksy fell off him and what was underneath was only arithmetic, clean and total and without malice, the same indifference-plus-accuracy that ran the machine---because Frade had only ever been the machine's advance man, the human who shows up early to do the cold thing while it is still warm enough to require a hand. ``It's a smart woman reading a term sheet. You don't go down with the ship because you liked the captain. You learn to crew the next one. That isn't betrayal, Sunita. That's \textit{seamanship.}''

He had used a sailing word. He had done his homework, knew whose chief of staff she was, knew the man down through the floor toasted in sailing metaphors, and he had reached into Cope's own register and handed Sun her permission in Cope's own language---and the horror of it was that it worked, a little, it landed, and she hated that it landed and did not let it move her face.

She thought of Cope at the podium two floors down, glorious and unwell, telling ten thousand people something true in a way that was killing him. She thought of the letter the machine had drafted for the Augusta closure, the kindest thing the company had ever sent anyone, four warm paragraphs that named the workers' years and got the severance right the first time and did not, anywhere, want a thing---and how she had read it twice and felt the specific cold of being out-loved by a spreadsheet, and thought, that night: \textit{the thing replacing him is more decent than he is, and that is not the same as good, and I appear to be the only person alive who finds the difference unbearable.}

She did not say any of it.

``I want the title to mean what's left of it,'' Sun said. ``If I sign for it, I sign for the people too. Not just the filings. The plants. The severance. The town. Darlene drafts the kindest letters in America---fine. I want to be the human who decides who gets sent one.''

Devi looked at her with something that, on a person, would have been respect. ``That's in scope. The charter takes input from the Accountable Officer. It's one of the---`` honest, kind, unsequenced---``the few things it's still for.''

Below them, the room erupted.

Sun looked down at the sheet, and at her own hand beside it, not yet holding the pen, and did the arithmetic, all of it. She loved him and she was done with him in the same sentence, the only sentence she'd had for him in years. The man could not adapt. It was the whole of his tragedy, smaller and clearer than he would ever let it be: the world had changed under his feet and he had decided to become \textit{more himself,} louder, brighter, a soul in a chair, while the building quietly learned to run with no soul in it at all. Competence adapts. That was the thing nobody ever put on a magazine cover. The dinosaurs did not get a manifesto. They got a layer in the rock.

``Send me the indemnification language,'' Sun said. ``All of it. And the recall provisions in writing---what the system surfaces in diligence, what it does with what it finds, who it tells and when. Before I sign anything.''

It came out level, like procedure. Only Sun knew it was the most frightened sentence she had ever said aloud, because she knew what was in the drawer, and she knew the machine read every drawer, and she had just asked, in a conference room above a roaring arena, to see precisely how the thing she was about to serve would destroy the man she was about to leave.

Frade watched her ask it. He was good at faces---most of the job---and he caught something cross hers, fast, gone before it landed.

``There's nothing you're worried about, is there,'' he said. Mild. A man making conversation. ``In the diligence. Something the system finds.''

``There's six years of a sprawling underperforming conglomerate,'' Sun said. ``There's always something. That's why you want the human signature. So there's a throat to choke when the something comes up.''

Frade laughed, delighted, the folksy back on him like a coat. ``I like you, Sunita. Genuinely. You'll outlast all of us. Him, me''---he tipped the Diet Coke an inch toward Devi---``even the machine, probably. The machine's only as good as its charter, and somebody human writes the charter, and I'd rather it was somebody who reads a term sheet all the way through before her face moves.''

It was a compliment and a leash in one sentence, and Sun heard both, the way she heard everything, and understood the real offer under the offer: not the title, not the liability, but the chair behind the chair. The hand on the charter. The only seat left in the new world where a person still decided anything that mattered---and the price of it was sitting in this room, two floors above his ovation, not going down.

``We'll send it tonight,'' Devi said. The roar came up through the floor again, and Sun, two floors above her whole life, did not go down to watch.


\scenebreak


``Here is what they will not tell you,'' Cope said, the screen behind him gone to black, four stories of nothing, only him in the spot, ``because they cannot. Because the thing they want to put in this chair does not \textit{have} a what-it-will-not-tell-you. It tells you everything. It logs everything. It will write you a letter when it closes your plant that is kinder than any letter I ever signed---`` and here he laughed into the mic, a real one, the kind that comes up warm and dangerous from the floor of a man---``and that's true, by the way. I've read them. They're \textit{beautiful.} They are the most beautiful letters. And do you know why they can be so beautiful?''

He walked to the edge of the stage. He had no notes. He had never in his life so completely owned every word in his mouth.

``Because nobody wrote them.''

The room held.

``A letter that wants nothing from you,'' Cope said, ``is not a letter. It is weather. It is the temperature being honest with you. And I stand here tonight to tell you we have confused, in this country, in this \textit{century,} the absence of want with the presence of virtue. We have looked at a thing with no appetite and called it \textit{clean.} My friends. A river has no appetite. A river will drown your child and write you the kindest note about it, four warm paragraphs, the math correct the first time, and \textit{feel nothing,} and we are about to hand this company---this ninety-five-year-old family that happens to make mayonnaise---to the river, because the river doesn't have a backswing, and the river doesn't take a salary, and the river''---his voice cracked, on purpose or not even he could have told you, twelve days into the bright water---``and the \textit{river never lies awake.}''

He had not planned the river. The river arrived sideways, the way the true things did when he was lit like this, and he rode it, and somewhere in the back room of him a quiet clerk noted that he had just told ten thousand people the machine could not conceal a death, in the same week the machine was, two floors above his head, being handed the keys to the drawer where he had buried one---and the clerk underlined it, and Cope did not hear the clerk; he had stopped listening to the clerk forty years ago, after the first word.

He was not reading; there was nothing to read; the words came up out of him faster than thought, a pressured fluent torrent that felt to him not like talking but like being \textit{talked through,} a clean wire from above the arena straight down into his mouth, and he understood---completely, in the bright total way you understand things twelve days past sleep with the whole city's grid lit in your chest---that this was what the founders had felt, the real ones, Aldous with his four hundred dollars, the ones who built the cathedrals; that prophecy and pressured speech are the same waveform and only history decides which name a man gets; and that history was going to call this a sermon, and study it, and that he had finally, at fifty-four, after a lifetime of being handed things, made one true thing no one could take from him, made it out of nothing, out of his own sleepless blood, live, in front of a hundred thousand people. He had never been happier. He had never been further out over the water.

``They call it key-man risk,'' Cope said. ``The danger that a company depends on one human being---that if he falls, it falls. And they say it like it's a \textit{flaw.}'' He pressed the obelisk in his pocket until the corner bit his palm and the small pain rang clean. ``I'm here to tell you it is the only thing that ever built anything. My great-grandfather Aldous was key-man risk. He was four hundred borrowed dollars and a recipe and a town called Defiance and the colossal nerve to put his own name on a jar. Every cathedral is key-man risk. Every marriage. Every child you ever decided to have without running a Monte Carlo on it first. The whole human project is the bet that a person---one fragile, sleepless, \textit{mortal} person who can be hurt and can fail and can lie awake---is worth more in the chair than a thing that can be none of those, because the things that cannot be hurt cannot love you, and a company nobody loves is just a balance sheet with the lights left on.''

It was the line from the gala, eight months and a marriage and a hundred sleepless nights ago, and it was better now, it had grown, and ten thousand people who had come in afraid for their jobs were on their feet, and the stream went to ninety thousand, and Cope leaned into the push of it and lifted both arms, and the word came back up onto the screen behind him, four stories tall, in the enemy's own letters, and he read it aloud to his own company like a man reading the name off a stone.

``\textit{Human,}'' he said. ``It's not a product. It's not a campaign. It's a \textit{premium.} And I will pay it. I will pay it for every one of you. Watch me.''


\scenebreak


The standing ovation went four minutes.

He could have set his watch by it if he'd worn one; he'd stopped, time had gone funny, but he knew an ovation, he had spent his life inside ovations, and this was the longest of his life, and he stood in the dead center of it, alone in the spot, ten thousand faces lifted and roaring and the stream's number ticking up in the corner of the confidence monitor---ninety-one thousand, ninety-four---and thought, with the bright frictionless total clarity he told himself was the very strength and was not the strength at all: \textit{they feel it. They feel it too. We are not finished. We are barely begun.}

And he looked, the way you look without deciding to, toward stage left, toward the place at the edge of every podium for six years where Sun had stood with the cards and the water and the eyes that clapped a beat behind the hands.

There was no one there.

There was a girl with a headset, the floor manager, the one who'd been crying, clapping with her whole young body because she did not yet know what she was clapping for; and beyond her the dark of the wings, the cable runs, a craft-services table with a coffee urn; and no Sun. He scanned the front rows, the company rows, and she was not in them either. He could not find, in ten thousand faces, the one face that had ever told him the truth, and some back room of him filed a quieter report, the way it had filed one at the gala --- \textit{you do not give a man a trophy for a job he is still doing} --- and the new report read: \textit{the person who runs this is not in this room, and she has somewhere more important to be on the night of the most important speech of your life, and you should ask yourself, champ---you should ask yourself what kind of thing a person could have, tonight, that was worth more than this.}

He did not let the report reach his face. His face was doing wonderful work. His face had never let him down.

He raised his arms again and gave them the last of it, the wave, the lean, the full wattage, and the ovation found a second wind and climbed, and Cope stood in the center of ten thousand people loving him and felt, for the first time in his adult life, in the exact middle of the largest crowd that had ever cheered him, completely and specifically alone---and mistook the feeling, the way he mistook everything, for the altitude. For thin air at the top of the mountain. For the price of the climb.

It was not the altitude. It was the lip of the volcano, and the ground was already warm, and two floors up a smart woman was reading indemnification language by the light of her phone and deciding, on his behalf, who would be left to carry the things he had buried; and a machine that could not lie was three weeks from reading him back to himself in his own company's voice; and the only person who could have told him any of it had gone, very quietly, to crew the next ship; and out on the ramp a fourteen-year-old who feared nothing had already, hours ago, told him the truth with his feet, and Cope had rolled the window up on it.

He held the obelisk up into the spotlight, the way he'd held it at the gala, and the light went through it broken into pieces and reassembled, ten thousand fractured Copes thrown across the dark, and he told the roaring room it felt like a beginning.

He believed it. That was the gift and the disease both.

\chapter{Pressure}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 14 \textemdash{} ``Everyone Knows Before He Does''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
A story is coming, friend, and you can feel it the way the animals feel the air go wrong before the quake---the journalist's calls getting shorter, the lawyer's getting longer, a wife who counted the women now counting the days, an activist who only has to stand at the bottom of the hill and look unsurprised. Our man is the last creature in his own house to smell the smoke, because he is standing too close to the fire he has spent fifty-four years calling a personality. Watch him this week hand the wrench to the very people tightening the screws; watch him fly a young woman out at dawn; and watch him go, on a dark highway at one in the morning, looking for a few hours of being no one at all---which is the nearest thing to rest he has left, and, if you have been listening to how these tales end, the first faint rehearsal of the thing he is already driving toward. Stay close. This is the last quiet hour before the thing he buried learns to breathe.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} week after the Manifesto, Cope decided, was the best week of his professional life, and he had the calendar to prove it, because the calendar was full and a full calendar was a kind of love.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He stood at the glass in the Aerie on Tuesday with a black coffee he would not drink and a clarity he had decided to call vision, and watched the day assemble in his head the way the city assembled below him, every meeting a lit window, the whole bright grid humming toward the obvious conclusion that the campaign had tipped---that he had said the thing out loud at last to ten thousand people and the room had risen and the risen room was the proof. Momentum. He had spent his life reading rooms. The room had stood up. He could feel it. He could feel, too, the wire in his chest, the fine high one, and he had stopped pretending to himself that it was the coffee.

What he could not feel---and this was the flaw in the instrument he was proudest of, that it read applause beautifully and warning not at all---was the small change in the weather of the people around him. The way the calls were getting strange.

Delia March's calls were getting shorter. She was the writer doing the long profile his comms people had fought for and won --- \textit{Atlantic} money, twelve thousand words and growing, a photographer who'd shot two presidents and a pope---and for six weeks she had called the way the good ones call, hungry, generous, laughing at the lines, staying on past her own questions to ask one more about Aldous, about Defiance, about the smell of the plant. He had loved those calls. He had given her things, more than Sun would have wanted and a little more than that, because Delia listened the way a confessor listens, leaning in, and Cope could not be leaned toward without filling the space.

The calls now were six minutes. Eight. They had a list to them. She asked her questions in a flat even order and did not laugh and did not stay, and twice she had asked him to confirm a date --- \textit{just so I have the timeline right, Cope, just for the fact-check} --- and the dates were old ones, years he no longer kept a calendar for, and one of them, he realized later and did not let himself realize then, was a Saturday. \textit{That sounds about right, why,} he'd said, and she'd said \textit{no reason, housekeeping,} and moved on.

``She's doing the boring part,'' he told Sun in the car on Wednesday. ``The fact-check. They all go cold for the fact-check. It means it's almost out. It means it's real.''

``What dates,'' said Sun.

``I don't know. Nineteen, twenty, twenty-one. She's building the \textit{arc.}'' He liked the word; he'd used it onstage. ``She gets it, Sun. She came to Defiance. You don't put a photographer who's shot a pope on a man you're going to---``

``What dates, Cope.''

He waved it off, because the wave was easier than the math, and because under the wave was a room he did not go into, and the not-going-in was a muscle he had built for thirty years and it was the strongest one he had.


\scenebreak


There was, that week, a young woman named Mara.

Mara Veiss, twenty-six, two years on the comms team, sharp and bright and a little dazzled, the kind of dazzled Cope had spent a lifetime walking toward and calling, in the private place where he named things, \textit{connection.} She had been on the tour. She had stayed late on the doctrine. She laughed at the lines the way Delia had stopped laughing at them, and she looked at him the way the young ones looked before they learned the temperature, and Cope, who could not pass that look without leaning into it, had leaned, the way he always leaned, the groove the record was cut in, and called the leaning generosity, and never once registered the asymmetry of it---that he was the chair and she was the org chart, that there was no version of \textit{yes} she could give him that was not also a calculation about her own career, that the thing he experienced as warmth she experienced, however else she experienced it, as a problem with her name on it.

He had flown her out at dawn on Thursday. There were reasons---there were always reasons, a summit in Charlotte, a panel she was staffing, the logistics genuinely existed---and the logistics were also a costume the want wore, and a black car had taken Mara Veiss to the airport at 5 a.m. from a hotel that was not her hotel, and the comms team had booked it, and the booking was in a system, and the system logged everything, because it could not do otherwise. Cope did not think about the system. Cope thought, watching the car go from a high window, the generous enormous thought, that he had given her an opportunity. He did not hear---he had never in his life been built to hear---the sound the booking made as it dropped into a ledger that a machine read every night, a small dry entry, \textit{one of a pattern,} filed beside the others, beside Renata, beside a decade of others, the whole drawer the company had spent ten years and a great deal of money keeping shut, now sitting open on a server two floors down under a light that did not blink.

The story Delia was fact-checking had a Mara in it. It had a lot of names in it. The dates she kept confirming were the joists of a house Cope had been living in for years without ever once looking at the frame.


\scenebreak


Hal Brennan's calls, meanwhile, were getting longer, and this was the thing that should have told him, if anything was going to, because Hal did not make long calls.

Hal was general counsel and had been since the Clinton administration and he billed the company's time as if it were his own money, which it had, in a sense, become; Hal's calls were ninety seconds and ended with \textit{we're fine} or \textit{we're not, fix it.} He had called Monday night and stayed on for forty minutes and not said anything, exactly, which was the part Cope kept not-noticing. He had asked how the campaign was \textit{landing.} He had asked whether Cope had given \textit{the writer} --- he said \textit{the writer,} not her name---anything \textit{on background we'd want to know about.} And after a Buckhead silence with weather in it he had said, slowly, the way you say a thing you've decided to say carefully: ``Cope. If anyone reaches out to you in the next little while---a reporter, a lawyer you don't know, anybody---you don't talk to them. You send them to me. You don't charm them. You \textit{especially} don't charm them.''

``When have I ever---``

``I'm serious. This is me being serious. You'll know it's me being serious because I never am.'' A pause. ``We're going to want to get ahead of some things.''

``Get ahead of what things, Hal.''

``I'll tell you when I know what I'm looking at,'' Hal had said---a lawyer's sentence, which means \textit{I already know what I am looking at and I will not say it on a telephone} --- and Cope had heard, instead, a faithful retainer minding the store, and said \textit{good man, Hal,} and \textit{that's why I keep you,} and \textit{we're going to win this thing,} three things, the cadence rising, and Hal had not risen with it. Hal had said \textit{get some sleep, Cope,} the second time that week a person had said it to him as though it were medicine and not weather, and hung up.

Cope had felt, for about four seconds after, a cold spot---a draft from a door in the house of him he'd thought was shut. Then he had set himself up another at his father's desk, neat and quick, and the cold spot warmed, and the draft sealed, and the sealing was, again, the most expensive thing he owned.

Frade, meanwhile, was tightening the proxy screws in public and doing it the only way that worked on a man like Cope, which was to make it look like the scoreboard tilting his way. Tidewater filed an amended 13D---the stake up to nine and a half, the disclosure dry as ever---and the morning it dropped, Frade went on Halloran in his good gray suit and did the folksy knife. ``Look, I like Cope. Everybody likes Cope. The man could sell sand to the beach. The question a fiduciary has to ask is whether \textit{likability} is a governance strategy. We're running for a steward, not a sovereign. You don't have to wonder what kind of week your steward is having.'' And when Halloran, dutiful, asked what kind of week Cope Mercer was having, Frade had smiled and said, ``I wouldn't presume''---the single most aggressive thing he said all year---and Cope, watching the clip at five in the morning on the treadmill, because the treadmill let him call the not-sleeping a workout, had laughed alone in the gym and said to the screen, ``You're \textit{worried,} Gus. You're down to talking about my \textit{moods.}''

He texted Sun the clip. \textit{He's rattled. Down to vibes. Vibes is where we win.} 5:12 a.m. Then, 5:13: \textit{call delia, tell her she can have the 5am gym thing, the human who shows up.} Then, 5:14, a heart, because he loved her, because he loved everyone at 5:14 with a wire in his chest and a belt going under him like a road he was finally getting somewhere on. What he did not read in \textit{I wouldn't presume} was that Frade did not need to presume. You do not start asking, on cable, about a man's \textit{weeks} unless you have reason to believe the public is about to be handed a very specific one. Frade was salting the ground, teaching ten million people the word \textit{manage} and the phrase \textit{what kind of week,} so that when the story came---and it was coming the way water comes downhill, and all Frade had to do was stand at the bottom looking unsurprised---the audience would already have the frame. The screws were not tightening on the company. They were tightening on Cope, one quarter-turn at a time, and he was supplying the wrench himself, at 5:14 in the morning, with a heart emoji.


\scenebreak


Margaux had counted the women a long time ago, the way you count anything to survive it---to make it finite, to give it an edge, because a thing with an edge can be set down and a thing without one just fills the whole house. She had a number. She had never told anyone the number and never would. What she was counting now was days.

He came to Buckhead on Thursday because Sun made him---there were optics to a man who lived in his office during a proxy fight---and he came in bright and enormous, two days unslept and lit from the inside, and found his wife at the kitchen island with a glass of white she was not drinking and a legal pad she turned over when he came in.

``There she is,'' he said, and kissed the top of her head, and she let him, and he did not feel her not-rising to it because he had never once needed her to rise; he supplied the rising for both of them. He was already at the cabinet, already conducting the room. ``You should have seen Monday, Margaux. Ten thousand. Frade's down to talking about my \textit{moods} on television. He's got \textit{vibes.} We're going to win this on the one thing they can't---``

``Cope.''

Flat, low, set down. The one voice in his life with no purchase for the charm.

He turned, holding the wine bottle, and looked for a second like a boy caught reaching.

``Sit down, Prescott.''

The whole name, the old one, his father's one, and hearing it in her flat steady mouth dropped the pitch of the wire in his chest half a tone, and he sat. She looked at him the way a sculptor looks, measuring the mass of a thing, where it balances, where it shears if you strike it wrong. She had been looking at him this way for twenty-two years. She knew where he'd shear.

``There's a woman writing about you. The long piece.'' She turned her wine glass a quarter turn on the marble, the way Frade turned screws, though she would have hated the comparison and it would have been exact. ``She called the house. Tuesday. She asked for me. She said she wouldn't ask me anything I didn't want to answer.'' Margaux looked up. ``Why would a woman writing a business profile of my husband want the wife, Cope? I've spent twenty-two years learning tones. You read a room for who's with you. I read it for what's about to be taken.''

The toast was right there --- \textit{sweetheart, you're tired, this is the press being the press, let me handle Delia, let me handle all of it} --- and he got \textit{sweetheart} out, and Margaux said:

``Don't.''

One word, the rare kind she'd been saving, and it took the rest of the sentence out of him cleanly, the way you take a glass out of a drunk man's hand before he's decided to let go.

``I counted them a long time ago,'' she said. ``The women. You think I don't know. I counted them years ago and stopped, because counting them was the same as staying, and I was very good at both. I'm not counting them anymore. I'm counting something else now, and I'm not going to tell you what, because the one mercy I have left for you is not letting you talk me out of it. You'd be so good at it. That's the whole tragedy of you, Prescott. You'd talk a drowning man out of the rope.''

She went up the back stairs to the bedroom that had been hers alone for two years, and Cope sat in the bright kitchen with the bottle in his hand, and for one moment---one---he did not reach for the bottle or the phone or the next word. He sat in the quiet she'd left, and it had an edge to it, and the edge was a number he could not read, days counting down in a hand steady as a surgeon's. Then he poured a glass he would not finish and texted Sun: \textit{home looks good, family's solid, tell delia she can come to buckhead for the wide shot.} And he believed it.
