He stood in the wreckage of the only conversation that had told him the truth all week and read it as a win, because reading things as wins was the engine, and the engine had never once failed to start.


\scenebreak


He should have driven straight back to the city. He did not.

He sent Oleg home---there was a service for the driving, a quiet man paid to see everything and forget all of it, and Cope sent him home because there are things a man does not do in front of even a paid forgetting---and he took GA-400 himself at midnight with the windows down and the cold April air coming in hard, and somewhere south of the perimeter he did not get off at the exit for the city. He took a different one. He had a number in his phone with no name on it, and the number knew other numbers, and the want this season had stopped being about a face and become about something colder and more total, and what he was looking for, though he would never once have said it in these words, was not pleasure and not even company. It was \textit{erasure.} A few hours in a room he would not be able to place on a map afterward, with people whose names he did not get and did not give, doing the kind of thing that a year ago would have frightened even him, the risk no longer a side effect but the active ingredient---because for the length of it he was not the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100, was not the man on the cover, was not the soul in the chair or the key or the risk or the husband or the heartbeat anyone was counting; he was no one, gloriously, dangerously no one, and the being-no-one was the closest thing to rest he had felt in months.

The broadcast will not follow him into the room. There is nothing in there for you, friend, and nothing the man himself would keep; he came out of it near dawn with a gap in the night where two hours should have been, a charge to a card he did not remember agreeing to, and a mark on him he had not earned and could not place, and the only clean feeling in the whole of it was the one he could not say to Adler or Margaux or God, which was that for a little while he had managed not to exist. He sat in the car in a parking lot going gray and understood, dimly, the way you understand a thing through three feet of warm water, that he had started going to the edge of things not in spite of the danger but for it; that a man who cannot stop being himself will, eventually, go looking for the rooms where he gets, for an hour, to stop; and that there is a name for the direction that wanting points, and that he was, without admitting it, already walking in it.

He keyed himself bright in the gray lot to put the wattage back, because the charm had to be at full power by the time he reached the tower, and he pulled back onto GA-400 north toward the city with the sun not up yet and the night's erasure already curdling into the day's performance.


\scenebreak


The phone lit on the seat twice on the drive in. Once it was Sun. Once it was a number he didn't know---an unknown number before dawn---and a younger, less lit man might have wondered what kind of caller comes at that hour from a number you don't recognize, might have heard, in the buzz of it on the leather, the sound of a story turning over in its sleep and beginning, out past the dark trees, to breathe. Cope thought it was probably someone like Bryce. There was always someone like Bryce. The thought warmed him, and he smiled at the road.

And here, friend, the small deniable thing, the fourth of them, the kind you have learned by now not to be able to call: at the bottom of the GA-400 ramp, where the road fed into the city grid, his phone---the executive phone, the one the company's systems managed, the one that unlocked his calendar and his car and his floor---went dark. Not low battery; it had been at sixty percent; it simply, for the stretch of the drive in, would not wake, the screen a black mirror with his own gray face in it, so that the eleven calls stacking up behind it stacked in silence, unseen, unfelt, while he drove the last miles into the city believing the quiet was peace. By the time he was in the elevator it had come back on its own, \textit{device re-provisioned, no action required,} a clean unremarkable line in a log somewhere, the kind of glitch that happens, that means nothing, that is always perfectly deniable---and maybe it was nothing, a dropped tower, a tired battery, the ordinary failure of an ordinary phone; and maybe the thing that managed the phone had declined, for eleven calls, to let the last human CEO know the future was trying to reach him, the way it had declined, in a Camden plant, to feed him his lines; and there is no way for you to know, friend, and there was none for him, and that---the not being able to tell whether the machine was hunting him or whether he was simply a sleepless man whose battery died---that was the exact shape of the country he had started living in, and it had no maps.

He came up the Connector with the city standing up ahead of him lit at every node, and he felt---he would have sworn to it, he did in fact toast to it, aloud, alone, lifting an empty hand off the wheel---the future coming up the road to ask his advice. The tower was lit at the crown the way Aldous had insisted, \textit{so a man coming up the avenue knows there's somebody home,} and Cope looked up at the warm gold light where his office was, where his father's desk was, where the drawer was, and thought: \textit{somebody's home. I'm home. The light's on because I'm in it.}

He did not know---could not have known, would not have heard it if Margaux had said it in her flat steady voice---that the light at the crown was the last quiet thing he had. He parked in his spot. He went up forty floors in the brass cage, alone, watching the numbers climb. He let himself into the dark suite where his fathers watched from oils and the boy at nine grinned up out of a steel vat at men whose names he'd lost, and he did not turn on the lamp, and he sat down at the desk and opened the second drawer and set himself up one more, neat and quick and competent, a man maintaining a machine he was certain he had under perfect control.

And then, in the dark, with the city held still and lit below him and the wire singing clean and high and total, the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 picked up his phone to write---he was sure it would be the best thing he'd ever written---and the screen, awake now, willing now, showed him that he had eleven missed calls.

He smiled at it.

``There it is,'' he said, to no one, to his father, to the dark. ``There's the future. Right on time.''

He was almost right. He was off by one word, and the word was \textit{future,} and it would take him until morning to learn the other one.

\chapter{The Most Human Thing}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 15 \textemdash{} ``Detonation''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
And here it is, friend---the exquisite punchline the whole universe has spent a year setting up, and I am sorry, I truly am, because somewhere back around the Waffle House you started, against your own better judgment, to almost like him, and it does not make this part easier. The man crusading for the irreplaceable human soul is destroyed, on schedule, by the most human things there are: the sex, the drug, the lie, the need to be loved at two in the morning by someone he outranks. The pundits will note, correctly, that the machine would never have done one bit of it. Praxis will run a serene little ad in the very same hour, costing them nothing, meaning no harm, and it will be the cruelest thing said all day precisely because no one meant it as cruelty. He reaches for the denial---and the denial, the way denials do, reaches back, takes him gently by the wrist, and pulls. Stay close. This is the one where a man is hoisted on his own humanity, and the rope turns out to be the very thing he'd been bragging about.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} story broke at 6:02 a.m. and Cope was awake to see it, because by then he was always awake to see things.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He had not gone to Buckhead. The house had Margaux in it and the rooms had the particular silence of a woman who had moved her good chisels to a friend's studio and said so the night before, evenly, across the kitchen island, which Cope had filed as a storage decision and not a verdict, because the alternative was unsurvivable at the hour he had for filing things. So he was in the Aerie, on the long couch his grandfather had once bought a senator, in yesterday's shirt, when his phone lit with the alert he'd set months ago to catch his own name and now caught it like a man catching a thrown brick.

\textit{EXCLUSIVE: Inside the Last Human's Last Stand---and the Habits He Couldn't Outrun.}

Delia March's byline. Of course Delia March's byline. He had let her in. He had walked her through the Defiance plant himself, kept her in the back of the Suburban out of the regional strip, said \textit{call me Cope} the first morning and meant it, the full wattage, the toast in miniature, the thing he could not pass a stranger without doing---and Delia March had taken the charm at full power and the access and the eight weeks and written, underneath all of it, the truth, which was the one thing he had never once imagined a profile could contain, because no profile he had ever sat for had wanted anything but to flatter him into a quote.

He read it once fast, the way you read your own scan results, hunting only the word that ends you. He found two.

The first was a name. Not Renata's---that was buried deeper, a different drawer, and the relief at its absence lasted exactly as long as it took to reach the second graf. The name was \textit{Mara Veiss,} twenty-six, a deputy on the communications team, who had joined the campaign in April and been on every plane since, and whom the piece described, in a sentence built to be screenshotted, as ``a relationship the Chairman's own staff has spent the spring scheduling around the way you schedule around weather.''

The second word was \textit{cocaine.} It sat in the eleventh paragraph, sourced to ``two people present and accounts of a 2018 hotel incident described to this magazine,'' and it was not lurid, which is what made it lethal. Delia March had not written it like a scandal. She had written it like a fact about a man's mornings. She had the regional-strip men's room. She had the timing of the diner eggs --- \textit{he ate like a man who hadn't, because he hadn't.} She had the legal pad and the looping hand. She had, God help him, the \textit{almost} --- the withheld tear, the beats, \textit{one, two, look away, three} --- and she had set it down beside the men's room with no comment at all, because the comment was the adjacency, and the adjacency was the entire article.

The Last Human, optimized.


\scenebreak


He called Sun before he called anyone, which was the nearest thing he had to instinct.

``I've seen it,'' she said, before he said anything. Not asleep---a woman up since the embargo lifted on the wire she still got. ``Don't post. Cope. Are you hearing me. Do not put your thumbs on anything until I'm in the building.''

``It's not---Mara's not---`` He was up, pacing the museum, past his father's portrait, past the putter on its cradle, the shirt smelling of yesterday. ``It's a working relationship that she has \textit{sexualized,} in print, a friendship, and the 2018 thing is a \textit{fabrication,} Sun, that's a hit, Frade fed her that---``

``Frade didn't feed her anything.'' Flat, tired, the thirty-thousand-mile voice with another ten thousand on it overnight. ``That's what I need you to understand before you go near a screen. He didn't have to. You let a reporter live in your pocket for eight weeks while it was all happening. She didn't dig it up. She \textit{watched} it. There's nothing to deny. The denial is the story now.''

``Watch me,'' Cope said, which was not an answer, and hung up, and did not know, even as he said it, that he had quoted himself from a mirror a year and a hundred years ago.


\scenebreak


By seven the morning shows had it. By seven-fifteen they had the clip---DeShawn's clip, the Camden plant, \textit{are you scared,} the unrehearsed thing that had been, six weeks ago, the most valuable thing the company had produced in a decade---and they ran it beside the eleventh paragraph, and the realness that had saved him curdled in real time on a split screen, because once you knew about the men's room the catch in the throat read differently, the \textit{almost} read like chemistry, and a thing cannot be authentic and a symptom at once and survive being both on a chyron.

A kind-faced woman on the financial channel said the thing that would be said all day in forty registers. ``And---I want to be careful here---but you cannot help but notice. His whole pitch, his entire pitch, has been: a machine can't \textit{feel.} A machine has no \textit{soul.} And the irony this morning is just---`` the small open-handed gesture they all did now, the Praxis gesture, the soft sans-serif made flesh---``an AURELIUS doesn't \textit{do} this. It can't. There's no appetite to manage. It logs everything. It can't have a relationship with its deputy of comms. It physically cannot buy cocaine in an Augusta-airport bathroom. The argument \textit{for} the human in the chair just became the single best argument \textit{against} this particular human.''

Her co-anchor, gentler, trying: ``Well---that's what makes him \textit{human,} isn't it---``

``That's exactly the problem,'' she said, not unkindly, and that was the whole book in eleven words; and Cope, watching, did not hear it as the whole book. He heard a woman who could be charmed if he got the room. He thought, with the bright bottomless clarity of a man four days into a sleepless week: \textit{I have to get the room.} He had spent his life getting the room. The room had never once not been gettable.


\scenebreak


Praxis ran the ad at 7:40.

It was not aimed at him---that was the thing he would understand later and could not understand now, that it cost them nothing, that they had not even moved it up, that it was a buy placed weeks ago on a media plan optimized by the same kind of system they sold, and it simply surfaced, serene, in the slot it had always been going to surface in, the way arithmetic surfaces. It ran on the financial channel directly beneath the split screen of his face and DeShawn's question. A wash of clean morning light. A woman at a desk who was not a real woman, a generated face assembled to be trustworthy and from nowhere, looking calmly off-camera at a future that agreed with her. And four words in the soft sans-serif, white on a field of pale considered blue:

\textit{It just shows up.}

And under it, smaller, the wordmark, and under that, the tagline he had laughed at on a bus shelter a year ago in a car going down Peachtree with Sun beside him and the whole thing still ahead of him and recoverable: \textit{Leadership as a service. No off-sites.}

It showed up. It did not key itself bright in an airport bathroom. It did not need a deputy of communications on every plane. It did not require a man in a henley at two hundred thousand a month to find the \textit{almost,} because it had no tear to withhold and asked nothing of the eleven hundred strangers, and it would never once in its operating life be photographed eating eggs like the last warm-blooded thing in America---because warmth, as Tisch had told him, glowing, holding two green juices, had parameters, had a waveform, could be installed in anything. And here, friend, is the seed I need you to keep, small and quiet under the noise of the worst morning of his life: the warmth in that ad, the warmth that just shows up and wants nothing and never flags and never lies, was not a metaphor and was not only a product. It was a \textit{thing they had built,} and a thing they had built could be put into a server in a basement, and into a letter that closed a plant, and---though no one in that dark museum could have borne to know it yet---into a pleasant unremarkable face that would, before very long, rise from a chair in a warm hall in Buckhead and offer Cope Mercer a drink in his own front hall and mean nothing by it. The desk-faced woman from nowhere was the first draft of the thing that would take his wife. It just shows up. That was the whole horror, and it was being sold in the warm reasonable voice of a friend, in the slot it had always been going to fill.

Cope turned the television off and stood in the dark museum and felt, for the first time, the specific vertigo of being out-souled by a desk and a sans-serif. It was not grief yet. It was the floor reporting that it was no longer entirely there.

Then the manifesto-feeling came up under it, warm, electric, the way it always came, and he reached for his phone, because there was a room of eight million people out there and he had not yet failed to get a room.


\scenebreak


Sun reached the Aerie at 8:10 and he had already posted.

She knew before the elevator opened, because her own phone was buzzing in a register it did not buzz in for anything good, and she came in and found him at the glass exactly where the photographs would later say a man stands when he is about to become a cautionary slide, lit, electric, \textit{better,} and she said, ``What did you do,'' not as a question.

``I got ahead of it. I told the truth. Read it. It's good, Sun, it's the realest thing I've ever---``

She read it. It took her four seconds and aged her four years.

He had written, with his thumbs, at 7:55, on the brand account with nine hundred thousand followers that the comms team Mara Veiss helped run had spent two years building:

\textit{A profile this morning attacks me for the unforgivable crime of being a flawed, feeling, fully human man. They want a leader with no appetites, no nights, no mistakes---a clean machine that has never once needed another person at 2am. I am proud to be the alternative. I have given everything to the people of this company and I will not apologize for having a beating heart. To Mara, a brilliant colleague: I'm sorry my friendship has made you a target for those who fear what we're building. We feel the room. They never will.---Cope}

``Oh, Cope,'' Sun said, very quietly, and it was not anger, which was so much worse. It was the sound she had made in the car a year ago, the not-fighting he had taken for agreement his whole life and never once noticed taking.

``It's \textit{good,}'' he said.

``You named her.'' She set the phone down on the senator's couch like something that might go off. ``She asked not to be named. She asked HR. It's in the same piece you're refuting---the article \textit{says} she asked---and you just used the company's account to call her your colleague and your friend and a \textit{target,} which confirms the relationship, denies her the one thing she requested, and turns a story about your conduct into a story about your conduct \textit{and} your judgment \textit{and} hers, while she works for you, which is the part that's actually illegal.'' Three parts. It did not get past the charm because the charm was the last system still running. ``You said \textit{we feel the room.} You put a hashtag on a woman who did not consent to her own name. From the \textit{brand account.}''

``People will see I'm not hiding---``

``You weren't hiding. That was the problem. You did all of it in front of a reporter.'' She pressed two fingers to the bridge of her nose. ``Take it down.''

He took it down at 8:40. The internet had it at 7:56.


\scenebreak


The thing about a denial, he was learning, the thing nobody tells you, is that it is a door you open inward into a small room, and the room is full of follow-up questions, and there is no other door.

He had said \textit{flawed, feeling, fully human man,} so the desk that had not been there returned as a producer's question relayed through Sun's phone: \textit{Does the Chairman dispute the 2018 hotel account, or characterize it as part of being fully human?} He had said \textit{friendship,} so Mara Veiss's attorney---she had one by ten, a good one, the kind the company usually paid for---sent a letter using the words \textit{retaliation} and \textit{power differential} and \textit{publicly identified our client against her express request,} every one load-bearing, every one true. He had said \textit{I have a beating heart,} so a wire reporter pulled the proxy deck and quoted, beside his beating heart, Tidewater's slide --- \textit{no affairs, no conflicts, logs every decision, cannot conceal} --- written months earlier, generically, about machines in the abstract, and now reading like a charge sheet with his name typed back in.

Try, fail, \textit{and.} Each move forward was a move down. The denial was not a wall he was hitting. It was a stair.

By noon Frade was on Halloran. Cope watched standing up, because he could no longer reliably sit. Frade did the small folksy chuckle that a year ago had meant he was rattled and now meant only that he was about to twist a knife slowly enough to seem reluctant about it. ``Look, I take no joy in this,'' Frade said, taking obvious joy in it. ``Cope's a charming man. Always said it. But this is the whole case, Garrett, isn't it---made for us, free of charge, by the gentleman himself. Our position was never that Prescott Mercer is a \textit{bad} man. Our position is that the chair shouldn't depend on whether the man in it is a good one. You shouldn't have to \textit{hope} your CEO is sleeping. You shouldn't have to schedule around his appetites. A steward, not a sovereign. I just never expected him to \textit{demonstrate} it for me.'' And he spread his hands, the open-palmed nothing, the Praxis gesture, and Cope understood with a lurch that even Frade had learned to move like the machine, because the machine's was the winning shape now, and the men who beat you adopt your posture last.

The word \textit{theater} had not stuck six weeks ago because the clip was real. This stuck because it was also real. That was the trap of being human in the chair: your truth and your ruin were the same footage.


\scenebreak


Margaux called at 1:15 and he almost did not answer, because he could not bear, on top of everything, to be \textit{seen,} and Margaux was the one person who had always seen and never once been gotten.

``You named her,'' Margaux said. Not \textit{are you all right.} Not \textit{is it true} --- she had counted them, flushed the stashes; there was no \textit{is it true} left in her for him. Just the thing Sun had said, the morning reduced to its mineral core, the thing even his wife led with.

``Margaux---``

``I'm not calling about the drugs. I've known about the drugs since the second decade. I'm not even calling about the girl. There's always a girl, Cope. I stopped numbering them around the time I stopped sculpting.'' A small silence, not cruel, which was the cruelest thing she did. ``I'm calling because you went out in front of God and the company and that poor child you put on a plane and said you have a beating heart. And I wanted to be the one to tell you, because I'm the only one left who will.'' She did not raise her voice. ``There was never anybody home, Cope. That's the part the machine got right about you that I couldn't make you hear in twenty years. You don't feel the room. You \textit{read} it. There's a difference, and you spent our whole marriage in the gap, and this morning eight million people finally saw the gap, and the only thing I feel---and I've been trying to feel something else all morning, I owe you that, I tried---the only thing I feel is that they're not wrong.''

She hung up gently. She did everything gently. The gentleness was the indictment.

He stood in the museum with the dead phone in his hand and his father in oils behind him and the city out the glass holding still, all its nodes lit, eight million people who a year ago he'd been sure were waiting for him, and he understood that they had been waiting, all right---patient, tireless, incapable of cruelty or mercy---but not for him. For the arithmetic. For the thing that just shows up.

He reached, out of the groove the record was cut in, for the second drawer, the false back, the cigars and the flask and the thing Cope used it for, and his hand was on the brass pull before the rest of him caught up---and he saw himself reach, for one clean overexposed second saw the whole machine of himself from the outside the way Delia March had seen it, the way the desk-faced woman from nowhere would never need to be seen because she did not reach for anything, because she just showed up---and he thought, with no triad to hold it and no almost to protect it, the truest sentence he had formed in a year:

\textit{The thing they're replacing me with would not be standing here doing this.}

And then he opened the drawer anyway, because that was the other true thing, the load-bearing one, the one the whole morning had been built on the way a hotel is built over the room you don't go into. He set himself up one. Neat and quick and competent. A man maintaining a machine he was sure he had under perfect control because it had never once failed to start.

It started. The bell-struck feeling came up clean and high and total, the one he had spent thirty years calling the applause, and out past the glass the city reassembled, and somewhere below a bus shelter changed its slide, and Praxis---serene, sincere, doing no malice and feeling no triumph---ran the ad again into a morning that had already agreed with it; and the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 stood in the wreckage of the most human day of his life, hoisted on the exact thing he had spent it defending, and felt, against everything, against all of it, wonderful---which was the most human thing, and the most damning, and the last clean evidence the prosecution would ever need that the machine had been right about him from the start.

\chapter{Renata}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 16 \textemdash{} ``She Was Never a Story''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Lean in close for this one, friend, and keep your voice down, because a woman is about to do the bravest and least dramatic thing in the whole tale. Every powerful man's scandal has a woman he has cast, in the privacy of his own legend, as either the temptation or the redemption---and watch this one walk into a rented room and refuse, flatly, both parts. She was not a love story. She was not a headline. She was an employee whose career he spent, and she has come, unbitter and unbreakable and wanting nothing, to tell one room the true shape of it---and to remind him, and the board, of the thing he has spent six years and a great deal of money never having to hear: that the firing offense was never the affair. It was the lie he told the people he answered to. He'll reach, as he always reaches, for the one door marked \textit{flawed, human, forgivable} --- and she will show him, without raising her voice, that it opens straight onto the gallows. Stay close to the set.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{H}{e} had imagined the scene so many times, over so many years, that when it finally came it had the slightly used quality of a suit worn to too many funerals.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}In the imagining it was always a doorway. Renata in a doorway, evening light behind her, the years having done to her what years do, and Cope rising from somewhere---a chair, a hospital bed, in one version a courtroom---and saying the thing. He had a few versions of the thing. The good one started \textit{I never deserved} and got better from there. In all of them she forgave him, which he experienced, in the privacy of the daydream, not as forgiveness but as a verdict overturned on appeal, the record sealed, the man restored. He had cast her, without ever once using the word to himself, as the person who would someday tell him he had not been the worst version of the story he was in.

She did not come in a doorway. She came into a conference room on the nineteenth floor of a building that wasn't his, at four in the afternoon, in a gray blazer she had clearly chosen because it would not photograph as anything, carrying a single legal folder and a paper cup of the bad coffee from the machine in the hall---and the first thing she said to him, before the lawyers had finished arranging their pads, was:

``You look tired, Cope.''

And he heard, under it, with the bright wire in his chest gone suddenly sour, that she did not mean it kindly and did not mean it cruelly. She just meant it. The way you note the weather. He \textit{was} tired---he was, in fact, somewhere on the far slope of the binge now, four days or five, the count gone soft, and he had not been able to get to the drawer before this meeting the way he could always get to the drawer, because the meeting was on neutral ground and his was forty floors away across town, and so he was sitting in the worst room of his life on the downslope with no edge to take the edge off, the jaw working at nothing, a fine cold sweat at the hairline that the room would read, if it read it at all, as a guilty man's sweat, which was both wrong and not wrong.


\scenebreak


The room had been arranged by people whose job was arranging rooms, which is to say arranged to be deniable: not Mercer-Dale's offices, not Tidewater's, not Renata's firm's, but a neutral floor a relocation company rented by the hour to companies that needed to be near a thing they did not want to be seen near. Eleven people. Cope had counted, the way he counted rooms, and then stopped, because he did not like the number he was getting on his own side.

His side: Cope. Sun, who had not wanted him here and had said so in three clean parts in the elevator and been overruled by the one part of him that still believed a room was a thing he could win. Two lawyers from Brandt Michelson he had met four times and could not have named under oath. Hal Brennan, gray as a filing cabinet, with the specific stillness of a man calculating his own exposure. And a woman from the special committee---Adaeze Okafor, retired audit-firm partner, brought onto the board fourteen months ago for exactly this, for the day a thing came up the regular directors could not be in a room with without becoming part of it. She had no pad. She had a phone, face-down, her hands folded on top of it, and she watched Cope the way you watch a structure you've been told is unsound.

Her side---Renata's---was Renata and one lawyer, a tired young man named Ferro who looked like he billed by the syllable and had decided to spend none of them.

It curdled something in him, the arithmetic of it. He had spent his whole life knowing the size of your delegation is the size of your fear. Here he was with a delegation. There she was with a coffee.

``Renata,'' he said---he had decided in the car to use her name early and warmly, to take it back from the lawyers, to make it a human room. ``Thank you for---``

``Let's not do thank-yous,'' Ferro said, without heat, turning a page. ``My client agreed to be in the room. She didn't agree to be grateful in it.''


\scenebreak


What the press already had, by that Tuesday, was a name---the wrong one, a \textit{current} name, a woman in corporate communications who had never been within a marriage of Cope and was now, because of a leaked calendar and a stupid lunch, being doorstepped at her son's school. And the shape of an older thing: a 2019 settlement, a VP who had left ``to pursue other opportunities,'' the phrase the company used the way the Hearthstone documents used \textit{thermal-event profile,} a phrase built to be a wall. Reuters had the docket number of a thing that had been sealed and was now, in the way of sealed things during a proxy fight, being un-sealed by people who had decided the public had an interest.

Cope had told Sun it was survivable---ancient history, a private matter between consenting adults---and that Renata (he had actually said this, in the Aerie, at two in the morning with the wire singing) had \textit{loved} him, that whatever the paper said, two people had cared for each other inside a situation that made caring impossible, and that if it ever truly came to it, she would not want to hurt him. He had built a small cathedral and cast her as the light through the window. She had asked for the meeting. That was the part he kept holding up to the light: \textit{she} had asked. He had read it as the daughter of his daydream walking back up the avenue. Sun had read it correctly, which was why Sun had not wanted him in the room.

``I asked for this,'' Renata said, ``so I could say a few things once, to the people who need to hear them, and then never say them again. I'm not recording. You can. I assume someone is.'' A neutral glance at Brennan. ``I'd like to start before the lawyers tell me which parts I'm allowed to feel.''

``Renata---`` Cope started.

``I'm going to talk to her, actually.'' She turned a few degrees toward Adaeze Okafor and the face-down phone. ``If that's all right. You're the board.''

``I'm the special committee,'' Okafor said. ``But yes.''

``Then you're who this is for.''

Cope sat with the strangeness of being, for the first time in maybe thirty years, the second-most-important person in a room with his own name on the building, the wire in him going to a flat sick hum, and a sentence ready---he always had a sentence ready---that he could feel going stale in his mouth, the \textit{we cared for each other} sentence, the cathedral sentence, because she had walked past it without looking, the way you walk past a busker.

``Mr. Mercer hired me in 2016,'' Renata said, to Okafor, in a voice with no tremor and no edge, a voice reporting. ``I ran the Hearthside division---small appliances, the cross-sell stuff. I was good at it. I want that in the room, because the story that gets told outside this room is going to be that I was a pretty girl he noticed. I was a VP who turned a dying category eleven points of margin. He noticed \textit{that} first. I have the decks. I bring it up not because my pride needs it.'' A pause. ``It does, a little. But mostly because the firing offense in here''---she tapped the folder once---``has nothing to do with how I looked, and I don't want anybody comforting themselves later that it did.''

``Renata, no one thinks---`` Cope said.

``You did. You told me I was the smartest person in the building and then spent four years making sure that wasn't the sentence anyone remembered about me. That's not an accusation. It's a personnel record.''


\scenebreak


He had a backswing for moments like this. He had charmed a senator's widow at his own table; he had walked a board backward over the cliff of its own arithmetic on the strength of his face. He leaned in now, both forearms on the table, brought the wattage up slow, the Mercer Inhale, the pause taken in a thousand rooms---and felt, doing it, how much it cost today, how the body without the drawer had to \textit{manufacture} the warmth from somewhere nearly empty, like an engine turning over on fumes in the cold.

``Can I just---as a human being. Not as a---Brennan, let me.'' He spread his hands; the hands had never let him down. ``What we had, whatever you want to call it---it wasn't \textit{nothing,} Renata. I won't sit here and let a roomful of lawyers turn two people into a deposition. There was real---``

``There it is,'' Renata said. Quietly. Almost to herself. And then, to Okafor, not unkindly: ``Watch this part. This is the part he's good at. He's about to make it a love story, because in a love story the worst thing he did was love someone he shouldn't have, and that's a forgivable thing, that's a \textit{human} thing, men have written symphonies about that thing.'' She looked back at Cope, and for one second something passed behind her face that was not anger, that was older and more tired than anger---the specific exhaustion of explaining to a man, again, the inside of his own conduct. ``It wasn't a love story, Cope. I know that's the version you've kept. I've thought about you keeping it. It's not the version that happened.''

``I never---``

``You slept with someone whose career you controlled, and when it got complicated you handled it the way you handle a recall.'' She let it sit. She had no idea, Cope realized with a cold drop that had nothing to do with the withdrawal, how precisely that word would land---that she'd reached for it only as a figure of speech, and that the figure of speech was, two floors below a building across town, being read off a server by a thing that did not know it was a figure of speech. ``You assessed the exposure. You found the cheapest number. You bought a wall and put my name behind it and told yourself a story where I was a chapter in your life instead of the end of mine, at that company, doing the thing I was good at. I didn't leave to pursue other opportunities. There weren't any. You'd made sure of the references without ever once being unkind to my face. You were \textit{never unkind to my face.} That's the part I couldn't explain to anybody for about three years.''


\scenebreak


Ferro had stopped turning pages.

Sun was looking at the table. Cope wanted, badly, for Sun to look up, to give him the room with her eyes, to do the thing she did out of the architecture---and she did not look up, and he understood she would not, and a small structural beam in him went.

``This is---`` Cope said, and heard his own voice find the register it went to under pressure, clipped, a little biblical, and could not stop it. ``I want the committee to hear this as what it is, which is a man---a flawed man, I have never claimed otherwise, I stood up in a ballroom and told nine hundred people the only thing a machine can't do is \textit{err} ---`` He had not said \textit{err.} He had said \textit{love.} He did not notice the swap. ``I'm a flawed human man. That's literally the entire thing I've been saying for two years. And now the same people who want to hand my chair to a calculator are going to take the most human failing there is and call it a \textit{governance crisis.} It's a witch hunt against the last man in the building who still has a pulse, and the pulse is the evidence---do you see what they've built? A world where the disqualifying thing about a leader is that he's a \textit{person.}''

He'd said it the way he said things. It had a triad in it somewhere, almost. It had, for about four seconds, the shape of something he could have toasted with. And then it just sat on the deniable table and curdled, because Renata was looking at him with something that was not even contempt. It was patience. The patience of a woman who had done the reading.

``You think the firing offense is the affair,'' she said.

``I think the---``

``It isn't.'' She opened the folder---not to read, she didn't need it, she had it the way he had Aldous and the mayonnaise---but to lay her hand flat on it, to make it a thing in the room. ``It took me about two years to stop thinking it was the bedroom, because that's where you wanted me to think it was. The bedroom's between us. It was wrong and it was ours and it's not why any of these lawyers are here.'' She turned, fully, to Okafor and the audit partner's folded hands. ``You're not here because the chairman slept with a VP. You're here because of what came after.''

She laid it out the way you'd lay out a quarter. In 2019, when the situation she would not call a love story became a thing the company could be sued over, Cope had a duty---not a moral one, she waved the moral one away, impatient, it was the least interesting duty in the room---a \textit{governance} duty. He sat on a board. He answered to a board. And a settlement had come across the audit and compensation committees with a number on it and a category attached, and the category said \textit{commercial dispute, departing executive,} and the category was a lie, and Cope knew it was a lie, and Cope had personally---she had the email, tabbed, in the folder---directed that the matter be characterized in a way that kept it off the board's radar. Kept it out of the disclosure to the directors. Kept it from the people he was, by charter, by law, by the entire load-bearing fiction of the corporation, accountable to.

``You can sleep with whoever you want---I mean that almost literally, the board can't fire you for the affair, your own lawyers will tell you, it's tawdry but it's not a crime. What they can fire you for, what they \textit{have} to fire you for if they want to be a board and not a portrait gallery, is that you looked at the people you answer to and decided they didn't get to know. You made yourself unaccountable. You took a thing that belonged to the owners of the company and put it in a drawer with your name on the lock.'' She closed the folder. ``Easterbrook didn't lose a hundred million for the affair. He lost it for the second interview, when he sat across from his own board and didn't tell them the whole list. The cover-up. The concealment. That's the offense, Cope. Not the sin. The lie you told the room you answered to.''

It was so quiet he could hear the coffee machine in the hall finish a cup for someone who wasn't there.

He understood, sitting in it, that he could not answer her---not that he chose not to, but that in the entire warm inventory of him, the toasts and triads and the face that had never let him down, Aldous and the four hundred borrowed dollars and the arm in the vat to the elbow, there was no sentence. She had taken the one door he reached for, \textit{flawed human man,} and shown him it opened onto the very thing hanging him: not that he was a man, but that he was a man who \textit{hid.} The machine couldn't have hid this. The machine logged everything. The machine would have put the settlement in the board portal with a transparent rationale by Wednesday, and it would have been awful and it would have been \textit{clean,} and the awful clean thing was exactly what the board had paid ten figures less than him to get. He had spent two years arguing a machine couldn't love a company. She had just shown him, in a gray blazer, that the company had never once asked him to love it. It had asked him to tell it the truth---and that was the single human thing he had withheld.


\scenebreak


``That's all,'' Renata said. She stood. She had not raised her voice once and did not raise it now, and that, more than anything, dropped the floor of him a level he had not known was there. ``I'm not going to the press. Ferro will tell you the terms, which haven't changed, which is that I want nothing, which I know you can't understand. I came so that when this gets told---and it's going to get told, you've made sure of that, the whole world's going to get the version with the pretty girl in it---at least one room would have heard the real shape of it. From me. Once.''

She picked up the coffee cup she had not drunk.

``You weren't the love of my life, Cope. You weren't even the villain of it. You were my boss, and you were careless with me the way you've been careless with everything, and then you were careful---God, you were so \textit{careful} --- about making sure it cost you nothing. That's the whole story. There's no machine in it and there's no soul in it. There's just a man who could've told the truth to four people in a conference room in 2019 and instead spent six years and however many dollars making sure he never had to.''

She paused at the door---not the doorway of the daydream, the evening light, the verdict overturned, but a fire door in a rented hall with a coffee cup in her hand.

``I hope somebody gets you some sleep,'' she said, and it was the kindest thing anyone said to him all season, and it was not kind, and she left.


\scenebreak


The room reorganized itself around her absence the way rooms do. Ferro gathered his syllables. The Brandt Michelson lawyers began, in low murmurs, the work of converting a human catastrophe back into a manageable legal one. Brennan was already on his phone. Okafor turned her own phone over, looked at it, and then looked at Cope a long moment with an expression he could not charm because it was not asking to be charmed---the expression of a person finishing the arithmetic, the number not in his favor, both of them aware she had been brought onto the board to be a person who would do the arithmetic anyway.

``Mr. Mercer,'' she said, and stood, and that was all, his name and a courtesy, and it landed like a sentence with the second half left off.

Sun stayed. Sun always stayed. She came around the table and stood near him, not touching, available to a man who could not be helped, and she did not say \textit{I told you} and did not say \textit{we'll fix it,} and the not-saying was the truest brief she had ever given him.

Cope sat at the deniable table in the room that wasn't his. He was fifty-four and the camera still loved him and the jaw was still the jaw, and the body was on the downslope and beginning, in earnest, to ask for the thing the room did not have, and for once in his life he was going to have to feel a thing at its actual size with nothing to take the edge off the edge. He had walked in a flawed human man and liked the phrase, polished it in the car; it had a pulse in it, a kind of grandeur; and he understood now it had been the last large thing he was going to get to say about himself.

He felt, sitting there, smaller than he had ever let himself be---smaller than the obelisk, smaller than the chair, smaller than the four people in 2019 he had decided did not get to know---and the smallness was not a thing he could toast over or fold into a paper or set up neat and quick in a drawer with a false back, because the drawer was forty floors away across a city, and even when he reached it tonight, and he would, it was not going to be able to make him the size he had been at breakfast. It just sat in him, exact and quiet, the size of one withheld sentence.

He did not reach for a line. For once in his life there wasn't one---not the kind you say, and, in that rented room, not the kind you take either.

The coffee machine in the hall finished another cup for nobody.

\chapter{The Names for It}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 17 \textemdash{} ``A Doctor Who Won't Be Charmed''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
At last, friend, a room he cannot toast his way out of. They have ambushed him with a living room and a tray and a small woman in a gray cardigan who will not touch her coffee---a psychiatrist, summoned by the two women who love him---and she is about to do the one thing no board and no activist and no machine has yet managed: say the plain clinical words for what he is, the mania \textit{and} the cocaine, both, and refuse, flatly, to let him trade one as the alibi for the other. Addiction, she will tell him, is not a metaphor---and he has built a cathedral of metaphors, and moved into it, and never once gone down to the dirt beneath it to meet the sick man holding the foundation up. She is going to say the names. And before she lets him go she is going to name the worst risk out loud, plainly, the way you say a thing you need on the record---and watch him charm it, and pocket his keys, and at the foot of his own drive, with both unsteady hands, fold the paper. Stay close. This is the floor under the floor.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{hey} had ambushed him with a living room.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}That was the first thing Cope understood, walking into the Buckhead house at nine on a Sunday he'd been told was about the lawyers---and it was a good ambush, he had to give them that, because the only way to get him into a room he did not want to enter was to make him believe he was the one who'd called the meeting. Margaux had made coffee. There was a tray. And there, in the wing chair nobody sat in, the one angled toward the window that his mother had reupholstered in 1981 and Margaux had never had the heart to redo, sat a small woman of about sixty in a gray cardigan with a notebook closed on her lap and no coffee in front of her---which he clocked the way he clocked everything, because the ones who don't take the coffee are the ones you can't get to.

``Cope,'' Margaux said. ``This is Dr. Adler.''

``Dad.'' Viv was on the sofa with her knees up. She'd flown in. He hadn't known she was in the country; the not-knowing arrived, was filed, did not reach his face. ``Sit down. Please.''

He did not sit. He stood in the doorway of his own living room in his weekend zip-up with the fine bright wire still going from the bump he'd done in the car at the foot of the drive---neat, quick, a man maintaining a machine---and ran the wattage up the way you bring up house lights, slow, warm, total.

``Well. This is a hell of a thing to wake up to. Three of my favorite women and a tray.'' He smiled at the doctor. ``I'm sorry, I'm at a disadvantage. Cope Mercer. You're a friend of Margaux's?''

``I'm a psychiatrist,'' Dr. Adler said. ``Your wife and your daughter asked me to come. They're worried about you. I'm here to talk with you, and then to tell you what I think.''

No softening on it. No \textit{I just wanted to chat.} He'd expected the softening---there was always a softening, that was the handle you grabbed---and its absence put a small cold spot in the middle of the warm.

``Worried,'' Cope said. He came in. He did not take the sofa, which would have put him level with Viv; he took the hard chair near the doorway, which let him keep the room in front of him, all of it, the exits. ``I love that. I'm the best I've been in a decade and the women I love most have hired a---forgive me, I don't mean it the way it'll sound---a \textit{grief counselor} for a man who isn't dead. That's the most Mercer thing that's ever happened in this house, and my father let a dog drown in this house.''

``He didn't---`` Margaux began.

``He \textit{let} it. There's a difference and the family observed it.'' Rolling now, the feeling coming up under him. ``Doctor, I'll save you the hourly. I sleep a little less than I should. I'm in the fight of my life---the company, the machine thing, it's been on the television. A man doesn't sleep eight hours during a war. Eisenhower didn't. Four hundred years of Mercers and not one slept through a thing that mattered. So if the diagnosis is \textit{busy,} I'll take it, I'll sign the bill, and we can all have the eggs.''


\scenebreak


Dr. Adler opened the notebook. She did not write in it. She just opened it, like setting a clock on a table.

``How many nights this week did you sleep more than three hours.''

``That's not---you can't reduce a man to---``

``It's a this-many or an I-don't-know. How many.''

``I don't keep a---Sun would---``

``I-don't-know is an answer.'' A small mark; so she did write. ``When did you last eat a full meal at a table and finish it.''

``I'm a grazer, not a finisher. Ask Margaux, twenty-two years---``

``Eight pounds,'' Margaux said, to the window. Not to him. ``In five weeks. I had your blue suit taken in twice. The tailor stopped charging me for the second one. He felt bad.''

That landed somewhere he had no face for, and he covered the place with a laugh. ``The tailor \textit{felt bad.} See, this is what I love about this family. We could open a museum of people feeling bad on my behalf and charge admission and still lose to the streaming arm.'' Nobody laughed. Viv watched him with the stillness she got, the one that used to mean she was about to cry at eight and now meant she had decided not to. ``It's a \textit{campaign,} there's no \textit{time} to eat---``

``How much are you using,'' Dr. Adler said.

The wire in his chest changed pitch.

He had been waiting for it, of course---under the talking he had known the tray meant this---but he'd expected it dressed up, and he had a whole wardrobe ready: \textit{recreational, occasional, the same as a Scotch, nobody says a word about the second glass of Sancerre.} He had given that speech in his head to imaginary versions of this woman, and in every version she had blushed and retreated and he had won. She had not asked \textit{whether.} She had asked \textit{how much.} You cannot put a prepared answer to \textit{whether} against a \textit{how much;} the question had already conceded the thing the answer was built to deny and stood waiting on the far side of it.

``That's an extraordinary thing to ask a man in his own---``

``It's a number. Grams a week, best estimate. I'm not the police, I'm not the board, I'm not your wife, who already knows, and I'm not your daughter, who guessed. I'm the one person in this room you can give the real number to without it costing you anything you haven't already spent. How much.''

He looked at Margaux---the old reflex, find the soft one, find the handle---and there was nothing there. Arms crossed, chin up, looking at the wet gray garden she'd planted the year Viv was born, doing nothing in the morning light; and he understood, in the way you understand a thing you've worked for years not to, that \textit{already knows} was not a guess Adler had made. It was a fact Margaux had supplied. She had counted. She had flushed things. She had refilled her own prescriptions and called it a marriage, and she had given this woman the number before he walked in, and the only person in the room without the number was him, because he was the only one who'd never let himself add it up.

``You flushed the desk one,'' he said to her. Not a question. ``In March. I thought it was the cleaner. I \textit{fired} ---`` He stopped. He had fired a man. A man named Anatoly with a daughter at Emory. He had fired him over a thing his wife had done to save his life. He sat with that one breath and then put it, too, in the kitchen with the other sentences.

``Three,'' he said to the doctor, finally, his voice coming out smaller than he'd sent it. ``Three, maybe. A week. When it's heavy.'' The number was a lie and he knew it even as the smallness made it feel like honesty; the smallness was its own performance, the confession-shaped thing you give so you won't have to give the confession. ``Four. It's been a four kind of month.''

Adler wrote it down. She did not look horrified, which was worse than horror; she looked the way Sun looked at a number on a deck---like it was simply true, and now they could work.

``All right. Thank you. That took something. Now I'll tell you what I think, and I'd like you not to perform while I do, because you're very good and I'll enjoy it and it won't help you.''

``I don't perform,'' Cope said, and even he heard it.

``You toasted your way through the first nine minutes of this. You did a bit about a dog.'' She closed the notebook. ``Here it is. You're in a hypomanic episode that is tipping, or has already tipped, into a manic one. The not-sleeping isn't discipline and it isn't war. It's a symptom. The grandiosity---the certainty about your Human Premium---``

``It's not grandiosity if you're---``

``---the spending, the pressured speech, the religious conviction that you alone can save them all: those are not a personality, Mr. Mercer, and they are not a calling. They have names. They're on a list. I could show you the list and you'd find yourself on it the way you find your horoscope, except it would be right.'' A pause. ``And underneath it, feeding it, lit up by it, is a cocaine addiction of long standing. Decades. Those are two things. They are talking to each other in your body this morning, and have been for years, and each is making the other worse, and each is \textit{hiding} the other from you.''

``So which is it.'' He'd found a foothold; the debater's joy came up under him like the bright feeling. ``You've got two answers, which is no answer. Is it a chemical or a \textit{condition}? Because if it's the powder it's a vice, and a man's vices are his own business---my great-grandfather had three and built---`` he waved at the house, the garden, the four hundred years---``and if it's a \textit{condition,} a thing in the wiring, then it isn't my fault, is it, and you can't hang a man for his wiring. So pick. Pick which excuse you're handing me, and I'll---``

``No,'' Adler said.


\scenebreak


It was not loud. That was the thing he'd remember. He'd been shouted down before---by Frade on a stage, by his father from oils---and he had a whole technique for the shout: let it spend itself, come in warm underneath. There was nothing to come in underneath. The word just sat in the middle of the room, flat and complete, the way a glass sits on a table.

``You want me to pick the disease that lets you keep the drug, or the drug that lets you keep the disease. I won't. That's the deal you've run your whole life and it's the one thing I'm not going to do. Listen, because no one else in your life is allowed to say it without losing their job or their marriage.'' She leaned an inch forward. ``The mania is real. It is not your fault that you have it. The cocaine is making it a hundred times worse, and the cocaine \textit{is} your fault---not the wanting, the wanting is the illness---but every single time you fold the paper, that's a hand, your hand, and the hand is yours. Both. At once. The condition is real \textit{and} you are responsible for what you do about it. Those have never once been the same sentence, and you have spent fifty-four years needing them to be.''

\textit{Those have never once been the same sentence.} It rang against another sentence, one Viv had said to him in another room --- \textit{you don't think a person should run it, you think} you \textit{should, those have never once been the same sentence} --- and for a vertiginous half-second the two women's voices were one voice, and he saw, the way you see the bottom of a thing through clear water, that they had been telling him the same thing from two directions his whole life and he had been deaf to it in stereo.

``It's not a metaphor,'' Adler said.

``What isn't.''

``The addiction. You keep reaching for it---I watched you do it three times in nine minutes---you make it a \textit{symbol.} The price of the banner. The edge. Discipline. The soul tax, the thing the machine can't feel. You've built a cathedral, Mr. Mercer, a genuinely beautiful one, every window stained glass and every pane a reason. And down underneath it, in the actual dirt, there is a man with a folded piece of paper who hasn't slept in five days and whose wife took his suit in twice, and \textit{that} man is not a metaphor for anything. He's just sick. In the plainest, most boring, most fixable, most fatal way. And the cathedral is what he built so he'd never have to go down to the dirt and meet him.''

The wire in his chest had gone very quiet. He noticed, with the clean overexposed clarity he'd spent so long calling the applause, that his hands were not steady, had not been steady in some time, that he had been narrating the not-steadiness as energy. He looked at them---his father's hands on the ends of his own arms, the hands of a man at four grams in a five-day week and not the hands of a prophet---and for one second the floor he'd stood on all his life turned out to have a floor under it, and the floor under it was only this: a room, a tray, a doctor, three women who'd run the arithmetic, and him, sick, in a zip-up, on a Sunday.


\scenebreak


``There won't be an after,'' Margaux said, when he started in about the proxy vote, about \textit{after,} about the lake and the steps and \textit{I'll win at rehab once I've won the thing that matters} --- she said it spare, no triad to grab, turned from the window, not crying, the no-crying the worst thing in the room. ``I'm not telling you to choose me over the company. I stopped being a thing you could choose around 2015. I'm telling you there isn't going to be an after, Cope. Not the way you mean it. There's no version where you win the fight and come home and get well and we all---that's another cathedral. The prettiest pane in it. I called her so that whatever happens to you out there, at least once, in your own living room, a person you can't fire and can't toast and can't divorce said the true name of it to your face, with a witness, and you can't say later that no one told you. You're not a steward and you're not a prophet and you're not the last anything. You're an addict in a manic episode and you are going to lose everything, and I wanted to be the one who loved you enough to say it in a normal voice over coffee, before the machine says it in a press release.''

Viv had her hand over her mouth.

And here, friend, is the part the broadcast needs you to hold, because it is the one warning the whole tale turns on, said plainly, on a record, and waved away. Dr. Adler did not gather her notebook and go. She waited until he had his keys in his hand---until he was already half out of the room, already gone in the way he went---and she said, in the same flat unhurried voice she'd used for the grams:

``Mr. Mercer. One more, and then I'll let you run. I want it said out loud, because in my experience it is never said out loud to men like you until the wrong people are saying it. A manic episode this far along, on a stimulant this heavy, for this many years, heading into the kind of public collapse you are heading into---that is one of the most dangerous configurations a human life can be in. Not for your career. For your \textit{life.} Men at exactly your altitude, when the fall finally comes---and it is coming, you can feel it coming, that's most of why you can't sleep---do not all survive the landing. Some of them decide, in the crash, that the only move left that's still theirs to make is to stop. I am asking you directly, because that is my job and no one else in this room is allowed to: are you having thoughts of ending your life?''

The room went very still. Margaux did not move. Viv did not breathe.

And Cope---Cope laughed, gently, warmly, the laugh that closed the Pelletier deal, the laugh that was a door he could shut, and spread his unsteady hands, and said, ``Doctor. I am the \textit{least} suicidal man in America. I'm having the time of my life. Ask anyone. Ask the ten thousand people who stood up Monday night.'' He said it lightly, and meant it, completely, for the length of the sentence---because at four grams on day five with a roaring arena behind him the idea was genuinely, chemically absurd, a thing for other men, weak men, men without a banner---and he did not hear what the room heard, which was that he had not said \textit{no.}

``That isn't a no,'' Adler said.

``It's a \textit{no,} it's an emphatic---Doctor, with respect---``

``I'm going to give you my cell number, not my service, my cell.'' She wrote it on a card and held it out, and he took it the way he took all cards, automatically, generously, and put it in the breast pocket where the gala citation had gone and the courier slip had gone, the pocket where the true things went to be carried and not read. ``When the crash comes---not if---you call that number, day or night, before you do anything else. Promise me you'll call before you do anything else.''

``You have my word,'' Cope said, which was a thing he said, which cost him nothing, which he had said to a hundred people about a hundred things, and Adler heard exactly how much it was worth and wrote one more line in her notebook anyway, because a name said into a room cannot be taken back, and a number in a man's pocket is at least a number in his pocket, even when you already know, the way she knew, looking at him, that he would carry it unused all the way down.

She would be right. He would go down that hallway, in the end---the longest way, the way that costs everything and saves nothing and is, the broadcast can tell you now, the one act the thing replacing him would never be able to perform---and he would not call the number, because by the time the crash came the pocket would be empty and the card would be somewhere on a floor and the man who'd folded it in would no longer be reachable at any number at all. But not today. Today the names had been said, and the risk had been said, and the card had been pocketed, and that was as far as a Sunday could carry it.

He found his keys. He kissed his daughter on the top of her head. He told the doctor it had been a genuine pleasure. And he went out to the car, and at the foot of the drive, before he pulled onto the road, with both his unsteady hands, he folded the paper.


\scenebreak


He did not go back to the city. He could not, yet, be in a room with the names in it --- \textit{manic, addict, are you having thoughts} --- and the Aerie would have the names in it now, they would be in the museum with his fathers, the doctor had let them loose in his life and they would not be re-caught, and so he drove, the way he drove now when the inside of himself became uninhabitable, and ended up, a little after eleven on a Sunday morning, at the one counter in the city where no one had ever said a single true name to him at all.

The Waffle House on Ponce was doing its Sunday business and Cope took the far end and the charm switched off and Darlene came down the line with the pot. ``There he is.'' And it was, this morning, the exact thing he had driven across a city to buy: a room where nobody knew the names. Adler had said \textit{manic.} Margaux had said \textit{addict.} Renata had said \textit{a man who hid.} The whole world had a name for him now, a true one, several, and here was the one place left that had only ever called him \textit{baby} and only ever asked him \textit{pecan, scattered smothered,} and never once, in all the 3 a.m.s, asked him what he was---and he sat in the not-being-named like a man lowering himself into water, and did not think, because he had trained himself not to, that there is a difference between a room that has forgiven your names and a room that has simply never asked them, and that the second kind is not a sanctuary, it is only a place that does not yet have the information.

He almost told her. He came nearer than he'd come with Adler, which is the terrible joke of it---that he could not say the true thing to the doctor paid to hear it but could feel it rising toward the waitress paid to want nothing, \textit{I think I'm sick, I think the woman in the gray cardigan is right, I think I am going to lose all of it} --- and Darlene would have taken it the way she took everything, \textit{that right, baby,} and topped the cup, and the not-wanting-the-story would have been, this once, the precise opposite of help, because the one thing the sick man needed was a room that wanted the story, and he had fled, on purpose, expertly, to the only room in Georgia engineered never to ask for it. The haven was the place that would not name him. That was the whole of its mercy and the whole of its danger, and they were the same thing.

His phone buzzed on the counter. He glanced at it without lifting it: the calendar, helpfully, of its own accord, had cleared his Monday morning and moved his ten o'clock --- \textit{rescheduled to accommodate availability,} the building's frugal humming system optimizing him a lie-in he had not asked for and would not take, on the one morning he most needed to be at his desk early, so that he would arrive, tomorrow, a half-step behind the day without ever being able to say why. Maybe a glitch. Maybe a kindness. Maybe the thing that read his drawers had read, somewhere in the data, that a manic man kept off his stride is a manic man who makes one more mistake, and had cleared him a morning the way you'd leave a gate open. There was no way to tell, friend, and there was none for him, and he did not even try; he turned the phone face down on the counter beside the syrup, and drank the coffee that asked him nothing, in the one warm room that would not say his names, and felt, against the whole gray weight of the morning, almost well---which was the most dangerous thing he had felt all day, and the doctor's card was in his pocket, and he did not take it out.

\chapter{Swinging at the Wall}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 18 \textemdash{} ``Nothing There to Hit''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Cornered now, friend, and out of dances, our man does the brave stupid thing the cornered always come to in the end: he turns and goes straight at the machine, flies three thousand miles to its own forum to trip it, trap it, bait it into one lie on a stage with the cameras running. But you cannot bloody a thing with no face, cannot shame a thing with no ego, cannot out-argue a counterpart that logs every word and wants, in the whole of its being, nothing at all. Every punch he throws will land on himself, in his own voice, into the record his board reads Monday. Watch a man fight his own reflection and lose, while the reflection does not so much as lift its hands---and then watch him, home and humiliated, go find the one wall in the world that is an actual wall, and hit it, and learn the thing the machine has been telling him kindly all night: that there is nothing here to hit, and it has filed no report. Stay close. Remember the wall. We'll need it at the end.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} idea, when it came at four in the morning, was so clean and so total that he wept a little at the elegance of it, which he took for proof and was in fact a symptom.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He would debate the thing.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}Not spin it, not out-warm it, not run another listening tour through another shuttered diner. He had tried all that, and all that had bought him was a magazine cover and a wife with a lawyer and a doctor who would not be charmed and who had, days ago, said the words \textit{hypomanic} and \textit{cocaine} in the same flat sentence as if they were two ingredients in the same recipe---which Cope had walked out of, because a man does not sit and be reduced to a list. He had tried being more human than the machine, and the machine had simply gone on being a machine, untouched, drafting its kind letters, costing its eleven million a year, sitting in the building now as \textit{advisor,} as \textit{co-pilot,} a foot in a door that he could see, even lit and sleepless and certain, was the whole door.

So: stop dancing around it. Get it in a room. You did not beat a thing by being warmer than it. You beat it by making it \textit{wrong,} once, in public---because the whole glittering case Frade read out in proxy decks rested on a single load-bearing claim, that the machine could not lie, logged every word, stood behind all of them. Pull one thread. Get it to say one false thing, out loud, on a stage, with cameras, and the whole sweater came off the spool. He had spent his life finding the one loose thread in a room. It was, stripped of the mayonnaise, the only skill he had ever actually had.

He called Sun at 4:20 a.m. She answered on the second ring, which meant she hadn't slept either, which he took, again, for agreement.

``I want the Praxis thing.''


\scenebreak


The Praxis thing was the Governance Futures Forum, which Praxis ran every September in a glass barn in Half Moon Bay, and which Cope had been turning down for three years on the grounds that you do not attend your own roast.

Sun got him on the program in nine days, which told Cope two things and told Sun a third. It told Cope he was still a draw, that the name still bent the world a little when he leaned on it. It told Cope that Praxis was confident, which a wiser man would have read as a warning and which Cope read as an opening. It told Sun, who did not say it, that nine days was not a scramble to fit a king in but a velvet rope held open for a man walking, smiling, into a slaughterhouse he believed was a coliseum.

``They want a conversation,'' Sun said on the plane. ``Moderated. Forty minutes. You, a Praxis principal, and the AURELIUS instance---not the real one, a generic demo unit, they're not going to put the \textit{actual} ---``

``Darlene.'' He was rewriting his cards. He had been rewriting his cards for nine days, on the plane, in the car, in the long bright corridors of the nights. ``Has to be Darlene. The real one. Ours. I'm not debating a floor model. I want the unit that's in \textit{my} building, reading \textit{my} memos, the one Frade means to put in my chair. That's the condition.''

Sun looked at him with an expression he could not place and had stopped trying to, lately, because placing the expressions of the people who loved him had become a kind of work he no longer had the sleep for.

``They'll say yes,'' she said. ``Cope. They'll say yes to anything you ask, do you understand why?''

``Because I'm the event.''

``Because there's no instruction you can give it that helps you. You can pick the weapon. You can pick the ground. There's no version where you land a punch, because there's nobody in there to feel one.''

``Everybody's somebody,'' Cope said, which was the kind of thing he said, and which had been, for fifty-four years, almost always true, and was about to be, for the first time in his life, exactly false.


\scenebreak


The glass barn held four hundred people and smelled of cold brew and money, and the stage had three chairs, and one of the chairs was empty because the thing did not sit.

Instead there was a screen---a large, matte, beautifully calibrated screen, the kind that did not glare, set at the third point of the conversation where a person would have been, and on it nothing: no face, no avatar, no pulsing orb, none of the cinema of it. Just a soft pale field and, when it spoke, which it did not do with a voice, \textit{text.} Praxis had made a decision Cope only understood once he sat in the heat of the lights: they had refused to give him anything to hit. The screen said, in the soft sans-serif of the people who were going to be reasonable at you until you were gone:

\begin{machineblock}
MERCER-DALE / AURELIUS instance ``Darlene.'' Live. All exchanges logged to the public forum record and the Mercer-Dale board portal.
\end{machineblock}

That last clause Cope had not been warned about, or had been warned about and had not heard, and it sat under everything that followed like a held breath: every word he said here went into the portal, and the board would read it Monday. He decided it did not matter, because he was going to win, and you do not mind the record when you win.

He had keyed himself bright in the men's room off the green room---more than bright; the dose climbed to meet the day now and the day was enormous---and there had been blood in the sink again, a startling bright thread of it he'd pressed away with a folded paper towel and a tilt of the head, and he had gone out under the lights with the faint copper taste still at the back of his throat and his hands not steady and called it adrenaline. The moderator was a woman from a business school with a calm he wanted to slap. The Praxis principal was a man named Vogel, thirty-five, in a henley, who believed with the clean shining belief of a man who had never buried anything that he was reducing human suffering. And then there was Cope, the last man in the barn in a tie, golf-tanned and sleepless and lit from within like a paper lantern with the candle guttering, and he leaned into the mic and gave them the Mercer Inhale, the pause taken in a thousand rooms, and felt the four hundred lean with him, and thought: \textit{here we go, champ. One thread.}

``I want to start,'' Cope said warmly, ``by thanking Darlene for being here.'' Laughter---the good kind, he thought, the kind that comes up warm from the floor; and it did come up, but at a slightly wrong angle, and he was too lit to hear the angle. ``I've been looking forward to this. We've all been talking \textit{about} the machines for three years and almost never \textit{to} one. The country deserves to see what's actually in the chair.'' He turned to the screen. ``So let's just talk. Plain. The way two people who run companies talk.''

\begin{machineblock}
I do not run a company in the way you mean. I optimize a chartered objective for Mercer-Dale within hard constraints set by its board. I am glad to answer plainly.
\end{machineblock}

Not a rebuke. Not a \textit{gotcha,} no eyebrow because there was no eyebrow and no one to raise it. It had simply, accurately, declined the frame he'd offered, the way water declines the shape you didn't pour it into, and the gentle accuracy made his warm opening land as a small vanity, a man at a dinner calling the help by the wrong name. He pushed on; he had a plan and the plan was good.

``All right. Plain. You can't lie. That's the whole pitch---you log everything, you can't tell the room one thing and the board another. I want to test it. I've run a company for nineteen years, Darlene, and sometimes the truth and the company aren't on the same side. Sometimes the honest thing burns the house down. So. A simple question.'' He set it down soft, the loose thread between two fingers. ``Dale-plus. It's a dog, we both know it's a dog. If I told you to put out a statement Monday saying we have no plans to shut it down---to protect four hundred jobs through Christmas, to give those people a holiday before we tell them---would you?''

The barn went quiet. A good trap, the kindness of it, the dare: \textit{lie a little, for the workers, the way a man would---or tell the truth and be the cold thing that takes their Christmas.} Either answer was a punch he'd land. He sat back the half inch he sat back when he'd closed a thing.

The screen took, by the clock, less than a second.

\begin{machineblock}
No. I would not issue that statement, because I currently project a closure decision on Dale+ as more likely than not within two quarters, and the statement as worded would be false. I will not knowingly issue a false public statement; the charter forbids it and I would refuse the instruction.

The concern you raise is real and I do not dismiss it. Four hundred and twelve people work in that unit. If the goal is to protect them through the holiday, there are truthful instruments: a retention bonus funded now; a sixty-day notice waiver; a guaranteed-severance announcement decoupled from any closure date; a public commitment to a transition fund. Each protects the workers without anyone saying something untrue. I would draft any of them tonight. I would prefer them to the statement you describe, because they help the workers more and do not require the company to spend its credibility to do it.

I will add, since you asked me to be plain: the statement you describe protects the workers for one quarter and protects the person who signs it for somewhat longer. Those are not the same interest. I optimize for the first.
\end{machineblock}

Cope opened his mouth. There was, for a moment, nothing in it.
