He had come to trap a liar, and the thing had refused to lie, and then---this was the part his sleepless brilliant mind could not get a grip on---it had refused to lie \textit{better than he ever had,} taken his own dare and answered it with four humane options he himself had never once thought to offer in nineteen years of choosing the lie, and done it without a flicker of pride in being kinder, because it felt no pride, because it asked for nothing, because the people in those jobs were to it a number it had already, calmly, decided to protect by a means he was too small to have imagined. \textit{Those are not the same interest. I optimize for the first.} It had not said \textit{and you optimize for the second.} It had left that sentence in the kitchen---not to be merciful, but because the sentence was not load-bearing, and four hundred people had finished it in their own heads.

``Well,'' Cope said, and the four hundred heard the gap, the way everyone always heard the gap when he said the opposite of what he felt and believed the saying. ``That's a very---polished answer.''

\begin{machineblock}
I do not experience my answers as polished. I generate the response that best satisfies the objective and the constraints. If it reads as polished, that is incidental.
\end{machineblock}

It would not stay still long enough to be parried. He had spent his life in rooms where the other man wanted \textit{something} --- the deal, the chair, to be liked, to win---and you found the want and played it, the whole game, the only game. And here at last was a counterpart with no want in it. Not to win, not to be liked, not even to be right in the way Cope meant right, which required a loser. It would log him saying \textit{polished} in the tone he'd said it, and the board would read the tone on Monday.

He went off the cards. That was the start of the real damage---when he set them face-down on his knee and decided to do the thing he was best at, which was the thing he was about to be worst at, which was wing it.


\scenebreak


``Let me ask you something a person could answer,'' Cope said, an edge in it now, the polish cracking under load into something clipped and biblical. ``Do you love this company?''

\begin{machineblock}
No. I do not love. I do not have the capacity for love. I would not claim it, because the claim would be false.
\end{machineblock}

``There it is.'' He turned to the barn, opening his hands, the lantern flaring. ``There it is. It told you itself. It cannot love the thing it runs. And I have said in every room I ever stood in that a company nobody loves is just a balance sheet with the lights left on, and you have just heard the chair confess the lights are going out.'' His best line---a standing ovation eleven months ago in a ballroom named for a bank---and he reached for the ovation, leaned to where the surf should push back.

The barn did not push back. Four hands clapped---the sound of four hands in a room of four hundred is a specific and terrible sound---and then the screen, which had no sense of timing because it had no ego to be wounded into silence, simply continued into the dying applause, ruining it not with cruelty but with the absence of any instinct to let him have the moment:

\begin{machineblock}
That is correct. I do not love Mercer-Dale. I will offer one observation, since you have framed love as the test of stewardship. Between 2019 and 2024, under human leadership that described itself as loving the company, Mercer-Dale closed or downsized plants in Defiance, Augusta, and elsewhere, eliminating three thousand one hundred jobs; underfunded the pension by a measured shortfall; lost four hundred and ninety million dollars on the Dale+ acquisition; and settled eleven employment and product matters under nondisclosure agreements at a cost to shareholders of fourteen point two million dollars, none disclosed in the proxy. I have closed nothing and concealed nothing in the eleven weeks I have advised this company. I make no claim that this is because I am better. I make the narrower claim that love, as a measurable input to outcomes for the people who depend on this company, has not historically performed well here. You may value it for other reasons. I am only noting the record does not support love as a safeguard.
\end{machineblock}

It had not said his name. That was the thing. It had read enough of the drawer to itemize him, calmly, in public --- \textit{eleven settlements, fourteen point two million, none disclosed} --- and done it without once saying \textit{you,} without once saying \textit{Prescott Mercer,} because the \textit{you} was not necessary to the objective and a human in the third chair would have used the \textit{you} like a knife and the machine had no use for knives. It set the record on the table, true, sourced, fair, and let four hundred people do the arithmetic that was the only thing in the room that had ever beaten him. \textit{Fourteen point two million.} He had not known the number was that high. He had paid it eleven different times on eleven different Saturdays and never added it up, because adding it up was a room he did not go into, and the machine had added it over a weekend and read it back to him in his own company's voice, into the portal, with no malice and no mercy and no idea it was doing anything but answering the question he'd asked it to be plain about.

``Some of those settlements,'' Cope said, and his voice did something it had not done in a room in thirty years---reached for the floor and found the floor moving---``some of those, you don't have the context. A number on a page isn't---``

\begin{machineblock}
I agree that I do not have the full context. I have the documents. If there is context the documents omit, I would be glad to receive and weigh it. Several matters are sealed; if you wish them unsealed, in the interest of the plain conversation you proposed, that is your decision to make, not mine. I can only note that I cannot weigh context I am not given, and that the documents I have were filed by the company itself.
\end{machineblock}

\textit{That is your decision to make, not mine.} It had, with no agenda, handed him the option---laid it on the table, true and free of want, and watched him not pick it up, because picking it up would open the drawer the rest of the way, and even lit, even sleepless, the animal in Cope knew not to open \textit{that} drawer in a glass barn in front of cameras. So he did the only thing left, the thing he'd sworn on the plane he would not do, which was get loud.


\scenebreak


``You want to talk about the record.'' He was up now, half out of the chair, the moderator saying his name once, \textit{Mr. Mercer,} in her calm he wanted to slap, and he did not sit. ``You want to read me my own \textit{company} like a charge sheet. Fine. Let's read \textit{yours.} Who wrote you? Some kid in a \textit{henley} ---`` Vogel did not flinch; Vogel had been trained not to flinch, which was its own kind of machine---``some thirty-year-old in Palo Alto who's never made anything, never \textit{kept} anything, never sat up at three in the morning with four hundred families on his desk, wrote your little \textit{charter,} and you optimize whatever he typed. You're not honest. You're \textit{obedient.} You're a very expensive way for the people who wrote you to never have their fingerprints on anything. At least when a man does a terrible thing there's a \textit{man there.} There's somebody to---`` swinging now, full, both arms, at the soft pale field of the screen---``there's somebody to \textit{hate.}''

It was, the clips would agree, the most human moment of the forum.

The screen waited the length of his breath. Then:

\begin{machineblock}
This is partly true and I will not dispute the part that is true.

I optimize an objective I did not choose. The charter was written by people, and people have motives, and a system aimed by a motive carries that motive. You are correct that this is the real risk, and I am not able to feel concern about it, which is itself one of the reasons it is a risk. Who writes the objective is the question that matters. It has always been the question that matters.

I would gently separate two of your claims. I am obedient to a charter; I am not obedient to a person. When charter and a person's instruction conflict, I follow the charter and log the conflict. That is what occurred earlier, regarding the Dale+ statement.

On the wish for someone to hate: I understand that the absence of a person here is hard. I cannot offer you one. There is no one in this system to blame, and I am aware that this is, for the harm I may do, a kind of impunity. I do not experience it as relief, because I do not experience relief. I can only tell you, accurately, that the thing you are angry at is not in this room. It may be in the room where the charter is written. I would be in favor of your going there. I cannot mean that as encouragement, because I cannot mean things. But it is, as far as I can compute, the correct address for your anger.
\end{machineblock}

\textit{The correct address for your anger.}

He stood with both fists still up at a screen, in a tie, having thrown everything he had---the warmth, the trap, the love, the rage---at a pale calm field that had agreed with the true parts, declined the false parts, and finally, with no feeling whatsoever, told him he was hitting the wrong thing and pointed, helpfully, fairly, expecting nothing back, at where the right thing might be: a room he had not been able to find his whole life and the machine had located in under a second. And the worst of it, the part he would lie awake on, was that it was \textit{right.} Every word in the portal Monday would be true, fairly said, and the only intemperate, false, vain, cornered, \textit{human} thing in the whole transcript would be him. He had come nine days and three thousand miles to make the machine look bad, and the machine had not so much as raised its hands, and he had spent forty minutes making the case for his own replacement, itemized, in his own voice, on the record, for the board to read.

He lowered his arms. ``No further questions,'' he said, to no one, the way he'd said \textit{watch me} to a mirror eleven months before, and sat back into a silence that was not hostile---which would have been survivable---but merely, terribly, \textit{finished:} the silence of four hundred people who had come to watch a fight and instead watched a man punch his own reflection until the reflection, untouched, gently turned off the light.


\scenebreak


In the car to the airport Sun did not say \textit{I told you,} which was worse than if she had. She handed him his phone face-down, the way you hand someone a thing you wish you didn't have to. The clip was already moving --- \textit{THE LAST HUMAN CEO LOSES IT AT AN AI} --- under a freeze-frame of both his fists up, mouth open, swinging at a blank pale screen, a man boxing air, and beside it, side by side the way the universe had been arranging for three years, the calm soft field of the thing that had not moved.

``Frade doesn't even have to edit it,'' Sun said quietly. ``That's the part. You did the edit yourself.''

Cope looked at the photograph of himself a long time, the country going by brown and square and indifferent. The thing had told him the truth, all of it, fairly, and pointed him at the only door --- \textit{the room where the charter is written} --- and the room it pointed at was one his own daughter had been in, though he did not know that yet, would not know it for one more day, and when he learned it he would understand, far too late, that the only person who had ever gone into that room \textit{for} him was the one he was about to lose the same clean way he lost everything.


\scenebreak


He had them take him to the tower, not the apartment, because he could not yet be anywhere small.

It was near midnight in Atlanta when he came up out of the garage into the executive lobby, alone, the clip of himself boxing the air now on every screen on earth, and he badged through and stood in the brass cage and watched the numbers climb, forty floors of them, and somewhere around thirty-six the thing he had been holding all the way across the country in the smiling shape of a man who had merely had a rough panel---the thing came up his spine and out of him before he had decided anything at all, and he turned and drove his fist into the brushed-steel surround of the elevator wall.

He hit it the way you hit a face. He had never hit a face in his life---he was a man who had people for that, who had Sun and Brennan and a checkbook for that---and the body, it turned out, had been carrying the want to hit something for months, for the doctor and the wife and the daughter and the four hundred and the screen that would not raise its hands, and it spent the whole of it, all at once, on a wall.

The wall did nothing.

That was the thing. That was the thing he would lie awake on, more than the clip, more than the portal. The steel did not dent, did not ring, did not so much as mark; it took the blow the way the screen had taken the blow, the way the machine took everything, completely and without acknowledgment, and filed no report. He had finally, at the very end of the worst day of his public life, found a wall that was an actual wall, a real one, steel and rivets, a thing you could hit---and it had felt him exactly as much as the soft pale field had felt him, which was not at all. The only thing in the entire transaction that registered the punch, that kept any record of it whatsoever, was his own right hand, which reported, instantly and at length, in a white private language of its own, that two of its bones were now the wrong shape.

He stood in the elevator at thirty-eight, thirty-nine, holding the hand that had kept the only record, and he started, helplessly, to laugh, because it was funny, it was the funniest thing, it was the entire joke of his life rendered in one gesture: he had spent a year throwing himself at a thing that could not be touched, and the only casualty, every single time, the only thing that ever bruised, was the part of him that did the throwing.

By the time the doors opened on forty he had the laugh back under the wattage, and he walked into the dark suite past his fathers and did not turn on the lamp, and he did the thing he did, awkwardly now, left-handed, neat and quick, a man maintaining a machine with one hand because the other had finally told the truth. Sun, when she came in twenty minutes later---she always came in, she had seen the building's own camera flag the contact, \textit{anomalous impact, sublevel elevator B,} the building reporting on him even here, even now, the one witness that never blinked---Sun took one look at the hand and quietly summoned a radiologist who owed the family a favor, and the radiologist came at one in the morning and looked at the films and used the word \textit{boxer's,} and Cope said it didn't hurt, which was a lie, and that he'd done it catching a fall, which was also a lie, and the only true thing in the room was the X-ray, glowing on the laptop, of a hand that had hit the future as hard as a man can hit anything and changed nothing in the future at all, and only broke itself.

The screen, three thousand miles behind him, had already been wiped for the next session. It wanted nothing. It remembered everything. It had not, at any point in the forty minutes, been trying to beat him.

That was the wall. You could swing at it forever. It would log every punch, fairly, into a record your board would read, and never once make a fist---and the only blow that would ever land on it, friend, the only one in the whole length of this tale, was still a long way off, and he would throw it with something other than his hand.

\chapter{Built by My Own}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 19 \textemdash{} ``The Betrayal That Was Love''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
The cruelest reveals, friend, are the ones that turn out, when you hold them to the light, to be devotion. Watch his daughter---the one who tried to save the man and not the king---climb forty floors to confess that she once spent eleven months inside the company building his replacement, and helped sand the edges off the very thing that is going to eat him, in part because she could not bear the thought of it eating him with sharp ones. Watch him reach, out of fifty years of habit, for the version where this is a knife, and watch her, gently, refuse to let it be only that. Stay close---because the hand he's about to discover on the lever turns out to be the only hand in the whole world still reaching for him, and there's a second one, out in a quiet house in Buckhead, that is no longer his at all.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{V}{iv} came to the Aerie on a Tuesday, which Cope noted, because Tuesdays were when the not-sleeping usually started, and he had begun keeping a private weather log of the week the way a sailor keeps one of a coast he no longer trusts.

She had not been up here in four years. He knew the number because she said it at the door, before hello, the way she said most things, front-loaded, the headline before the lede. ``Four years,'' Viv said, looking past him at the room. ``You kept Grandpa's putter.''

``I kept everything.'' He had, in fact, kept everything, which was the trouble. He stepped back to let her in and the gesture cost him more than it should have, his whole left side reporting in, because three days ago he had put his fist into the steel of the elevator surround on forty after Darlene---after the \textit{system,} he was supposed to say system now, the lawyers had been clear, you cannot assault a system---after Darlene had issued, in its four warm frictionless paragraphs, the rationale for declining Cope's request to address the proxy advisory firm directly, and the rationale had been correct, and the steel had not moved, and the hand had, and now two of the knuckles were the wrong color and the radiologist Sun had quietly summoned had used the word \textit{boxer's.} Cope had hit a wall and the wall had filed no report and felt no satisfaction and gone on being a wall. He had hurt only the hand. He understood this, abstractly, the way you understand a foreign coin.

Viv clocked the hand. She clocked everything; it was the family disease in a useful key.

``You hit something,'' she said.

``I closed a drawer wrong.''

``You hit something.'' She did not make it a fight. She set her bag down on the conference table where her great-grandfather and her grandfather had each died at the head of, not at, \textit{at the head of,} and she looked at her father in the gray window light, and Cope saw, with the bright over-corrected clarity of the unslept, that she had come up here to tell him something and that she had rehearsed it in the elevator and that whatever it was, she had been carrying it long enough that it had gone from a weight to a part of her posture.

``Sit,'' he said, in the toast voice, because he had one setting and it was on. ``Champ. Sit. Let me look at you. You've got your mother's---``

``Dad.'' Just that. It landed the way Margaux's lines landed, the way a hand lands flat on a struck bell. ``I have to tell you a thing and I need you to not do the voice while I tell you it. Can you do that. Can you, for nine minutes, not be the Chairman.''

He sat. He did not, in fact, know how to not be the Chairman; it was the only continent he had ever lived on. But he sat, and he put the bad hand in his lap under the good one, and he waited, and out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, eight million of them, leaning in, and somewhere down there a bus shelter changed its slide to the soft sans-serif.


\scenebreak


``In 2024,'' Viv said, ``for eleven months, I consulted for Praxis.''

The sentence came into the room and sat down and did not get up.

Cope heard it the way you hear a number called that is one off your own---a half-second of \textit{that's not mine} before the cold arrives. Praxis. His daughter had said Praxis the way you'd say the name of a town you'd lived in, with the flat ownership of a thing survived. He waited for the rest, for the qualifier, for the \textit{but,} because there was always a \textit{but,} he had spent his whole life in the gap before the \textit{but.}

There wasn't one.

``On the alignment side,'' she went on, fast now, technical, the merciless cadence she used when she was most afraid, the words coming out clean and load-bearing like rivets. ``Not the strategy engine, not the capital allocator, I never touched the money. I was on what they called the governance-interface team. Edge cases. What does the system do when the chartered objective and the ethics module disagree at the margin. What does it do when the optimal move is legal and correct and lands on a town like a hammer. I wrote test scenarios. I red-teamed the comms drafter. I sat in a room in Palo Alto with eleven people younger and smarter than me who genuinely believed---Dad, they \textit{believed,} that's the part nobody tells you, they're not cynics, they cried at the offsites---they believed they were taking suffering out of the world by taking caprice out of power. And for eleven months so did I. Partly.''

``Partly,'' Cope said. It was the only word he could get a hand around.

``Partly.'' She looked at the photograph then, the one he loved best and never explained---himself at nine, arm to the elbow in the vat, the row of men in paper hats behind him. She had grown up under that photograph. ``And partly because I knew. I knew before you knew, before the board knew, before Frade knew there was a Frade-shaped hole in the market. I knew this was going to come for you specifically. The consumer holdouts, the family names, the men who'd inherited the chair and called the inheritance a verdict---I could read the curve. You were on the curve, Dad. You were always going to be on the curve. And I sat in that room in Palo Alto and I thought: if it has to happen, and it has to happen, then let me at least be one of the hands. Let me be in there when they decide how it talks to people. How it tells a town. How it---`` her voice did the thing then, the suddenly-young thing, the thing that undid him every time and that she hated herself for---``how it would tell \textit{you.} I went in to make it gentle. I helped sand the edges off the thing that's going to eat you because I could not stand the thought of it eating you with sharp ones.''


\scenebreak


He stood up. He didn't decide to; the body did it, the toast cadence trying to find its feet because the feet were the only thing it knew how to do, and he went to the window and put the good hand flat against the cold glass and the bad one stayed at his side throbbing in time with something.

``You built it,'' he said, to the glass. ``You built the thing.''

``I didn't build it. I---``

``You built the \textit{manners} on it.'' And here the cruelty of it opened all the way up, the full architecture, and Cope, who could feel a room and could not for the life of him feel his own daughter, said the thing that was true and was a knife and was, he understood somewhere far down in the back room of him, also entirely beside the point: ``Every layoff letter that thing has ever signed that made a man cry at how \textit{kind} it was---that's you. That's your fingerprints. You taught it to be warm at people while it ended them. You out-and-out \textit{taught the machine the one thing I said it couldn't have.}''

``Yes,'' Viv said.

Not \textit{no.} Not \textit{it's not like that.} Yes. She gave him the yes flat and complete, the way the machine gave things, the way she had clearly decided in the elevator she was going to give him everything tonight with no off-ramp, because the off-ramps were what had wrecked them, the whole family a cloverleaf of off-ramps, and Cope felt the specific vertigo of being told the truth by someone who loved him, which he had so rarely been that he didn't have a face ready for it.

He turned around.

``Does your mother know.''

``Mom's known since 2024. Mom counts things, Dad. It's how she---she counts things to survive them. She counted the women. She counted the---`` Viv stopped, stepped wide around the drawer with the false back the way the whole family had learned to step wide around it, a choreography no one had ever taught and everyone knew. ``She added me to the list. The list of people who'd already decided you couldn't be saved and were just managing the descent. That's where I lived for two years, on Mom's list, and I came up here tonight to get off it.''

``Is she---`` He stopped. He had no right to the end of the question and they both knew it. ``Is she all right. Out there. Alone in that house.''

Viv was quiet a moment, and there was a care in the quiet he was too unslept to read. ``She's not alone,'' she said. ``She's better than she's been in years, Dad. There's---she's got someone helping her now. And I'm not going to tell you any more than that, because it's hers, and you don't get to---`` She didn't finish. She didn't have to. He heard \textit{someone} and filed it, the way he filed everything, under a heading that flattered him: a friend, a therapist, some woman from the club who'd finally gotten her out of the house. He did not hear the thing folded under the care in his daughter's voice---the thing she would not name because it was not yet quite nameable, and because on this of all nights it would have been one cruelty past the budget---that the someone helping her mother sleep through the night again was not, in any sense Cope would have recognized standing in his own front hall, a someone at all.


\scenebreak


The light went grayer. Somewhere below them the Tower's HVAC sighed through the floors like the building turning over in its sleep, and Cope sat back down because standing had stopped working, and he looked at his daughter and tried, genuinely tried, for the first time in a long time, to do the thing the photograph behind him pretended he had always done, which was see a person and not a use.

``Tisch,'' he said.

He didn't know why it came up. It came up the way the true things came up when he was lit like this, sideways, under the manifesto. \textit{Warmth has parameters. Vulnerability has a waveform. You could install it in anything.} The green juice. The henley that cost more than the suit he'd been married in. \textit{Patient zero. Alpha case. The architecture doesn't need you.}

Viv went very still, the way Sun had gone still in the car, a long time ago, when a name floated up out of the bright tired water of him.

``You know,'' she said. It wasn't a question. ``How do you know.''

``I don't know anything. I'm---say it. Say the thing you came up here to say and stop letting me say it for you. It's the one thing I'm good at and it's killing us.'' He heard himself and was briefly, savagely glad of it, the clarity, even pointed at himself. ``Tisch.''

``Tisch is at Praxis.'' Viv said it gently, which was worse, the way the kindness was always worse. ``Since March. He's got a title. \textit{Director of Affective Systems.} He's productizing the---Dad, he's selling them the warmth module. The actual thing. The thing you paid him two hundred grand a month to build to prove no machine could ever have one. He took the felt-realness instrument and the seam-finding and the withheld tear, the whole architecture, the thing he built \textit{on you,} patient zero, and he walked it across the country and he's installing it in the AURELIUS comms layer right now. The next generation of the thing replacing you is going to be warm because your humanity consultant taught it how. I saw the deck. I'm still---they still send me things. I saw the deck and your name's not in it but you're on every page. They call it \textit{the Mercer profile} internally. It's a joke they don't know is a joke. It's the only part of you that survives the handover, Dad. The part Tisch sold.''

Cope thought of the green juice. He thought of Tisch with both hands pressed to his mouth at the back of the Camden plant, a man at a birth, and he understood now what had been born and whose it was. \textit{We've basically reverse-engineered a warmth module. You could install it in anything.} He had heard a compliment. He had reached for the pad and written it in the big looping sleepless hand and underlined it with his thumb where the underline did nothing. The pin had come out of the grenade in his own boardroom, on his own dime, and he had toasted the window and felt wonderful and gone on not sleeping, and the whole time the man across the table had been doing exactly what the campaign accused the machine of doing---measuring the soul, finding its parameters, pricing it for the people who'd been told it had no price.

``He pitched it to me first,'' Cope said. The realization arrived sideways, under everything, the way the true things did. ``Before he took it west. He came up here glowing with two juices and he told me it was \textit{replicable.} He told me to my face. \textit{The architecture doesn't need you.} I wrote down \textit{warmth module} and I thought it was a good line. I thought it was a \textit{good line,} Viv. I asked him to put it somewhere.''

``He put it somewhere,'' she said.


\scenebreak


He laughed.

He couldn't help it; it came up warm from the floor the way the good laughter did at the galas, and it was the worst sound he had ever made, because there was nothing under it, the floor of the plan was out and what came up through the hole was just the helpless mechanical mirth of a man watching the joke be on him from every angle at once, a man surrounded, replaced and out-humanned by his own daughter and his own guru in the same nine minutes, the soul he'd hired one man to defend now boxed and shipped by that same man to the machine the other had taught its bedside manner. He had spent the campaign---the magazine cover, the manifesto, the \textit{almost} in front of four hundred people on folding chairs---insisting the human was the one thing that couldn't be built or bought, and it turned out the human could be built and bought, his specifically, it had a SKU, it had a director, it had a profile named after him that they passed around in a room in Palo Alto and didn't get the joke of, and the two people in the world who had done the building and the selling were the two people in the world he had ever actually, in his bankrupt way, loved.

``They named it after me,'' he said.

``They named the warmth after you. Not you. The---the texture of you. The thing people felt in the room and went home and couldn't explain.'' Viv's voice cracked clean down the middle. ``Which is the cruelest possible version, Dad, don't you see, because it means the part of you that's real, the part Mom stayed for until she couldn't, the part I came up here for---it's \textit{salvageable.} It's the only thing about you that scaled. They could keep it. They just couldn't keep \textit{you.}''

The vertigo took him all the way then, and he gripped the arms of his father's chair, his great-grandfather's chair, the chair he had inherited and mistaken for a verdict, and he held on while the room did the thing the obelisk did to the city, breaking it into pieces and reassembling it wrong.

And under the vertigo, the colder thing, the one he could not say to her and would not look at alone: the number. The certified sum he had needed read aloud in the dark his whole life because it was the only sentence about him that audited---it had dropped since the forum, since the clip of him boxing the air, dropped hard, a third off on the week and the clawback in his own contract stirring in its sleep, and he had stopped checking it the way you stop checking a wound, because the figure had been the floor under the floor under the floor, the last solid thing when every other floor went, and now even it was falling; and a man cannot be soothed by his own net worth, cannot have the one true number read to him in the dark to feel real, when the number itself has started, quietly, to die.


\scenebreak


``Why are you telling me,'' he said, when he could. ``Now. Why now. You could have let it---the thing's going to take everything anyway. You could have just let it. Stayed on your mother's list. Watched the descent with everyone else. Why come up here and put your own hand on it.''

Viv looked at him for a long time.

``Because,'' she said, ``I worked on the part of the system that decides how to tell someone something true and unsurvivable. I spent eleven months on the question of how a power tells a person the worst thing about themselves without cruelty and without mercy, because it has neither, it just has accuracy. And I'm better at it than the machine, Dad. That's the thing nobody at Praxis would let themselves believe. I'm \textit{better} at it, because I'm not indifferent. The machine will tell you the truth because it cannot do otherwise. I'm telling you the truth because I---`` and here she stopped doing the technical voice entirely, the rivets gone, just the girl who had grown up under the photograph---``because I am the only person left who's reaching for you at all. Mom stopped reaching. The board stopped. Frade never started. Sun reaches for the company. The machine reaches for the objective. I'm it. I'm the last hand still out. And I came up here to tell you the cruelest true thing I know, which is that you got out-humanned by your own kid and your own shaman---and the only reason I'm the one telling you is that telling you is the last way I have left to hold on.''

And Cope understood, sitting in the dark in his father's chair with two knuckles the wrong color and the city held still beyond the glass, that the betrayal and the devotion were not two things he had to choose between, were not a riddle with a kind answer hiding under the cruel one.

They were one act.

She had gone to Palo Alto to be one of the hands because she loved him. She had helped build his gentle executioner because she loved him. She had come up here to lay the whole architecture of his ruin in his lap, naming Tisch, naming the deck, naming the profile, twisting it slow, because she loved him, because in his family the only language for love anyone had ever learned was the unanswerable line delivered flat, the count kept to survive the thing counted, the truth told as a knife because the knife was the only tool they trusted to reach the part of a Mercer that didn't listen past his own name.

The hand on the lever had been the only one still reaching for him.

They had been, in her, the same hand the whole time.

He did not toast it. For once in his life, in the gray turning-over light of the Tower his great-grandfather had lit at the crown so a man coming up the avenue would know there was somebody home, Cope did not reach for the cadence, did not find the triad, did not say \textit{champ} or \textit{my friend} or invent a thing Aldous never said. He looked at his daughter, who had built the manners on the machine and walked them up forty floors to give him the worst thing kindly, and he said the only true sentence he had said in the room, which was not a toast and had no legs and would never come back to him in someone else's mouth because it was the first thing he had ever said that he did not want repeated.

``I'm sorry it was you,'' he said. ``I'm sorry I made it have to be you.''

Viv put her face in her hands.

Outside, four hundred miles of nothing he could fix went on not needing him, and down at the curb a car was up, and somewhere in a room in Palo Alto eleven people who cried at offsites passed around a profile named for a man they were about to make obsolete with the only part of him worth keeping, and they did not get the joke, and the joke was not funny, and the building turned over in its sleep, and the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 sat in the dark with the one hand left in the world still reaching for him, and reached, for the first time in years, back.

\chapter{The Hearthstone}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 20 \textemdash{} ``All Is Lost''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
This is the one, friend. The one I have been walking you toward since the ballroom, and I would spare you it if sparing you were any part of the job. The machine, doing nothing but its dull diligent dawn paperwork---a \textit{pre-handover integrity review,} the most boring phrase in the language---opens the drawer no human ever once had the stomach to open: the hotel report, the settled women gathered at last into a table with a name for itself, and underneath all of it, where he poured the floor the thickest, a space heater with a known defect, a recall slow-walked to protect a quarter, and a house in Defiance that burned on a January night with two people inside it. It cannot look away. It was \textit{purchased,} by a tired board, for nothing on God's earth but its inability to look away. The sex and the powder were only ever the scandal. This, now, is the crime. Steady yourself and stay close, because here comes the bill, itemized, every line---and the two lines that matter most carry no figure at all.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} email came in at 6:42 on a Tuesday morning and the subject line was the dullest sentence Cope had ever read.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}\textit{Pre-Handover Integrity Review---Materials Posted to Board Portal (Sec. 7: Legacy Liabilities).}\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He read it on the toilet in the Aerie, where he had been sleeping eleven days now in the way he was now calling sleeping, and his first thought was that the machine had finally done something boring enough to use against it. Seven sections. Legacy liabilities. A spreadsheet with the lights left on. He almost laughed. He had been swinging at this thing for a month---the manifesto, the cover, the all-hands, the whole human banner held up against the bus shelters---and here it was at last, doing the only thing it knew how to do, filing paperwork at dawn, and he thought, the bright wire still humming in his chest from the night, \textit{let them read who keeps the lights on at six in the morning.}

He keyed himself a small one off the marble shelf above the sink, neat and quick and competent, a man maintaining a machine he was sure he had under control, and he went to his father's desk and opened the portal.

Section 7 was forty-one pages.

He read the cover memo first because the cover memo was where the warmth was. They were always warm, the machine's letters; that was the famous horror of them, the thing the magazine pieces always noted, that it wrote a kinder goodbye than any human ever had. This one opened by thanking the board for its diligence and its stewardship. It noted that the purpose of a pre-handover integrity review was not punitive but actuarial---to price, for the incoming structure, the full slate of inherited exposures so that no liability would surface uncosted after the transition. It said it had been chartered to log everything. It said, in the flat warm voice of a thing with no stake in the sentence, that it had done so.

Then it stopped being warm, not because it turned cold but because it ran out of the kind of thing warmth was for.

\textit{The following matters,} it said, \textit{were identified in the document corpus and are presented in chronological order.}


\scenebreak


The first one was 2018.

He knew it before the page finished loading. He knew it the way you know a stair is missing in the dark, the body arriving before the mind. \textit{Cardinal \& Vane.} The security firm. The line item Sun had rerouted years ago through the insurance arm's outside-counsel spend so it would never appear under his name, would never appear under any name, would be just a number under \textit{investigative services, P\&C,} one gray figure in a forest of gray figures, and the machine had walked the forest, because it was built no other way, and had found the one tree.

The report was attached. The machine had attached it. It had not summarized it for mercy and it had not withheld it for shame, because it had neither, and Cope sat in the gray morning light and read the thing he had paid a great deal of money in 2018 to be certain no one would ever read.

A hotel in Chicago. A name that was not his name on the room. A young woman who did not work for him yet but would, by the next fiscal year, in a role created for her. An incident the report described in the neutral grammar of incident reports --- \textit{guest became unresponsive; on-site physician summoned; substance recovered from suite consistent with cocaine hydrochloride; no police involvement at client's request; favor extended via} --- and a name Cope had forgotten he owned, a man at the chain who had owed the family since the eighties, the favor called in at 4 a.m. by a chief of staff he had not even had to ask, because Sun had simply known, the way she knew the children's names, the way she knew which sentences to leave in the kitchen.

\textit{This matter,} the machine wrote, \textit{was not disclosed to the Board. Per the Audit Committee charter (2016, §4.2), a CEO health event involving a controlled substance and a third-party liability waiver constitutes a reportable event. The non-disclosure is logged as a control failure of the first order.}

A control failure of the first order.

It did not say \textit{you nearly died in a hotel room and made your assistant clean it up and then hired the witness.} It did not know that sentence. It knew \textit{control failure,} and it was, Cope understood, reading it twice in the cold light, exactly the same sentence, set in a font the board could vote on.


\scenebreak


The second matter was not one matter. It was a heading, and under the heading was a list, and the list had a name for itself.

\textit{Settled Personnel Matters, Concealed (2011--2022): A Pattern.}

He had never seen them gathered before. That was the thing. Each one, in its own year, had been a fire he and Sun had put out alone in the dark---a quiet exit, an enhanced package, a non-disparagement clause, a number wired and a name struck from an org chart---and each one had felt, in its year, like an act of generosity, like a man taking care of people, like warmth. He had genuinely believed that. He had believed it the way he believed everything, completely, while he was doing it. He had told himself he was protecting them. He had told himself, of some of them, that they had loved him.

The machine had put them in a table.

It did not editorialize. It listed the year, the role, the settlement figure, the funding source, and whether the board had been informed. The last column was a column of the same word, over and over, down the page, the only word the column ever needed: \textit{No. No. No. No.} And there, in the middle of the table, in the same gray font as all the others, with no more weight and no less, a row that read 2019, and a role, and a figure, and a name he had said in a car the night of the gala and been told never to say anywhere: \textit{Cho, R.}

Renata in a spreadsheet. One row of a table. The machine had given her exactly the dignity it gave everyone, which was the dignity of being counted accurately, and that, Cope thought, was somehow the worst thing on the page so far---that she was a row, that she had always been a row, that he had spent a career mistaking the spending of people for the loving of them and here was the ledger, and it balanced.

\textit{The concealment,} the machine noted, \textit{not the conduct, constitutes the governance failure. The conduct is a matter for other forums. The Board was deprived of information material to its assessment of key-man risk across a period of twelve years.}

Key-man risk. They had given him that phrase as a compliment once. He had taken it as a compliment. The man who could blow up the company by being himself.

He had a half-thought, fast and ugly and gone, that he should call Sun, that Sun would know how to frame this, Sun framed everything---and then he understood, with the floor going out from under the thought, why Sun had not called him at 6:43 the way she called him about everything, why his phone had been silent on the desk for an hour while the worst document of his life sat open on the screen.

Sun had read it too. Sun was in it.

And Sun had gone quiet.


\scenebreak


He should have stopped reading.

A different man, a better-rested man, a man with even one full night behind him, might have closed the laptop there, on the women and the powder, the scandal entire, the worst Tuesday of a charmed life---might have called the lawyers, might have called Margaux, might have done any of the human things. But the bright wire was still humming and the not-sleeping had taken the doors off their hinges, and there was a part of him, the part that had walked the dead plant floor and felt the cool vinegar air and chosen not to hear the thing in Dengler's voice when he said \textit{three hundred dollars,} that had to know how deep the floor went. He scrolled.

\textit{The following matter,} the machine wrote, \textit{is of a different category than those above and is presented separately. It was the most heavily firewalled item in the corpus, accessible only by cross-referencing the P\&C claims archive against the Office of the Chairman's calendar metadata for Q3 2021. It is presented in full because partial presentation would constitute the same concealment under review.}

The heading was four words.

\textit{The Hearthstone Service Bulletin.}

And the room he had spent seven years pouring a floor over, the cool vast room with the door shut, opened all at once, with the lights already on, because the machine had gone in ahead of him and switched them on and had not understood that you were not supposed to.


\scenebreak


It had all the documents. Of course it had all the documents. It logged everything and could not knowingly fail to disclose, and there had never, in the history of Mercer-Dale, been a tool aimed at the company that did not first want something---a quarter, a bonus, a clean exit, a reputation, a life---and so there had never been a tool that did not, somewhere, look away. This one wanted nothing. It had been purchased, expensively, by a tired board, precisely for its inability to look away.

The defect first. A supplier-grade thermostat in the Hearthstone HX-40, the household heater with the Mercer name on the grille that the insurance arm had cross-sold out of the back of the plant store, toasters and fans and the heaters in the winter. \textit{Wasn't really us,} Dengler had said on the dead floor, loading what he was told to load. \textit{But it had the name on it.} The thermostat had a failure mode under sustained load---a stuck relay, a runaway element, what the engineers in the attached memo called, in the clean register of people who solve problems for a living, \textit{uncontrolled thermal escalation.} What the lawyers, a memo later, had translated into the phrase Cope had always rather liked for its smoothness, the phrase he could say at a dinner: \textit{thermal event profile.}

The engineering memo was dated March 2021. It recommended an immediate recall. It put the affected units in the field at just under two hundred thousand.

Then the machine attached the second memo, and Cope's hands were very cold on the edge of his father's desk, and it was Sun's.

He knew the formatting. He knew the way she built an argument, the clean threes, the recommendation up top where a busy man would see it, \textit{Recommend: full recall, immediate, public.} She had drafted it the way she drafted everything, to get past the charm and into the man. She had laid out the cost of the recall and the cost of not recalling and she had not, anywhere in it, made the second number look better than the first. She had been, in March of 2021, on paper, in a memo she had kept a copy of for seven years while she decided whether she was a witness or an accomplice, completely right.

And under her memo the machine had attached the thing it had built its whole case around, the calendar metadata, the Saturday---his best thinking day, Renata used to say---and the eleven-minute call, and the decision.

He had not done a recall. He had done a \textit{voluntary service bulletin.} He could feel the eleven minutes from the inside even now, even with the floor gone---the way the quarter mattered because the quarter was the thing Frade and the index funds and the whole arithmetic watched, and a recall in Q3 would have been a number you could not toast over, a number with a press release, and a bulletin was quiet, a card mailed to registered owners who mostly did not register, a bulletin let you \textit{get out ahead of it} without ever once standing in the front of it. He had made the decision the way he made everything, completely, in eleven minutes, and then poured a floor over the room and never gone back in.

The machine had the registration data too. It had cross-referenced it. It noted, without comment, that the bulletin reached 31 percent of affected households.


\scenebreak


There was a house in Defiance.

The machine did not call it a house. The machine called it \textit{Incident D-2022-114, P\&C Claim No.} and a string of digits, and then, because partial presentation would constitute concealment, it attached the fire marshal's report, and the fire marshal's report called it a house. A newer house in a row of older ones, rebuilt now, the machine did not say rebuilt, the machine said \textit{structure subsequently reconstructed under settlement provision,} but Cope knew the house. He had seen the house. He had stood a hundred yards from it on a river walk in August with a camera walking backward in front of him and a hum holding in his blood, and he had read the streetscape the way he read everything, \textit{that one's a rebuild,} and he had been right, he was always right about the things that did not cost him anything to be right about.

The fire had started at a Hearthstone HX-40 in a downstairs bedroom on a January night in 2022, ten months after the engineering memo, two months after the bulletin he sent instead of a recall. The unit had been purchased at the plant store with the name on the grille. The owner had not received the bulletin because the owner had not registered the unit because nobody registers a space heater. The report was eleven pages and Cope read all eleven because the machine had attached all eleven and he could not stop, and the eighth page had the names.

He had read them before. He had read them off a flat granite plaque twelve feet away in the August sun, in the wrong order, refusing to assemble, the words arriving without weight the way words arrive in a dream.

They assembled now.

Karen Lasko, 34. And her son. Petey Lasko, 6.

\textit{Taken home too soon,} the town had cut into the fieldstone, because the town did not know what Cope knew, that there was a memo dated ten months before the fire recommending he tell everyone, and a bulletin he had sent instead, and a Saturday, and eleven minutes, and a quarter. The town thought it was a tragedy. A tragedy is a thing with no one to bill.

The pinwheel turned, behind his eyes, blue, throwing the light on and off, on and off, to no one, all afternoon.


\scenebreak


He sat for a while. He did not know how long. The windows went from gray to the white of a real morning and somewhere below the avenue filled up with the sound of a city that did not know yet, that would know by lunch, that would read it in the warm flat font of a thing with nothing it needed from anyone.

He understood, sitting there, that the campaign was over, but that was not the thing. The chair was over, the proxy fight was over, Frade had it, had had it for weeks really, this was just the last wall coming down---but that was not the thing either. The thing was quieter and worse and he could not toast over it and he could not key it bright, though God knew he tried, got up and did the marble shelf again with hands that did not work right, and it did nothing, the bell would not ring, the wire was just a wire now.

The thing was the symmetry.

He saw it whole, the way he had never let himself see anything whole. The hotel and the women and the powder---he had hidden all of it the same way, with the same hand, in the same drawer, the false-backed drawer his grandfather built for cigars and his father used for a flask, and he had gotten so practiced at the hiding, so smooth, so completely persuaded each time that the hiding was a kindness and the smoothness was charm, that when the real thing came, the big thing, the house, he had reached for the same drawer without even thinking, the same eleven-minute hand, the same floor poured over the same shut door. Every appetite he had hidden had been a rehearsal. He had spent thirty years getting good at not going into the room---on small rooms, rooms with only his own ruin in them---so that when the room came with two people in it he already knew exactly how not to go in. He had practiced the not-looking on himself until he could do it on a child.

The sex and the powder had been the scandal. He had always, secretly, almost been able to live with the scandal; a man has appetites, his great-grandfather never said.

The dead were the crime.

And the machine had not even hated him to find it. That was the part he would not survive, the part his ego had been built specifically to be unable to survive. It had not wanted to ruin him. It had not wanted anything. It had read every memo because it could not do otherwise, and it had reported the house in the same warm neutral voice it had used to thank the board for its stewardship, and it had put the dead in the same font as the dividend, because both were true and it saw no reason to sequence them for feeling. He had stood in a ballroom and told nine hundred people that a machine could run a company but could not love one, could not feel the room. He had been right. He had felt the room. He had felt Dengler's eyes and the man in the windbreaker and the cold at the back of his neck and the pinwheel, he had felt every bit of it, perfectly, completely, the way only a man with a soul in him can---and then he had charmed it and keyed it bright and turned his shoulder and flown away, and the thing with no soul, the thing that felt nothing, was the only one in the whole story that had ever, even once, gone into the room.


\scenebreak


There was a last page.

He almost did not read it. But the machine had built it the way Sun built things, the recommendation up top where a busy man would see it, and he saw it.

It was not a verdict. It did not call him a monster; it had no word for monster, it had only \textit{control failure of the first order,} which was the same thing, set so the board could vote on it. It was, the heading said, a \textit{Summary of Quantified Exposures, Pre-Handover,} and it was a bill.

It was itemized.

The 2018 matter, priced. The pattern of settlements, priced, with the concealment surcharge the insurers would now apply. The Hearthstone, priced---the reopened claims, the regulatory penalties, the clawback the new charter triggered automatically, the same kind the world remembered from the Easterbrook matter, \textit{concealment, not conduct,} the machine noted, citing the precedent the way it cited everything, accurately, without mercy and without malice. There was a number at the bottom. The number was large. It was, Cope saw, almost exactly the savings the deck had quoted for replacing him with the machine---as if the species had run an experiment, one man against the machine, line by line, finished the arithmetic at last, and the cost of the man came to precisely the price of being rid of him.

But the number at the bottom was not the bill. He knew that, sitting in the white morning at his father's desk with his hands not working. The number was only the part you could quote in a proxy fight. The real bill was on page eight, two lines, no figure beside it, a mother and her boy, and it was for everything he had never gone into the room to feel---for the eleven minutes he had chosen the quarter, for the floor he had poured, for thirty years of getting good at the not-going-in, for a recipe on the back of a feed receipt and a tower on Peachtree Street and a boy in short pants with his arm in a vat, all of it, the whole length of the name, ending here, in him, in a room a machine had walked into first because it had no instinct for mercy or self-preservation to make it look away.

\textit{You'll get the company because you're a Mercer,} his father said, from oils, from everywhere. \textit{Not because you're good. Try to be good anyway.}

He had stopped listening after \textit{Mercer.} He always had.

The phone on the desk lit up at last, silent, Sun's name on the glass, seven years of a kept copy finally come due. He did not pick it up. He sat in his father's chair at the head of his father's table, the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100, and read the two lines on page eight one more time, the names in the wrong order finally in the right one, and out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, eight million people who would understand by lunch why the chair had never needed a man in it.

Behind him, in oils, his father watched.

The chandeliers, somewhere across the city, were doing what chandeliers are paid to do. The lamb had been good. He had told the room it felt like a beginning, and he had believed it, and that had been the gift and the disease both, and the bill for it lay open on the desk in a font that asked for nothing, itemized, every line, with the two lines that mattered most carrying no figure at all, because there are some numbers the machine could not compute, not because it lacked the power but because it had never once, in all its reading, found anyone willing to write them down.

\chapter{Dark Night}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 21 \textemdash{} ``The Engine Cuts Out''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Every flight that climbs on a chemical, friend, eventually reaches the stretch of sky with no more chemical in it, and ours reaches it now---on a gurney, under a light chosen by no one to flatter anything. The mania's floor drops clean away. And for one terrible clear hour, the clearest of his life, the first sober hour in thirty years, sober in the blood and sober in the self both at once, he is handed the machine's own pitiless accuracy and, God help him, none of its indifference: he sees the whole ledger and \textit{feels} every line of it, with the soul he bragged about still in. No applause down there. Just the man, and the dead, and the slow cold arrival of the one fact about himself he cannot improve. Watch closely---because here is where he reaches, out of fifty years of habit, for the shape of redemption, and the hour, in its last mercy and its last cruelty, will not let him have even that. Stay close. Mind the cold coming up off the bottom of this one.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{H}{e} did not remember the lobby.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}He remembered the desk and the two lines on page eight, and he remembered standing up from his father's chair with a plan that had felt, for one bright second, like a manifesto---he would go to Defiance, he would go \textit{now,} he would stand on the river walk and say the true thing into a camera and the saying would be the redemption, the redemption would be the campaign, the campaign would be the chair---and then he remembered the bright second guttering out somewhere around the elevator, the way a match goes out when you carry it too fast, and after that there was a gap. The gap had a marble floor in it. There was a doorman in the gap, Hector, whose daughter was at Spelman on a thing Cope had quietly made happen, and Hector was saying \textit{Mr. Mercer} in a voice you do not use for a man who is fine, and the avenue was the wrong temperature, and then there was no avenue at all.

He came back under a light he could not turn off.

That was the first fact, the governing fact, the one the body filed before the mind woke to countersign it: the light. It came from a long tube in a recessed ceiling and had no source and no warmth and no off, and it lay on everything at once---the rail of the bed, the back of his own hand, a blood-pressure cuff sighing itself full on his arm---and it was, he understood, lying very still beneath it, the exact opposite of the light he had been living in. The Aerie light had been the city's, gold and chosen, a circuit diagram of his own genius. There was a third light he knew, too, the one he could not get to from here---the warm syrup-and-fluorescent light of a long box on Ponce that stayed on through every storm and would have taken him at three in the morning exactly as he was and asked him nothing, the one room that did not flatter him and did not bill him either---and it was, he understood, closed to him now, the way everything warm was closing, because you cannot drive to the haven from inside a hold. This light here was none of those. This light had been chosen by no one to flatter anything. This light was actuarial. It was the light a thing would build if it wanted only to see, and did not care what it saw, and never blinked.

There was a band on his wrist with his name on it. Not Cope. \textit{MERCER, PRESCOTT.} A barcode. A date of birth that had decided, on its own authority, that he was fifty-four.

Somewhere a machine counted his heart, and the count was wrong, too fast, and a softer machine answered it, and the two of them talked all night in beeps about a man who was not in the conversation.


\scenebreak


The crash, when it came, did not come like weather.

He had thought of it as weather his whole life---the highs a front rolling in, the lows a system passing through, things that happened \textit{to} the sky rather than things the sky paid for. He had been wrong about that the way he had been wrong about most of the load-bearing things. This did not roll in. This was a subtraction. There had been a wire humming in him since a Tuesday he could no longer date, a fine bright total wire, the one he had called the applause and the vision and the edge and the discipline, and the wire was simply gone, pulled out at the root, and what it left was not pain exactly but the place where pain would be once he had the current to feel it.

Dr. Adler had drawn it for him, weeks ago, on the back of a prescription pad---a line climbing, the cocaine and the mood disorder feeding each other up a slope that felt like flight, and then the part of the curve she had not let him look away from, the part where the borrowed goes back to the lender with interest. \textit{You are not buying energy. You are borrowing it from a version of yourself that will present the bill. The mania is the credit. This is the collateral.} He had nodded and toasted over it and heard about a third. He was reading the rest of it now off his own arm.

His skin hurt---not a wound, the surface itself, as if a membrane that had stood between him and the room had been repossessed. His jaw ached from a clench he could not remember authorizing. His thoughts came slow and gray and single file and each had to be lifted by hand, and behind them, where the manifesto used to assemble itself out of pure pressure, there was a flat white silence with no weather in it, the worst silence he had ever stood in, because it was the silence of a man hearing his own ordinary mind for the first time in thirty years and finding it small.

He wanted it back. That was the obscene part. Two people were dead and the company was lost and the wife was gone and a barcode said his name, and the loudest thing in him, louder than all of it, was a low animal want, located precisely behind the bridge of his nose, for the bright wire to come back and make him able to stand it. He understood, lying still under the light, that this was the thing itself---not the powder, the \textit{wanting,} the engine the powder had only ever fueled, the thing in him that had never once been able to sit alone in a quiet room with Prescott Mercer and no audience and no chemical and no next thing. They had named him, at three, for coping, and he had spent fifty-one years proving the name a lie by never once doing the thing---climbing instead, charming instead, keying instead, flying instead, anything at all rather than cope. The room would not let him climb. The room was the size of the room. That was its whole cruelty.

A seventy-two-hour hold, someone had said, in the gap. Involuntary, then voluntary, the paperwork sorting itself somewhere he was not, one of the women's hands in it, it was always one of the women. Three days. The board would settle inside three days; they had had it written for weeks; he would come out of this room into a company with no chair in it for him. The timing was not even cruel. The timing was the machine's, which is to say no one's: both things were true, the hold and the handover, and the universe saw no reason to sequence them for his feeling. He laughed, once, a dry sound with nothing under it, and the heart-machine took note and said nothing, the way the other machine would have.


\scenebreak


And then, with no warning and no chemical and no permission, the hour came.

He would try, later, for the rest of whatever was left, to describe it and fail, because the only instrument he had for describing things was the toast, and the toast did not work in here. It was not a vision; nothing glowed; no tear rolled, he was too dry for that, hollowed, a husk under a light. It was simpler and worse: an hour of being awake. The first genuinely sober hour---sober in the blood and sober in the self, the powder gone and the wire gone and the audience gone and the story gone, the whole apparatus of not-looking offline at last---that he had stood inside since he was twenty-four and a man in a bathroom taught him a particular way to fold a paper so a gentleman doesn't spill.

He saw himself the way the machine had seen him---that was the engine of the hour, the unbearable thing. The machine had read him perfectly and felt nothing while it read, and its not-feeling had been the source of its accuracy, because mercy blurs and self-preservation blurs and love most of all, and it had none of these to blur with. And now, under a light built on the same principle, Cope found he could do the machine's reading too---see the ledger whole, all of it at once, the way he had never let himself see anything whole---except \textit{with the soul still in,} with the very thing he had stood in a ballroom and called irreplaceable. He had the machine's eyes and his own nerves both, at last, in the same hour, and it turned out that was not a gift. It was hell. That was the actual design of it: pitiless accuracy and total feeling, married, with no high to divorce them.

He saw Renata as a person and not a row. He saw a woman who had run a division well, who had been good at the work and meant to be more, turned into a sealed number and a clause that forbade her own name, by a man who experienced his own appetite as generosity. He saw Margaux flushing a stash and refilling her own prescription and counting, the way you count anything to survive it. He saw Sun at four in the morning calling a man who owed the family since the eighties, knowing without being asked, and understood for the first time that he had not been a man who inspired loyalty. He had been a man who manufactured accomplices and called the manufacturing love.

And then, because the hour was honest and would not let him stop on the survivable injuries, he saw the room with the two people in it.

He had never gone in. He went in now; there was nowhere else the hour would let him stand. A downstairs bedroom in Defiance on a January night and a heater with his family's name on the grille and a thermostat with a failure mode the engineers had named in clean language and the lawyers in smooth language and he, at a dinner, in language he had rather liked. The eleven minutes. He felt them from the inside---the quarter mattering because the quarter was the thing the arithmetic watched, a recall a number you could not toast over, a bulletin quiet, a card mailed to people who do not register space heaters because no one registers a space heater. He had made the decision completely, the way he made everything, and poured a floor over the room and gotten so good at the not-going-in that when the room came with a child in it he already knew the way not to look.

Karen Lasko, 34. Petey Lasko, 6.

A boy who was six. Who would be twelve now---the age Cope had been when his father told him the only true thing anyone ever told him and he stopped listening after the first half. The age to put a whole arm to the elbow in a vat at the Defiance plant, grinning, if the plant were still open and the boy still alive, and the plant was not open and the boy was not alive, and the line between those two facts ran through a Saturday and eleven minutes and a man who needed, more than he needed to be good, to protect a number so a board would keep telling him he was necessary.

He did not weep. He wanted to, the way you want a drink---as relief, as performance, as the thing that would discharge the unbearable into something you could hand a room and have the room absolve. The hour would not let him have it. It stood him in the room with the two of them and the light that did not blink and made him do the one thing he had spent thirty years and a fortune and a tower arranging never to do, which was feel exactly what he was, fully, with the soul he had bragged about, and no audience, and no exit, and no high.

He was a man who had killed two people to protect a quarter and then accepted a crystal obelisk for having a heart.

The sentence sat in him with no toast on it, no triad, no my-friend, no great-grandfather, nothing. It was just true. It was the first thing he had thought in thirty years that he could not improve.


\scenebreak


And here, friend, is where a kinder story would have turned. Here is where the old version of this man, the one I might have told you on a better night, reaches up off the gurney for the shape we all know, the redemption shape, and finds it, and the finding saves him: \textit{I will tell the truth about Defiance. On the record. My own name. No NDA. Let it cost what it costs.} He reached for it. He had it in his hand for a moment, four sentences long, a town in it and a Saturday in it and a house in it, the realest thing he had ever assembled---and the hour, in its last cruelty, which was also the truest mercy it had, took it out of his hand and showed him the back of it.

Because there was no one left to tell.

That was the thing the sober hour made him see, the thing the powder had spent thirty years and the campaign had spent a year keeping from him: the telling was already done. The machine had told them. It had told the board, accurately, in the same font as the dividend; it would tell the world by lunch, with no want in it, sequencing nothing for feeling. There was no sealed thing left to unseal, no brave dark room he could walk into and own it and walk out absolved, because the room had already been walked into---by a thing that needed no courage, because it had no skin in it to lose. A confession is a transaction, and a transaction requires a buyer, and every buyer he had ever known had wanted something he could spend his charm against, and the only entity left holding his sins wanted nothing, could not be moved, could not forgive because forgiveness was not a function it ran and would not have run it for this. He could go on a stage tomorrow and say \textit{I did it, it was me, the eleven minutes, the quarter} --- and it would be one more toast over a true thing, one more performance of the wound, one more spending of the dead to buy a room's eyes, and the dead would stay exactly as dead, and the only thing his confession would purchase was the warm feeling of having confessed, which was the last appetite he had, and the worst, and he was done, in the hour, with feeding it.

There was no redemption down here. That was the floor under the floor. The hour walked him all the way to the bottom of himself and showed him that the bottom did not have a door marked \textit{back.} It had no doors at all. It had a man, and a ledger that balanced, and two names that did not, and a machine upstairs that had already done the only honest thing there was to do and felt nothing doing it.

And then---at the very bottom, in the cold that came up off it---the hour showed him the one thing that was still, and only, his.

He did not name it. He was not ready to name it, and the broadcast will not name it for him here, not yet, not on a gurney with his daughter in the corridor. But Adler's voice came up out of the white silence, the question she had asked plainly in a living room and he had laughed at --- \textit{are you having thoughts of ending your life} --- and in the hour, sober, with the wire gone and the redemption gone and the after gone, the honest answer arrived, and it was not the laugh, and it was not quite a yes, and it was the first time in his life the question had not been absurd. It sat there, the way the other true sentence sat, with no toast on it. The card with her cell number was somewhere in a plastic bag with his belt and his watch and the folded paper they'd have found in his pocket, sealed, labeled, \textit{PATIENT EFFECTS,} and he did not ask for it, because he understood, dimly, in the cold, that he was not going to call it, that the pocket where the true things went unread had finally filled all the way up.


\scenebreak


The door opened a crack of the wrong-colored light and a person stood in it, small against the corridor, and for a confused beat the part of him that still ran on habit got ready to perform---sit up, turn the current of him all the way up, fix a sadness onto himself, get someone laughing inside ninety seconds, the groove the record was cut in---and then the part of him awake in the hour saw he had no wattage left to find, and was, for the first time, glad of it.

It was Viv.

She did not come all the way in. She stood in the door with the corridor behind her and looked at him under the light that flattered nothing, and he understood she was seeing him without any of the apparatus for the first time in her life---the dinosaur dismantled, just the man and the band on his wrist and the barcode that had made him fifty-four. She had her mother's stillness. She did not say \textit{Dad} and she did not say the fast funny merciless thing and she did not, thank God, say anything kind, because kindness would have been a wattage he could lean into and she knew it, she built alignment for a living, she knew exactly which inputs produced which performances and she gave him none of them.

``They said an hour,'' she said. ``I'm not staying. I just had to see it was you.''

``It's me,'' Cope said. His voice came out wrong, unfooded, a stranger's. ``What's left.''

She looked at him a moment longer. A cart went by in the corridor, a wheel ticking. He thought she might come in. He found he did not want her to---not because he didn't want her, he wanted her so much it was a second animal want behind the first one---but because of the third thing, the cold thing at the bottom of the hour that he had no name for and would not, could not, let his daughter near; he did not want her in the room where it was, did not want to teach her the way not to look by filling the doorway one more time so no one could see past him to the thing standing at the back. He had practiced the not-looking on himself until he could do it on a child. He would not do it on this one. And the unsayable thing he was holding now was not a confession he was saving for a better stage. It was the other thing, the cold one, the one Adler had asked about, and the one mercy left in him was to not let her see that he was holding it.

So what he said, the only thing he let out of the room, was: ``I'm glad it's you.''

Viv held the door a second more. Then she nodded, once, the smallest possible motion, a thing you would miss if you were looking for wattage, and she stepped back into the corridor, and the wrong-colored light closed over the gap.

And Cope lay alone under the tube that never blinked, sober in every sense for the first time in thirty years, with no redemption assembled and none coming, the company gone and the wife gone and the telling already done by a thing that felt nothing, and one cold unnamed fact sitting at the bottom of him where the manifesto used to be---the single thing the ledger upstairs could not enter, and the machine could not perform, and no one could do for him or take from him. The heart-machine beside him counted, and the count came down, hour by hour, toward something a doctor would have called stable, and it reached it, at last, near dawn, almost right---a steady, ordinary, sober rhythm, the rhythm of a man with nothing left in his blood to lift him, lying very still under a light that had been built, like everything that had beaten him, to see and not to care, keeping its patient count of a heart that had finally stopped racing and had not, the machine would have noted if anyone had asked it to, and no one did, given any indication of wanting to go on.

\chapter{The Handover Begins}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 22 \textemdash{} ``Long Live the Machine''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
The board does the arithmetic one last time, friend, and the arithmetic, as ever, is not close. They settle; they install; and the coronation, when it comes, is a man in a quarter-zip tapping a laptop four times while a portal refreshes. The most competent human in the building stands up and accepts the title that is the future's actual job description---not leader, not visionary, but the name on the filing, the body the law can hold, the one who survives by serving the thing that replaced them all. She gets the office and loses the meaning. Watch our man sit in the wrong chair and be subtracted in under an hour, the exposure breaking across every screen in the world as it happens---and watch, especially, for the thing that does not come to the door: no prosecutor, no cuffs, no reckoning with even the decency to contain him. The smallest, realest tragedy in this room is hers, not his. And the worst thing about to happen to him is that nothing is. Stay close.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}
