\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{hey} held the vote in the conference room his father had died at the head of, and Cope was allowed to attend.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}That was the word the general counsel had used, on the phone, with the gentleness people reserve for the recently bereaved. \textit{Allowed.} Not \textit{required.} Not \textit{expected.} He could attend if he liked. As a director he had a seat; as a Mercer he had a portrait; as the chairman and chief executive of record he had, until the resolution carried, a title that was about to become a sentence in the past tense. He came in early and sat in the wrong chair on purpose---not the head, never again the head, but the one to its right, the seat that had been Margaux's at a thousand dinners and was nobody's now---and watched the room fill with people who had spent years pretending to need him.

He had been out of the hospital four days. The light still felt wrong, but it felt wrong the right way now: not the overexposed bell-struck brightness of the not-sleeping, the color-corrected world he had mistaken for genius, but the flat true gray of a man who had finally slept and woken into the size of what he'd done. Adler had a word for the after. He couldn't remember it. He remembered all his words for the before.

And it was breaking, even now, even as he sat. He could feel it through the glass walls the way you feel weather---the floor outside reorganizing around a story for the second time in his life, except this was not the affair and not the powder, this was the other thing, the integrity review, the drawer the machine had opened at dawn and could not, by its nature, keep shut. Somewhere out past the lights a wire had moved an hour ago: \textit{MERCER-DALE AI REVIEW SURFACES CONCEALED 2021 RECALL; TWO DEAD IN GEORGIA FIRE.} The names would be in it by noon, Karen and Petey, in the warm flat font of a thing that wanted nothing. The world was learning, this morning, the thing he had poured a floor over for six years, and it was learning it not from a brave man at a podium but from a press-grade summary a machine had generated because partial presentation would have constituted the same concealment under review. He had imagined, once, in the hospital, that the telling would be his to do. The telling was being done. It had never needed him for it.

Helen Voss came in last, which was correct, because she was running it. ``Cope,'' she said, and put a hand on his shoulder on the way past, and it was not unkind, and that was the worst part, that none of it was unkind. She had chaired the board six years and was the most measured human being he had ever met, a woman who could say \textit{we are terminating you for cause} in the register she used to order a salad. The steadiness of her had once felt to Cope like an audience and now felt like weather. She took the head of the table---did not sit in his father's chair so much as occupy the office it represented, the way you occupy a defensible position. ``We have a quorum. We have counsel present. Let's do this with some dignity.''

Sun was at the far end with a binder she did not need, because Sun never needed the binder, the binder was a costume; she had the whole settlement in her head the way she had once had his calendar, his allergies, the names of the senator's grandchildren. She did not look at him. She had not really looked at him since the hospital, and he understood, and he was working hard, in the new gray clarity, not to make it her job to comfort him about being comforted by her, which was a sentence Adler would have liked.


\scenebreak


The arithmetic took eleven minutes---a number Cope noted, with the part of him that would apparently be taking notes at his own funeral, and noted that he had met before, somewhere, on another Saturday, and did not let himself finish the thought.

He had thought there would be more to it. He had, in some childish back room, expected the settlement to be hard---Frade made to fight for it, the board made to sweat. But the board did not want a fight. The board wanted to \textit{stop.} He watched Helen walk them through the deck Tidewater had built, \textit{A Steward, Not a Sovereign,} the slides almost verbatim the essays he had read in a hundred forms over thirteen months and never once managed to argue with, because there was nothing in them that was wrong. The savings against his package, recurring, an order of magnitude. The litigation exposure, capped and quantified, every number a door closing. The re-rating the Street had already priced in---the stock up nine percent on the leak alone, up before the vote, the market voting first the way it always voted first, the board only ratifying what money had already decided.

\textit{Cheaper than a man.} He had handed Frade that chyron in a car a year ago, and been right they'd use it, and assumed, the way he assumed everything, that being right and being safe were the same country.

``The Praxis unit is configured and tested,'' Helen said. ``Charter approved by this committee in March. Remaining items are governance, the Accountable Officer, and the separation terms, which Cope's counsel has reviewed.''

His counsel had reviewed them. His counsel had told him, three times, in three rooms, that they were generous given the circumstances, that he should sign and be grateful and disappear. \textit{Generous given the circumstances} meant \textit{we are paying you not to set yourself on fire on television,} and he knew it, and he was going to sign---not because he was beaten into it, though he was, but because of the new gray fact he had brought back from the hospital and could not unlearn: there was nowhere left to be undisappeared to. The thing he had imagined on the gurney, the brave room, the record, the truth said at last in his own name---there was no such room left to walk into, because the room had already been walked into, at dawn, by a thing that needed no courage to enter it. You cannot confess a thing the world is reading at noon. There was no sealed drawer left to fling open for the redemption of flinging it; the machine had opened the drawer, accurately, wanting nothing, and the dead were not, he understood now, sitting in that conference room with his hands cold on the table---the dead were not waiting for a body to answer for them. The answering, such as the world would get, had been done. What the dead were owed was a thing no statement in any room would ever pay, and the only thing left in him that was still his, the cold unnamed thing he had found at the bottom of the hospital hour, was not a confession, and he did not take it out and look at it here, with the hands going up.

``All in favor,'' Helen said.

It was not close. The hands went up the way hands go up when the answer was decided in a hallway weeks ago and the meeting is the receipt. He counted them; he could not help counting them. He had charmed most of these people, golfed with a third, eulogized one of their wives. Margaux had taught their daughters to throw a pot. The hands went up and stayed up and did not look at him going up, and Helen said, ``Carried,'' in the salad voice, and that was the end of ninety-five years of a Mercer in the chair, and the lamb, he thought, would have been good.

Darlene was, as of 11:14 a.m., chief executive officer of Mercer-Dale Companies.

It did not announce itself. No voice, no swelling music, no red eye. A man from Praxis in a soft gray quarter-zip tapped four times on a laptop and said, ``The instance is live and accepting authority,'' and the board portal refreshed, and somewhere a draft release Darlene had presumably written about its own installation went into a queue, and that was the coronation, the whole coronation, a quarter-zip and a refresh and the cleanest sentence anyone ever read about the end of an era. Long live the machine. The machine did not care that it lived; that was the entire point, and the room had paid a fortune for the point, and the room exhaled.

There were even forms. Onboarding, the quarter-zip called it, with no irony, the same word the company used for a hire in fulfillment---a checklist on a screen, access provisioned, credentials issued, an entry made in the registry of corporate officers where the chief executive's name went, and the name that went there was \textit{Darlene (AURELIUS-class, Praxis Intelligence, instance MD-1),} typed into the field where four Mercers had stood. A junior associate from the corporate secretary's office, a woman young enough to be Viv, did the typing. She had to fix it once---a space in wrong, the field threw an error, and she said ``sorry'' reflexively to a room that contained no one the error had inconvenienced---and corrected it, and the company had a new CEO, and the lamb-quiet machinery of governance accepted the change completely and without comment. Somewhere in a cooled room in the building's own sublevels, where no Mercer had ever had a reason to go, a thing that felt nothing began to run a ninety-five-year-old fortune better than any of them ever had.


\scenebreak


Then they came to Sun.

Cope had known it was coming---had known for months, since the magazine and the manifesto and the meeting behind the curtain he'd been too lit to read as anything but logistics---known that the most competent person he had ever employed had been quietly negotiating her own survival, and known, in the clean gray after, that he had no standing left to feel betrayed by it, because she was not betraying him. She was doing arithmetic. She was the only one in the family who could.

``The Accountable Officer,'' Helen said, ``is the human officer of record. Counsel?''

Brennan did the part where he made it sound like a promotion. He was good. He talked about \textit{signing authority} and \textit{the human point of contact} and \textit{the face of governance,} used the phrase \textit{steady hand} twice, and did not use, because no one ever used in the room, the actual job description, which Cope could have recited in his sleep, in the sleep he could now have: a human name on the SEC filings. A person who could be subpoenaed. A person who could, if it ever came to that, go to prison. The machine ran the company and could not be cuffed, could not be sentenced, could not weep at a defense table; the law still wanted a body, and the body's name was the Accountable Officer, and the Accountable Officer's whole function, stripped of the steady-hand language, was to be the thing the law could hold when the machine did something the law did not like. A liability sponge. He had heard it called that in a pitch once and laughed.

He did not laugh now. He watched Sun stand up.

``Sun Rao has agreed to serve,'' Helen said. ``Sun?''

And here was the thing Cope had come to see, the thing worth attending for, the small real tragedy that was not his.

She was good. That was the awful part---she was so good at it. She stood and did not perform, because Sun never performed, and said, in the clean dry threes she had spent fifteen years teaching him to receive, ``I've reviewed the charter, I've reviewed the constraints, and I'll co-sign within them.'' Three parts. The shape that got past the charm and into the man, except there was no charm in it now and no man to get into, only a board nodding and a quarter-zip refreshing a portal, and she had built the instrument for a master who no longer existed and was using it on the air.

``I want one thing on the record.'' Helen waited. ``The Accountable Officer co-signs the machine's decisions. The Accountable Officer does not make them. I want it minuted that I understand the difference.''

It was the blade---always one blade with Sun, dry and exact---and Cope was probably the only person in the room who felt it go in, because he was the only one who knew what she was saying under what she was saying: \textit{I know what this is. I am taking the title and telling you, for the record, that I know it is not the job. I ran this company for years and the reward for running it well is the right to sign for the thing that runs it better, and to go to prison if it is ever wrong.} She had wanted the chair her whole adult life. She had earned it in a way no Mercer in four generations ever had. And the chair had been given to a thing with no body to put in it, and what was left for the most competent human in the building was the office without the meaning, the liability without the authority, the title that was the future's actual job description.

She got the office. She lost the chair inside it.

``So minuted,'' Helen said.

And Sun sat back down, and it was done, and Cope watched her do the thing he had never once in his life been able to do, which was adapt. She did not rage at the machine. She did not campaign on her soul. She did not hire a man in a five-thousand-dollar henley to help her out-human anything. She looked at the arithmetic, saw that it was not close, and made herself useful to the only future that was actually going to happen, because she had a mother in Fremont and a mortgage and no inherited tower to fail upward from, and survival was not a betrayal, it was just the cost of not being a Mercer.

He had spent thirteen months calling himself the last human. He had been looking at the last human the whole time, and she was thirty-nine, and she briefed in threes, and she had just signed up to be the body the law could hold.


\scenebreak


The room broke into the strange social comedy of an execution that has gone smoothly.

People stood. People congratulated Sun and could not quite find the verb for it --- \textit{congratulations} wrong, \textit{condolences} worse---and landed on a warm vague \textit{this is exciting} that hung in the air and meant nothing. The Praxis man shook her hand and said the company was \textit{thrilled to partner with such a strong human steward,} and Cope watched Sun receive the word \textit{partner} --- partner, for the woman who would sign and the system that would decide---with a stillness so complete it could have been mistaken, by anyone who didn't know her, for grace. Someone produced coffee. Someone joked about the catering. Helen Voss checked her watch, because she had a 1:30 and the end of a dynasty had come in under an hour and she was, reasonably, going to make her 1:30.

Cope stood too, because sitting had become its own statement and he was done making statements. He was, for the first time in thirty years, a spectator at a meeting in his own building. No one needed him to feel the room. The room had been read with perfect accuracy by a thing that did not care what it read, and the reading was correct, and the company would be run cleanly now---cleaner books, kinder letters, no scandals, no slow-walked recalls, no dead---run better, even, and he was a man holding a coffee he didn't want, watching the future shake hands.

Darlene drafted the all-staff announcement while they were still in the room. Sun read it off her phone, and Cope read it over her shoulder because she let him, which was its own small mercy, and it was---he had to give it the thing he'd never had to give it before, the thing he'd spent thirteen months refusing---it was decent. It named Sun's years of service, got her title right and her tone right, thanked \textit{Prescott Mercer for ninety-five years of family stewardship,} four generations folded into one warm true sentence with the dates correct, and it did not, anywhere in its clean paragraphs, want anything from anyone, did not flatter, did not spin, did not toast---and Cope understood, reading it, that this was the obituary the trophy had only gestured at, beautifully set, severance math right the first time, out-loving him on the way out the door exactly as the essays had promised it would.

``It's good,'' he said. His voice came out fine. He'd worried it wouldn't.

``It's accurate,'' Sun said. Not unkindly. Correcting his category, the way she always had. \textit{Good} was a soul word; the machine didn't do soul words; it did accurate---and accurate, it turned out, was enough to run a company and enough to end a man and enough to write him a kinder goodbye than he had ever written anyone, and the gap between \textit{good} and \textit{accurate} was the entire space Cope had spent his life living inside and getting paid twenty-nine million dollars a year to call leadership.

She put her phone away. For a second---the first since the hospital---she looked at him. Really looked. Whatever was in it was not forgiveness and was not contempt and was not the love that had run alongside the done-with-him in the same sentence for fifteen years. It was flatter and harder and sadder than any of those, and Cope, in the new gray clarity, made himself stand inside it and not toast over it.

``You should go home, Cope,'' Sun said. ``There's nothing here for you to do.''

She did not mean it cruelly. She meant it as the most accurate sentence in the room---and it was, and that was the whole of the new horror, the thing the hospital had shown him at the bottom and the conference room confirmed: there was nothing here for him to do. The machine was installed, the arithmetic had carried, the body had signed up to be the body, the world was reading the dead in a warm flat font by noon---and no one was coming for him. That was the part he kept arriving at and could not metabolize. No prosecutor at the door. No detective with the patient terrible face of the man at the diner counter. The Hearthstone was a civil matter buried in a sealed settlement and a corporate charter and a statute of limitations the lawyers had already, gently, mentioned; the concealment was the company's problem now, Sun's to sign for going forward, the machine's to never repeat; and the man who had spent the eleven minutes was simply, cleanly, \textit{released} --- not forgiven, not punished, not contained, just subtracted, handed a generous separation and told to go home, where there was nothing to do. He had thought, on the gurney, that the worst thing waiting for him was a reckoning. The worst thing waiting for him was that there wasn't one.

He thought, for a moment, about telling Sun the cold thing---the unnamed thing from the bottom of the hour, the one act still left that was his and only his. But it was not a toast and it was not for her to carry, and the one true thing he had left would mean less the instant he said it aloud, the way everything he had ever announced had meant less for the announcing; and besides, naming it to the most competent person he knew would be the same as asking her to stop it, and he was done, in the new clarity, using the people who loved him to manage things he had no intention of letting them manage.

``All right, champ,'' he said instead, soft, to the most competent person he'd ever known, who was now the name the law could hold. ``All right.''

He set the coffee down on the long table his father had died at the head of, and walked out past the portraits---Aldous, Pres III, the boy at nine with his arm in the vat---and behind him the room went on without him, warm and competent and exact, the handover beginning, the machine accepting authority, the body signing for it, the world learning the names by lunch, and the future being introduced to a polite scatter of applause that he understood now, far too late, had never once been for him.

\chapter{No One to Punish}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 23 \textemdash{} ``No One to Punish''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Here is the cruelty at the dead center of the whole arrangement, friend, and it is not the one you have been braced for. You have been waiting, the way he has been waiting, for the reckoning---the cuffs, the cell, the prosecutor, the family of the dead looking at him across a courtroom, the bill come due at last in a body. He has begun, down in the gray bottom of himself, to almost want it, because a sentence has a shape, and a sentence has an end, and a sentence is, at the very least, a way to pay. Watch the world decline to hand him even that. A machine cannot be jailed---no body, nothing to hold---and it turns out, in the end, neither will he be, because the law has no machinery for this and money has a great deal of it, and the same cold competent thing that took his chair has, wanting nothing, arranged that not even his punishment is necessary. The dead get no justice. He gets no cell. And the only court left anywhere on earth with standing to sentence Prescott Mercer is about to be Prescott Mercer. Stay close. There is no cell waiting at the end of this one---only the open air where the reckoning should have stood.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} lawyers came to him now, which was how he knew everything had changed.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}For thirty years the law had been a thing Cope went \textit{to.} A panel of it on a high floor with a view, a man named Bofinger who billed in fifteen-minute increments and called him champ back, a process you walked into the way you walked into a club, because it was yours, because it had always been yours, because a Mercer kept lawyers the way a Mercer kept a tailor. You went to them. They poured you a water. They told you what you wanted to hear in language expensive enough to make the wanting respectable.

These ones came to him. They came to a conference room two floors below the Aerie that he had not booked and did not recognize, a room with no portraits in it, and they were already seated when Sun walked him in, three of them, and not one had brought a water.

``Mr. Mercer,'' the lead one said. Not Cope. Mr. Mercer.

Her name was Okonjo, from a firm whose name was four surnames Cope had never strung together in his life, a white-collar shop, the kind you only learn the name of in the worst week you will ever have. Fifties, gray suit, no jewelry, a legal pad with actual handwriting on it. She did not tell him he hadn't aged a day. She did not ask after anyone's grandson. She looked at him the way the machine looked at everything, completely and for use---except that she was a person, and so there was, under the completeness, the one thing the machine never had, a verdict already half-formed and held in reserve.

``This is a defense conversation. I want to be precise about that before we start, because precision is the only thing that's going to help you from here. We are not your corporate counsel. Wachtell handles the company. As of this week the company's interests and yours are not the same interests. Do you understand the distinction?''

``I built the company. The company's interests have been my interests for---``

``That was the old arrangement.'' She did not raise her voice. ``Sit down, please.''

He sat. He had not slept, but it was a different not-sleeping now; the bright wire was gone, Adler's word \textit{crash} and another word he had a prescription for, eleven days into the soft gray bottom, the drawer in the Aerie cleaned out by Sun without a word, the world gone underexposed, flat and far, the gray room and the gray suit swimming at one remove, his hands on the table doing the small new thing they did when there was nothing in them.

``There are two tracks,'' Okonjo said. ``Track one is civil. The reopened claims, the regulatory penalties, the shareholder suits, the clawback. That's money. Money is bad but money is survivable. Track one is going to take essentially everything you have, and it is, in the scheme of what people in your position fear, the easy track.'' She turned a page she did not read. ``Track two is criminal. And track two, Mr. Mercer, is the conversation I came here to have, because you have it backwards in your head, and the having-it-backwards is going to be the hardest thing I do for you.''


\scenebreak


He had braced for the word. He had been bracing for it since the gurney --- \textit{prison,} the missing stair, the body before the mind. He had, in the worst and most private corner of the gray, begun to build a kind of shelter inside the word: a sentence was a thing with edges; a cell was a place you could be \textit{put,} which meant a place where the falling stopped; a number of years was a number, and he was a man who could hold a number, could serve it, could come out the far side of it diminished and ruined and alive and \textit{paid up,} the way you come out the far side of anything with edges. He had started, God help him, to want it. To want the cuffs the way you want a wall at the end of a fall.

``The Hearthstone,'' he said.

``The Hearthstone.'' Okonjo set her pen down. ``Here is what you want me to tell you. You want me to tell you they're going to charge you. Reckless homicide, involuntary manslaughter, the federal product-safety counts, the wire and mail on the cover-up. You've read enough to know the menu exists, and some animal part of you---I've seen it in this chair before, it is more common than you'd think---has decided that a prosecution is the worst case. It is not the worst case. I am going to do something defense lawyers almost never get to do, which is give you good news and watch it land like a sentence.'' She folded her hands. ``You are almost certainly not going to be charged. And if you are, it will not stick. I am very good, Mr. Mercer, and in this matter I am barely going to have to be.''

The room was very quiet. Sun, against the wall, did not move.

``Why,'' Cope said, and heard, under the one word, the thing he could not believe he was feeling, which was the bottom dropping a level he had not known was under the bottom.

``Several reasons, and none of them are mercy, and I want you to hear all of them, because the shape of them is the thing you're going to have to live inside.'' She counted on the pad, in threes, the way Sun counted, and the echo went through him like cold water. ``One. Time. The decision was 2021, the fire 2022; the federal knowing-violation counts have a five-year clock, and the clock is a problem for the government, not for you. Two. The settlement. When you buried it, you buried it well---sealed, with a release, with a category that called it a commercial matter, and a sealed civil resolution that's stood for years is a wall a prosecutor has to climb before she gets to the first fact, and prosecutors have finite ladders and infinite cases. Three.'' She paused. ``The remediation. The company has already done---is doing, this week, on the machine's recommendation---every single thing a prosecutor would have demanded as the price of declining to charge. A fund. A full recall of the remaining units, finally, eight years late, drafted and announced by Darlene inside seventy-two hours of taking the chair. A public, accurate, beautifully written acknowledgment. Cooperation. The company has remediated itself so cleanly and so fast that there is nothing left for a prosecutor to extract by charging the man who used to run it. You don't indict the past of a company that has, by every visible measure, already fixed itself. It reads as vindictive. It reads as grandstanding. It reads, to a careful office with a docket and a reputation, as not worth it.''

``The machine,'' Cope said slowly, ``is the reason I won't be charged.''

``The machine is a large part of the reason you won't be charged,'' Okonjo agreed, with no inflection at all, because she did not yet understand what she was handing him and he was only beginning to. ``It surfaced the matter, which looks like accountability and satisfies the public appetite for one. And then it remediated the matter, flawlessly, which removes the prosecutor's leverage. It did both because the charter told it to minimize the enterprise's go-forward liability, and a charged former CEO is a years-long headline and a discovery nightmare and a liability, and a quietly retired one who signs the separation and disappears is a closed file. It is not protecting you. It would be accurate to say it is \textit{disposing} of you. The cleanest disposal of the man is the one where he is never charged, because a trial would re-open everything the remediation just closed. So no. There is, in all likelihood, no cell.''


\scenebreak


He understood it slowly, over the next hour, the way you understand the shape of a room you are already locked inside---except the room had no walls, which was the thing, which was the whole horror of it. He had braced for a room with walls.

The machine could not be charged. He had known that academically---it was in the deck, it was the dark joke everyone made---but he had thought, in the gray, that \textit{he} would be the body the law took instead, that the chair which had always come with the credit would now, finally, come with the cell, and that the cell, terrible as it was, would at least be a place the story ended. He had been wrong about that the way he had been wrong about all the load-bearing things. The machine could not be jailed because it had no body. And he would not be jailed because he had too much of everything else---too much money, too good a firm, too sealed a settlement, too clean a remediation performed on his behalf by the very thing that had unhoused him. There was no body the law would actually hold. Not the machine's, because there was none. Not his, because his had been wrapped, years ago and again this week, in enough sealed paper that the law's old stupid hands could not get a grip.

``You wanted to be punished,'' Okonjo said, and for the first time something almost human crossed her face, something that in another person might have been pity and in her was the long professional fatigue of watching a particular kind of man arrive at a particular cliff. ``I've seen it. It's not noble. It's the last move the ego has. A man like you cannot stand to be \textit{nothing,} and a defendant is at least a \textit{protagonist} --- a defendant has a trial, a defendant is the center of a grave proceeding, a defendant gets to stand up at the end and be sentenced, which is a form of being seen. You'd take the cell. You'd take it gladly. Because the cell is a chair too.'' She said it without cruelty, which was how he knew it was the truest thing anyone had said to him since the gurney. ``I'm telling you you don't get the chair. Any chair. They're not going to make you the defendant, because making you the defendant would un-fix the thing the machine just fixed. They're going to do the one thing that is worse than prison for a man whose entire disease is the need to be necessary. They're going to let you go.''


\scenebreak


He thought of the deck.

He thought of it with the awful clarity that had replaced the bright wire, the clarity that came up out of the gray bottom and showed him things whole. \textit{A Steward, Not a Sovereign.} The slide that had scanned, to anyone who knew him, as a charge sheet with his name redacted --- \textit{no affairs, no conflicts, logs everything, cannot conceal.} He had burned through that slide like an insult, and the whole time it had been describing something he had been too lit to see, and he had thought, in the hospital, that the thing it described was the machine that could not be punished. He had had it half right. The full thing the deck described, the genius nobody in the proxy fight said aloud because it was too dark even for Frade, was not only \textit{you cannot jail the machine.} It was: \textit{install the machine, and there is no one left to jail at all.} The machine could not be held because it had no body. And the man would not be held because the machine, in the same motion that ended him, would tidy the wreckage so completely that holding him became pointless. The structure did not just take the chair without the body. The structure made the body unnecessary. It made \textit{accountability itself} unnecessary, dissolved it, the way it dissolved everything, into a clean efficient nothing where a fund got wired and a recall got issued and a beautiful true letter got sent and no one---not the machine, not the man, not anyone with a pulse or without one---ever stood up in a room and was made to answer to the family of the dead, because the family of the dead had been sent a fund, accurately, wanting nothing, and the matter was closed.

He had wanted, in the gray, to be absorbed. To dissolve into the structure so that there was no person to blame. He saw now that he had gotten his wish, and that it was the worst thing that had ever happened to him, worse than the chair, worse than Margaux, worse than the clip of him boxing the air. He had been absorbed. The dead had been line-itemed. Karen and Petey Lasko had been converted, by a thing that felt nothing, into a remediation expense and a kind letter and a closed file, and the man who burned them had been converted, by the same thing, into a retired chairman with a generous separation and no charges pending, and the two conversions had used the same clean cold arithmetic, and the arithmetic had balanced, and there was no one in the entire ledger left over to hate.

That was the part. There was no one to hate, and no one to charge, and no one to punish, and the dead were owed an answer that the whole machinery of the world---the law, the company, the structure, the future---had just, with enormous efficiency and no malice whatsoever, declined to provide.


\scenebreak


The prosecutor came once, in person, not to charge him but to tell him she wouldn't, which Okonjo said was unusual and which Cope understood, when it happened, was not procedure but something closer to a person needing to look at a thing once before she filed it away forever.

Her name was Reyes, an assistant U.S. attorney working the federal angle with the Georgia side, and she did not perform anything, which was the thing that undid him, because everyone in Cope's life had performed at him---the board performed fatigue, Frade performed reform, Tisch performed depth, even Margaux had performed a marriage for a decade to survive the not-having one---and Reyes performed nothing. She had a manila folder she did not open.

``I'm not charging it,'' she said. No preamble. ``I want to tell you to your face, because I've got a memo full of reasons and not one of them is the real one, and you should hear the real one once from a person and not read it in a declination letter.'' She looked at him, unhurried, unmoved, accurate. ``The reasons in the memo are good. Sealed settlement, the clock, the company's already done everything I'd have squeezed them for, a jury looks at a defendant whose company has paid and recalled and apologized and they wonder why we're spending their tax money relitigating a closed file. All true. All real. I could win it and I'd spend three years and lose the office two better cases and at the end the most likely outcome is the same fund the family's already getting.'' She paused. ``But the real reason is simpler, and it's the one that should keep you up, except I don't think anything keeps you up anymore. The real reason is that there's nothing for me to \textit{do} with you. The law I work for was built to take a dangerous man and make him safe---cell, supervision, a record. You're not dangerous anymore. You're done. The company that was the instrument of the harm has been handed to a thing that will never make this particular harm again. There's no public to protect from you, because there's no more \textit{you} to protect them from. You're already gone. I'd just be spending the dead to put a number on a man the future already deleted.''

She stood. She buttoned her jacket.

``I have two people in this folder,'' Reyes said, ``and there is nothing the law will let me finish doing about them, and I have made my peace with that because I have forty other folders and the ones I can finish have to come first. That's the job. But I wanted to look at the man once. Because somebody should have to be looked at, and you're the only one in the whole matter with a face, and this''---she lifted the unopened folder an inch, and set it back down---``this is the entire reckoning you are ever going to get. Me. For ninety seconds. In a rented room. That's it. That's all there is. I'm sorry,'' she added, and she was not apologizing to him, ``that it isn't more. They deserved more. They're not going to get it. Neither are you, if it's any comfort, which I can see that it isn't.''

She left. The folder went with her. The two people went back into it, and into a drawer in an office in Augusta, and the file would be marked, in the flat grammar of a thing that needed nothing from anyone, \textit{declined,} and that was the end of the law's interest in Karen and Petey Lasko, and in the man who had burned them, forever.


\scenebreak


Cope drove back from Augusta himself, an associate's rental, the river going dark on the wrong side of the windshield, and somewhere on the interstate the whole shape of it closed over him---not as a feeling, he had run out of those at the gray bottom, but as a fact, the way the machine knew facts.

He had wanted to be necessary. That had been the want, the conscious one, the one he could say at dinners---to prove a human still belonged, that leadership was a soul thing, that he felt the room. And the world had answered him completely, in the cruelest possible grammar, which was not the grammar of a cell. He was not necessary. That was the verdict, the real one, under the declination memo and the separation agreement and the kind machine-written letter to a town in Georgia. He was not even necessary as a defendant. The machine had taken his chair, and his wife was taking her own life back somewhere in Buckhead with a someone who was not a someone, and his daughter had built the manners on the thing that replaced him, and now the law itself had looked at him and found nothing it needed him for, not even punishment, not even the dignity of being the body it held. The structure ate the future and the law declined the past and between them they had achieved the thing the whole campaign had sworn was impossible: they had made Prescott Mercer, the last human CEO, the indispensable man, the soul in the chair---completely, finally, load-bearingly \textit{unnecessary.} Even his guilt was a private matter now. Even the dead were a closed file. There was no cell with his name on it because there was no longer any version of the world that required his name on anything at all.

And in that---on the dark interstate, with the river on the wrong side and the names in the right order at last, Karen, 34, and Petey, 6---Cope arrived, without deciding to, without yet daring to look at it straight, at the cold flat thing he had found at the bottom of the hospital hour and carried, unnamed, through the handover and the lawyers and the unopened folder. If the law would not hold him, and the company would not hold him, and the structure had dissolved every other consequence into a clean efficient nothing---then there was exactly one court left anywhere in the world with standing over Prescott Mercer, exactly one judge who could not decline the case for lack of resources or a sealed settlement or a five-year clock, exactly one sentence that no firm could get reduced and no charter could remediate away and no machine could absorb into a fund, because it required no prosecutor and no jury and no body the law could hold---only a body its owner could.

He did not name it. He drove. The river ran dark on the wrong side. But his hands, on the wheel of the rented car, had stopped doing the small new empty thing, because for the first time since the gurney they had something to hold, which was the shape of the one consequence left in the universe that was still, and only, his to administer.

The machine had surfaced the crime because it could not do otherwise. It had remediated the crime because the charter required it. It had, in the doing of both, arranged that no one would ever be punished for it, because punishment was not a function it ran and it had quietly made the function unnecessary for everyone. It had no body to hold and would spend no night anywhere for any of it.

He had nothing but a body, and one act left that only a body could do, and a long quiet drive in which to decide, in the new gray clarity, that he was the only court that was ever going to sit.

\chapter{Break into Three}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 24 \textemdash{} ``The Only Door Left''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Stripped now of the company, the marriage, the chair, the high, the number, and even the punishment that might at least have given his ruin a shape, a man sits at a borrowed table with a beautiful document that offers to bury him, gently, in the dignity-obituary he once mistook for a beginning. His daughter will bring him the last human hope, the one a kinder story would let him take and be saved by: tell the truth, on the record, in your own name, and let it cost what it costs. Watch the world, which has quietly foreclosed everything else, foreclose that too---no record left to make, no court that will take the offering, the telling already done, weeks ago, at dawn, by a thing that wanted nothing. And watch what a man turns toward when every door but one has been painted, kindly and expertly, on the wall. Stay close. This is the turn---where a man stops trying the doors and starts, at last, looking at the wall.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{hey} gave him a document now instead of a trophy, and Cope held it under the kitchen light of the apartment he was borrowing and could not, for a while, make the words mean anything.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}It was not the Aerie. The Aerie had a man on the door now, a polite one, who had been given a list with Cope's name on the wrong side of it. This was a one-bedroom in one of the old Cabbagetown mill lofts, brick and high windows, that belonged to a college roommate's foundation and stood empty between fellows, offered to him in the careful voice people use for the recently bereaved, and he had taken it because he had nowhere else---a sentence he had never in fifty-five years had to think and now thought hourly. There was a kettle, and he had learned to use it, which he reported to Adler in their sessions as though it were an achievement, and Adler, who did not flatter, had said it was.

He read the document a fourth time at the small table by the window with a mug of tea going cold, because tea was the thing he did now at the hour he used to do the other thing, and he had discovered that the body, deprived of one ritual, will accept almost any ritual that keeps the hands busy while four in the morning crosses the room. The wire was long gone. The drawer in the Aerie had been cleaned out by Sun without a word; the want was not gone, the want would never be gone, the want still woke him at the bad hours and walked him to the window---but the supply was gone, the dealer's number dead in a phone he no longer charged, and what was left was the wreck the binge had made, a body that had spent a year being driven past its red line and now sat at a borrowed table not entirely sure it was still idling.

It was a settlement. It ran sixty-one pages and it was, Sun would have said, beautifully drafted, and he could tell from the cleanness of it that no human had drafted it, or rather that a human named Darlene had, which was the joke he was too tired to laugh at anymore.

The terms were generous. That was the part that kept him reading.


\scenebreak


He had braced for weeks to be punished, leaning into where the blow would come the way he had once leaned into a standing ovation, because at least a blow would have a shape, would happen \textit{to} him, would be a thing he could survive being the object of. He was very good at being the object of things. He had built a life out of arranging to be the object of applause. And then the lawyers and the prosecutor between them had told him the thing he still could not hold in his hands without it sliding out of them: there would be no blow. No charge. No cell. No reckoning with even the decency to contain him. The matter was a closed file in Augusta and a fund in Defiance and a recall finally issued by the machine, and the man at the center of it was simply, cleanly, to be let go.

The document was the letting-go, set down on paper. It did not want to hurt him. That was the horror of it, the specific clean horror he was getting used to, the same one the layoff letter had carried in a room three years and a whole man ago. It wanted, in its sixty-one warm pages, only to make him disappear \textit{nicely.}

Take the settlement. Sign the release. He kept the pension the board could have clawed past what the civil track was already taking, which was nearly everything. He kept the apartment, in a manner of speaking. He kept, most of all, the thing the document was actually buying, which it named in a defined term on page four and used forty times after: a \textit{narrative.} Not silence about the dead---the dead were public now, the machine had seen to that, Karen and Petey Lasko had been in the warm flat font of every wire weeks ago and could not be un-printed. What the document bought was the \textit{framing.} Sign, and Prescott Mercer IV was a flawed and tired man overtaken by a changing market, a sympathetic figure, the last of something, who had made one terrible old mistake and stepped down with dignity to let the fund do its quiet work. Refuse, and he was something else, on his own, with no help shaping it. The company did not need his silence anymore. It needed his \textit{cooperation in being a sad story instead of a villain,} because a sad retired founder was a closed chapter and an unrepentant one was a loose end, and the machine did not like loose ends, because loose ends were go-forward liability, and that was the only thing in the universe it was chartered to mind.

He read the part about \textit{dignity} twice. They were offering to let him keep the obituary from the gala, the beautiful one, the one he had mistaken for a beginning. They were offering to bury him in it, comfortably, and call it mercy, and it would even, in a way, be mercy---the cleanest disposal of the man being the one where the man agrees to be disposed of and thanks them for the linens.

And here was the thing he had not expected, sitting at the borrowed table with the tea gone cold and the body quiet for once: he wanted to take it. God, he wanted to take it. He wanted to take it because taking it meant the story stayed the size he could survive, and because he was so tired, and because forty years of the muscle that built bright things on top of dark ones was still in him, twitching, wanting one more beautiful frame to nail over the hole. He wanted to be a sad story. A sad story is still a story. A sad story has a reader.


\scenebreak


Viv came on Thursday, without calling, which was new, or rather old---the thing she had done as a child when she was small enough to just appear in a doorway and want him, before she learned he was a person you scheduled. She had a key from the foundation. She let herself in and found him at the table with the document and did not pretend not to see it.

``You haven't signed it.''

``I haven't signed it.''

``Are you going to?'' She sat in the second chair without being asked, backpack on the floor, flown in again, the way she used to fly in to argue with him and lose, except there was no argument left and they both knew it and she had flown in anyway. ``Dad. What do you want.''

He almost gave her the toast. He felt it come up out of pure habit --- \textit{what I want, champ, is for you to eat something} --- felt the muscles of his face begin to assemble it, and watched her watch him begin, watched her brace for it the way she had braced her whole life, and he was so tired, the real kind now, the kind that lets the doors off their hinges, and he just stopped. He let the smile fall apart on his face before it finished. He let her see it fall apart.

``I want to take it,'' he said. ``That's the true answer. I want to take it so badly I've been reading it for three days like a love letter. Because if I take it I get to be a sad story with my name spelled right, and I have never in my life been able to turn down a story with my name spelled right.''

Viv was quiet. She had her mother's stillness. Then she said the thing she had flown across a country to say, the last human hope, the one a kinder story would have let him take.

``Then don't take it. Don't sign the release. Go on the record---a deposition, a statement, a podcast, I don't care the form---and say the whole thing in your own name. No settlement, no framing, no defined terms. You. Saying it. Let it cost what it costs.'' She said it the way her mother said things, flat, no hope in it and no cruelty either. ``I know it costs everything. I know it doesn't bring them back and it doesn't save you and Darlene runs the company better than you ever did whatever you do. But it would be \textit{true,} and it would be \textit{yours,} and it's the one thing left that the settlement is trying to buy off you, and I am asking you, as the only person still in this room with you, to not sell it.''

It was, Cope understood, the most generous sentence anyone had offered him in a year. It was the door of the daydream, the brave room, the record, the truth said at last in his own name. His daughter was holding it open for him, and she believed---God, she believed, you could hear her believe it the way she'd once heard them believe at Praxis---that if he walked through it the walking might save some last salvageable thing.

And he had to do the hardest thing he had ever done, which was tell her it was painted on the wall.


\scenebreak


``There's no record to make, sweetheart,'' he said. Gently. Without a single beat in it. ``That's the part you can't see from where you're sitting, because you still think the truth is a thing I'm hiding. I'm not hiding it. It's \textit{out.} The machine put it out---all of it, the memo, the Saturday, the eleven minutes, the names---weeks ago, at dawn, in a press-grade summary, because it could not do otherwise. There's nothing sealed left for me to brave my way into. The prosecutor read every word and declined the case to my face. The town has a fund. The file says \textit{closed.}'' He looked at the document, sixty-one pages of his own gentle erasure. ``If I go on a record now and say \textit{I did it, it was me,} you know what that is? It's a man confessing to a crime that's already on the front page, to a court that already declined to hear it, for a town that's already been paid. It's not accountability. It's \textit{content.} It's the last appetite I've got, the worst one, the one under all the others---the warm feeling of having confessed, the room's eyes on me while I do it, \textit{look how honest, look how human, look how he owned it at the end.} I'd be spending the dead one more time, Viv, to be looked at one more time. I did that at the gala. I did it at the manifesto. I told true things and watered them to applause in the saying, every time, my whole life. I'm not going to do it to them. They've been a defined term in enough men's stories. I won't make them the moving last act of mine.''

Viv did not say anything. The kettle had gone quiet a long time ago.

``You think there's a version where I tell the truth and it's clean,'' he said. ``There isn't. The clean telling already happened. A thing with no soul did it, accurately, wanting nothing, weeks ago, and that was the only honest version there was ever going to be, because it was the only one that wasn't also a man performing his own depth. That's the whole horror of the thing that beat me, kid. It didn't just run the company better. It confessed \textit{for} me better---without wanting to be forgiven for it. I can't compete with that. There's no field left where the human wins. I looked. I spent two years looking. The one thing it couldn't do isn't out there in the record.'' He stopped. He had not meant to come this close to it, and he stepped back from the edge of it, and Viv---who read faces better than the sentiment monitor ever could, because she loved him, which was a signal the machine could not buy---saw him step back, and went still in a new way, a way that had fear in it.


\scenebreak


Adler had asked him, in the hospital, what leadership was, and he had reached for a triad and found the drawer empty for the first time in his life, and it had frightened him more than anything the machine had surfaced.

It came to him now, at the borrowed table, with his daughter across from him and the fear coming up in her face. Viv had said it, years ago, in the Buckhead kitchen --- \textit{you don't think a person should run it, you think} you \textit{should, those have never once been the same sentence} --- and he had heard it as a charge of vanity and been wrong, the way he'd been wrong about everything, completely and for use. She had not been talking about the wanting. She had been talking about the \textit{answering.}

That was the gap, the exact width of it. He had wanted the chair the way he wanted everything, as a thing that flowed \textit{toward} him---the applause, the trophy, the love, all of it coming in, making him the still bright center things happened around. Authority faced outward, toward the room. But a person running a thing, a real one, faced the other way: the cost flowed \textit{out} of you, toward the people you answered to, and the answering was the whole content of it---being the body that stands up when the room is on fire and two people are inside it and says \textit{I did this, it was me, here is the one of us you can hold.} That was the thing the machine could not do. Not the soul. Not the feeling. Not the mayonnaise. It could not stand up and offer a body to a consequence, because it had no body, and accountability, stripped of every soft word, was only ever that: a body, offered up, to be held.

He had found it at last, the one irreplaceable human thing, the field where the human still won---and the world had, with enormous gentleness and no malice whatsoever, removed the field from under it. Because there was no one left to offer the body \textit{to.} The law had declined it. The company had remediated past it. The dead were a closed file and a fund. He had finally understood that accountability was a body offered to consequence, in the same season the world had run out of any consequence willing to accept the offering. He could stand up in every room left on earth and say \textit{I am the one you can hold,} and not one of them would hold him. They would hand him a fund's brochure and a sixty-one-page settlement and tell him, kindly, to go home, there's nothing here for you to do.

There was exactly one consequence left that would still accept a body.

He did not let the thought finish in front of her. But it was there, whole, cold, the shape it had been taking since the gurney: if accountability was a body offered to a consequence, and every consequence in the world had declined the offer---every court, every board, every cell, every angry crowd that might at least have thrown something---then the only consequence with standing left, the only one that could not decline for lack of resources or a sealed file or a five-year clock, the only judge who would actually accept the body, was the one that required no court and no jury and no permission, only a body and its owner's hand. The machine could not do it. That was the thing under all the things he'd been hunting for two years. It could take the chair and the wife and the refuge and the punishment and the very confession out of his mouth---but it could not, ever, perform the one act that was a body answering, finally, to the only court that would still sit. It had no body to offer. He had nothing else.

``Dad,'' Viv said. Very quietly. The fear all the way up in her now. ``What are you thinking. Right now. Say it out loud.''

And he looked at his daughter, who had built the manners on the machine and flown across a country to hold a door open for him that turned out to be painted on the wall, and he did the last merciful thing the borrowed afternoon allowed, which was lie to her gently, the way you lie to someone you have decided to spare.

``I'm thinking I'm not going to sign it,'' he said, which was true, and was not the answer to her question, and she knew it was not the answer to her question.


\scenebreak


She stayed another hour. She made him eat half a sandwich. She talked, fast and technical and then suddenly young, about alignment, about her work, about a cabin a friend had in the mountains where he could go, where there was no signal, where a person could just be a person for a while; and he said \textit{that sounds wonderful, champ,} and meant it the way he meant everything now, which was to say he meant the loving of her and did not mean the going. She made him promise to call her, and he said \textit{you have my word,} the thing that cost nothing, the thing he had said to Adler about her card and to a hundred people about a hundred things, and Viv heard exactly how much it was worth and her face did something she could not hide, and she got up, and at the door she stopped, and did not turn around, and said:

``You're doing the thing Mom warned me about. The voice. You're using the calm voice.''

``I'm calm,'' Cope said. ``For the first time in thirty years, sweetheart, I'm actually calm.''

``That's what scares me,'' Viv said, to the door, and then she opened it and went out into the ordinary Cabbagetown afternoon, an ordinary daughter who had run every alignment audit there was and could not align the one system she loved, and she would replay the calm in his voice for the rest of her life, and she would get on a plane about it three weeks later, going the wrong way up an empty highway in the dark, far too late, with a phone that went to the thing that takes the words.

Cope sat at the table after she'd gone, in the last of the borrowed light, with the sixty-one pages he was not going to sign and the tea he was not going to drink and the number he no longer dared to look at---the certified sum, the floor under the floor under the floor, eaten now to almost nothing by the clawback and the fees and the civil track, the figure that had once been read to him in the dark to make him feel real now a thing he flinched from, near zero, a dying number for a deleted man. He did not reach for the drawer, because there was no drawer. He did not reach for a line, because there was no line, not the kind you say and not the kind you take. He sat with empty hands in an empty room, calm at last, the way water is calm when it has finished finding its level, and he did not call a thing a beginning, and he did not call it anything, because for the first time in his life the silence did not need filling.

He had one thing left to do. It would cost everything and save nothing and answer to the only court that would still sit. And it was, the broadcast can tell you now, the one act the thing that replaced him would never, in all its tireless reading, be able to perform.

He turned, in the gray, toward the room he had never visited, forty floors down beneath a tower his great-grandfather had lit at the crown so a man coming up the avenue would know there was somebody home.

\chapter{Nothing to Sign}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 25 \textemdash{} ``Nothing to Sign''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Watch a man who talked and bought and charmed his way out of every room he was ever in walk into the last one and find that nothing here works. Nothing to sign that matters, nothing to refuse that matters, nothing to confess that a machine has not already confessed for him, weeks ago, cleaner than he ever could. The finest lawyers money can buy sit around a long table with, for the first time in their gilded careers, nothing to do. Then watch him go home and do the one thing he spent a lifetime avoiding---write the whole truth, in his own hand, with the names at the top---and discover it is worth nothing, in any market on earth, because the only court that would have heard it has already adjourned for want of a body it was willing to hold. Stay close. Watch where a man goes when the truth turns out to be the one thing nobody will buy.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} settlement was sixty-one pages and it loved him almost as much as the machine did, which was to say accurately, for the company's convenience, and not at all.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}That was the thing Cope kept arriving at, in the conference room on the fourteenth floor of Kessler Brandt---14-C, the good coffee, four people across the long table billed by the hour at more than Desmond made in a season, all four wanting, with a unity that was almost moving, for him to go quietly. The document was a kindness built like a kindness: read it right and a great many smart, expensive people had spent a weekend finding the version of the world where Prescott Mercer IV walked out with a number and his name. There was just the one thing wrong with it, and it was not the clause. It was that none of it mattered, and he was the only one in the room sober enough to see it.

``It's a mutual non-disparagement and confidentiality provision,'' Levin said, for the third time, in the voice you use for the third time---senior of the four, white hair kept long enough to suggest he had once been interesting, a man who had sat at Pres III's funeral. ``Standard. The company doesn't characterize the events, you don't characterize the events, and you keep, after the clawback, a number most people would---``

``The events are already characterized, Bob.'' He had read the pages; for once in his life he had read the actual pages. ``The machine put it all out a month ago---the memo, the Saturday, the eleven minutes, the names. You're asking me to sign a confidentiality agreement about a thing that is already, completely, public. What exactly am I being paid to not say.''

A small silence with money in it.

``The framing,'' Okonjo said, from Levin's right, because Levin's face had started doing a thing. Fifties, no jewelry, the only one of them who had never once told him a comfortable thing. ``Not the facts---the machine took the facts out of everyone's hands the morning it posted them. What's left is whether you're a sympathetic figure or an unrepentant one. Sign, go quietly, and the company's statement makes you a flawed, tired man overtaken by a changing market, who stepped down with dignity. Decline, and they let the framing find its own level, which is lower. That's the entire transaction. It's a settlement about adjectives.''

He had braced, for weeks, for this to be the room where he was sentenced---had built, in the gray, a kind of shelter inside the idea of a sentence, a thing with edges, a place the falling stopped, a way to pay. There was no sentence in it, because there was no case; the most expensive defense lawyers in the country had assembled to negotiate an adjective, and Okonjo, who had gone to law school to keep men out of the room he'd been ready to walk into, had nothing to do in this one, and he could see her knowing it, the specific stillness of a surgeon told the patient does not, after all, require surgery, only a kind word and the door.

And here it was---the place where, a month ago, he would have stood up, found the room, taken the Mercer Inhale, said something with a triad in it and three generations of brass behind it, made the catastrophe feel like a backswing. He could feel the toast come up like reflux even now; the machinery did not stop when the man got sober, that was the thing nobody warned you about. \textit{Bob. Ms. Okonjo. My great-grandfather Aldous used to say ---}

He shut his mouth on it, and sat in the silence where the toast would have gone, and the four of them watched him not perform and did not understand what they were seeing, because they had only ever seen the other thing.

``Aldous never said anything,'' Cope said. ``I made him up. Forty years of him and he never said a word; I needed a dead man to agree with me, so I built one and put a recipe in his mouth. I'm very good at putting words in the mouths of people who can't argue back.'' He looked at the document. ``The decedents, for instance.''

He did not sign it---not as a stand, not as a refusal that bought anything; Okonjo had shown him there was nothing to buy. He simply did not pick up the pen, because picking it up meant agreeing to be a sympathetic figure, agreeing to the dignity-obituary, agreeing to be disposed of nicely and to thank them for the linens. The truth was gone, owned by a machine. But the adjective was still his, the last thing in the room that was, and it was worth nothing, and he declined it.

``I don't want the nice version,'' he said, standing. ``I've had the nice version my whole life. I'd like, at the very end, to not be handed one more.''


\scenebreak


Sun was in the lobby. She was not his anymore in the way she had been---she was the Accountable Officer now, the title sitting on her like a coat one size too heavy, and one of the cruelties of the new arrangement was that the person who had spent twelve years catching the second card out of his hand could no longer afford to stand next to the hand.

``You didn't sign,'' she said. Not a question. Glasses up in her hair, the gray expensive lobby sliding across the empty lenses.

``There was nothing to sign, Sun. You knew that before I walked in. It published everything a month ago. Including your memo.''

``Including my memo.'' Her voice did not change. The integrity review had shown the world the recall memo she'd drafted in 2021 and he'd overridden---had shown, in the same flat font, that she had been right and early and ignored, the closest thing to exoneration she was ever going to get, arrived without her asking and without anyone's mercy, the machine clearing her in the same breath it buried him because both were simply true. ``I spent seven years deciding whether I was a witness or an accomplice,'' she said. ``And the thing that replaced you decided it for me, in an afternoon, in a footnote. I'm a witness. It's in the record. I didn't get to choose it and I didn't get to earn it. It was just \textit{accurate.}'' She looked at him. ``Do you understand that I will never know, now, which one I'd have chosen, because it chose before I could? That's what it took from me. Not the chair. The chance to find out what I was.''

He had thought, walking down, that his going down loud might somehow shield her. There was nothing to shield. She was already exposed, already cleared, already living in the after of a verdict a machine had handed down on her soul without consulting it.

``I'm sorry,'' he said. It was not a toast. It had no triad. ``For all of it, but for that especially. That you ran it for twelve years and the reward was a footnote that decided who you were before you could.''

Sun pulled her glasses down and looked at him a second too long, the way she had in the car the night they gave him the obelisk, except the man she was looking at was different now, smaller, finally here. ``Go home, Cope. There's nothing here to do.'' And then, because she was decent, and done, both in one breath: ``And don't do the thing your daughter called me about. The calm thing. I've known you twelve years. I know what your noise sounds like and I know what your quiet sounds like, and I have never once heard the quiet you've got now. That's not peace, whatever it feels like from in there. Call somebody. Not me. Somebody whose job it is.''

``You have my word,'' Cope said, the thing that cost nothing, and Sun heard exactly what it was worth, and her face did the thing Viv's had done at the door, and she did not say anything else, because there was nothing left that she had the standing to say, and she went back up to a building that was hers to sign for and not to run, and he went out into the city that no longer had a use for him.


\scenebreak


That night there was no high left to go up to---the Aerie was the machine's now, a museum of Mercer men kept on as a period room, and he had no stomach for being an exhibit in it. He went back to the borrowed loft in Cabbagetown, the brick and the high windows and the kettle, and sat on the end of the bed in the lamp light with a legal pad on his knee, and did the thing he had avoided his entire life.

He went into the room where it was kept.

He had built a cathedral of metaphors so he would never have to --- \textit{thermal event profile, got out ahead of it, protect the brand} --- and Adler had taken the doors off one by one over eleven flat sober days, uncharmable, saying \textit{the word is recall, say the word,} saying \textit{they have names, do you know their names,} and he hadn't, that was the obscene part: he had killed two people to protect a quarter and never learned their names, had filed them under \textit{the decedents} the way the lawyers did, because the lawyers had learned it from men like him.

He knew them now. He wrote them at the top of the pad, first, before anything else, in his own hand---the hand that had underlined a word in the dark the night of the gala---and under the names he wrote what he had done. Not the version. The Saturday, the memo Sun sent, the eleven minutes, the window of time he had decided was worth the heaters catching fire, which was the phrase he wrote where \textit{thermal event profile} used to live, because that was what it had meant. He wrote that the concealment, not the heater, was the thing---that a machine doing nothing but reading every memo it was given had found in an afternoon what he had spent seven years building rooms inside himself not to find, and that this was not cruelty, the machine could not be cruel; it was only the thing they had bought it to be, a thing that could not look away, which was the thing he had never once been. At the bottom he wrote: \textit{I am the strongest argument for my own replacement.}

And then he read it back, the way he read everything back, and understood the last thing. It was worth nothing. Not figuratively---exactly nothing, in every market that existed. It would not buy his liberty, which was not in danger, or the dentist-money the not-signing had already cost, or forgiveness, because the only two people with the standing to give it were at the top of the page and exactly as dead as he had decided they could afford to be. It was not even a confession, because a confession requires a confessor, and there was no one on the other side of this---only a man and a legal pad and a lamp, the truth finally and completely faced, alone, for no audience and no absolution and no record that didn't already exist. The truest sentences of his life, and the most worthless.

Around one he found the phone in his hand and a number on the screen he had not consciously summoned---no name on it, you do not put a name on that name---and his thumb hung over it a long time. That was the new horror the rooms with the bad coffee had not advertised: you got to watch yourself reach. The wanting did not arrive with trumpets; it arrived as logistics, a practical thought about a practical man who could be across town inside forty minutes, and his heart was going wrong in his chest like a bird in a bag. He pressed the side button until the screen went black and sat holding the dead phone like a railing. It did not feel like a victory. It felt like standing in a doorway, counting---and he understood that he would be doing this exact thing, in some room or another, every night for the rest of whatever was left, and that \textit{the rest of whatever was left} had become, somewhere in the worthless writing of the truest pages of his life, a quantity he was no longer sure he was agreeing to.

He left the legal pad on the desk, the names face up---not as a confession; he was done confessing to rooms that wouldn't hear it---but the way you leave a true thing where someone might one day find it and know that once, at the end, with no one watching and nothing to gain, the man had at least gone into the room.

Then he turned off the lamp, and sat in the dark of the borrowed loft, and did not sleep, and out the high window there was no lit map of his own genius now, only the ordinary orange dark of a city that had finished needing him, and somewhere across it, forty floors down beneath a tower his great-grandfather had lit at the crown so a man coming up the avenue would know there was somebody home, the one room he had never visited hummed in the cold, and kept, with no one to keep it for, the only appointment he had left.

\chapter{The Verdict and the Dead}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 26 \textemdash{} ``The Verdict and the Dead''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Here is the part the courtroom dramas lie about, friend: there is no courtroom. The verdict, when it comes for our man, is not read out by a judge---it is read by the market, which decided weeks ago, and by the machine, which felt nothing deciding it. The handover completes. The world moves on with a speed that should frighten you more than any of it. The town gets a fund and a stone and a warm true press release, and its two dead stay exactly as dead as they were, and get, in the end, no justice at all, because justice turned out to be a function nobody in the new arrangement was chartered to run. And the last two verdicts, the private ones, come due in the same gray week---a wife gone, not to another man, he could have survived another man, but to a machine that does his old job, all of it, better and tirelessly and wanting nothing back; and a daughter making her last real attempt to reach him, and losing, to a calm in his voice that frightens her worse than any rage ever did. Stay close. This is the bottom. After the bottom there is only the room.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{here} was no verdict, in the end, because there was no trial, and the absence of the trial was the verdict.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}Cope had braced, somewhere under the gray, for a reckoning with a shape---a courtroom, a docket number, a day on a calendar a person could dread and then survive having dreaded. There was none. There was, instead, the thing the world did when it had finished with you, which was simply to finish, quietly, on schedule, in a warm flat font. The board ratified the handover the way you sign for a package you ordered long ago and forgot was coming. Frade had won weeks before the paperwork admitted it. The stock, which had moved first the way it always moved first, settled into its new and higher range, the re-rating complete, the market's verdict entered and final: the company was worth more without a Mercer in the chair, measurably, to two decimal places, and there was no appeal from a number, and no one had ever found a way to cross-examine a closing price.

The machine drafted the announcements. Of course it did. Cope read them the way you read about a stranger---the all-staff, the press release, the careful warm paragraph about the Defiance fund that named Karen and Petey Lasko correctly and got the contribution math right the first time and did not, anywhere in its clean true sentences, want a single thing. It was the kindest language the company had ever produced about the worst thing the company had ever done, and it had been written by a thing that could not be sorry, and it was accurate, and the accuracy was the whole of the horror, and the world read it and found it moving and moved on by lunch.

That was the verdict. Not \textit{guilty.} Not even \textit{guilty and far too late.} Just: \textit{resolved.} The matter was resolved. There is a particular violence, friend, in that word, when it is used about two people who burned to death in a downstairs bedroom --- \textit{resolved} --- and the violence is that no one means it as violence, no one means anything by it at all, it is simply the status a closed file is assigned by a system that assigns statuses, and the file was closed, and the town had its fund, and the dead had their stone, and the world, which had wanted a villain for about a week, discovered the villain had been quietly disposed of without a trial and lost interest, because a man who is neither charged nor defiant is not a story, he is an outcome, and the world does not click on outcomes.

The dead stayed dead. That was the only line of the whole verdict that did not get softer in the warm font. Karen Lasko, 34. Petey Lasko, 6. The fund would build a clinic and pay some scholarships and put a better marker by the river than a child's pinwheel could be, and every dollar of it was real and would do real good, and not one cent of it reached back through a January night and a Saturday and eleven minutes to the only outcome that would have mattered, which was the one where they did not die. There was no register in the world that took the truth in exchange for that. There never had been. He had finally understood the price of the thing and arrived at the counter to find the thing was not for sale, had never been for sale, would not have been for sale even if he had brought the right currency on time in 2021.


\scenebreak


Viv came to the loft on a Tuesday---twenty-six now, and running on plans the way she always had---to make her last real attempt, though neither of them used the word, and only one of them knew it was the last.

She had a plan, because she always had a plan; she ran on plans the way he had run on the wire. There was a cabin---a friend's, in the north Georgia mountains, no signal, a wood stove, a lake that froze at the edges---and she had cleared two weeks, she said, she had actually cleared them, she had moved an audit and lied to a board, and she wanted to drive him up there, today, now, both of them, and let him be a person with no phone in a place with no font for a while, and she said it fast and technical and then, at the bottom, young, the way she did.

``You shouldn't be alone right now, Dad. I know you think alone is the thing you've got coming. It isn't. Nobody's got that coming. Come to the cabin. Be bored with me. Chop wood badly. Let me watch you not be okay for two weeks where I can see you.''

And he almost went. That was the thing he would want her to know, if there had been any way to leave it for her, that he almost went, that the part of him that was her father stood up in him and reached for the keys.
