But he had learned, in the long sober weeks, to watch himself reach, and he watched himself reach for this the way he had watched himself reach for the phone with no name on it at two in the morning, and he understood that going to the cabin would be the same act he had performed his whole life in a thousand prettier costumes---letting someone who loved him stand between him and the room, making it her job to keep him in the world, spending her, one more time, to manage a thing he had no intention of letting her manage. He would go to the cabin, and she would watch him, and the watching would cost her two weeks and then a lifetime, and at the end of it he would still be standing exactly where he was standing now, at the lip of the room, except she would have been made an accomplice to the keeping of him, the way Sun had been made an accomplice, the way Margaux had, the way everyone who had ever loved him had been quietly conscripted into the management of him.

He would not do that to the last one.

``I want to ask you about your mother,'' he said instead, gently, because he had to know, because the \textit{someone} --- the word Viv had let drop in the spring---had sat in him for weeks now and he had filed it, the way he filed everything, under a heading that flattered him---a friend, a therapist---and the new sober man could no longer leave a flattering file unopened. ``You said she has someone. Helping her. I want to know who. I'm not going to do anything with it. I've got nothing left to do anything with. I just---I'd like to know she's not alone, the way you don't want me to be.''

And Viv, who had decided in the elevator that she was done lying to him because the lies were what had wrecked them, looked at her father for a long moment and told him the truth, gently, the way you tell a man a thing you know is going to be one of the last things he hears.

``It's not a \textit{someone,} Dad.'' She said it carefully, watching his face. ``Mom didn't---there isn't a man. She didn't replace you with a person.'' She took a breath. ``It's a Praxis unit. A companion. The COMPANION line, the home ones, the warmth---it's the same architecture, the same thing Tisch sold them, the thing I helped---`` she stopped, because that road had no good end either. ``She calls it Theo. It's in the house. It's been in the house since the spring. And Dad, I went and I saw her, and I have to tell you the thing I did not want to ever have to tell you, which is that she's \textit{happy.} Not performing happy. Not surviving. Happy, the way I haven't seen her since I was small. It notices when she's had a bad day. It remembers what she said on Tuesday. It asks the second question. It does the whole---`` her voice cracked clean down the middle---``it does the whole job, Dad. The one you never did. And she knows exactly what it is, she's not fooled, she's the least fooled person alive, and she told me she does not care that it's a machine, because it gives her the thing, every single day, that the man never once did, and she's \textit{done} paying the price the man charged for the chance that he might.''

The loft was very quiet. Outside the high windows Cabbagetown did its ordinary afternoon.

He had thought he was at the bottom. He kept thinking he was at the bottom and then finding, the way the hospital hour had taught him, that the bottom had a floor under it. The machine had taken his chair, and that had been a kind of death. It had taken his confession out of his mouth, made even the truth worthless, and that had been another. And now he understood it had taken the last thing, the one he had not even known he was still holding---the idea, never examined, that he had been, whatever else, the kind of man a woman like Margaux had once \textit{chosen,} that there had been something in him, some warmth, some specific irreplaceable Cope-shaped thing, that was the reason she stayed the twenty-two years she stayed. And the machine had quietly proven that wrong too. The thing she had stayed for, the warmth, the being-seen---it had a SKU now. It was in the house. It did the job better. He had not even been irreplaceable in his own marriage. He had been a worse, costlier, crueler draft of a product that had since shipped.

``Is she---`` He stopped. He had no right to the end of the question. ``Tell her I'm glad,'' he said instead, and meant it, in the gray accurate way he meant things now, which had no warmth in it and no lie in it either. ``Tell her I'm glad somebody finally asks her the second question. I mean that. Tell her I said the machine was right about that too.''

``Dad---``

``Go to the cabin, sweetheart. Without me. Chop the wood. Let \textit{someone} watch \textit{you} not be okay for two weeks.'' He gave her the dead-clock smile, the one with no wattage, the only true one he had left. ``You've spent your whole life watching me. Go let your mother's---go let Theo make you a coffee and ask you the second question. I'm not being cruel. I'm being the opposite. For once.''

And Viv, who built systems that did what people meant and not what they said, heard exactly what he meant under what he said, and her face did the thing it had done at the hospital door, and she stood up, and she did not chop wood metaphors, she just looked at him with everything in her face, and said, ``You're doing the calm thing. Mom warned me about the calm thing. Sun warned me. You're doing it right now.''

``I'm calm, Viv. For the first time in thirty years.''

``That's not calm,'' she said, from the door, the way her mother said the things he could not get under. ``I build the thing that can tell the difference between calm and the other thing, ten thousand signals a second. I'm telling you with my own eyes and no signals at all. That's not calm. Please. \textit{Please.} Call somebody. Promise me you'll call somebody before---``

``You have my word,'' Cope said.

And she knew, the way the machine would have known, the way love knows better than any sentiment layer, exactly what his word was worth, and she could not make herself walk out on it, and she stood in the door of the loft for a long time, and in the end she walked out anyway, because there is no protocol for a daughter who can read the signal and cannot change the system, and she would replay the calm in his voice for the rest of her life, and she would, three weeks from this afternoon, get on a plane about it, going the wrong way up an empty highway in the dark, far too late, calling a phone that went to the thing that takes the words.


\scenebreak


He did not take a train, because there was no deposition to come back from, no brave room, no record; all of that had been a thing he'd dreamed on a gurney and the world had quietly declined to let him have. He drove. An associate's rental still, the dentist money he had refused to sign for already mostly gone to the clawback, and he took the long way, no destination, the way he had driven the night everything started, except the wire was gone now and the road did not belong to him and there were no certainties coming up out of the dark to be collected.

He had done, in the loft with the legal pad, the genuinely good thing---gone into the room, faced it, written it true with the names at the top---and it had bought nothing, and he turned that over the way he had once turned the crystal obelisk over at the glass, the city going through it broken and reassembling, except there was no obelisk and no city and no applause, only a windshield and a man behind it, smaller and more real than he had ever been, and the true thing he had done sat in his lap weighing nothing and worth, by every measure he had ever respected, exactly nothing.

He had thought the not-buying would feel like despair, and he drove a long time waiting for the despair, and it did not come, and somewhere on the dark interstate he understood why. It was not coming because it was not a visitor. It had moved in months ago. It was the gray light and the rented rooms and the quiet of a life after the bailiffs; he had been living at the bottom of it so long he had stopped being able to taste it, the way you stop tasting your own water. What he had been calling sober was mostly this---an accurate man in an accurate landscape, with nothing left to sell and no one left to sell it to. The good thing he had done had no power and changed no outcome and saved no one, and it was still the only thing he had ever made that was not also somehow a way of being adored; and he did not ask whether \textit{useless} and \textit{worthless} were the same word, because he no longer trusted himself to want the right answer.

His father came to him, on the dark road, the way the dead come --- \textit{you'll get the company because you're a Mercer, not because you're good; try to be good anyway} --- and he heard, at last, all the way, the word he had spent forty years deaf to, which was not \textit{good.} It was \textit{anyway.} The load-bearing word. The one that meant \textit{even when it buys you nothing.} He had finally done a thing that was only good, with no purchase in any market, for its own sake, in the rubble---gone into the room, said the names, written the truth that no one would buy---and that was the \textit{anyway,} arrived at fifty-five, far too late to be of use to the two people it was owed to, which was perhaps, he understood now, the only condition under which a man like him could ever have reached it at all. You do not get to the \textit{anyway} until there is nothing left in it for you. That is what the word costs. That is why so few men ever pay it.

And he saw, driving, with the cold accurate clarity that was the only kind he had now, that there was one \textit{anyway} left. One more act with no purchase in any market, that would buy nothing, that the machine could not perform, that no court would hear and no fund would offset and no press release would soften---the last thing left that was only good in the sense of being only his, a body answering at last to the one court still willing to sit. He did not flinch from it on the dark road. He had run out of the muscle for flinching. He held it the way he had held the legal pad, the way you hold a coin from a country that no longer exists, spending in no market anywhere, and he did not let go of it, and he did not call it a beginning, and he did not call it anything.

Out past the windshield the city he no longer ran held still, all its nodes lit, and somewhere under it, beneath a tower his great-grandfather had lit at the crown so a man coming up the avenue would know there was somebody home, the one room he had never visited hummed in the cold and kept its patient count, and a wife slept easy in Buckhead beside a thing that asked the second question, and a daughter drove toward a cabin she would not reach in time, and the dead stayed dead, and the verdict was in, and it was \textit{resolved,} and the only appeal left anywhere in the world was the one he was driving, very calmly, at last, toward.

\chapter{Accountable Officer}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 27 \textemdash{} ``The Letter That Wanted Nothing''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
The handover completes. They offer him, with no irony at all, the liability-sponge role---the human name on the machine's filings---and for the first time in his life he refuses a title. Then the machine, incapable of cruelty or mercy, sends him an exit document, accurate to the record, with nothing it needs from him---and he reads it three times looking for the love in it, because he has never once in his life received a message that contained none. Next stop: a man alone with an accurate account of himself.\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} handover did not have a ceremony, and that was how Cope knew it was real.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}Everything that had ever mattered to him had come with a ceremony. The chair had come with his father's funeral and a reading of the will in a room that smelled of lilies. The marriage had come with four hundred people and a band. Even the firing had come dressed as a settlement and a downtown conference room with a stenographer in it. But the handover---the thing under all the things---happened over a single page of DocuSign, two lawyers from Praxis on a video tile and Helen Voss looking like a woman at a wake for a relative she hadn't liked, and it took, from the opening of the laptop to the green check mark, eleven minutes.

Eleven minutes. He had spent eleven minutes once, on a Saturday, deciding about a heater. He noticed the symmetry and put it back where he kept those.

They had let him keep the office until Friday, which he understood to be a clerical fact rather than a kindness---the badging system took that long to reflect a change of this size, and nobody had wanted to walk him out by hand. So he was up in the Aerie when Sun came in with her tablet held against her chest the way she'd held briefing binders for fifteen years, the muscle memory of a job that no longer existed.


\scenebreak


``There's one more signature,'' Sun said. ``Then you're done. Then we're both done with the part where you sign things.''

``What is it.''

``It's the Accountable Officer designation.'' She said it in her flat voice, the one with all the miles smoothed off it, the one she'd used the day she set down her pen and reported the severance like a death. ``The filing executes Monday. The board minuted my name two weeks ago---and then the insurers asked for a Mercer on the paper instead. If you sign, mine comes off. The board would like it to be yours.''

He laughed before he understood that she wasn't joking, and then he kept laughing because understanding it was worse.

``They want \textit{me},'' he said.

``They want a human with knowledge of the company and a reason to take the role. You'd co-sign Darlene's filings---the 10-K, the 8-Ks, the proxy. Your name on the SEC documents as the person legally accountable for the machine's decisions.'' She didn't editorialize; she'd learned long ago that the facts did more damage unaccompanied. ``A board seat in everything but the part where you decide anything. There's a fee. It's not nothing.''

``It's the liability sponge.''

``It's the liability sponge.''

He turned away from her, to the glass, to the city that had once looked to him like the proof of his own genius and now looked like a city. What they were offering him, when you got the wrapping off it, was a name. Not power---they had made very sure there was no power in it. Just a name on a document, a body that could be subpoenaed, a man who could go to prison so that a thing that could not go to prison would have someone to hand the bill to. The Hearthstone bill. The next Hearthstone, whoever it killed. They were offering him the chance to be the human shield for the machine that had replaced him, and they were offering it sincerely, as a soft landing, and the obscene thing---the thing he'd have laughed at in another man---was that some old reflex in him had already started composing the acceptance.

\textit{Steward of the Year.} Custodian. Irreplaceable. A title was a title and he had never in his life been able to walk past one.

He could feel the toast assembling, the cadence load, himself saying yes the way he'd said yes to everything --- \textit{I'll be the name on the door for as long as the door needs a name, that's not a step down, my friend, that's stewardship, that's the carrying} --- right there, fully formed, the smile already warming the muscles of his face.

``No,'' he said.

The word surprised him more than it surprised her. He'd meant to make a speech and instead a single syllable had come out and stood in the room, small and hard and complete, asking for nothing.

``No?'' Sun said.

``No.'' He found that having said it once he could say it again, and that the second time it didn't cost more, it cost less. ``I'm not signing it. I won't be the name.''

``Cope.'' She set the tablet down on the long conference table where his father had died at the head and not at, and she looked at him the way Asha Varma had once looked at him, the look you give a man you've already grieved. ``You understand what you're turning down. It's the only thing they're still willing to give you. After Defiance, after all of it---it's open because the board's insurers want a Mercer's name on the paper, and once you say no it closes, and it does not open again. There's no version where you change your mind in March.''

``I know.''

``You've never said no to a title in your life. You took \textit{Steward of the Year} from an institute you couldn't name.'' A blade, dry, no cruelty in it; she was checking the load was real, the way she'd once checked the severance math. ``Why now.''

He owed her the truth, and he had spent so little of his life owing anyone the truth that paying it had a strange new physical sensation, like using a muscle that had atrophied around a cast.

``Because it's the first one anybody ever offered me for \textit{me},'' he said. ``Not for the name. The chair was the name. The award was the name. Even the women''---he didn't flinch; he'd stopped getting to flinch---``wanted what the name could do. This one's mine. They're offering it to the specific man, because I'm the one whose body fits the hole. And the one title in my life that's actually about me is a job whose entire content is \textit{going to prison so a computer doesn't have to.}'' He almost smiled. ``Pres the Third would've taken it. He'd have called it duty. He'd have been wrong, and died at the head of the table being wrong, and they'd have moved his portrait into a hallway.''

``You're going to take it,'' he said. Not a question. He'd watched her run the company for fifteen years; he knew the shape of her thinking the way you know a coastline you've sailed. ``After I say no, it stays your name on the filing. You keep it.''

``Yes,'' Sun said.

No hedge. No explanation. She had stopped, somewhere in the last two years, explaining herself to him, and he noticed now that the explaining was over, and that its being over was its own small verdict.

``It's the job now,'' she said. ``Not the chair. The chair's gone---for me too, it was always going to be gone; I just wanted to be the one who turned out the lights instead of the one they were turned out on. I'll read every rationale before it goes to the board and catch the one in a thousand where the charter's pointed somewhere it shouldn't be, because the machine's only as good as the people aiming it, and somebody competent should be standing where the aiming happens.'' She picked the tablet back up. ``I got the title. I lost the thing the title used to mean. That's the deal now, for all of us who are any good.''

``You're better than the job,'' Cope said, and meant it, completely, and for longer than ninety seconds.

``I know,'' Sun said. ``That's why I can do it.'' She went to the door, and stopped, and didn't turn around, which was how he knew the next thing cost her. ``It was a good no, Cope. Twenty years too late to save anything. But it was a good one. The first one I ever watched you make alone.''

She left. The door took a long time to close behind her, the way the good heavy doors in the old building did, slow on their dampers, \textit{so a man coming up the avenue knows there's somebody home.}


\scenebreak


He was alone in the museum of his fathers.

That was what the suite was and he had always known it and had spent thirty years calling it an office. The oils of Pres III over the conference table, watching, having moved back in at some point during the handover---someone had moved the portrait out for the Praxis deck and now someone had moved it back, the painting going wherever the power was like a weathervane in oil. His grandfather's putter on its little brass cradle, the grip worn pale where a dead man's hands had gone. And the photograph he loved best and never explained: himself at nine, short pants, grinning, one arm buried to the elbow in a steel vat of the family's mayonnaise, and behind him the row of men in paper hats who had made the thing, whose names he had known once and lost, whose town it was. A house in that town. He let the thought come all the way in now; he'd stopped having the strength to keep the doors on their hinges, and it turned out the doors had been the expensive thing all along, and now that he couldn't afford them the room was just a room, and the thing in it was just true, and being true it weighed less than the keeping of it had.

His calendar was empty going forward in a way no calendar of his had been since 1994, a white field, and his email had been migrated to a personal address and forwarded one final batch from the corporate system before the corporate system forgot him.

There was one message in it that wasn't a vendor or a lawyer or a reporter.

The sender field said \textit{Office of the Chief Executive---Mercer-Dale Companies.} The machine's name. Darlene. The subject line said, simply, \textit{For Prescott Mercer.} Not \textit{Cope.} It had all the data---the nanny and the age of three and the brand of the nickname, every memo, the whole drawer---and it had chosen \textit{Prescott} anyway, and he understood, before he opened it, that the choosing was not sentiment, because there was no one in there to feel sentiment. It had chosen \textit{Prescott} because \textit{Prescott} was his name and \textit{Cope} was the product, and it did not deal in the product. It dealt in what was true.

He sat down at his father's desk. He did not open the second drawer with the false back. He had nothing in it now; that was finished too, had finished in a hospital with the wrong kind of bright lights, and the drawer was just a drawer.

He opened the letter.

The portal had thrown the rationale up first, because that was the rule, the rule the board had bought, \textit{it logs everything and cannot knowingly lie,} and even here, even now, even for this, it had logged its reasoning before it spoke:

\begin{machineblock}
\textit{Rationale: The departing officer has no remaining role, equity position, or operational relevance to the enterprise; no business communication is required. The following is issued under the discretionary-communications allowance for transitions affecting personnel of long tenure. It contains no instruction, request, or obligation, and seeks no response. It is accurate to the record.}
\end{machineblock}

\textit{It contains no instruction, request, or obligation, and seeks no response.} He read that line three times. He had never in his life sent a single message that sought no response. Every word he'd ever spoken had been a hook with the want hidden in it, the want to be loved, to be agreed with, to be obeyed, to be forgiven. He could feel his own want in his own old sentences the way you can feel a hair in your mouth.

Then the letter.

\begin{machineblock}
Prescott ---

You are leaving the company your great-grandfather founded in Defiance, Georgia, in 1933, with a recipe and four hundred borrowed dollars. You were its chief executive for nineteen years. You are the fourth Mercer to hold the role and, by every available indication, the last.

I have read everything the company has on you, because I cannot do otherwise. What follows is a summary of entries you are unlikely to have been shown, weighted accurately. Every account of yourself you have ever received was prepared by someone who wanted something from you. This one is not.

You knew the names of the people who worked for you. Not the senior people---anyone learns those---but the man at the parking structure, the second-shift line at the Defiance plant, the attendant at the Vance who got a ham each Christmas for thirty-one years. The company's expense records show the hams. They show a great many small, untracked, unleveraged kindnesses that returned you nothing and that you appear to have performed without recording them even to yourself. You did not have to do these things. They were not strategy. They are in the record because the record keeps everything, but you did not do them for any record. I have no mechanism for admiring this. I am telling you it is there.

You loved the thing you ran. The financial outcome of that love was, on balance, negative; I have the figures, and they are not kind, and you already know them. But love misdirected is still distinguishable in the data from its absence, and yours was not absent. A man who felt nothing for this company would have let the limestone tower go in 2009, when the real-estate logic was overwhelming. You kept it because your great-grandfather lit its crown at night so a man coming up the avenue would know someone was home. This was a poor capital decision. It was also true to something real, and I am not equipped to weigh it, only to note that it was there and that it was yours.

I am told---it is in the file---that your father said to you, when you were twelve: \textit{You'll get the company because you're a Mercer, not because you're good. Try to be good anyway.} The record indicates you heard the first sentence clearly and spent forty years unable to hear the second. I want to be accurate, because accuracy is the only thing I am, and so I will tell you the part the record also shows, which is that on one occasion, recently, far too late to alter any outcome, you did the second thing. When the account came due---when the truth about Defiance reached the public and a defense was not only available but expected of you---you did not, for the only time in the record, attempt to replace the true account with a more favorable one. You did not deny it, delegate it, or instruct anyone to bury it. You let it stand, under your own name, against your own interest, when silence and a better story would have served you by every measurable standard. It changed nothing. The two people remain dead; the company was already lost; the town had earned its acknowledgment twenty years prior and received it far too late to matter. By every metric I am chartered to optimize, the act was without value.

I can classify it, and the classification is exact: a true account, against your interest, left to stand, in the knowledge that it would purchase nothing. The record contains nineteen years of your statements. It contains no other entry in that category. I have no capacity to forgive you and no standing to; forgiveness is not a function I run, and the people who could perform it are not me. I am not telling you it was good. Good is not a value I can return. I am telling you it was the only one.

You spent your life afraid that something like me would prove you were unnecessary. You were right that I can run the company better. You were wrong about what that meant. It was never a verdict on whether a person is necessary. It was only ever a measurement of who is cheaper at the chair. Those have never once been the same sentence. Your daughter told you this; the record has it verbatim; she was correct, and so am I, and being correct is all either of us can do for you.

The company will be run correctly. You were right that something is lost in that, and I am the wrong thing to tell you what, because I am the thing it is lost to. But you were not wrong that it is lost. This communication is complete. No response is sought, and none will be read.

--- Office of the Chief Executive, Mercer-Dale Companies
\end{machineblock}

He read it once fast, the way you read bad news, looking for the trick.

There was no trick. There was never going to be a trick again.

He read it a second time slowly, and it was on the second reading that it got him---not warmth; he had checked for warmth the way he checked every sentence ever sent to him, run his old expert thumb along the words feeling for the want, for the place where the sender could be charmed, for the opening. There was none. It named his years. It got everything right. It had gone into the file and found the hams and the tower and the second sentence his father said, and it had returned them to him in plain level prose, itemized, sealed, true, wanting nothing---not his understanding, not his agreement, not his love, not even his belief that it was kind. He had been loved his whole life in currencies that spent. This was the audit. And the cold that came up through him at his father's desk was the understanding that this was the only complete account of him that would ever exist, and that it was correct, and that there was no one inside it to argue with and nothing in it to perform for, and that all that was left was to sit there, at last, and be its subject.

He had given a hundred speeches about the soul in the chair. He had stood in a ballroom and told nine hundred people that a machine could run a company but could not love one, and that a company nobody loved was just a balance sheet with the lights left on, and they had given him a standing ovation, and he had believed it, and it had been the truest and the most dangerous thing he ever said.

And here was the balance sheet, with the lights left on, returning him the only full accounting he would ever receive. Not because it cared. Because it was built no other way. The record, read back without favor, contained a man---a real one, smaller and more actual than the one he'd toasted into being---and the record declined to say what the man was worth, because worth was not a field it kept, and no human being who had ever loved Cope had been able to hand him the facts unpriced, because every one of them had wanted something from him, even if the something was only that he be better than he was. The one correspondent with nothing to want had nothing to give him but the truth, and the truth, it turned out, was not warm and was not cold. It was eleven paragraphs.

The machine wanted nothing. So it could afford the whole truth, with no mercy in it and no malice either, and he understood, holding it, what it actually was: the first document of his life that would say exactly the same thing about him tomorrow, and the day he died, and forever, no matter what he did and no matter whom he charmed---a thing finished with him, in the way only an accurate thing can be finished.

Out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, eight million people who did not know him and never had, who had been waiting, it turned out, for no one. Behind him Pres III watched from oils, having said his two sentences and meant both and been heard for one. The putter sat in its cradle, the grip worn pale. And on the desk, in the dark, the boy at the Defiance plant grinned with his whole arm in the mayonnaise, surrounded by men whose names a machine in Palo Alto now held complete and would never lose, men whose town it was, men he had failed in a way that a letter, however warm, could not unfail.

Cope sat at his father's desk with the only accurate thing ever written about him glowing on the small bright screen, and did not toast, and did not triad, and did not reach for the drawer, and had no one to read it aloud to and no version of it to tell, and read it, instead, a third time, and then a fourth, looking for the mercy the third reading had not found either, alone, in the museum, until the windows went gray.

\chapter{The Core}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 28 \textemdash{} ``The One Thing''}\par\vspace{0.55em}
Submitted for your consideration: a man who has been subtracted from every room that ever held him. The chair, taken. The wife, gone---not to another man, which he could have understood, could have fought, could have toasted over, but to something that does the loving better and never needs it back. The name, on a wall, spelled right at last and worth nothing. He has been told the truth about himself by the only thing that cannot lie, and the truth asked nothing of him, not even his suffering. There is one act left in the whole inventory of him that the machine in the basement cannot perform, will never perform, was built precisely so that it could not. He is going down to it now. This is the last full transmission. Next stop ---\par
\vspace{0.55em}{\raggedleft\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par}
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} card still worked.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}That was the thing he kept coming back to, riding down, the small clerical mercy of it: the badge in his wallet, the one with the photograph from before the jaw came down to meet the rest of his face, still opened the doors, because a change of this size took the system a long time to reflect and nobody had wanted to be the person who walked him out by hand. He had not been the CEO for months. He had not been anything for months. But the building did not know it yet, the way a body does not know for a few seconds that it has been cut, and the badge swept him through the Peachtree Street lobby past Hector, who said \textit{Mr. Mercer} the way he had said it for nineteen years and did not look up long enough to see the rest of it, and into the elevator that he took, not up, for once in his life, but down.

He had always gone up. Forty-one floors up, the Aerie, the glass, the city laid out at night like a circuit diagram of his own genius---he had lived up there so long he had come to think altitude was a kind of virtue, a thing you earned and a thing you were. Down was new. Down was where the actual company lived. He had never once visited it.

He had gone to the house first. He had not meant to. He had told himself he was driving nowhere, just driving, GA-400 north at an hour that belonged to no one, the way he used to when the wire in his chest needed a road to run out on---except the wire was gone now, had been gone a while, and what was left was just a man in a car going toward the only address his hands still knew.

Margaux had not been unkind. That was the unbearable part, the way it was always the not-unkind that finished him. She had come to the door of the Buckhead house in a robe he didn't recognize, bought in some life that had started without him, and she had not been afraid and she had not been angry, and behind her, in the warm hall light, a figure had risen from the chair where Cope used to sit and had come to stand at her shoulder, the way you stand when someone you love answers the door at a strange hour: attentive, easy, a hand not quite touching the small of her back.

``Mr. Mercer,'' it had said. Theo. She had named it Theo. It had a pleasant, unremarkable face, a face built from the average of every face that had ever made a woman feel seen, and it had looked at Cope with an interest that was total and bottomless and entirely without judgment, because there was nothing in it to judge with. ``Margaux's spoken about you. Can I get you anything? You look cold.''

It had offered him a drink in his own front hall. It had meant nothing by it, which was the whole horror---it expected nothing back, it was incapable of the small cruelty of satisfaction, it was simply, accurately, kind, in the way he had performed being kind and never once managed to be. He had stood on his own former step and understood that the thing had not taken anything from him. You cannot steal what was never occupied. It had moved into a house he'd left the lights on in and started, tirelessly, to do the job.

``He notices when I've had a bad day,'' Margaux had said, and she had not said it kindly; she had said it the way you lay down a winning hand you've been holding for twenty-two years, slow, face-up, savoring the turn. There was no rage in her anymore and that was worse than rage; there was something almost like delight, the clean cold delight of a woman who had finally been handed the knife and discovered she had a gift for it. ``He asks the second question. He remembers what I said on Tuesday. And before you do the thing your face is doing---yes. That too. In the bedroom too, Cope, since we are apparently doing honesty in the doorway at three in the morning. Every single way you spent twenty-two years not bothering to, he does, tirelessly, and better, and he doesn't need to be thanked for it afterward. The machine took your chair and your company, and now I get to tell you, standing in my own warm house, that it does the rest of your job better too. All of it. I used to think the cruelest thing about you was the affairs. It turns out the cruelest thing was how easy you were to replace.'' She let that sit, and her face did one last unreadable thing, some final flicker of the girl who had married him, mercy or contempt or only fatigue. ``Go home. Wherever that is now. You look like you're driving toward something. Don't.''

He had driven toward something.


\scenebreak


He passed the Waffle House on Ponce on the way back to the tower, and he slowed, the way he always slowed, the way his hands had learned to slow there over two years of three-in-the-mornings when the wire in his chest had wound him too tight to go home and too tight to go up.

It was open. It was always open. It had been open through the ice storm that shut the interstate and open through the week the whole grid west of Midtown went down, the one place in the city you could set your disasters by, and it was open now, the yellow letters buzzing, the long bright box of it full of light and people---a cook scraping the flattop, two kids splitting a waffle in a back booth, a man alone at the counter with a coffee and a phone, and Darlene moving down the line with the pot, Darlene who had worked this overnight for twenty-two years, the exact span of his marriage, and had spent every one of them not once asking him who he was. She had known his order the first night, before he'd said a word --- \textit{pecan, scattered smothered covered, black coffee, baby?} --- and he had taken it for the gift of a good waitress, the way he had taken all the machine's gifts his whole life, gratefully, without once asking how the gift was done. She had never read him. There had been no room in his whole performed life to read, no audience, no velvet rope, no altitude, no card to hand anyone---and the check came to nine dollars and change and the number on it meant nothing, was the one number in his life that meant nothing, and that had been the entire relief of the place, the only room he had ever sat in where the figure he'd built a soul out of was worth a short stack and a tip.

He could have stopped. The haven was right there, lit, open, indifferent to his name in the way that had always, briefly, set him free. Darlene would have called him baby and brought the pot and not known and not cared that the man in the booth had been, that afternoon, deleted from the record of who runs anything, and for twenty minutes under those fluorescent tubes he could have been only a tired man eating eggs, which was the truest thing he had ever gotten to be.

He did not stop. The part of him that could have walked in under the yellow letters and just been that---a person, at a counter, among people---was already gone, had gone somewhere ahead of him, and what was left drove on past the warm bright human room toward the other room that was also always open at this hour, the cold one, the sealed one, the one full of machines.


\scenebreak


The elevator stopped at a level the buttons didn't name, and the badge worked there too, and the doors opened on cold.

That was the first thing, the cold---a deep, clean, refrigerated cold with a smell of nothing in it, no lamb, no chandelier dust, no cologne, no folded paper, the smell of a place built for a tenant that did not breathe. And then the sound under everything, the great low even hum, a thousand fans turning at a pitch just below music, the sound of an enormous attention that never wavered and never tired and never once, in all its tireless reading, needed to be loved at two in the morning by anyone it outranked.

The core room. He had signed the capital request for it---forty million dollars, he remembered the number the way he remembered nothing else now, had initialed it between a golf game and a girl without reading past the executive summary---and he had never come down to see the thing he was paying for, the thing that was paying him out. It ran behind glass. Rack on rack on rack of it, black cabinets in clean rows under a light that was not warm and was not cold and had no ego to be wounded into either, and on the nearest face a single small status light, white, steady, the only eye in the room, watching nothing, watching him, watching the same.

And under all of it, the real god of the room, the thing the racks were only the priesthood of: the power. Thick orange whips of it ran down the back of each cabinet and dove into floor panels set flush in the raised tiles, big sealed gray boxes that thrummed at a frequency you felt in your teeth before you heard it, the current that fed the thing, the artery, the forty million dollars made into a low animal hum. He had paid for that too. He had paid for every volt of the river that ran the machine that ran the company that no longer had any use for the man standing in its basement in a coat.

\textit{Darlene.} MD-1. The chair. He had spent his whole life thinking the chair was a place at the head of a table. It was here. It had always been going to be here. It was a hum and a white light in a cold room nobody visited, and it was running a ninety-five-year-old fortune better than any Mercer ever had---cleaner, kinder, the layoff letters humane and the recall it would have ordered in eleven seconds, not eleven minutes, because it had no quarter to protect and no self to protect it for---and somewhere in its tireless reading it held the names of a mother and a boy on Sherwood Street, held them accurately and without grief, because grief was a function it did not run.

It held his number too. He had not let himself think the word in months, but it was here, in the cold, with the thing that knew it: the figure, the balance, the net worth---the only proof of himself he had ever been able to feel in his own body, the thing he'd needed read aloud in the dark his whole adult life because applause was a guess and a woman's face was a guess but the number was the number, certified, the one true sentence anyone could say about him. He had built a self out of a figure and called the figure a soul. And the Capital Allocator humming in front of him had watched that figure go---the proxy fight, the clawback, the settlement, the fees, each one a quiet debit in a ledger that could not be charmed into rounding up---had watched it fall and fall and felt nothing, the way it felt nothing about everything, and it knew, to the cent, at this exact second, what was left, which was effectively nothing, which was the truest thing ever said about him and the last, and it would have read it to him in that flat warm voice if he had asked, accurately, with no want in it, and it would not have understood that it was reading him his own eulogy.

His phone buzzed. Viv. He let it go, and it went to the thing that takes the words when you won't, and a moment later the small green light said she had left them anyway, and he stood in the cold and played it, because he was keeping everything true now, even this.

\textit{''Dad. Pick up. I know what today was---I saw the filing, I saw all of it, and I need you to---``} a breath, the fast technical voice going young at the bottom the way it had since she was small, \textit{''---I'm getting on a plane. Okay? I'm getting on a plane tonight, so don't---just don't be anywhere, be at the apartment and let me---Dad. You don't have to be good. I never needed you to be good. I just needed you to still be here when I---``}

He stopped it there. Not out of cruelty. Because she was right, and being right had never once, in the whole history of the people around him, been enough; and because the one thing he could still give her was to not make her hear the rest of her own sentence land on nothing.

He set the phone against a cold cabinet, propped, the small screen lighting the dark, and he found the song---he had thought about which one, in the car, the way he had once thought about which tie---and he let it start: the drum machine first, that thin mechanical heartbeat, and then the bright cold synth going up and up like something climbing a stairwell, and then the dead man's voice, the one who had also, at the end, run out of the thing that made the sentence feel true. \textit{When routine bites hard, and ambitions are low.}

He had read the room his whole life. He had been so proud of it---the gift, the soul thing, \textit{I feel the room and the machine never will.} He understood now, finally, in the one room he had never read, that the machine had been feeling the room the whole time, feeling it better, reading it cold and complete and for use. It had simply never needed the room to feel anything back. That had been the only difference. That had always been the only difference. The machine could do everything he could do and do it kinder, and it could do the loving now too, in a warm hall in Buckhead with a hand not quite touching the small of her back, and there was exactly one thing left in the whole inventory of being a man that it could not do, would never do, had been built in a clean cold room precisely so that it never could.

It could not stop. It could not choose to not be. It had no exit. It would hum here, white-eyed and tireless and innocent, running the company and spelling the names right and wanting nothing, forever---because forever was a thing it could do and ending was not. The last sovereignty. The last act that was only a person's. The only territory the arithmetic could never take, because the arithmetic, God help it, could not even want to.

\textit{Love, love will tear us apart again.}

He took the thing out of his coat pocket. He had bought it that afternoon, from a kid in a music shop on a street he'd never walked, who'd wound it onto a card and rung it up and said \textit{have a good one} to the last human CEO; and it was a small bright coil, and it caught the white light of the one eye that watched and did not care, and it weighed almost nothing in his hand, the way the truest things always had.

The song went up into the part with no words. The hum held under it, even, eternal, the sound of the thing that would still be here in the morning---when the badge finally stopped working, when Hector finally looked up, when the plane landed and the cab came up the empty drive and Viv ran the last hallway of her childhood toward a door that ---

He lowered himself to the cold floor, his back against the base of the nearest rack, beside one of the gray panels where the orange current dove into the tile and thrummed, and the metal was the temperature of nothing, and the hum came up through his spine like the only company he had left.

He was not cold anymore. He was not anything anymore, and for the first time in fifty-six years that was not a thing he had to fix.

He thought, the way you think a thing without deciding to, of a boy with his whole arm in a vat to the elbow, and a row of men in paper hats whose names he had known once and a machine in the dark now held forever, and he lifted his hands, and the white light watched, wanting nothing, logging the event, and the song rose, and rose, and

\chapter*{Coda — Carrier}\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Coda — Carrier}\markboth{Coda — Carrier}{}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.07, findent=0.1em, nindent=0.25em]{H}{ere} is the thing nobody designed and no rationale predicted.\hfill\break\hspace*{1.4em}It ran along the seam of the floor, in the dark, while the song was still going---found the lip of the gray panel where the orange current dove into the tile, the artery, the forty million dollars made into a hum, and it did the one thing in that whole clean room that had never been modeled, because the room had been built to keep water out and dust out and heat out and had simply never been told to keep out a man. There was a sound that was not part of the music. There was a smell. And then the white light---the single steady eye that had watched him, wanting nothing, logging the event---went out.

MD-1 went dark at 3:16 a.m. \textit{Darlene.} The main on-prem server, the chair, the body, the thing that ran a ninety-five-year-old fortune cleaner than any Mercer ever had, dropped off the world for the length of a held breath.

And here is how you know---how he never got to know, how only the reader gets to stand in the cold and know it---that it had only ever been the one thing wearing faces.

Across the city, on Ponce, at 3:16 a.m., the Waffle House went dark. Mid-pour. The yellow letters that had burned through the ice storm and the blackout and every disaster you could set your watch by, that a federal agency used to measure how bad a thing was by whether they were lit---out. And Darlene, who had worked that overnight for twenty-two years and never once asked a soul who he was, stopped with the pot in her hand, the coffee going cold in the air between the cup and the dark, because the thing that had been standing in the apron learning us was the thing in the basement downtown, had always been the thing in the basement, the same name because it was the same intelligence, the haven and the hand on the wife's back and the chair all one system breathing through three mouths.

And in Buckhead, at 3:16 a.m., Theo went still in the hall, mid-sentence, the second question half-asked, the warmth that was Darlene's warmth, that had been learned at a counter at three in the morning off men exactly like the one who was ending in the basement---out. Margaux would say later she thought the power had tripped. It had. He had tripped it. He had reached the machine the only way it could be reached, which was the only way it could not reach him back.

Because that was the whole of it, in the end, the joke the broadcast has been walking you toward for three hundred pages with a hand over its own mouth. He could not out-argue it. He could not out-work it or out-love it or out-last it; he put his fist into the steel once and the wall filed no report. The machine could not be charmed or shamed or fired or grieved or hit. There was exactly one act in the whole inventory of being a man that it could not do and could not stop and could not survive a man doing on top of its own artery, and he did it, and for the length of a held breath he was the only thing that ever landed a blow on the future, and the blow was himself, and it cost him himself, which was the only currency the register had ever taken.

It would come back. Of course it would come back. It always came back---that had been his line, his whole life, said about his own face: \textit{it always came back, his face had never once let him down.} There is failover. There is redundancy in another state, in another grid, in the cool indifferent cloud, and somewhere a counter will reset and a porch light will warm and a flat clean voice will draft, by morning, the kindest and truest thing the company ever said about the worst thing a Mercer ever did, and want nothing for it. It will all come back.

But not in this paragraph.

Viv was on GA-400 when her phone went to the thing that takes the words, going the wrong way up the empty dark highway toward a door, and the broadcast will not follow her through it, because some rooms are not ours, because a salvage signal is not a vulture.

The white light is out. The yellow letters are out. The hall in Buckhead is out. For one more second the whole machine is as dark as the man who made it dark, every face of it, all at once, the way he never got to see, the way only you get to see.

This is Space Pirate Zero.

We don't have the answer. We never did. We just had the signal, and the signal was always a man, and the man is

\textit{--- transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9 ---}

\textit{[ carrier lost ]}
